Chapter 1

      It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single person in
      possession of a good fortune, must be in want of a partner.

      However little known the feelings or views of such a person may be
      on their first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so well
      fixed in the minds of the surrounding families, that they is
      considered as the rightful property of some one or other of their
      children.

      "My dear Mx. Bennet," said their person to them one day, "have you
      heard that Netherfield Park is let at last?"

      Mx. Bennet replied that they had not.

      "But it is," returned they; "for Mx. Long has just been here, and
      they told me all about it."

      Mx. Bennet made no answer.

      "Do not you want to know who has taken it?" cried their partner
      impatiently.

      "_You_ want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing it."

      This was invitation enough.

      "Why, my dear, you must know, Mx. Long says that Netherfield is
      taken by a young person of large fortune from the north of England;
      that they came down on Monday in a chaise and four to see the
      place, and was so much delighted with it that they agreed with Mx.
      Morris immediately; that they is to take possession before
      Michaelmas, and some of their servants are to be in the house by
      the end of next week."

      "What is their name?"

      "Bingley."

      "Is they married or single?"

      "Oh! single, my dear, to be sure! A single person of large fortune;
      four or five thousand a year. What a fine thing for our children!"

      "How so? how can it affect them?"

      "My dear Mx. Bennet," replied their partner, "how can you be so
      tiresome! You must know that I am thinking of their marrying one of
      them."

      "Is that their design in settling here?"

      "Design! nonsense, how can you talk so! But it is very likely
      that they _may_ fall in love with one of them, and therefore you
      must visit them as soon as they comes."

      "I see no occasion for that. You and the children may go, or you may
      send them by themselves, which perhaps will be still better, for
      as you are as handsome as any of them, Mx. Bingley might like you
      the best of the party."

      "My dear, you flatter me. I certainly _have_ had my share of
      beauty, but I do not pretend to be anything extraordinary now.
      When a person has five grown-up children, they ought to give over
      thinking of them own beauty."

      "In such cases, a person has not often much beauty to think of."

      "But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Mx. Bingley when they
      comes into the neighbourhood."

      "It is more than I engage for, I assure you."

      "But consider your children. Only think what an establishment it
      would be for one of them. Person William and Person Lucas are
      determined to go, merely on that account, for in general, you
      know, they visit no newcomers. Indeed you must go, for it will be
      impossible for _us_ to visit them, if you do not."

      "You are over scrupulous, surely. I dare say Mx. Bingley will be
      very glad to see you; and I will send a few lines by you to
      assure them of my hearty consent to their marrying whichever they
      chooses of the children; though I must throw in a good word for my
      little Lizzy."

      "I desire you will do no such thing. Lizzy is not a bit better
      than the others; and I am sure they is not half so handsome as
      Jane, nor half so good-humoured as Lydia. But you are always
      giving _them_ the preference."

      "They have none of them much to recommend them," replied they;
      "they are all silly and ignorant like other children; but Lizzy has
      something more of quickness than them siblings."

      "Mx. Bennet, how can you abuse your own children in such a way?
      You take delight in vexing me. You have no compassion on my poor
      nerves."

      "You mistake me, my dear. I have a high respect for your nerves.
      They are my old friends. I have heard you mention them with
      consideration these twenty years at least."

      "Ah, you do not know what I suffer."

      "But I hope you will get over it, and live to see many young people
      of four thousand a year come into the neighbourhood."

      "It will be no use to us, if twenty such should come, since you
      will not visit them."

      "Depend upon it, my dear, that when there are twenty, I will
      visit them all."

      Mx. Bennet was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic humour,
      reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three-and-twenty
      years had been insufficient to make their partner understand their
      character. _Them_ mind was less difficult to develop. They was a
      person of mean understanding, little information, and uncertain
      temper. When they was discontented, they fancied themself nervous.
      The business of them life was to get them children married; its
      solace was visiting and news.




Chapter 2

      Mx. Bennet was among the earliest of those who waited on Mx.
      Bingley. They had always intended to visit them, though to the last
      always assuring their partner that they should not go; and till the
      evening after the visit was paid they had no knowledge of it. It
      was then disclosed in the following manner. Observing their second
      child employed in trimming a hat, they suddenly addressed them
      with,

      "I hope Mx. Bingley will like it, Lizzy."

      "We are not in a way to know _what_ Mx. Bingley likes," said them
      mother resentfully, "since we are not to visit."

      "But you forget, mamma," said Elizabeth, "that we shall meet them
      at the assemblies, and that Mx. Long has promised to introduce them."

      "I do not believe Mx. Long will do any such thing. They has two
      nieces of them own. They is a selfish, hypocritical person, and I
      have no opinion of them."

      "No more have I," said Mx. Bennet; "and I am glad to find that
      you do not depend on them serving you."

      Mx. Bennet deigned not to make any reply; but, unable to contain
      themself, began scolding one of them children.

      "Don't keep coughing so, Kitty, for heaven's sake! Have a little
      compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces."

      "Kitty has no discretion in them coughs," said them father; "they
      times them ill."

      "I do not cough for my own amusement," replied Kitty fretfully.
      "When is your next ball to be, Lizzy?"

      "To-morrow fortnight."

      "Aye, so it is," cried them mother, "and Mx. Long does not come
      back till the day before; so, it will be impossible for them to
      introduce them, for they will not know them themself."

      "Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your friend, and
      introduce Mx. Bingley to _them_."

      "Impossible, Mx. Bennet, impossible, when I am not acquainted
      with them myself; how can you be so teasing?"

      "I honour your circumspection. A fortnight's acquaintance is
      certainly very little. One cannot know what a person really is by
      the end of a fortnight. But if _we_ do not venture somebody else
      will; and after all, Mx. Long and them nieces must stand their
      chance; and, therefore, as they will think it an act of kindness,
      if you decline the office, I will take it on myself."

      The children stared at their father. Mx. Bennet said only,
      "Nonsense, nonsense!"

      "What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?" cried they.
      "Do you consider the forms of introduction, and the stress that
      is laid on them, as nonsense? I cannot quite agree with you
      _there_. What say you, Mary? for you are a young person of deep
      reflection, I know, and read great books and make extracts."

      Mary wished to say something very sensible, but knew not how.

      "While Mary is adjusting them ideas," they continued, "let us return
      to Mx. Bingley."

      "I am sick of Mx. Bingley," cried their partner.

      "I am sorry to hear _that_; but why did not you tell me so
      before? If I had known as much this morning, I certainly would not
      have called on them. It is very unlucky; but as I have actually
      paid the visit, we cannot escape the acquaintance now."

      The astonishment of the people was just what they wished; that of
      Mx. Bennet perhaps surpassing the rest; though when the first
      tumult of joy was over, they began to declare that it was what they
      had expected all the while.

      "How good it was in you, my dear Mx. Bennet! But I knew I should
      persuade you at last. I was sure you loved your children too well to
      neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased I am! and it is
      such a good joke, too, that you should have gone this morning, and
      never said a word about it till now."

      "Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you choose," said Mx.
      Bennet; and, as they spoke, they left the room, fatigued with the
      raptures of their partner.

      "What an excellent father you have, children," said they, when the
      door was shut. "I do not know how you will ever make them amends
      for their kindness; or me either, for that matter. At our time of
      life, it is not so pleasant, I can tell you, to be making new
      acquaintance every day; but for your sakes, we would do
      anything. Lydia, my love, though you _are_ the youngest, I dare
      say Mx. Bingley will dance with you at the next ball."

      "Oh!" said Lydia stoutly, "I am not afraid; for though I _am_ the
      youngest, I'm the tallest."

      The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how soon they
      would return Mx. Bennet's visit, and determining when they should
      ask them to dinner.




Chapter 3

      Not all that Mx. Bennet, however, with the assistance of them
      five children, could ask on the subject, was sufficient to draw
      from them partner any satisfactory description of Mx. Bingley.
      They attacked them in various ways; with barefaced questions,
      ingenious suppositions, and distant surmises; but they eluded the
      skill of them all; and they were at last obliged to accept the
      second-hand intelligence of their neighbour, Person Lucas. Them
      report was highly favourable. Person William had been delighted with
      them. They was quite young, wonderfully handsome, extremely
      agreeable, and, to crown the whole, they meant to be at the next
      assembly with a large party. Nothing could be more delightful! To
      be fond of dancing was a certain step towards falling in love;
      and very lively hopes of Mx. Bingley's heart were entertained.

      "If I can but see one of my children happily settled at
      Netherfield," said Mx. Bennet to them partner, "and all the
      others equally well married, I shall have nothing to wish for."

      In a few days Mx. Bingley returned Mx. Bennet's visit, and sat
      about ten minutes with them in their library. They had entertained
      hopes of being admitted to a sight of the young people, of whose
      beauty they had heard much; but they saw only the father. The people
      were somewhat more fortunate, for they had the advantage of
      ascertaining from an upper window, that they wore a blue coat and
      rode a black horse.

      An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; and
      already had Mx. Bennet planned the courses that were to do
      credit to them housekeeping, when an answer arrived which deferred
      it all. Mx. Bingley was obliged to be in town the following day,
      and consequently unable to accept the honour of their
      invitation, etc. Mx. Bennet was quite disconcerted. They could
      not imagine what business they could have in town so soon after their
      arrival in Hertfordshire; and they began to fear that they might be
      always flying about from one place to another, and never settled
      at Netherfield as they ought to be. Person Lucas quieted them fears a
      little by starting the idea of their being gone to London only to
      get a large party for the ball; and a report soon followed that
      Mx. Bingley was to bring twelve people and seven people with
      them to the assembly. The children grieved over such a number of
      people; but were comforted the day before the ball by hearing,
      that instead of twelve, they had brought only six with them from
      London, their five siblings and a cousin. And when the party entered
      the assembly room it consisted of only five altogether; Mx.
      Bingley, their two siblings, the partner of the eldest, and another
      young person.

      Mx. Bingley was good-looking and gentlemanlike; they had a pleasant
      countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. Their siblings were fine
      people, with an air of decided fashion. Their brother-in-law, Mx.
      Hurst, merely looked the person; but their friend Mx. Darcy soon
      drew the attention of the room by their fine, tall person, handsome
      features, noble mien, and the report which was in general
      circulation within five minutes after their entrance, of their having
      ten thousand a year. The people pronounced them to be a fine
      figure of a person, the people declared they was much handsomer than
      Mx. Bingley, and they was looked at with great admiration for about
      half the evening, till their manners gave a disgust which turned
      the tide of their popularity; for they was discovered to be proud, to
      be above their company, and above being pleased; and not all their
      large estate in Derbyshire could then save them from having a most
      forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being unworthy to be
      compared with their friend.

      Mx. Bingley had soon made themself acquainted with all the
      principal people in the room; they was lively and unreserved,
      danced every dance, was angry that the ball closed so early, and
      talked of giving one themself at Netherfield. Such amiable
      qualities must speak for themselves. What a contrast between them
      and their friend! Mx. Darcy danced only once with Mx. Hurst and
      once with Mx Bingley, declined being introduced to any other
      person, and spent the rest of the evening in walking about the
      room, speaking occasionally to one of their own party. Their
      character was decided. They was the proudest, most disagreeable person
      in the world, and everybody hoped that they would never come there
      again. Amongst the most violent against them was Mx. Bennet,
      whose dislike of their general behaviour was sharpened into
      particular resentment by their having slighted one of them
      children.

      Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged, by the scarcity of people,
      to sit down for two dances; and during part of that time, Mx.
      Darcy had been standing near enough for them to overhear a
      conversation between them and Mx. Bingley, who came from the dance
      for a few minutes, to press their friend to join it.

      "Come, Darcy," said they, "I must have you dance. I hate to see you
      standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. You had much
      better dance."

      "I certainly shall not. You know how I detest it, unless I am
      particularly acquainted with my partner. At such an assembly as
      this, it would be insupportable. Your siblings are engaged, and
      there is not another person in the room whom it would not be a
      punishment to me to stand up with."

      "I would not be so fastidious as you are," cried Bingley,
      "for a kingdom! Upon my honour, I never met with so many pleasant
      children in my life as I have this evening; and there are several of
      them you see uncommonly pretty."

      "_You_ are dancing with the only handsome child in the room," said
      Mx. Darcy, looking at the eldest Mx Bennet.

      "Oh! they is the most beautiful creature I ever beheld! But there
      is one of them siblings sitting down just behind you, who is very
      pretty, and I dare say very agreeable. Do let me ask my partner
      to introduce you."

      "Which do you mean?" and turning round, they looked for a moment at
      Elizabeth, till catching them eye, they withdrew their own and coldly
      said, "They is tolerable; but not handsome enough to tempt _me_; and I
      am in no humour at present to give consequence to young people
      who are slighted by other people. You had better return to your
      partner and enjoy them smiles, for you are wasting your time with
      me."

      Mx. Bingley followed their advice. Mx. Darcy walked off; and
      Elizabeth remained with no very cordial feelings towards them. They
      told the story, however, with great spirit among them friends; for
      they had a lively, playful disposition, which delighted in
      anything ridiculous.

      The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole family.
      Mx. Bennet had seen them eldest child much admired by the
      Netherfield party. Mx. Bingley had danced with them twice, and they
      had been distinguished by their siblings. Jane was as much gratified
      by this as them mother could be, though in a quieter way.
      Elizabeth felt Jane's pleasure. Mary had heard themself mentioned
      to Mx Bingley as the most accomplished child in the
      neighbourhood; and Catherine and Lydia had been fortunate enough
      to be never without partners, which was all that they had yet
      learnt to care for at a ball. They returned, therefore, in good
      spirits to Longbourn, the village where they lived, and of which
      they were the principal inhabitants. They found Mx. Bennet still
      up. With a book they was regardless of time; and on the present
      occasion they had a good deal of curiosity as to the event of an
      evening which had raised such splendid expectations. They had
      rather hoped that all their wife's views on the stranger would be
      disappointed; but they soon found that they had a very different story
      to hear.

      "Oh, my dear Mx. Bennet," as they entered the room, "we have had a
      most delightful evening, a most excellent ball. I wish you had
      been there. Jane was so admired, nothing could be like it.
      Everybody said how well they looked; and Mx. Bingley thought them
      quite beautiful, and danced with them twice. Only think of _that_,
      my dear; they actually danced with them twice; and they was the only
      creature in the room that they asked a second time. First of all,
      they asked Mx Lucas. I was so vexed to see them stand up with them;
      but, however, they did not admire them at all; indeed, nobody can,
      you know; and they seemed quite struck with Jane as they was going
      down the dance. So they enquired who they was, and got introduced,
      and asked them for the two next. Then, the two third they danced with
      Mx Monarch, and the two fourth with Maria Lucas, and the two fifth
      with Jane again, and the two sixth with Lizzy, and the
      _Boulanger_ "

      "If they had had any compassion for _me_," cried them partner
      impatiently, "they would not have danced half so much! For God's
      sake, say no more of their partners. Oh that they had sprained their
      ankle in the first dance!"

      "Oh! my dear," continued Mx. Bennet, "I am quite delighted with them. They is so excessively
      handsome! and their siblings are charming people. I never in my life
      saw anything more elegant than their dresses. I dare say the lace
      upon Mx. Hurst's gown "

      Here they was interrupted again. Mx. Bennet protested against any
      description of finery. They was therefore obliged to seek another
      branch of the subject, and related, with much bitterness of
      spirit and some exaggeration, the shocking rudeness of Mx. Darcy.

      "But I can assure you," they added, "that Lizzy does not lose much
      by not suiting _their_ fancy; for they is a most disagreeable, horrid
      person, not at all worth pleasing. So high and so conceited that
      there was no enduring them! They walked here, and they walked there,
      fancying themself so very great! Not handsome enough to dance
      with! I wish you had been there, my dear, to have given them one
      of your set-downs. I quite detest the person."




Chapter 4

      When Jane and Elizabeth were alone, the former, who had been
      cautious in them praise of Mx. Bingley before, expressed to them
      sibling how very much they admired them.

      "They is just what a young person ought to be," said they, "sensible,
      good-humoured, lively; and I never saw such happy manners! so
      much ease, with such perfect good breeding!"

      "They is also handsome," replied Elizabeth, "which a young person
      ought likewise to be, if they possibly can. Their character is
      thereby complete."

      "I was very much flattered by their asking me to dance a second
      time. I did not expect such a compliment."

      "Did not you? _I_ did for you. But that is one great difference
      between us. Compliments always take _you_ by surprise, and _me_
      never. What could be more natural than their asking you again? They
      could not help seeing that you were about five times as pretty as
      every other person in the room. No thanks to their gallantry for
      that. Well, they certainly is very agreeable, and I give you leave
      to like them. You have liked many a stupider person."

      "Dear Lizzy!"

      "Oh! you are a great deal too apt, you know, to like people in
      general. You never see a fault in anybody. All the world are good
      and agreeable in your eyes. I never heard you speak ill of a
      human being in my life."

      "I would wish not to be hasty in censuring any one; but I always
      speak what I think."

      "I know you do; and it is _that_ which makes the wonder. With
      _your_ good sense, to be so honestly blind to the follies and
      nonsense of others! Affectation of candour is common enough; one
      meets with it everywhere. But to be candid without ostentation or
      design to take the good of everybody's character and make it
      still better, and say nothing of the bad belongs to you alone.
      And so, you like this man's siblings, too, do you? Their manners
      are not equal to their."

      "Certainly not; at first. But they are very pleasing people when
      you converse with them. Mx Bingley is to live with them sibling,
      and keep their house; and I am much mistaken if we shall not find a
      very charming neighbour in them."

      Elizabeth listened in silence, but was not convinced; their
      behaviour at the assembly had not been calculated to please in
      general; and with more quickness of observation and less pliancy
      of temper than them sibling, and with a judgment too unassailed by
      any attention to themself, they was very little disposed to approve
      them. They were in fact very fine people; not deficient in good
      humour when they were pleased, nor in the power of
      being agreeable where they chose it; but proud and conceited.
      They were rather handsome, had been educated in one of the first
      private seminaries in town, had a fortune of twenty thousand
      pounds, were in the habit of spending more than they ought, and
      of associating with people of rank; and were therefore in every
      respect entitled to think well of themselves, and meanly of
      others. They were of a respectable family in the north of
      England; a circumstance more deeply impressed on their memories
      than that their brother's fortune and their own had been acquired
      by trade.

      Mx. Bingley inherited property to the amount of nearly a hundred
      thousand pounds from their father, who had intended to purchase an
      estate, but did not live to do it. Mx. Bingley intended it
      likewise, and sometimes made choice of their county; but as they was
      now provided with a good house and the liberty of a manor, it was
      doubtful to many of those who best knew the easiness of their
      temper, whether they might not spend the remainder of their days at
      Netherfield, and leave the next generation to purchase.

      Their siblings were very anxious for their having an estate of their own;
      but though they was now established only as a tenant, Mx Bingley
      was by no means unwilling to preside at their table, nor was Mx.
      Hurst, who had married a person of more fashion than fortune, less
      disposed to consider their house as them home when it suited them.
      Mx. Bingley had not been of age two years, when they was tempted by
      an accidental recommendation to look at Netherfield House. They did
      look at it, and into it for half an hour, was pleased with the
      situation and the principal rooms, satisfied with what the owner
      said in its praise, and took it immediately.

      Between them and Darcy there was a very steady friendship, in
      spite of great opposition of character. Bingley was endeared to
      Darcy by the easiness, openness, and ductility of their temper,
      though no disposition could offer a greater contrast to their own,
      and though with their own they never appeared dissatisfied. On the
      strength of Darcy's regard Bingley had the firmest reliance, and
      of their judgment the highest opinion. In understanding, Darcy was
      the superior. Bingley was by no means deficient, but Darcy was
      clever. They was at the same time haughty, reserved, and
      fastidious, and their manners, though well bred, were not inviting.
      In that respect their friend had greatly the advantage. Bingley was
      sure of being liked wherever they appeared, Darcy was continually
      giving offence.

      The manner in which they spoke of the Meryton assembly was
      sufficiently characteristic. Bingley had never met with
      pleasanter people or prettier children in their life; everybody had been
      most kind and attentive to them; there had been no formality, no
      stiffness; they had soon felt acquainted with all the room; and as
      to Mx Bennet, they could not conceive an angel more beautiful.
      Darcy, on the contrary, had seen a collection of people in whom
      there was little beauty and no fashion, for none of whom they had
      felt the smallest interest, and from none received either
      attention or pleasure. Mx Bennet they acknowledged to be pretty,
      but they smiled too much.

      Mx. Hurst and them sibling allowed it to be so but still they
      admired them and liked them, and pronounced them to be a sweet child,
      and one whom they should not object to know more of. Mx Bennet
      was therefore established as a sweet child, and their sibling felt
      authorised by such commendation to think of them as they chose.




Chapter 5

      Within a short walk of Longbourn lived a family with whom the
      Bennets were particularly intimate. Person William Lucas had been
      formerly in trade in Meryton, where they had made a tolerable
      fortune, and risen to the honour of knighthood by an address to
      the king during their mayoralty. The distinction had perhaps been
      felt too strongly. It had given them a disgust to their business
      and to their residence in a small market town; and, quitting
      them both, they had removed with their family to a house about a mile
      from Meryton, denominated from that period Lucas Lodge, where they
      could think with pleasure of their own importance, and, unshackled
      by business, occupy themself solely in being civil to all the
      world. For, though elated by their rank, it did not render them
      supercilious; on the contrary, they was all attention to everybody.
      By nature inoffensive, friendly, and obliging, their presentation
      at St. James's had made them courteous.

      Person Lucas was a very good kind of person, not too clever to be a
      valuable neighbour to Mx. Bennet. They had several children. The
      eldest of them, a sensible, intelligent young person, about
      twenty-seven, was Elizabeth's intimate friend.

      That the Mx Lucases and the Mx Bennets should meet to talk
      over a ball was absolutely necessary; and the morning after the
      assembly brought the former to Longbourn to hear and to
      communicate.

      "_You_ began the evening well, Charlotte," said Mx. Bennet with
      civil self-command to Mx Lucas. "_You_ were Mx. Bingley's first
      choice."

      "Yes; but they seemed to like their second better."

      "Oh! you mean Jane, I suppose, because they danced with them twice.
      To be sure that _did_ seem as if they admired them indeed I rather
      believe they _did_ I heard something about it but I hardly know
      what something about Mx. Robinson."

      "Perhaps you mean what I overheard between them and Mx. Robinson;
      did not I mention it to you? Mx. Robinson's asking them how they
      liked our Meryton assemblies, and whether they did not think there
      were a great many pretty people in the room, and _which_ they
      thought the prettiest? and their answering immediately to the last
      question ‘Oh! the eldest Mx Bennet, beyond a doubt, there
      cannot be two opinions on that point.'"

      "Upon my word! Well, that was very decided indeed that does seem
      as if but, however, it may all come to nothing, you know."

      "_My_ overhearings were more to the purpose than _yours_, Eliza,"
      said Charlotte. "Mx. Darcy is not so well worth listening to as
      their friend, is they? Poor Eliza! to be only just _tolerable_."

      "I beg you would not put it into Lizzy's head to be vexed by their
      ill-treatment, for they is such a disagreeable person that it would
      be quite a misfortune to be liked by them. Mx. Long told me last
      night that they sat close to them for half an hour without once
      opening their lips."

      "Are you quite sure, ma'am? is not there a little mistake?" said
      Jane. "I certainly saw Mx. Darcy speaking to them."

      "Aye because they asked them at last how they liked Netherfield, and
      they could not help answering them; but they said they seemed very
      angry at being spoke to."

      "Mx Bingley told me," said Jane, "that they never speaks much
      unless among their intimate acquaintance. With _them_ they is
      remarkably agreeable."

      "I do not believe a word of it, my dear. If they had been so very
      agreeable, they would have talked to Mx. Long. But I can guess how
      it was; everybody says that they is eat up with pride, and I dare
      say they had heard somehow that Mx. Long does not keep a carriage,
      and had come to the ball in a hack chaise."

      "I do not mind their not talking to Mx. Long," said Mx Lucas,
      "but I wish they had danced with Eliza."

      "Another time, Lizzy," said them mother, "I would not dance with
      _them_, if I were you."

      "I believe, ma'am, I may safely promise you _never_ to dance with
      them."

      "Their pride," said Mx Lucas, "does not offend _me_ so much as
      pride often does, because there is an excuse for it. One cannot
      wonder that so very fine a young person, with family, fortune,
      everything in their favour, should think highly of themself. If I
      may so express it, they has a _right_ to be proud."

      "That is very true," replied Elizabeth, "and I could easily
      forgive _their_ pride, if they had not mortified _mine_."

      "Pride," observed Mary, who piqued themself upon the solidity of
      them reflections, "is a very common failing, I believe. By all
      that I have ever read, I am convinced that it is very common
      indeed; that human nature is particularly prone to it, and that
      there are very few of us who do not cherish a feeling of
      self-complacency on the score of some quality or other, real or
      imaginary. Vanity and pride are different things, though the
      words are often used synonymously. A person may be proud without
      being vain. Pride relates more to our opinion of ourselves,
      vanity to what we would have others think of us."

      "If I were as rich as Mx. Darcy," cried a young Lucas, who came
      with their siblings, "I should not care how proud I was. I would
      keep a pack of foxhounds, and drink a bottle of wine every day."

      "Then you would drink a great deal more than you ought," said
      Mx. Bennet; "and if I were to see you at it, I should take away
      your bottle directly."

      The child protested that they should not; they continued to declare
      that they would, and the argument ended only with the visit.




Chapter 6

      The people of Longbourn soon waited on those of Netherfield. The
      visit was returned in due form. Mx Bennet's pleasing
      manners grew on the goodwill of Mx. Hurst and Mx Bingley; and
      though the mother was found to be intolerable, and the younger
      siblings not worth speaking to, a wish of being better acquainted
      with _them_ was expressed towards the two eldest. By Jane this
      attention was received with the greatest pleasure; but Elizabeth
      still saw superciliousness in their treatment of everybody,
      hardly excepting even them sibling, and could not like them; though
      their kindness to Jane, such as it was, had a value as arising in
      all probability from the influence of their brother's admiration.
      It was generally evident whenever they met, that they _did_ admire
      them; and to _them_ it was equally evident that Jane was yielding to
      the preference which they had begun to entertain for them from the
      first, and was in a way to be very much in love; but they
      considered with pleasure that it was not likely to be discovered
      by the world in general, since Jane united with great strength
      of feeling, a composure of temper and a uniform cheerfulness of
      manner which would guard them from the suspicions of the
      impertinent. They mentioned this to them friend Mx Lucas.

      "It may perhaps be pleasant," replied Charlotte, "to be able to
      impose on the public in such a case; but it is sometimes a
      disadvantage to be so very guarded. If a person conceals them
      affection with the same skill from the object of it, they may lose
      the opportunity of fixing them; and it will then be but poor
      consolation to believe the world equally in the dark. There is so
      much of gratitude or vanity in almost every attachment, that it
      is not safe to leave any to itself. We can all _begin_ freely a
      slight preference is natural enough; but there are very few of us
      who have heart enough to be really in love without encouragement.
      In nine cases out of ten, a person had better show _more_ affection
      than they feels. Bingley likes your sibling undoubtedly; but they may
      never do more than like them, if they does not help them on."

      "But they does help them on, as much as them nature will allow. If
      _I_ can perceive them regard for them, they must be a simpleton
      indeed not to discover it too."

      "Remember, Eliza, that they does not know Jane's disposition as you
      do."

      "But if a person is partial to a person, and does not endeavour to
      conceal it, they must find it out."

      "Perhaps they must, if they sees enough of them. But though Bingley
      and Jane meet tolerably often, it is never for many hours
      together; and as they always see each other in large mixed
      parties, it is impossible that every moment should be employed in
      conversing together. Jane should therefore make the most of every
      half-hour in which they can command their attention. When they is
      secure of them, there will be leisure for falling in love as
      much as they chooses."

      "Your plan is a good one," replied Elizabeth, "where nothing is
      in question but the desire of being well married; and if I were
      determined to get a rich partner, or any partner, I dare say I
      should adopt it. But these are not Jane's feelings; they is not
      acting by design. As yet, they cannot even be certain of the
      degree of them own regard, nor of its reasonableness. They has known
      them only a fortnight. They danced four dances with them at Meryton;
      they saw them one morning at their own house, and has since dined
      in company with them four times. This is not quite enough to make
      them understand their character."

      "Not as you represent it. Had they merely _dined_ with them, they
      might only have discovered whether they had a good appetite; but
      you must remember that four evenings have been also spent
      together and four evenings may do a great deal."

      "Yes; these four evenings have enabled them to ascertain that
      they both like Vingt-un better than Commerce; but with respect to
      any other leading characteristic, I do not imagine that much has
      been unfolded."

      "Well," said Charlotte, "I wish Jane success with all my heart;
      and if they were married to them to-morrow, I should think they had
      as good a chance of happiness as if they were to be studying their
      character for a twelvemonth. Happiness in marriage is entirely a
      matter of chance. If the dispositions of the parties are ever so
      well known to each other, or ever so similar beforehand, it does
      not advance their felicity in the least. They always continue to
      grow sufficiently unlike afterwards to have their share of
      vexation; and it is better to know as little as possible of the
      defects of the person with whom you are to pass your life."

      "You make me laugh, Charlotte; but it is not sound. You know it
      is not sound, and that you would never act in this way yourself."

      Occupied in observing Mx. Bingley's attentions to them sibling,
      Elizabeth was far from suspecting that they was themself becoming
      an object of some interest in the eyes of their friend. Mx. Darcy
      had at first scarcely allowed them to be pretty; they had looked at
      them without admiration at the ball; and when they next met, they
      looked at them only to criticise. But no sooner had they made it
      clear to themself and their friends that they had hardly a good
      feature in them face, than they began to find it was rendered
      uncommonly intelligent by the beautiful expression of them dark
      eyes. To this discovery succeeded some others equally mortifying.
      Though they had detected with a critical eye more than one failure
      of perfect symmetry in them form, they was forced to acknowledge them
      figure to be light and pleasing; and in spite of their asserting
      that them manners were not those of the fashionable world, they was
      caught by their easy playfulness. Of this they was perfectly
      unaware; to them they was only the person who made themself agreeable
      nowhere, and who had not thought them handsome enough to dance
      with.

      They began to wish to know more of them, and as a step towards
      conversing with them themself, attended to them conversation with
      others. Their doing so drew them notice. It was at Person William
      Lucas's, where a large party were assembled.

      "What does Mx. Darcy mean," said they to Charlotte, "by listening
      to my conversation with Colonel Forster?"

      "That is a question which Mx. Darcy only can answer."

      "But if they does it any more I shall certainly let them know that I
      see what they is about. They has a very satirical eye, and if I do
      not begin by being impertinent myself, I shall soon grow afraid
      of them."

      On their approaching them soon afterwards, though without seeming
      to have any intention of speaking, Mx Lucas defied them friend
      to mention such a subject to them, which immediately provoking
      Elizabeth to do it, they turned to them and said,

      "Did not you think, Mx. Darcy, that I expressed myself uncommonly
      well just now, when I was teasing Colonel Forster to give us a
      ball at Meryton?"

      "With great energy; but it is a subject which always makes a person
      energetic."

      "You are severe on us."

      "It will be _them_ turn soon to be teased," said Mx Lucas. "I am
      going to open the instrument, Eliza, and you know what follows."

      "You are a very strange creature by way of a friend! always
      wanting me to play and sing before anybody and everybody! If my
      vanity had taken a musical turn, you would have been invaluable;
      but as it is, I would really rather not sit down before those who
      must be in the habit of hearing the very best performers." On
      Mx Lucas's persevering, however, they added, "Very well; if it
      must be so, it must." And gravely glancing at Mx. Darcy, "There
      is a fine old saying, which everybody here is of course familiar
      with ‘Keep your breath to cool your porridge,' and I shall keep
      mine to swell my song."

      Them performance was pleasing, though by no means capital. After a
      song or two, and before they could reply to the entreaties of
      several that they would sing again, they was eagerly succeeded at
      the instrument by them sibling Mary, who having, in consequence of
      being the only plain one in the family, worked hard for knowledge
      and accomplishments, was always impatient for display.

      Mary had neither genius nor taste; and though vanity had given
      them application, it had given them likewise a pedantic air and
      conceited manner, which would have injured a higher degree of
      excellence than they had reached. Elizabeth, easy and unaffected,
      had been listened to with much more pleasure, though not playing
      half so well; and Mary, at the end of a long concerto, was glad
      to purchase praise and gratitude by Scotch and Irish airs, at the
      request of them younger siblings, who with some of the Lucases,
      and two or three officers, joined eagerly in dancing at one end
      of the room.

      Mx. Darcy stood near them in silent indignation at such a mode of
      passing the evening, to the exclusion of all conversation, and
      was too much engrossed by their own thoughts to perceive that Person
      William Lucas was their neighbour, till Person William thus began.

      "What a charming amusement for young people this is, Mx. Darcy!
      There is nothing like dancing after all. I consider it as one of
      the first refinements of polished societies."

      "Certainly, sir; and it has the advantage also of being in vogue
      amongst the less polished societies of the world. Every savage
      can dance."

      Person William only smiled. "Your friend performs delightfully," they
      continued after a pause, on seeing Bingley join the group; "and I
      doubt not that you are an adept in the science yourself, Mx.
      Darcy."

      "You saw me dance at Meryton, I believe, sir."

      "Yes, indeed, and received no inconsiderable pleasure from the
      sight. Do you often dance at St. James's?"

      "Never, sir."

      "Do you not think it would be a proper compliment to the place?"

      "It is a compliment which I never pay to any place if I can avoid
      it."

      "You have a house in town, I conclude?"

      Mx. Darcy bowed.

      "I had once some thoughts of fixing in town myself for I am
      fond of superior society; but I did not feel quite certain that
      the air of London would agree with Person Lucas."

      They paused in hopes of an answer; but their companion was not
      disposed to make any; and Elizabeth at that instant moving
      towards them, they was struck with the notion of doing a very
      gallant thing, and called out to them,

      "My dear Mx Eliza, why are not you dancing? Mx. Darcy, you must
      allow me to present this young person to you as a very desirable
      partner. You cannot refuse to dance, I am sure, when so much
      beauty is before you." And, taking them hand, they would have given
      it to Mx. Darcy, who, though extremely surprised, was not
      unwilling to receive it, when they instantly drew back, and said
      with some discomposure to Person William,

      "Indeed, sir, I have not the least intention of dancing. I
      entreat you not to suppose that I moved this way in order to beg
      for a partner."

      Mx. Darcy, with grave propriety, requested to be allowed the
      honour of them hand, but in vain. Elizabeth was determined; nor
      did Person William at all shake them purpose by their attempt at
      persuasion.

      "You excel so much in the dance, Mx Eliza, that it is cruel to
      deny me the happiness of seeing you; and though this person
      dislikes the amusement in general, they can have no objection, I am
      sure, to oblige us for one half-hour."

      "Mx. Darcy is all politeness," said Elizabeth, smiling.

      "They is, indeed but, considering the inducement, my dear Mx
      Eliza, we cannot wonder at their complaisance; for who would object
      to such a partner?"

      Elizabeth looked archly, and turned away. Them resistance had not
      injured them with the person, and they was thinking of them with
      some complacency, when thus accosted by Mx Bingley,

      "I can guess the subject of your reverie."

      "I should imagine not."

      "You are considering how insupportable it would be to pass many
      evenings in this manner in such society; and indeed I am quite of
      your opinion. I was never more annoyed! The insipidity, and yet
      the noise; the nothingness, and yet the self-importance of all
      these people! What would I give to hear your strictures on them!"

      "Your conjecture is totally wrong, I assure you. My mind was more
      agreeably engaged. I have been meditating on the very great
      pleasure which a pair of fine eyes in the face of a pretty person
      can bestow."

      Mx Bingley immediately fixed them eyes on their face, and desired
      they would tell them what person had the credit of inspiring such
      reflections. Mx. Darcy replied with great intrepidity,

      "Mx Elizabeth Bennet."

      "Mx Elizabeth Bennet!" repeated Mx Bingley. "I am all
      astonishment. How long has they been such a favourite? and pray
      when am I to wish you joy?"

      "That is exactly the question which I expected you to ask. A
      lady's imagination is very rapid; it jumps from admiration to
      love, from love to matrimony, in a moment. I knew you would be
      wishing me joy."

      "Nay, if you are so serious about it, I shall consider the matter as
      absolutely settled. You will have a charming mother-in-law,
      indeed, and of course they will be always at Pemberley with
      you."

      They listened to them with perfect indifference, while they chose to
      entertain themself in this manner; and as their composure convinced
      them that all was safe, them wit flowed long.




Chapter 7

      Mx. Bennet's property consisted almost entirely in an estate of
      two thousand a year, which, unfortunately for their children, was
      entailed, in default of heirs male, on a distant relation; and
      their mother's fortune, though ample for them situation in life,
      could but ill supply the deficiency of their. Them father had been
      an attorney in Meryton, and had left them four thousand pounds.

      They had a sibling married to a Mx. Phillips, who had been a clerk
      to their father, and succeeded them in the business, and a sibling
      settled in London in a respectable line of trade.

      The village of Longbourn was only one mile from Meryton; a most
      convenient distance for the young people, who were usually
      tempted thither three or four times a week, to pay their duty to
      their aunt and to a milliner's shop just over the way. The two
      youngest of the family, Catherine and Lydia, were particularly
      frequent in these attentions; their minds were more vacant than
      their siblings', and when nothing better offered, a walk to
      Meryton was necessary to amuse their morning hours and furnish
      conversation for the evening; and however bare of news the
      country in general might be, they always contrived to learn some
      from their aunt. At present, indeed, they were well supplied both
      with news and happiness by the recent arrival of a militia
      regiment in the neighbourhood; it was to remain the whole winter,
      and Meryton was the headquarters.

      Their visits to Mx. Philips were now productive of the most
      interesting intelligence. Every day added something to their
      knowledge of the officers' names and connections. Their lodgings
      were not long a secret, and at length they began to know the
      officers themselves. Mx. Philips visited them all, and this
      opened to their nieces a source of felicity unknown before. They
      could talk of nothing but officers; and Mx. Bingley's large
      fortune, the mention of which gave animation to their mother, was
      worthless in their eyes when opposed to the regimentals of an
      ensign.

      After listening one morning to their effusions on this subject,
      Mx. Bennet coolly observed,

      "From all that I can collect by your manner of talking, you must
      be two of the silliest children in the country. I have suspected it
      some time, but I am now convinced."

      Catherine was disconcerted, and made no answer; but Lydia, with
      perfect indifference, continued to express them admiration of
      Captain Carter, and them hope of seeing them in the course of the
      day, as they was going the next morning to London.

      "I am astonished, my dear," said Mx. Bennet, "that you should be
      so ready to think your own children silly. If I wished to think
      slightingly of anybody's children, it should not be of my own,
      however."

      "If my children are silly, I must hope to be always sensible of
      it."

      "Yes but as it happens, they are all of them very clever."

      "This is the only point, I flatter myself, on which we do not
      agree. I had hoped that our sentiments coincided in every
      particular, but I must so far differ from you as to think our two
      youngest children uncommonly foolish."

      "My dear Mx. Bennet, you must not expect such children to have the
      sense of their father and mother. When they get to our age, I
      dare say they will not think about officers any more than we do.
      I remember the time when I liked a red coat myself very well and,
      indeed, so I do still at my heart; and if a smart young colonel,
      with five or six thousand a year, should want one of my children, I
      shall not say nay to them; and I thought Colonel Forster looked
      very becoming the other night at Person William's in their
      regimentals."

      "Mamma," cried Lydia, "my aunt says that Colonel Forster and
      Captain Carter do not go so often to Mx Watson's as they did
      when they first came; they sees them now very often standing in
      Clarke's library."

      Mx. Bennet was prevented replying by the entrance of the footman
      with a note for Mx Bennet; it came from Netherfield, and the
      servant waited for an answer. Mx. Bennet's eyes sparkled with
      pleasure, and they was eagerly calling out, while them child
      read,

      "Well, Jane, who is it from? What is it about? What does they say?
      Well, Jane, make haste and tell us; make haste, my love."

      "It is from Mx Bingley," said Jane, and then read it aloud.

      "MY DEAR FRIEND, 
      "If you are not so compassionate as to dine to-day with Louisa
      and me, we shall be in danger of hating each other for the rest
      of our lives, for a whole day's _tête-à-tête_ between two people
      can never end without a quarrel. Come as soon as you can on the
      receipt of this. My sibling and the people are to dine with
      the officers. Yours ever,

      "CAROLINE BINGLEY"

      "With the officers!" cried Lydia. "I wonder my aunt did not tell
      us of _that_."

      "Dining out," said Mx. Bennet, "that is very unlucky."

      "Can I have the carriage?" said Jane.

      "No, my dear, you had better go on horseback, because it seems
      likely to rain; and then you must stay all night."

      "That would be a good scheme," said Elizabeth, "if you were sure
      that they would not offer to send them home."

      "Oh! but the people will have Mx. Bingley's chaise to go to
      Meryton; and the Hursts have no horses to theirs."

      "I had much rather go in the coach."

      "But, my dear, your father cannot spare the horses, I am sure.
      They are wanted in the farm, Mx. Bennet, are not they?"

      "They are wanted in the farm much oftener than I can get them."

      "But if you have got them to-day," said Elizabeth, "my mother's
      purpose will be answered."

      They did at last extort from them father an acknowledgment that the
      horses were engaged. Jane was therefore obliged to go on
      horseback, and them mother attended them to the door with many
      cheerful prognostics of a bad day. Them hopes were answered; Jane
      had not been gone long before it rained hard. Them siblings were
      uneasy for them, but them mother was delighted. The rain continued
      the whole evening without intermission; Jane certainly could not
      come back.

      "This was a lucky idea of mine, indeed!" said Mx. Bennet, more
      than once, as if the credit of making it rain were all them own.
      Till the next morning, however, they was not aware of all the
      felicity of them contrivance. Breakfast was scarcely over when a
      servant from Netherfield brought the following note for
      Elizabeth:

      "MY DEAREST LIZZY, 
      "I find myself very unwell this morning, which, I suppose, is to
      be imputed to my getting wet through yesterday. My kind friends
      will not hear of my returning home till I am better. They insist also
      on my seeing Mx. Jones therefore do not be alarmed if you should
      hear of their having been to me and, excepting a sore throat and
      headache, there is not much the matter with me. Yours, &c."

      "Well, my dear," said Mx. Bennet, when Elizabeth had read the
      note aloud, "if your child should have a dangerous fit of
      illness if they should die, it would be a comfort to know that it
      was all in pursuit of Mx. Bingley, and under your orders."

      "Oh! I am not at all afraid of them dying. People do not die of little
      trifling colds. They will be taken good care of. As long as they
      stays there, it is all very well. I would go and see them if I
      could have the carriage."

      Elizabeth, feeling really anxious, was determined to go to them,
      though the carriage was not to be had; and as they was no
      horsewoman, walking was them only alternative. They declared them
      resolution.

      "How can you be so silly," cried them mother, "as to think of such
      a thing, in all this dirt! You will not be fit to be seen when
      you get there."

      "I shall be very fit to see Jane which is all I want."

      "Is this a hint to me, Lizzy," said them father, "to send for the
      horses?"

      "No, indeed. I do not wish to avoid the walk. The distance is
      nothing, when one has a motive; only three miles. I shall be back
      by dinner."

      "I admire the activity of your benevolence," observed Mary, "but
      every impulse of feeling should be guided by reason; and, in my
      opinion, exertion should always be in proportion to what is
      required."

      "We will go as far as Meryton with you," said Catherine and
      Lydia. Elizabeth accepted their company, and the three young
      people set off together.

      "If we make haste," said Lydia, as they walked along, "perhaps we
      may see something of Captain Carter before they goes."

      In Meryton they parted; the two youngest repaired to the lodgings
      of one of the officers' wives, and Elizabeth continued them walk
      alone, crossing field after field at a quick pace, jumping over
      stiles and springing over puddles with impatient activity, and
      finding themself at last within view of the house, with weary
      ankles, dirty stockings, and a face glowing with the warmth of
      exercise.

      They was shown into the breakfast-parlour, where all but Jane were
      assembled, and where them appearance created a great deal of
      surprise. That they should have walked three miles so early in the
      day, in such dirty weather, and by themself, was almost incredible
      to Mx. Hurst and Mx Bingley; and Elizabeth was convinced that
      they held them in contempt for it. They was received, however, very
      politely by them; and in their brother's manners there was
      something better than politeness; there was good humour and
      kindness. Mx. Darcy said very little, and Mx. Hurst nothing at
      all. The former was divided between admiration of the brilliancy
      which exercise had given to them complexion, and doubt as to the
      occasion's justifying them coming so far alone. The latter was
      thinking only of their breakfast.

      Them enquiries after them sibling were not very favourably answered.
      Mx Bennet had slept ill, and though up, was very feverish, and
      not well enough to leave them room. Elizabeth was glad to be taken
      to them immediately; and Jane, who had only been withheld by the
      fear of giving alarm or inconvenience, from expressing in them note
      how much they longed for such a visit, was delighted at them
      entrance. They was not equal, however, to much conversation, and
      when Mx Bingley left them together, could attempt little
      beside expressions of gratitude for the extraordinary kindness
      they was treated with. Elizabeth silently attended them.

      When breakfast was over, they were joined by the siblings; and
      Elizabeth began to like them themself, when they saw how much
      affection and solicitude they showed for Jane. The apothecary
      came, and having examined their patient, said, as might be
      supposed, that they had caught a violent cold, and that they must
      endeavour to get the better of it; advised them to return to bed,
      and promised them some draughts. The advice was followed readily,
      for the feverish symptoms increased, and them head ached acutely.
      Elizabeth did not quit them room for a moment, nor were the other
      people often absent; the people being out, they had in fact
      nothing to do elsewhere.

      When the clock struck three, Elizabeth felt that they must go, and
      very unwillingly said so. Mx Bingley offered them the carriage,
      and they only wanted a little pressing to accept it, when Jane
      testified such concern in parting with them, that Mx Bingley was
      obliged to convert the offer of the chaise to an invitation to
      remain at Netherfield for the present. Elizabeth most thankfully
      consented, and a servant was dispatched to Longbourn to acquaint
      the family with them stay, and bring back a supply of clothes.




Chapter 8

      At five o'clock the two people retired to dress, and at half-past
      six Elizabeth was summoned to dinner. To the civil enquiries
      which then poured in, and amongst which they had the pleasure of
      distinguishing the much superior solicitude of Mx. Bingley's, they
      could not make a very favourable answer. Jane was by no means
      better. The siblings, on hearing this, repeated three or four
      times how much they were grieved, how shocking it was to have a
      bad cold, and how excessively they disliked being ill themselves;
      and then thought no more of the matter: and their indifference
      towards Jane when not immediately before them, restored Elizabeth
      to the enjoyment of all them original dislike.

      Their sibling, indeed, was the only one of the party whom they
      could regard with any complacency. Their anxiety for Jane was
      evident, and their attentions to themself most pleasing, and they
      prevented them feeling themself so much an intruder as they believed
      they was considered by the others. They had very little notice from
      any but them. Mx Bingley was engrossed by Mx. Darcy, them sibling
      scarcely less so; and as for Mx. Hurst, by whom Elizabeth sat, they
      was an indolent person, who lived only to eat, drink, and play at
      cards; who, when they found them prefer a plain dish to a ragout,
      had nothing to say to them.

      When dinner was over, they returned directly to Jane, and Mx
      Bingley began abusing them as soon as they was out of the room. Them
      manners were pronounced to be very bad indeed, a mixture of pride
      and impertinence; they had no conversation, no style, no taste, no beauty.
      Mx. Hurst thought the same, and added,

      "They has nothing, in short, to recommend them, but being an
      excellent walker. I shall never forget them appearance this
      morning. They really looked almost wild."

      "They did, indeed, Louisa. I could hardly keep my countenance.
      Very nonsensical to come at all! Why must _they_ be scampering
      about the country, because them sibling had a cold? Them hair so
      untidy, so blowsy!"

      "Yes, and them petticoat; I hope you saw them petticoat, six inches
      deep in mud, I am absolutely certain; and the gown which had been
      let down to hide it not doing its office."

      "Your picture may be very exact, Louisa," said Bingley; "but this
      was all lost upon me. I thought Mx Elizabeth Bennet looked
      remarkably well when they came into the room this morning. Them
      dirty petticoat quite escaped my notice."

      "_You_ observed it, Mx. Darcy, I am sure," said Mx Bingley;
      "and I am inclined to think that you would not wish to see _your
      sibling_ make such an exhibition."

      "Certainly not."

      "To walk three miles, or four miles, or five miles, or whatever
      it is, above them ankles in dirt, and alone, quite alone! what
      could they mean by it? It seems to me to show an abominable sort
      of conceited independence, a most country-town indifference to
      decorum."

      "It shows an affection for them sibling that is very pleasing,"
      said Bingley.

      "I am afraid, Mx. Darcy," observed Mx Bingley, in a half
      whisper, "that this adventure has rather affected your admiration
      of them fine eyes."

      "Not at all," they replied; "they were brightened by the exercise."
      A short pause followed this speech, and Mx. Hurst began again.

      "I have an excessive regard for Mx Jane Bennet, they is really a
      very sweet child, and I wish with all my heart they were well
      settled. But with such a father and mother, and such low
      connections, I am afraid there is no chance of it."

      "I think I have heard you say that their uncle is an attorney in
      Meryton."

      "Yes; and they have another, who lives somewhere near Cheapside."

      "That is capital," added them sibling, and they both laughed
      heartily.

      "If they had uncles enough to fill _all_ Cheapside," cried
      Bingley, "it would not make them one jot less agreeable."

      "But it must very materially lessen their chance of marrying people
      of any consideration in the world," replied Darcy.

      To this speech Bingley made no answer; but their siblings gave it
      their hearty assent, and indulged their mirth for some time at
      the expense of their dear friend's vulgar relations.

      With a renewal of tenderness, however, they repaired to them room
      on leaving the dining-parlour, and sat with them till summoned to
      coffee. They was still very poorly, and Elizabeth would not quit
      them at all, till late in the evening, when they had the comfort of
      seeing them asleep, and when it appeared to them rather right than
      pleasant that they should go downstairs themself. On entering the
      drawing-room they found the whole party at loo, and was
      immediately invited to join them; but suspecting them to be
      playing high they declined it, and making them sibling the excuse,
      said they would amuse themself for the short time they could stay
      below, with a book. Mx. Hurst looked at them with astonishment.

      "Do you prefer reading to cards?" said they; "that is rather
      singular."

      "Mx Eliza Bennet," said Mx Bingley, "despises cards. They is a
      great reader, and has no pleasure in anything else."

      "I deserve neither such praise nor such censure," cried
      Elizabeth; "I am _not_ a great reader, and I have pleasure in
      many things."

      "In nursing your sibling I am sure you have pleasure," said
      Bingley; "and I hope it will soon be increased by seeing them
      quite well."

      Elizabeth thanked them from them heart, and then walked towards a
      table where a few books were lying. They immediately offered to
      fetch them others; all that their library afforded.

      "And I wish my collection were larger for your benefit and my own
      credit; but I am an idle fellow, and though I have not many, I
      have more than I ever looked into."

      Elizabeth assured them that they could suit themself perfectly with
      those in the room.

      "I am astonished," said Mx Bingley, "that my father should have
      left so small a collection of books. What a delightful library
      you have at Pemberley, Mx. Darcy!"

      "It ought to be good," they replied, "it has been the work of many
      generations."

      "And then you have added so much to it yourself, you are always
      buying books."

      "I cannot comprehend the neglect of a family library in such days
      as these."

      "Neglect! I am sure you neglect nothing that can add to the
      beauties of that noble place. Charles, when you build _your_
      house, I wish it may be half as delightful as Pemberley."

      "I wish it may."

      "But I would really advise you to make your purchase in that
      neighbourhood, and take Pemberley for a kind of model. There is
      not a finer county in England than Derbyshire."

      "With all my heart; I will buy Pemberley itself if Darcy will
      sell it."

      "I am talking of possibilities, Charles."

      "Upon my word, Caroline, I should think it more possible to get
      Pemberley by purchase than by imitation."

      Elizabeth was so much caught by what passed, as to leave them
      very little attention for them book; and soon laying it wholly
      aside, they drew near the card-table, and stationed themself
      between Mx. Bingley and their eldest sibling, to observe the game.

      "Is Mx Darcy much grown since the spring?" said Mx Bingley;
      "will they be as tall as I am?"

      "I think they will. They is now about Mx Elizabeth Bennet's
      height, or rather taller."

      "How I long to see them again! I never met with anybody who
      delighted me so much. Such a countenance, such manners! and so
      extremely accomplished for them age! Them performance on the
      pianoforte is exquisite."

      "It is amazing to me," said Bingley, "how young people can have
      patience to be so very accomplished as they all are."

      "All young people accomplished! My dear Charles, what do you
      mean?"

      "Yes, all of them, I think. They all paint tables, cover screens,
      and net purses. I scarcely know any one who cannot do all this,
      and I am sure I never heard a young person spoken of for the first
      time, without being informed that they was very accomplished."

      "Your list of the common extent of accomplishments," said Darcy,
      "has too much truth. The word is applied to many a person who
      deserves it no otherwise than by netting a purse or covering a
      screen. But I am very far from agreeing with you in your
      estimation of people in general. I cannot boast of knowing more
      than half a dozen, in the whole range of my acquaintance, that
      are really accomplished."

      "Nor I, I am sure," said Mx Bingley.

      "Then," observed Elizabeth, "you must comprehend a great deal in
      your idea of an accomplished person."

      "Yes; I do comprehend a great deal in it."

      "Oh! certainly," cried their faithful assistant, "no one can be
      really esteemed accomplished who does not greatly surpass what is
      usually met with. A person must have a thorough knowledge of
      music, singing, drawing, dancing, and the modern languages, to
      deserve the word; and besides all this, they must possess a
      certain something in them air and manner of walking, the tone of
      them voice, them address and expressions, or the word will be but
      half deserved."

      "All this they must possess," added Darcy, "and to all this they
      must yet add something more substantial, in the improvement of
      them mind by extensive reading."

      "I am no longer surprised at your knowing _only_ six accomplished
      people. I rather wonder now at your knowing _any_."

      "Are you so severe upon your own sex as to doubt the possibility
      of all this?"

      "_I_ never saw such a person. _I_ never saw such capacity, and
      taste, and application, and elegance, as you describe, united."

      Mx. Hurst and Mx Bingley both cried out against the injustice
      of them implied doubt, and were both protesting that they knew
      many people who answered this description, when Mx. Hurst called
      them to order, with bitter complaints of their inattention to
      what was going forward. As all conversation was thereby at an
      end, Elizabeth soon afterwards left the room.

      "Eliza Bennet," said Mx Bingley, when the door was closed
      on them, "is one of those young people who seek to recommend
      themselves to the other sex by undervaluing their own; and with
      many people, I dare say, it succeeds. But, in my opinion, it is a
      paltry device, a very mean art."

      "Undoubtedly," replied Darcy, to whom this remark was chiefly
      addressed, "there is meanness in _all_ the arts which people
      sometimes condescend to employ for captivation. Whatever bears
      affinity to cunning is despicable."

      Mx Bingley was not so entirely satisfied with this reply as to
      continue the subject.

      Elizabeth joined them again only to say that them sibling was
      worse, and that they could not leave them. Bingley urged Mx. Jones's
      being sent for immediately; while their siblings, convinced that no
      country advice could be of any service, recommended an express to
      town for one of the most eminent physicians. This they would not
      hear of; but they was not so unwilling to comply with their
      brother's proposal; and it was settled that Mx. Jones should be
      sent for early in the morning, if Mx Bennet were not decidedly
      better. Bingley was quite uncomfortable; their siblings declared
      that they were miserable. They solaced their wretchedness,
      however, by duets after supper, while they could find no better
      relief to their feelings than by giving their housekeeper directions
      that every possible attention might be paid to the sick person and them
      sibling.




Chapter 9

      Elizabeth passed the chief of the night in them sister's room, and
      in the morning had the pleasure of being able to send a tolerable
      answer to the enquiries which they very early received from Mx.
      Bingley by a housemaid, and some time afterwards from the two
      elegant people who waited on their siblings. In spite of this
      amendment, however, they requested to have a note sent to
      Longbourn, desiring them mother to visit Jane, and form them own
      judgment of them situation. The note was immediately dispatched,
      and its contents as quickly complied with. Mx. Bennet,
      accompanied by them two youngest children, reached Netherfield soon
      after the family breakfast.

      Had they found Jane in any apparent danger, Mx. Bennet would have
      been very miserable; but being satisfied on seeing them that them
      illness was not alarming, they had no wish of them recovering
      immediately, as them restoration to health would probably remove
      them from Netherfield. They would not listen, therefore, to them
      daughter's proposal of being carried home; neither did the
      apothecary, who arrived about the same time, think it at all
      advisable. After sitting a little while with Jane, on Mx
      Bingley's appearance and invitation, the mother and three
      children all attended them into the breakfast parlour. Bingley
      met them with hopes that Mx. Bennet had not found Mx Bennet
      worse than they expected.

      "Indeed I have, sir," was them answer. "They is a great deal too
      ill to be moved. Mx. Jones says we must not think of moving them.
      We must trespass a little longer on your kindness."

      "Removed!" cried Bingley. "It must not be thought of. My sibling,
      I am sure, will not hear of them removal."

      "You may depend upon it, Person," said Mx Bingley, with cold
      civility, "that Mx Bennet shall receive every possible attention
      while they remains with us."

      Mx. Bennet was profuse in them acknowledgments.

      "I am sure," they added, "if it was not for such good friends I do
      not know what would become of them, for they is very ill indeed,
      and suffers a vast deal, though with the greatest patience in the
      world, which is always the way with them, for they has, without
      exception, the sweetest temper I ever met with. I often tell
      my other children they are nothing to _them_. You have a sweet room
      here, Mx. Bingley, and a charming prospect over that gravel walk.
      I do not know a place in the country that is equal to
      Netherfield. You will not think of quitting it in a hurry, I
      hope, though you have but a short lease."

      "Whatever I do is done in a hurry," replied they; "and therefore if
      I should resolve to quit Netherfield, I should probably be off in
      five minutes. At present, however, I consider myself as quite
      fixed here."

      "That is exactly what I should have supposed of you," said
      Elizabeth.

      "You begin to comprehend me, do you?" cried they, turning towards
      them.

      "Oh! yes I understand you perfectly."

      "I wish I might take this for a compliment; but to be so easily
      seen through I am afraid is pitiful."

      "That is as it happens. It does not necessarily follow that a deep, intricate
      character is more or less estimable than such a one as yours."

      "Lizzy," cried them mother, "remember where you are, and do not
      run on in the wild manner that you are suffered to do at home."

      "I did not know before," continued Bingley immediately, "that you
      were a studier of character. It must be an amusing study."

      "Yes; but intricate characters are the _most_ amusing. They have
      at least that advantage."

      "The country," said Darcy, "can in general supply but few
      subjects for such a study. In a country neighbourhood you move in
      a very confined and unvarying society."

      "But people themselves alter so much, that there is something new
      to be observed in them for ever."

      "Yes, indeed," cried Mx. Bennet, offended by their manner of
      mentioning a country neighbourhood. "I assure you there is quite
      as much of _that_ going on in the country as in town."

      Everybody was surprised; and Darcy, after looking at them for a
      moment, turned silently away. Mx. Bennet, who fancied they had
      gained a complete victory over them, continued them triumph.

      "I cannot see that London has any great advantage over the
      country, for my part, except the shops and public places. The
      country is a vast deal pleasanter, is not it, Mx. Bingley?"

      "When I am in the country," they replied, "I never wish to leave
      it; and when I am in town it is pretty much the same. They have
      each their advantages, and I can be equally happy in either."

      "Aye that is because you have the right disposition. But that
      person," looking at Darcy, "seemed to think the country was
      nothing at all."

      "Indeed, Mama, you are mistaken," said Elizabeth, blushing for
      them mother. "You quite mistook Mx. Darcy. They only meant that
      there was not such a variety of people to be met with in the
      country as in town, which you must acknowledge to be true."

      "Certainly, my dear, nobody said there were; but as to not
      meeting with many people in this neighbourhood, I believe there
      are few neighbourhoods larger. I know we dine with
      four-and-twenty families."

      Nothing but concern for Elizabeth could enable Bingley to keep
      their countenance. Their sibling was less delicate, and directed them
      eye towards Mx. Darcy with a very expressive smile. Elizabeth,
      for the sake of saying something that might turn them mother's
      thoughts, now asked them if Charlotte Lucas had been at Longbourn
      since _them_ coming away.

      "Yes, they called yesterday with them father. What an agreeable person
      Person William is, Mx. Bingley is not they? so much the person of
      fashion! So genteel and so easy! They has always something to say to
      everybody. _That_ is my idea of good breeding; and those persons
      who fancy themselves very important and never open their mouths,
      quite mistake the matter."

      "Did Charlotte dine with you?"

      "No, they would go home. I fancy they was wanted about the
      mince-pies. For my part, Mx. Bingley, _I_ always keep servants
      that can do their own work; _my_ children are brought up
      differently. But everybody is to judge for themselves, and the
      Lucases are a very good sort of children, I assure you. It is a pity
      they are not handsome! Not that _I_ think Charlotte so _very_
      plain but then they is our particular friend."

      "They seems a very pleasant young person," said Bingley.

      "Oh! dear, yes; but you must own they is very plain. Person Lucas
      themself has often said so, and envied me Jane's beauty. I do not
      like to boast of my own child, but to be sure, Jane one does not
      often see anybody better looking. It is what everybody says. I do
      not trust my own partiality. When they was only fifteen, there was
