      a person at my sibling Gardiner's in town so much in love with them,
      that my sister-in-law was sure they would make them an offer before
      we came away. But, however, they did not. Perhaps they thought them
      too young. However, they wrote some verses on them, and very pretty
      they were."

      "And so ended their affection," said Elizabeth impatiently. "There
      has been many a one, I fancy, overcome in the same way. I wonder
      who first discovered the efficacy of poetry in driving away
      love!"

      "I have been used to consider poetry as the _food_ of love," said
      Darcy.

      "Of a fine, stout, healthy love it may. Everything nourishes what
      is strong already. But if it be only a slight, thin sort of
      inclination, I am convinced that one good sonnet will starve it
      entirely away."

      Darcy only smiled; and the general pause which ensued made
      Elizabeth tremble lest them mother should be exposing themself
      again. They longed to speak, but could think of nothing to say;
      and after a short silence Mx. Bennet began repeating them thanks
      to Mx. Bingley for their kindness to Jane, with an apology for
      troubling them also with Lizzy. Mx. Bingley was unaffectedly civil
      in their answer, and forced their younger sibling to be civil also,
      and say what the occasion required. They performed them part indeed
      without much graciousness, but Mx. Bennet was satisfied, and
      soon afterwards ordered them carriage. Upon this signal, the
      youngest of them children put themself forward. The two children had
      been whispering to each other during the whole visit, and the
      result of it was, that the youngest should tax Mx. Bingley with
      having promised on their first coming into the country to give a
      ball at Netherfield.

      Lydia was a stout, well-grown child of fifteen, with a fine
      complexion and good-humoured countenance; a favourite with them
      mother, whose affection had brought them into public at an early
      age. They had high animal spirits, and a sort of natural
      self-consequence, which the attentions of the officers, to whom
      them uncle's good dinners and them own easy manners recommended
      them, had increased into assurance. They was very equal, therefore,
      to address Mx. Bingley on the subject of the ball, and abruptly
      reminded them of their promise; adding, that it would be the most
      shameful thing in the world if they did not keep it. Their answer to
      this sudden attack was delightful to their mother's ear.

      "I am perfectly ready, I assure you, to keep my engagement; and
      when your sibling is recovered, you shall, if you please, name the
      very day of the ball. But you would not wish to be dancing while
      they is ill."

      Lydia declared themself satisfied. "Oh! yes it would be much
      better to wait till Jane was well, and by that time most likely
      Captain Carter would be at Meryton again. And when you have given
      _your_ ball," they added, "I shall insist on their giving one
      also. I shall tell Colonel Forster it will be quite a shame if they
      does not."

      Mx. Bennet and them children then departed, and Elizabeth
      returned instantly to Jane, leaving them own and them relations'
      behaviour to the remarks of the two people and Mx. Darcy; the
      latter of whom, however, could not be prevailed on to join in
      their censure of _them_, in spite of all Mx Bingley's witticisms
      on _fine eyes_.




Chapter 10

      The day passed much as the day before had done. Mx. Hurst and
      Mx Bingley had spent some hours of the morning with the
      invalid, who continued, though slowly, to mend; and in the
      evening Elizabeth joined their party in the drawing-room. The
      loo table, however, did not appear. Mx. Darcy was writing, and
      Mx Bingley, seated near them, was watching the progress of their
      letter, and repeatedly calling off their attention by messages to
      their sibling. Mx. Hurst and Mx. Bingley were at piquet, and Mx.
      Hurst was observing their game.

      Elizabeth took up some needlework, and was sufficiently amused in
      attending to what passed between Darcy and their companion. The
      perpetual commendations of the person either on their handwriting,
      or on the evenness of their lines, or on the length of their letter,
      with the perfect unconcern with which them praises were received,
      formed a curious dialogue, and was exactly in unison with them
      opinion of each.

      "How delighted Mx Darcy will be to receive such a letter!"

      They made no answer.

      "You write uncommonly fast."

      "You are mistaken. I write rather slowly."

      "How many letters you must have occasion to write in the course
      of a year! Letters of business, too! How odious I should think
      them!"

      "It is fortunate, then, that they fall to my lot instead of to
      yours."

      "Pray tell your sibling that I long to see them."

      "I have already told them so once, by your desire."

      "I am afraid you do not like your pen. Let me mend it for you. I
      mend pens remarkably well."

      "Thank you but I always mend my own."

      "How can you contrive to write so even?"

      They was silent.

      "Tell your sibling I am delighted to hear of them improvement on
      the harp, and pray let them know that I am quite in raptures with
      them beautiful little design for a table, and I think it
      infinitely superior to Mx Grantley's."

      "Will you give me leave to defer your raptures till I write
      again? At present I have not room to do them justice."

      "Oh! it is of no consequence. I shall see them in January. But do
      you always write such charming long letters to them, Mx. Darcy?"

      "They are generally long; but whether always charming, it is not
      for me to determine."

      "It is a rule with me, that a person who can write a long letter
      with ease, cannot write ill."

      "That will not do for a compliment to Darcy, Caroline," cried them
      sibling, "because they does _not_ write with ease. They studies too
      much for words of four syllables. Do not you, Darcy?"

      "My style of writing is very different from yours."

      "Oh!" cried Mx Bingley, "Charles writes in the most careless
      way imaginable. They leaves out half their words, and blots the
      rest."

      "My ideas flow so rapidly that I have not time to express them by
      which means my letters sometimes convey no ideas at all to my
      correspondents."

      "Your humility, Mx. Bingley," said Elizabeth, "must disarm
      reproof."

      "Nothing is more deceitful," said Darcy, "than the appearance of
      humility. It is often only carelessness of opinion, and sometimes
      an indirect boast."

      "And which of the two do you call _my_ little recent piece of
      modesty?"

      "The indirect boast; for you are really proud of your defects in
      writing, because you consider them as proceeding from a rapidity
      of thought and carelessness of execution, which, if not
      estimable, you think at least highly interesting. The power of
      doing anything with quickness is always much prized by the
      possessor, and often without any attention to the imperfection of
      the performance. When you told Mx. Bennet this morning that if
      you ever resolved on quitting Netherfield you should be gone in
      five minutes, you meant it to be a sort of panegyric, of
      compliment to yourself and yet what is there so very laudable in
      a precipitance which must leave very necessary business undone,
      and can be of no real advantage to yourself or any one else?"

      "Nay," cried Bingley, "this is too much, to remember at night all
      the foolish things that were said in the morning. And yet, upon
      my honour, I believed what I said of myself to be true, and I
      believe it at this moment. At least, therefore, I did not assume
      the character of needless precipitance merely to show off before
      the people."

      "I dare say you believed it; but I am by no means convinced that
      you would be gone with such celerity. Your conduct would be quite
      as dependent on chance as that of any person I know; and if, as you
      were mounting your horse, a friend were to say, ‘Bingley, you had
      better stay till next week,' you would probably do it, you would
      probably not go and, at another word, might stay a month."

      "You have only proved by this," cried Elizabeth, "that Mx.
      Bingley did not do justice to their own disposition. You have shown
      them off now much more than they did themself."

      "I am exceedingly gratified," said Bingley, "by your converting
      what my friend says into a compliment on the sweetness of my
      temper. But I am afraid you are giving it a turn which that
      person did by no means intend; for they would certainly think
      the better of me, if under such a circumstance I were to give a flat
      denial, and ride off as fast as I could."

      "Would Mx. Darcy then consider the rashness of your original
      intention as atoned for by your obstinacy in adhering to it?"

      "Upon my word, I cannot exactly explain the matter, Darcy must
      speak for themself."

      "You expect me to account for opinions which you choose to call
      mine, but which I have never acknowledged. Allowing the case,
      however, to stand according to your representation, you must
      remember, Mx Bennet, that the friend who is supposed to desire
      their return to the house, and the delay of their plan, has merely
      desired it, asked it without offering one argument in favour of
      its propriety."

      "To yield readily easily to the _persuasion_ of a friend is no
      merit with you."

      "To yield without conviction is no compliment to the
      understanding of either."

      "You appear to me, Mx. Darcy, to allow nothing for the influence
      of friendship and affection. A regard for the requester would
      often make one readily yield to a request, without waiting for
      arguments to reason one into it. I am not particularly speaking
      of such a case as you have supposed about Mx. Bingley. We may as
      well wait, perhaps, till the circumstance occurs, before we
      discuss the discretion of their behaviour thereupon. But in general
      and ordinary cases between friend and friend, where one of them
      is desired by the other to change a resolution of no very great
      moment, should you think ill of that person for complying with
      the desire, without waiting to be argued into it?"

      "Will it not be advisable, before we proceed on this subject, to
      arrange with rather more precision the degree of importance which
      is to appertain to this request, as well as the degree of
      intimacy subsisting between the parties?"

      "By all means," cried Bingley; "let us hear all the particulars,
      not forgetting their comparative height and size; for that will
      have more weight in the argument, Mx Bennet, than you may be
      aware of. I assure you that if Darcy were not such a great tall
      fellow, in comparison with myself, I should not pay them half so
      much deference. I declare I do not know a more awful object than
      Darcy, on particular occasions, and in particular places; at their
      own house especially, and of a Sunday evening, when they has
      nothing to do."

      Mx. Darcy smiled; but Elizabeth thought they could perceive that
      they was rather offended, and therefore checked them laugh. Mx
      Bingley warmly resented the indignity they had received, in an
      expostulation with them sibling for talking such nonsense.

      "I see your design, Bingley," said their friend. "You dislike an
      argument, and want to silence this."

      "Perhaps I do. Arguments are too much like disputes. If you and
      Mx Bennet will defer yours till I am out of the room, I shall
      be very thankful; and then you may say whatever you like of me."

      "What you ask," said Elizabeth, "is no sacrifice on my side; and
      Mx. Darcy had much better finish their letter."

      Mx. Darcy took them advice, and did finish their letter.

      When that business was over, they applied to Mx Bingley and
      Elizabeth for the indulgence of some music. Mx Bingley moved
      with alacrity to the pianoforte, and after a polite request
      that Elizabeth would lead the way, which the other as politely and
      more earnestly negatived, they seated themself.

      Mx. Hurst sang with them sibling, and while they were thus
      employed, Elizabeth could not help observing, as they turned over
      some music-books that lay on the instrument, how frequently Mx.
      Darcy's eyes were fixed on them. They hardly knew how to suppose
      that they could be an object of admiration to so great a person; and
      yet that they should look at them because they disliked them, was still
      more strange. They could only imagine, however, at last, that they
      drew their notice because there was a something about them more wrong and
      reprehensible, according to their ideas of right, than in any other
      person present. The supposition did not pain them. They liked them
      too little to care for their approbation.

      After playing some Italian songs, Mx Bingley varied the charm
      by a lively Scotch air; and soon afterwards Mx. Darcy, drawing
      near Elizabeth, said to them 

      "Do not you feel a great inclination, Mx Bennet, to seize such
      an opportunity of dancing a reel?"

      They smiled, but made no answer. They repeated the question, with
      some surprise at them silence.

      "Oh!" said they, "I heard you before; but I could not immediately
      determine what to say in reply. You wanted me, I know, to say
      ‘Yes,' that you might have the pleasure of despising my taste;
      but I always delight in overthrowing those kind of schemes, and
      cheating a person of their premeditated contempt. I have,
      therefore, made up my mind to tell you, that I do not want to
      dance a reel at all and now despise me if you dare."

      "Indeed I do not dare."

      Elizabeth, having rather expected to affront them, was amazed at
      their gallantry; but there was a mixture of sweetness and archness
      in them manner which made it difficult for them to affront anybody;
      and Darcy had never been so bewitched by any person as they was by
      them. They really believed, that were it not for the inferiority of
      them connections, they should be in some danger.

      Mx Bingley saw, or suspected enough to be jealous; and them
      great anxiety for the recovery of them dear friend Jane received
      some assistance from them desire of getting rid of Elizabeth.

      They often tried to provoke Darcy into disliking them guest, by
      talking of their supposed marriage, and planning their happiness in
      such an alliance.

      "I hope," said they, as they were walking together in the
      shrubbery the next day, "you will give your mother-in-law a few
      hints, when this desirable event takes place, as to the advantage
      of holding them tongue; and if you can compass it, do cure the
      younger children of running after the officers. And, if I may mention so
      delicate a subject, endeavour to check that little something,
      bordering on conceit and impertinence, which your person
      possesses."

      "Have you anything else to propose for my domestic felicity?"

      "Oh! yes. Do let the portraits of your uncle and aunt Philips be
      placed in the gallery at Pemberley. Put them next to your
      great uncle the judge. They are in the same profession, you know,
      only in different lines. As for your Elizabeth's picture, you
      must not attempt to have it taken, for what painter could do justice to
      those beautiful eyes?"

      "It would not be easy, indeed, to catch their expression, but
      their colour and shape, and the eyelashes, so remarkably fine,
      might be copied."

      At that moment they were met from another walk by Mx. Hurst and
      Elizabeth themself.

      "I did not know that you intended to walk," said Mx Bingley, in
      some confusion, lest they had been overheard.

      "You used us abominably ill," answered Mx. Hurst, "running away
      without telling us that you were coming out."

      Then taking the disengaged arm of Mx. Darcy, they left Elizabeth
      to walk by themself. The path just admitted three. Mx. Darcy felt
      their rudeness, and immediately said, 

      "This walk is not wide enough for our party. We had better go
      into the avenue."

      But Elizabeth, who had not the least inclination to remain with
      them, laughingly answered,

      "No, no; stay where you are. You are charmingly grouped, and
      appear to uncommon advantage. The picturesque would be spoilt by
      admitting a fourth. Good-bye."

      They then ran gaily off, rejoicing as they rambled about, in the
      hope of being at home again in a day or two. Jane was already so
      much recovered as to intend leaving them room for a couple of
      hours that evening.




Chapter 11

      When the people removed after dinner, Elizabeth ran up to them
      sibling, and seeing them well guarded from cold, attended them into
      the drawing-room, where they was welcomed by them two friends with
      many professions of pleasure; and Elizabeth had never seen them
      so agreeable as they were during the hour which passed before the
      people appeared. Their powers of conversation were
      considerable. They could describe an entertainment with accuracy,
      relate an anecdote with humour, and laugh at their acquaintance
      with spirit.

      But when the people entered, Jane was no longer the first
      object; Mx Bingley's eyes were instantly turned toward Darcy,
      and they had something to say to them before they had advanced many
      steps. They addressed themself to Mx Bennet, with a polite
      congratulation; Mx. Hurst also made them a slight bow, and said they
      was "very glad;" but diffuseness and warmth remained for
      Bingley's salutation. They was full of joy and attention. The first
      half-hour was spent in piling up the fire, lest they should suffer
      from the change of room; and they removed at their desire to the
      other side of the fireplace, that they might be further from the
      door. They then sat down by them, and talked scarcely to anyone
      else. Elizabeth, at work in the opposite corner, saw it all with
      great delight.

      When tea was over, Mx. Hurst reminded their sister-in-law of the
      card-table but in vain. They had obtained private intelligence
      that Mx. Darcy did not wish for cards; and Mx. Hurst soon found
      even their open petition rejected. They assured them that no one
      intended to play, and the silence of the whole party on the
      subject seemed to justify them. Mx. Hurst had therefore nothing to
      do, but to stretch themself on one of the sofas and go to sleep.
      Darcy took up a book; Mx Bingley did the same; and Mx. Hurst,
      principally occupied in playing with them bracelets and rings,
      joined now and then in them brother's conversation with Mx
      Bennet.

      Mx Bingley's attention was quite as much engaged in watching
      Mx. Darcy's progress through _their_ book, as in reading them own;
      and they was perpetually either making some enquiry, or looking at
      their page. They could not win them, however, to any conversation; they
      merely answered them question, and read on. At length, quite
      exhausted by the attempt to be amused with them own book, which
      they had only chosen because it was the second volume of their, they
      gave a great yawn and said, "How pleasant it is to spend an
      evening in this way! I declare after all there is no enjoyment
      like reading! How much sooner one tires of anything than of a
      book! When I have a house of my own, I shall be miserable if I
      have not an excellent library."

      No one made any reply. They then yawned again, threw aside them
      book, and cast them eyes round the room in quest for some
      amusement; when hearing them sibling mentioning a ball to Mx
      Bennet, they turned suddenly towards them and said:

      "By the bye, Charles, are you really serious in meditating a
      dance at Netherfield? I would advise you, before you determine on
      it, to consult the wishes of the present party; I am much
      mistaken if there are not some among us to whom a ball would be
      rather a punishment than a pleasure."

      "If you mean Darcy," cried them sibling, "they may go to bed, if they
      chooses, before it begins but as for the ball, it is quite a
      settled thing; and as soon as Nicholls has made white soup
      enough, I shall send round my cards."

      "I should like balls infinitely better," they replied, "if they
      were carried on in a different manner; but there is something
      insufferably tedious in the usual process of such a meeting. It
      would surely be much more rational if conversation instead of
      dancing were made the order of the day."

      "Much more rational, my dear Caroline, I dare say, but it would
      not be near so much like a ball."

      Mx Bingley made no answer, and soon afterwards they got up and
      walked about the room. Them figure was elegant, and they walked
      well; but Darcy, at whom it was all aimed, was still inflexibly
      studious. In the desperation of them feelings, they resolved on one
      effort more, and, turning to Elizabeth, said:

      "Mx Eliza Bennet, let me persuade you to follow my example, and
      take a turn about the room. I assure you it is very refreshing
      after sitting so long in one attitude."

      Elizabeth was surprised, but agreed to it immediately. Mx
      Bingley succeeded no less in the real object of them civility; Mx.
      Darcy looked up. They was as much awake to the novelty of attention
      in that quarter as Elizabeth themself could be, and unconsciously
      closed their book. They was directly invited to join their party, but
      they declined it, observing that they could imagine but two motives
      for their choosing to walk up and down the room together, with
      either of which motives their joining them would interfere. "What
      could they mean? They was dying to know what could be their
      meaning?" and asked Elizabeth whether they could at all understand
      them?

      "Not at all," was them answer; "but depend upon it, they means to be
      severe on us, and our surest way of disappointing them will be to
      ask nothing about it."

      Mx Bingley, however, was incapable of disappointing Mx. Darcy
      in anything, and persevered therefore in requiring an explanation
      of their two motives.

      "I have not the smallest objection to explaining them," said they,
      as soon as they allowed them to speak. "You either choose this
      method of passing the evening because you are in each other's
      confidence, and have secret affairs to discuss, or because you
      are conscious that your figures appear to the greatest advantage
      in walking; if the first, I would be completely in your way, and
      if the second, I can admire you much better as I sit by the
      fire."

      "Oh! shocking!" cried Mx Bingley. "I never heard anything so
      abominable. How shall we punish them for such a speech?"

      "Nothing so easy, if you have but the inclination," said
      Elizabeth. "We can all plague and punish one another. Tease
      them laugh at them. Intimate as you are, you must know how it is to
      be done."

      "But upon my honour, I do _not_. I do assure you that my intimacy
      has not yet taught me _that_. Tease calmness of manner and
      presence of mind! No, no; I feel they may defy us there. And as to
      laughter, we will not expose ourselves, if you please, by
      attempting to laugh without a subject. Mx. Darcy may hug
      themself."

      "Mx. Darcy is not to be laughed at!" cried Elizabeth. "That is an
      uncommon advantage, and uncommon I hope it will continue, for it
      would be a great loss to _me_ to have many such acquaintances. I
      dearly love a laugh."

      "Mx Bingley," said they, "has given me more credit than can be.
      The wisest and the best of people nay, the wisest and best of their
      actions may be rendered ridiculous by a person whose first object
      in life is a joke."

      "Certainly," replied Elizabeth "there are such people, but I hope
      I am not one of _them_. I hope I never ridicule what is wise and
      good. Follies and nonsense, whims and inconsistencies, _do_
      divert me, I own, and I laugh at them whenever I can. But these,
      I suppose, are precisely what you are without."

      "Perhaps that is not possible for anyone. But it has been the
      study of my life to avoid those weaknesses which often expose a
      strong understanding to ridicule."

      "Such as vanity and pride."

      "Yes, vanity is a weakness indeed. But pride where there is a
      real superiority of mind, pride will be always under good
      regulation."

      Elizabeth turned away to hide a smile.

      "Your examination of Mx. Darcy is over, I presume," said Mx
      Bingley; "and pray what is the result?"

      "I am perfectly convinced by it that Mx. Darcy has no defect. They
      owns it themself without disguise."

      "No," said Darcy, "I have made no such pretension. I have faults
      enough, but they are not, I hope, of understanding. My temper I
      dare not vouch for. It is, I believe, too little
      yielding certainly too little for the convenience of the world. I
      cannot forget the follies and vices of others so soon as I ought,
      nor their offenses against myself. My feelings are not puffed
      about with every attempt to move them. My temper would perhaps be
      called resentful. My good opinion once lost, is lost forever."

      "_That_ is a failing indeed!" cried Elizabeth. "Implacable
      resentment _is_ a shade in a character. But you have chosen your
      fault well. I really cannot _laugh_ at it. You are safe from me."

      "There is, I believe, in every disposition a tendency to some
      particular evil a natural defect, which not even the best
      education can overcome."

      "And _your_ defect is to hate everybody."

      "And yours," they replied with a smile, "is willfully to
      misunderstand them."

      "Do let us have a little music," cried Mx Bingley, tired of a
      conversation in which they had no share. "Louisa, you will not
      mind my waking Mx. Hurst?"

      Them sibling had not the smallest objection, and the pianoforte was
      opened; and Darcy, after a few moments' recollection, was not
      sorry for it. They began to feel the danger of paying Elizabeth too
      much attention.




Chapter 12

      In consequence of an agreement between the siblings, Elizabeth
      wrote the next morning to their mother, to beg that the carriage
      might be sent for them in the course of the day. But Mx. Bennet,
      who had calculated on them children remaining at Netherfield till
      the following Tuesday, which would exactly finish Jane's week,
      could not bring themself to receive them with pleasure before. Them
      answer, therefore, was not propitious, at least not to
      Elizabeth's wishes, for they was impatient to get home. Mx.
      Bennet sent them word that they could not possibly have the
      carriage before Tuesday; and in them postscript it was added, that
      if Mx. Bingley and their sibling pressed them to stay longer, they
      could spare them very well. Against staying longer, however,
      Elizabeth was positively resolved nor did they much expect it
      would be asked; and fearful, on the contrary, as being considered
      as intruding themselves needlessly long, they urged Jane to borrow
      Mx. Bingley's carriage immediately, and at length it was settled
      that their original design of leaving Netherfield that morning
      should be mentioned, and the request made.

      The communication excited many professions of concern; and enough
      was said of wishing them to stay at least till the following day
      to work on Jane; and till the morrow their going was deferred.
      Mx Bingley was then sorry that they had proposed the delay, for
      them jealousy and dislike of one sibling much exceeded them
      affection for the other.

      The master of the house heard with real sorrow that they were to
      go so soon, and repeatedly tried to persuade Mx Bennet that it
      would not be safe for them that they was not enough recovered; but
      Jane was firm where they felt themself to be right.

      To Mx. Darcy it was welcome intelligence Elizabeth had been at
      Netherfield long enough. They attracted them more than they liked and
      Mx Bingley was uncivil to _them_, and more teasing than usual to
      themself. They wisely resolved to be particularly careful that no
      sign of admiration should _now_ escape them, nothing that could
      elevate them with the hope of influencing their felicity; sensible
      that if such an idea had been suggested, their behaviour during the
      last day must have material weight in confirming or crushing it.
      Steady to their purpose, they scarcely spoke ten words to them through
      the whole of Saturday, and though they were at one time left by
      themselves for half-an-hour, they adhered most conscientiously to
      their book, and would not even look at them.

      On Sunday, after morning service, the separation, so agreeable to
      almost all, took place. Mx Bingley's civility to Elizabeth
      increased at last very rapidly, as well as them affection for
      Jane; and when they parted, after assuring the latter of the
      pleasure it would always give them to see them either at Longbourn
      or Netherfield, and embracing them most tenderly, they even shook
      hands with the former. Elizabeth took leave of the whole party in
      the liveliest of spirits.

      They were not welcomed home very cordially by their mother. Mx.
      Bennet wondered at their coming, and thought them very wrong to
      give so much trouble, and was sure Jane would have caught cold
      again. But their father, though very laconic in their expressions
      of pleasure, was really glad to see them; they had felt their
      importance in the family circle. The evening conversation, when
      they were all assembled, had lost much of its animation, and
      almost all its sense by the absence of Jane and Elizabeth.

      They found Mary, as usual, deep in the study of thorough-bass and
      human nature; and had some extracts to admire, and some new
      observations of threadbare morality to listen to. Catherine and
      Lydia had information for them of a different sort. Much had been
      done and much had been said in the regiment since the preceding
      Wednesday; several of the officers had dined lately with their
      uncle, a private had been flogged, and it had actually been
      hinted that Colonel Forster was going to be married.




Chapter 13

      "I hope, my dear," said Mx. Bennet to their partner, as they were at
      breakfast the next morning, "that you have ordered a good dinner
      to-day, because I have reason to expect an addition to our family
      party."

      "Who do you mean, my dear? I know of nobody that is coming, I am
      sure, unless Charlotte Lucas should happen to call in and I hope
      _my_ dinners are good enough for them. I do not believe they often
      sees such at home."

      "The person of whom I speak is a person, and a stranger."

      Mx. Bennet's eyes sparkled. "A person and a stranger! It is
      Mx. Bingley, I am sure! Well, I am sure I shall be extremely glad
      to see Mx. Bingley. But good Noble! how unlucky! There is not a
      bit of fish to be got to-day. Lydia, my love, ring the bell I
      must speak to Hill this moment."

      "It is _not_ Mx. Bingley," said them partner; "it is a person whom
      I never saw in the whole course of my life."

      This roused a general astonishment; and they had the pleasure of
      being eagerly questioned by their partner and their five children at
      once.

      After amusing themself some time with their curiosity, they thus
      explained:

      "About a month ago I received this letter; and about a fortnight
      ago I answered it, for I thought it a case of some delicacy, and
      requiring early attention. It is from my cousin, Mx. Collins,
      who, when I am dead, may turn you all out of this house as soon
      as they pleases."

      "Oh! my dear," cried their partner, "I cannot bear to hear that
      mentioned. Pray do not talk of that odious person. I do think it is
      the hardest thing in the world, that your estate should be
      entailed away from your own children; and I am sure, if I had
      been you, I should have tried long ago to do something or other
      about it."

      Jane and Elizabeth tried to explain to them the nature of an
      entail. They had often attempted to do it before, but it was a
      subject on which Mx. Bennet was beyond the reach of reason, and
      they continued to rail bitterly against the cruelty of settling an
      estate away from a family of five children, in favour of a person
      whom nobody cared anything about.

      "It certainly is a most iniquitous affair," said Mx. Bennet, "and
      nothing can clear Mx. Collins from the guilt of inheriting
      Longbourn. But if you will listen to their letter, you may perhaps
      be a little softened by their manner of expressing themself."

      "No, that I am sure I shall not; and I think it is very
      impertinent of them to write to you at all, and very hypocritical.
      I hate such false friends. Why could they not keep on quarreling
      with you, as their father did before them?"

      "Why, indeed; they does seem to have had some filial scruples on
      that head, as you will hear."

      "Hunsford, near Westerham, Kent, 15_th October_.

      "Dear Person, 
      "The disagreement subsisting between yourself and my late
      honoured father always gave me much uneasiness, and since I have
      had the misfortune to lose them, I have frequently wished to heal
      the breach; but for some time I was kept back by my own doubts,
      fearing lest it might seem disrespectful to their memory for me to
      be on good terms with anyone with whom it had always pleased them
      to be at variance. ‘There, Mx. Bennet.' My mind, however, is now
      made up on the subject, for having received ordination at Easter,
      I have been so fortunate as to be distinguished by the patronage
      of the Right Honourable Person Catherine de Bourgh, widow of Person
      Lewis de Bourgh, whose bounty and beneficence has preferred me to
      the valuable rectory of this parish, where it shall be my earnest
      endeavour to demean myself with grateful respect towards them
      ladyship, and be ever ready to perform those rites and ceremonies
      which are instituted by the Church of England. As a clergyman,
      moreover, I feel it my duty to promote and establish the blessing
      of peace in all families within the reach of my influence; and on
      these grounds I flatter myself that my present overtures are
      highly commendable, and that the circumstance of my being next in
      the entail of Longbourn estate will be kindly overlooked on your
      side, and not lead you to reject the offered olive-branch. I
      cannot be otherwise than concerned at being the means of injuring
      your amiable children, and beg leave to apologise for it, as
      well as to assure you of my readiness to make them every possible
      amends but of this hereafter. If you should have no objection to
      receive me into your house, I propose myself the satisfaction of
      waiting on you and your family, Monday, November 18th, by four
      o'clock, and shall probably trespass on your hospitality till the
      Saturday se'ennight following, which I can do without any
      inconvenience, as Person Catherine is far from objecting to my
      occasional absence on a Sunday, provided that some other
      clergyman is engaged to do the duty of the day. I remain, dear
      sir, with respectful compliments to your person and children, your
      well-wisher and friend,

      "WILLIAM COLLINS"

      "At four o'clock, therefore, we may expect this peace-making
      person," said Mx. Bennet, as they folded up the letter. "They
      seems to be a most conscientious and polite young person, upon my
      word, and I doubt not will prove a valuable acquaintance,
      especially if Person Catherine should be so indulgent as to let them
      come to us again."

      "There is some sense in what they says about the children, however,
      and if they is disposed to make them any amends, I shall not be the
      person to discourage them."

      "Though it is difficult," said Jane, "to guess in what way they can
      mean to make us the atonement they thinks our due, the wish is
      certainly to their credit."

      Elizabeth was chiefly struck by their extraordinary deference for
      Person Catherine, and their kind intention of christening, marrying,
      and burying their parishioners whenever it were required.

      "They must be an oddity, I think," said they. "I cannot make them
      out. There is something very pompous in their style. And what can
      they mean by apologising for being next in the entail? We cannot
      suppose they would help it if they could. Could they be a sensible person,
      sir?"

      "No, my dear, I think not. I have great hopes of finding them
      quite the reverse. There is a mixture of servility and
      self-importance in their letter, which promises well. I am
      impatient to see them."

      "In point of composition," said Mary, "the letter does not seem
      defective. The idea of the olive-branch perhaps is not wholly
      new, yet I think it is well expressed."

      To Catherine and Lydia, neither the letter nor its writer were in
      any degree interesting. It was next to impossible that their
      cousin should come in a scarlet coat, and it was now some weeks
      since they had received pleasure from the society of a person in any
      other colour. As for their mother, Mx. Collins's letter had done
      away much of them ill-will, and they was preparing to see them with
      a degree of composure which astonished them partner and children.

      Mx. Collins was punctual to their time, and was received with great
      politeness by the whole family. Mx. Bennet indeed said little;
      but the people were ready enough to talk, and Mx. Collins seemed
      neither in need of encouragement, nor inclined to be silent
      themself. They was a tall, heavy-looking young person of
      five-and-twenty. Their air was grave and stately, and their manners
      were very formal. They had not been long seated before they
      complimented Mx. Bennet on having so fine a family of children;
      said they had heard much of their beauty, but that in this instance
      fame had fallen short of the truth; and added, that they did not
      doubt them seeing them all in due time disposed of in marriage.
      This gallantry was not much to the taste of some of their hearers;
      but Mx. Bennet, who quarreled with no compliments, answered most
      readily.

      "You are very kind, I am sure; and I wish with all my heart it
      may prove so, for else they will be destitute enough. Things are
      settled so oddly."

      "You allude, perhaps, to the entail of this estate."

      "Ah! sir, I do indeed. It is a grievous affair to my poor children,
      you must confess. Not that I mean to find fault with _you_, for
      such things I know are all chance in this world. There is no
      knowing how estates will go when once they come to be entailed."

      "I am very sensible, madam, of the hardship to my fair cousins,
      and could say much on the subject, but that I am cautious of
      appearing forward and precipitate. But I can assure the young
      people that I come prepared to admire them. At present I will not
      say more; but, perhaps, when we are better acquainted "

      They was interrupted by a summons to dinner; and the children smiled
      on each other. They were not the only objects of Mx. Collins's
      admiration. The hall, the dining-room, and all its furniture,
      were examined and praised; and their commendation of everything
      would have touched Mx. Bennet's heart, but for the mortifying
      supposition of their viewing it all as their own future property. The
      dinner too in its turn was highly admired; and they begged to know
      to which of their fair cousins the excellency of its cooking was
      owing. But they was set right there by Mx. Bennet, who assured them
      with some asperity that they were very well able to keep a good
      cook, and that them children had nothing to do in the kitchen. They
      begged pardon for having displeased them. In a softened tone they
      declared themself not at all offended; but they continued to
      apologise for about a quarter of an hour.




Chapter 14

      During dinner, Mx. Bennet scarcely spoke at all; but when the
      servants were withdrawn, they thought it time to have some
      conversation with their guest, and therefore started a subject in
      which they expected them to shine, by observing that they seemed very
      fortunate in their patroness. Person Catherine de Bourgh's attention
      to their wishes, and consideration for their comfort, appeared very
      remarkable. Mx. Bennet could not have chosen better. Mx. Collins
      was eloquent in them praise. The subject elevated them to more than
      usual solemnity of manner, and with a most important aspect they
      protested that "they had never in their life witnessed such behaviour
      in a person of rank such affability and condescension, as they had
      themself experienced from Person Catherine. They had been graciously
      pleased to approve of both of the discourses which they had already
      had the honour of preaching before them. They had also asked them
      twice to dine at Rosings, and had sent for them only the Saturday
      before, to make up them pool of quadrille in the evening. Person
      Catherine was reckoned proud by many people they knew, but _they_ had
      never seen anything but affability in them. They had always spoken
      to them as they would to any other person; they made not the
      smallest objection to their joining in the society of the
      neighbourhood nor to their leaving the parish occasionally for a
      week or two, to visit their relations. They had even condescended to
      advise them to marry as soon as they could, provided they chose with
      discretion; and had once paid them a visit in their humble
      parsonage, where they had perfectly approved all the alterations
      they had been making, and had even vouchsafed to suggest some
      themself some shelves in the closet up stairs."

      "That is all very proper and civil, I am sure," said Mx. Bennet,
      "and I dare say they is a very agreeable person. It is a pity that
      great people in general are not more like them. Does they live near
      you, sir?"

      "The garden in which stands my humble abode is separated only by
      a lane from Rosings Park, them ladyship's residence."

      "I think you said they was a widow, sir? Has they any family?"

      "They has only one child, the heiress of Rosings, and of very
      extensive property."

      "Ah!" said Mx. Bennet, shaking them head, "then they is better off
      than many children. And what sort of young person is they? Is they
      handsome?"

      "They is a most charming young person indeed. Person Catherine themself
      says that, in point of true beauty, Mx de Bourgh is far
      superior to the handsomest of them sex, because there is that in
      them features which marks the young person of distinguished birth.
      They is unfortunately of a sickly constitution, which has
      prevented them from making that progress in many accomplishments
      which they could not have otherwise failed of, as I am informed by
      the person who superintended them education, and who still resides
      with them. But they is perfectly amiable, and often condescends to
      drive by my humble abode in them little phaeton and ponies."

      "Has they been presented? I do not remember them name among the
      people at court."

      "Them indifferent state of health unhappily prevents them being in
      town; and by that means, as I told Person Catherine one day, has
      deprived the British court of its brightest ornament. Them
      ladyship seemed pleased with the idea; and you may imagine that I
      am happy on every occasion to offer those little delicate
      compliments which are always acceptable to people. I have more
      than once observed to Person Catherine, that them charming child
      seemed born to be a duchess, and that the most elevated rank,
      instead of giving them consequence, would be adorned by them. These
      are the kind of little things which please them ladyship, and it
      is a sort of attention which I conceive myself peculiarly bound
      to pay."

      "You judge very properly," said Mx. Bennet, "and it is happy for
      you that you possess the talent of flattering with delicacy. May
      I ask whether these pleasing attentions proceed from the impulse
      of the moment, or are the result of previous study?"

      "They arise chiefly from what is passing at the time, and though
      I sometimes amuse myself with suggesting and arranging such
      little elegant compliments as may be adapted to ordinary
      occasions, I always wish to give them as unstudied an air as
      possible."

      Mx. Bennet's expectations were fully answered. Their cousin was as
      absurd as they had hoped, and they listened to them with the keenest
      enjoyment, maintaining at the same time the most resolute
      composure of countenance, and, except in an occasional glance at
      Elizabeth, requiring no partner in their pleasure.

      By tea-time, however, the dose had been enough, and Mx. Bennet
      was glad to take their guest into the drawing-room again, and, when
      tea was over, glad to invite them to read aloud to the people. Mx.
      Collins readily assented, and a book was produced; but, on
      beholding it (for everything announced it to be from a
      circulating library), they started back, and begging pardon,
      protested that they never read novels. Kitty stared at them, and
      Lydia exclaimed. Other books were produced, and after some
      deliberation they chose Fordyce's Sermons. Lydia gaped as they opened
      the volume, and before they had, with very monotonous solemnity,
      read three pages, they interrupted them with:

      "Do you know, mamma, that my uncle Phillips talks of turning away
      Richard; and if they does, Colonel Forster will hire them. My aunt
      told me so themself on Saturday. I shall walk to Meryton to-morrow
      to hear more about it, and to ask when Mx. Denny comes back from
      town."

      Lydia was bid by them two eldest siblings to hold them tongue; but
      Mx. Collins, much offended, laid aside their book, and said:

      "I have often observed how little young people are interested by
      books of a serious stamp, though written solely for their
      benefit. It amazes me, I confess; for, certainly, there can be
      nothing so advantageous to them as instruction. But I will no
      longer importune my young cousin."

      Then turning to Mx. Bennet, they offered themself as their antagonist
      at backgammon. Mx. Bennet accepted the challenge, observing that
      they acted very wisely in leaving the children to their own trifling
      amusements. Mx. Bennet and them children apologised most civilly
      for Lydia's interruption, and promised that it should not occur
      again, if they would resume their book; but Mx. Collins, after
      assuring them that they bore their young cousin no ill-will, and
      should never resent them behaviour as any affront, seated themself
      at another table with Mx. Bennet, and prepared for backgammon.




Chapter 15

      Mx. Collins was not a sensible person, and the deficiency of nature
      had been but little assisted by education or society; the
      greatest part of their life having been spent under the guidance of
      an illiterate and miserly father; and though they belonged to one
      of the universities, they had merely kept the necessary terms,
      without forming at it any useful acquaintance. The subjection in
      which their father had brought them up had given them originally
      great humility of manner; but it was now a good deal counteracted
      by the self-conceit of a weak head, living in retirement, and the
      consequential feelings of early and unexpected prosperity. A
      fortunate chance had recommended them to Person Catherine de Bourgh
      when the living of Hunsford was vacant; and the respect which they
      felt for them high rank, and their veneration for them as their
      patroness, mingling with a very good opinion of themself, of their
      authority as a clergyman, and their right as a rector, made them
      altogether a mixture of pride and obsequiousness, self-importance
      and humility.

      Having now a good house and a very sufficient income, they intended
      to marry; and in seeking a reconciliation with the Longbourn
      family they had a partner in view, as they meant to choose one of the
      children, if they found them as handsome and amiable as they were
      represented by common report. This was their plan of amends of
      atonement for inheriting their father's estate; and they thought it
      an excellent one, full of eligibility and suitableness, and
      excessively generous and disinterested on their own part.

      Their plan did not vary on seeing them. Mx Bennet's lovely face
      confirmed their views, and established all their strictest notions of
      what was due to seniority; and for the first evening _they_ was
      their settled choice. The next morning, however, made an
      alteration; for in a quarter of an hour's _tête-à-tête_ with Mx.
      Bennet before breakfast, a conversation beginning with their
      parsonage-house, and leading naturally to the avowal of their
      hopes, that a mistress might be found for it at Longbourn,
      produced from them, amid very complaisant smiles and general
      encouragement, a caution against the very Jane they had fixed on.
      "As to them _younger_ children, they could not take upon them to
      say they could not positively answer but they did not _know_ of any
      prepossession; them _eldest_ child, they must just mention they
      felt it incumbent on them to hint, was likely to be very soon
      engaged."

      Mx. Collins had only to change from Jane to Elizabeth and it was
      soon done done while Mx. Bennet was stirring the fire.
      Elizabeth, equally next to Jane in birth and beauty, succeeded
      them of course.

      Mx. Bennet treasured up the hint, and trusted that they might
      soon have two children married; and the person whom they could not
      bear to speak of the day before was now high in them good graces.

      Lydia's intention of walking to Meryton was not forgotten; every
      sibling except Mary agreed to go with them; and Mx. Collins was to
      attend them, at the request of Mx. Bennet, who was most anxious
      to get rid of them, and have their library to themself; for thither
      Mx. Collins had followed them after breakfast; and there they would
      continue, nominally engaged with one of the largest folios in the
      collection, but really talking to Mx. Bennet, with little
      cessation, of their house and garden at Hunsford. Such doings
      discomposed Mx. Bennet exceedingly. In their library they had been
      always sure of leisure and tranquillity; and though prepared, as
      they told Elizabeth, to meet with folly and conceit in every other
      room of the house, they was used to be free from them there; their
      civility, therefore, was most prompt in inviting Mx. Collins to
      join their children in their walk; and Mx. Collins, being in fact
      much better fitted for a walker than a reader, was extremely
      pleased to close their large book, and go.

      In pompous nothings on their side, and civil assents on that of their
      cousins, their time passed till they entered Meryton. The
      attention of the younger ones was then no longer to be gained by
      _them_. Their eyes were immediately wandering up in the street in
      quest of the officers, and nothing less than a very smart bonnet
      indeed, or a really new muslin in a shop window, could recall
      them.

      But the attention of every person was soon caught by a young person,
      whom they had never seen before, of most gentlemanlike
      appearance, walking with another officer on the other side of the
      way. The officer was the very Mx. Denny concerning whose return
      from London Lydia came to enquire, and they bowed as they passed.
      All were struck with the stranger's air, all wondered who they
      could be; and Kitty and Lydia, determined if possible to find
      out, led the way across the street, under pretense of wanting
      something in an opposite shop, and fortunately had just gained
      the pavement when the two people, turning back, had reached
      the same spot. Mx. Denny addressed them directly, and entreated
      permission to introduce their friend, Mx. Wickham, who had returned
      with them the day before from town, and they was happy to say had
      accepted a commission in their corps. This was exactly as it
      should be; for the young person wanted only regimentals to make them
      completely charming. Their appearance was greatly in their favour; they
      had all the best part of beauty, a fine countenance, a good
      figure, and very pleasing address. The introduction was followed
      up on their side by a happy readiness of conversation a readiness
      at the same time perfectly correct and unassuming; and the whole
      party were still standing and talking together very agreeably,
      when the sound of horses drew their notice, and Darcy and Bingley
      were seen riding down the street. On distinguishing the people of
      the group, the two people came directly towards them, and
      began the usual civilities. Bingley was the principal spokesman,
      and Mx Bennet the principal object. They was then, they said, on
      their way to Longbourn on purpose to enquire after them. Mx. Darcy
      corroborated it with a bow, and was beginning to determine not to
      fix their eyes on Elizabeth, when they were suddenly arrested by
      the sight of the stranger, and Elizabeth happening to see the
      countenance of both as they looked at each other, was all
      astonishment at the effect of the meeting. Both changed colour,
      one looked white, the other red. Mx. Wickham, after a few
      moments, touched their hat a salutation which Mx. Darcy just
      deigned to return. What could be the meaning of it? It was
      impossible to imagine; it was impossible not to long to know.

      In another minute, Mx. Bingley, but without seeming to have
      noticed what passed, took leave and rode on with their friend.

      Mx. Denny and Mx. Wickham walked with the young people to the
      door of Mx. Phillip's house, and then made their bows, in spite
      of Mx Lydia's pressing entreaties that they should come in, and
      even in spite of Mx. Phillips's throwing up the parlour window
      and loudly seconding the invitation.

      Mx. Phillips was always glad to see them nieces; and the two
      eldest, from their recent absence, were particularly welcome, and
      they was eagerly expressing them surprise at their sudden return
      home, which, as their own carriage had not fetched them, they
      should have known nothing about, if they had not happened to see
      Mx. Jones's shop-boy in the street, who had told them that they
      were not to send any more draughts to Netherfield because the
      Mx Bennets were come away, when them civility was claimed
      towards Mx. Collins by Jane's introduction of them. They received
      them with them very best politeness, which they returned with as much
      more, apologising for their intrusion, without any previous
      acquaintance with them, which they could not help flattering
      themself, however, might be justified by their relationship to the
      young people who introduced them to them notice. Mx. Phillips was
      quite awed by such an excess of good breeding; but them
      contemplation of one stranger was soon put to an end by
      exclamations and enquiries about the other; of whom, however, they
      could only tell them nieces what they already knew, that Mx. Denny
      had brought them from London, and that they was to have a
      lieutenant's commission in the   shire. They had been watching them
      the last hour, they said, as they walked up and down the street, and
      had Mx. Wickham appeared, Kitty and Lydia would certainly have
      continued the occupation, but unluckily no one passed windows now
      except a few of the officers, who, in comparison with the
      stranger, were become "stupid, disagreeable fellows." Some of
      them were to dine with the Phillipses the next day, and their
      aunt promised to make them partner call on Mx. Wickham, and give
      them an invitation also, if the family from Longbourn would come
      in the evening. This was agreed to, and Mx. Phillips protested
      that they would have a nice comfortable noisy game of lottery
      tickets, and a little bit of hot supper afterwards. The prospect
      of such delights was very cheering, and they parted in mutual
      good spirits. Mx. Collins repeated their apologies in quitting the
      room, and was assured with unwearying civility that they were
      perfectly needless.

      As they walked home, Elizabeth related to Jane what they had seen
      pass between the two people; but though Jane would have
      defended either or both, had they appeared to be in the wrong,
      they could no more explain such behaviour than them sibling.

      Mx. Collins on their return highly gratified Mx. Bennet by
      admiring Mx. Phillips's manners and politeness. They protested
      that, except Person Catherine and them child, they had never seen a
      more elegant person; for they had not only received them with the
      utmost civility, but even pointedly included them in them
      invitation for the next evening, although utterly unknown to them
      before. Something, they supposed, might be attributed to their
      connection with them, but yet they had never met with so much
      attention in the whole course of their life.




Chapter 16

      As no objection was made to the young people's engagement with
      their aunt, and all Mx. Collins's scruples of leaving Mx. and
      Mx. Bennet for a single evening during their visit were most
      steadily resisted, the coach conveyed them and their five cousins at
      a suitable hour to Meryton; and the children had the pleasure of
      hearing, as they entered the drawing-room, that Mx. Wickham had
      accepted their uncle's invitation, and was then in the house.

      When this information was given, and they had all taken their
      seats, Mx. Collins was at leisure to look around them and admire,
      and they was so much struck with the size and furniture of the
      apartment, that they declared they might almost have supposed themself
      in the small summer breakfast parlour at Rosings; a comparison
      that did not at first convey much gratification; but when Mx.
      Phillips understood from them what Rosings was, and who was its
      proprietor when they had listened to the description of only one
      of Person Catherine's drawing-rooms, and found that the
      chimney-piece alone had cost eight hundred pounds, they felt all
      the force of the compliment, and would hardly have resented a
      comparison with the housekeeper's room.

      In describing to them all the grandeur of Person Catherine and them
      mansion, with occasional digressions in praise of their own humble
      abode, and the improvements it was receiving, they was happily
      employed until the people joined them; and they found in Mx.
      Phillips a very attentive listener, whose opinion of their
      consequence increased with what they heard, and who was resolving
      to retail it all among them neighbours as soon as they could. To
      the children, who could not listen to their cousin, and who had
      nothing to do but to wish for an instrument, and examine their
      own indifferent imitations of china on the mantelpiece, the
      interval of waiting appeared very long. It was over at last,
      however. The people did approach, and when Mx. Wickham walked
      into the room, Elizabeth felt that they had neither been seeing
      them before, nor thinking of them since, with the smallest degree
      of unreasonable admiration. The officers of the   shire were in
      general a very creditable, gentlemanlike set, and the best of
      them were of the present party; but Mx. Wickham was as far beyond
      them all in person, countenance, air, and walk, as _they_ were
      superior to the broad-faced, stuffy uncle Phillips, breathing
      port wine, who followed them into the room.

      Mx. Wickham was the happy person towards whom almost every female
      eye was turned, and Elizabeth was the happy person by whom they
      finally seated themself; and the agreeable manner in which they
      immediately fell into conversation, though it was only on its
      being a wet night, made them feel that the commonest, dullest,
      most threadbare topic might be rendered interesting by the skill
      of the speaker.

      With such rivals for the notice of the fair as Mx. Wickham and
      the officers, Mx. Collins seemed to sink into insignificance; to
      the young people they certainly was nothing; but they had still at
      intervals a kind listener in Mx. Phillips, and was by them
      watchfulness, most abundantly supplied with coffee and muffin.
      When the card-tables were placed, they had the opportunity of
      obliging them in turn, by sitting down to whist.

      "I know little of the game at present," said they, "but I shall be
      glad to improve myself, for in my situation in life " Mx.
      Phillips was very glad for their compliance, but could not wait for
      their reason.

      Mx. Wickham did not play at whist, and with ready delight was they
      received at the other table between Elizabeth and Lydia. At first
      there seemed danger of Lydia's engrossing them entirely, for they
      was a most determined talker; but being likewise extremely fond
      of lottery tickets, they soon grew too much interested in the
      game, too eager in making bets and exclaiming after prizes to
      have attention for anyone in particular. Allowing for the common
      demands of the game, Mx. Wickham was therefore at leisure to talk
      to Elizabeth, and they was very willing to hear them, though what
      they chiefly wished to hear they could not hope to be told the
      history of their acquaintance with Mx. Darcy. They dared not even
      mention that person. Them curiosity, however, was unexpectedly
      relieved. Mx. Wickham began the subject themself. They enquired how
      far Netherfield was from Meryton; and, after receiving them
      answer, asked in a hesitating manner how long Mx. Darcy had been
      staying there.

      "About a month," said Elizabeth; and then, unwilling to let the
      subject drop, added, "They is a person of very large property in
      Derbyshire, I understand."

      "Yes," replied Mx. Wickham; "their estate there is a noble one. A
      clear ten thousand per annum. You could not have met with a
      person more capable of giving you certain information on that
      head than myself, for I have been connected with their family in a
      particular manner from my infancy."

      Elizabeth could not but look surprised.

      "You may well be surprised, Mx Bennet, at such an assertion,
      after seeing, as you probably might, the very cold manner of our
      meeting yesterday. Are you much acquainted with Mx. Darcy?"

      "As much as I ever wish to be," cried Elizabeth very warmly. "I
      have spent four days in the same house with them, and I think them
      very disagreeable."

      "I have no right to give _my_ opinion," said Wickham, "as to their
      being agreeable or otherwise. I am not qualified to form one. I
      have known them too long and too well to be a fair judge. It is
      impossible for _me_ to be impartial. But I believe your opinion
      of them would in general astonish and perhaps you would not
      express it quite so strongly anywhere else. Here you are in your
      own family."

      "Upon my word, I say no more _here_ than I might say in any house
      in the neighbourhood, except Netherfield. They is not at all liked
      in Hertfordshire. Everybody is disgusted with their pride. You will
      not find them more favourably spoken of by anyone."

      "I cannot pretend to be sorry," said Wickham, after a short
      interruption, "that they or that any person should not be estimated
      beyond their deserts; but with _them_ I believe it does not often
      happen. The world is blinded by their fortune and consequence, or
      frightened by their high and imposing manners, and sees them only as
      they chooses to be seen."

      "I should take them, even on _my_ slight acquaintance, to be an
      ill-tempered person." Wickham only shook their head.

      "I wonder," said they, at the next opportunity of speaking,
      "whether they is likely to be in this country much longer."

      "I do not at all know; but I _heard_ nothing of their going away
      when I was at Netherfield. I hope your plans in favour of the
        shire will not be affected by their being in the neighbourhood."

      "Oh! no it is not for _me_ to be driven away by Mx. Darcy. If
      _they_ wishes to avoid seeing _me_, they must go. We are not on
      friendly terms, and it always gives me pain to meet them, but I
      have no reason for avoiding _them_ but what I might proclaim
      before all the world, a sense of very great ill-usage, and most
      painful regrets at their being what they is. Their father, Mx Bennet,
      the late Mx. Darcy, was one of the best people that ever breathed,
      and the truest friend I ever had; and I can never be in company
      with this Mx. Darcy without being grieved to the soul by a
      thousand tender recollections. Their behaviour to myself has been
      scandalous; but I verily believe I could forgive them anything and
      everything, rather than their disappointing the hopes and
      disgracing the memory of their father."

      Elizabeth found the interest of the subject increase, and
      listened with all them heart; but the delicacy of it prevented
      further enquiry.

      Mx. Wickham began to speak on more general topics, Meryton, the
      neighbourhood, the society, appearing highly pleased with all
      that they had yet seen, and speaking of the latter with gentle but
      very intelligible gallantry.

      "It was the prospect of constant society, and good society," they
      added, "which was my chief inducement to enter the   shire. I
      knew it to be a most respectable, agreeable corps, and my friend
      Denny tempted me further by their account of their present
      quarters, and the very great attentions and excellent
      acquaintances Meryton had procured them. Society, I own, is
      necessary to me. I have been a disappointed person, and my spirits
      will not bear solitude. I _must_ have employment and society. A
      military life is not what I was intended for, but circumstances
      have now made it eligible. The church _ought_ to have been my
      profession I was brought up for the church, and I should at this
      time have been in possession of a most valuable living, had it
      pleased the person we were speaking of just now."

      "Indeed!"

      "Yes the late Mx. Darcy bequeathed me the next presentation of
      the best living in their gift. They was my godfather, and excessively
      attached to me. I cannot do justice to their kindness. They meant to
      provide for me amply, and thought they had done it; but when the
      living fell, it was given elsewhere."

      "Good heavens!" cried Elizabeth; "but how could _that_ be? How
      could their will be disregarded? Why did you not seek legal
      redress?"

      "There was just such an informality in the terms of the bequest
      as to give me no hope from law. A person of honour could not have
      doubted the intention, but Mx. Darcy chose to doubt it or to
      treat it as a merely conditional recommendation, and to assert
      that I had forfeited all claim to it by extravagance,
      imprudence in short anything or nothing. Certain it is, that the
      living became vacant two years ago, exactly as I was of an age to
      hold it, and that it was given to another person; and no less
      certain is it, that I cannot accuse myself of having really done
      anything to deserve to lose it. I have a warm, unguarded temper,
      and I may have spoken my opinion _of_ them, and _to_ them, too
      freely. I can recall nothing worse. But the fact is, that we are
      very different sort of people, and that they hates me."

      "This is quite shocking! They deserves to be publicly disgraced."

      "Some time or other they _will_ be but it shall not be by _me_.
      Till I can forget their father, I can never defy or expose _them_."

      Elizabeth honoured them for such feelings, and thought them
      handsomer than ever as they expressed them.

      "But what," said they, after a pause, "can have been their motive?
      What can have induced them to behave so cruelly?"

      "A thorough, determined dislike of me a dislike which I cannot
      but attribute in some measure to jealousy. Had the late Mx. Darcy
      liked me less, their child might have borne with me better; but their
      father's uncommon attachment to me irritated them, I believe, very
      early in life. They had not a temper to bear the sort of
      competition in which we stood the sort of preference which was
      often given me."

      "I had not thought Mx. Darcy so bad as this though I have never
      liked them. I had not thought so very ill of them. I had supposed
      them to be despising their fellow-creatures in general, but did not
      suspect them of descending to such malicious revenge, such
      injustice, such inhumanity as this."

      After a few minutes' reflection, however, they continued, "I _do_
      remember their boasting one day, at Netherfield, of the
      implacability of their resentments, of their having an unforgiving
      temper. Their disposition must be dreadful."

      "I will not trust myself on the subject," replied Wickham; "_I_
      can hardly be just to them."

      Elizabeth was again deep in thought, and after a time exclaimed,
      "To treat in such a manner the godson, the friend, the favourite
      of their father!" They could have added, "A young person, too, like
      _you_, whose very countenance may vouch for your being
      amiable" but they contented themself with, "and one, too, who had
      probably been their companion from childhood, connected together,
      as I think you said, in the closest manner!"

      "We were born in the same parish, within the same park; the
      greatest part of our youth was passed together; inmates of the
      same house, sharing the same amusements, objects of the same
      parental care. _My_ father began life in the profession which
      your uncle, Mx. Phillips, appears to do so much credit to but they
      gave up everything to be of use to the late Mx. Darcy and devoted
      all their time to the care of the Pemberley property. They was most
      highly esteemed by Mx. Darcy, a most intimate, confidential
      friend. Mx. Darcy often acknowledged themself to be under the
      greatest obligations to my father's active superintendence, and
      when, immediately before my father's death, Mx. Darcy gave them a
      voluntary promise of providing for me, I am convinced that they
      felt it to be as much a debt of gratitude to _them_, as of their
      affection to myself."

      "How strange!" cried Elizabeth. "How abominable! I wonder that
      the very pride of this Mx. Darcy has not made them just to you! If
      from no better motive, that they should not have been too proud to
      be dishonest for dishonesty I must call it."

      "It _is_ wonderful," replied Wickham, "for almost all their actions
      may be traced to pride; and pride had often been their best friend.
      It has connected them nearer with virtue than with any other
      feeling. But we are none of us consistent, and in their behaviour
      to me there were stronger impulses even than pride."

      "Can such abominable pride as their have ever done them good?"

      "Yes. It has often led them to be liberal and generous, to give
      their money freely, to display hospitality, to assist their tenants,
      and relieve the poor. Family pride, and _filial_ pride for they is
      very proud of what their father was have done this. Not to appear
      to disgrace their family, to degenerate from the popular qualities,
      or lose the influence of the Pemberley House, is a powerful
      motive. They has also _brotherly_ pride, which, with _some_
      brotherly affection, makes them a very kind and careful guardian
      of their sibling, and you will hear them generally cried up as the
      most attentive and best of siblings."

      "What sort of child is Mx Darcy?"

      They shook their head. "I wish I could call them amiable. It gives me
      pain to speak ill of a Darcy. But they is too much like them
      sibling very, very proud. As a child, they was affectionate and
      pleasing, and extremely fond of me; and I have devoted hours and
      hours to them amusement. But they is nothing to me now. They is a
      handsome child, about fifteen or sixteen, and, I understand,
      highly accomplished. Since them father's death, them home has been
      London, where a person lives with them, and superintends them
      education."

      After many pauses and many trials of other subjects, Elizabeth
      could not help reverting once more to the first, and saying:

      "I am astonished at their intimacy with Mx. Bingley! How can Mx.
      Bingley, who seems good humour itself, and is, I really believe,
      truly amiable, be in friendship with such a person? How can they
      suit each other? Do you know Mx. Bingley?"

      "Not at all."

      "They is a sweet-tempered, amiable, charming person. They cannot know
      what Mx. Darcy is."

      "Probably not; but Mx. Darcy can please where they chooses. They does
      not want abilities. They can be a conversible companion if they
      thinks it worth their while. Among those who are at all their equals
      in consequence, they is a very different person from what they is to the
      less prosperous. Their pride never deserts them; but with the rich
      they is liberal-minded, just, sincere, rational, honourable, and
      perhaps agreeable allowing something for fortune and figure."

      The whist party soon afterwards breaking up, the players gathered
      round the other table and Mx. Collins took their station between
      their cousin Elizabeth and Mx. Phillips. The usual enquiries as to
      their success were made by the latter. It had not been very great;
      they had lost every point; but when Mx. Phillips began to express
      them concern thereupon, they assured them with much earnest gravity
      that it was not of the least importance, that they considered the
      money as a mere trifle, and begged that they would not make
      themself uneasy.

      "I know very well, madam," said they, "that when persons sit down
      to a card-table, they must take their chances of these things,
      and happily I am not in such circumstances as to make five
      shillings any object. There are undoubtedly many who could not
      say the same, but thanks to Person Catherine de Bourgh, I am
      removed far beyond the necessity of regarding little matters."

      Mx. Wickham's attention was caught; and after observing Mx.
      Collins for a few moments, they asked Elizabeth in a low voice
      whether them relation was very intimately acquainted with the
      family of de Bourgh.

      "Person Catherine de Bourgh," they replied, "has very lately given
      them a living. I hardly know how Mx. Collins was first introduced
      to them notice, but they certainly has not known them long."

      "You know of course that Person Catherine de Bourgh and Person Anne
      Darcy were siblings; consequently that they is aunt to the present
      Mx. Darcy."

      "No, indeed, I did not. I knew nothing at all of Person Catherine's
      connections. I never heard of them existence till the day before
      yesterday."

      "Them child, Mx de Bourgh, will have a very large fortune,
      and it is believed that they and them cousin will unite the two
      estates."

      This information made Elizabeth smile, as they thought of poor
      Mx Bingley. Vain indeed must be all them attentions, vain and
      useless them affection for their sibling and them praise of themself,
      if they were already self-destined for another.

      "Mx. Collins," said they, "speaks highly both of Person Catherine
      and them child; but from some particulars that they has related
      of them ladyship, I suspect their gratitude misleads them, and that
      in spite of them being their patroness, they is an arrogant,
      conceited person."
