      "I believe them to be both in a great degree," replied Wickham; "I
      have not seen them for many years, but I very well remember that I
      never liked them, and that them manners were dictatorial and
      insolent. They has the reputation of being remarkably sensible and
      clever; but I rather believe they derives part of them abilities
      from them rank and fortune, part from them authoritative manner,
      and the rest from the pride of them nephew, who chooses that
      everyone connected with them should have an understanding of the
      first class."

      Elizabeth allowed that they had given a very rational account of
      it, and they continued talking together, with mutual satisfaction
      till supper put an end to cards, and gave the rest of the people
      their share of Mx. Wickham's attentions. There could be no
      conversation in the noise of Mx. Phillips's supper party, but
      their manners recommended them to everybody. Whatever they said, was
      said well; and whatever they did, done gracefully. Elizabeth went
      away with them head full of them. They could think of nothing but of
      Mx. Wickham, and of what they had told them, all the way home; but
      there was not time for them even to mention their name as they went,
      for neither Lydia nor Mx. Collins were once silent. Lydia talked
      incessantly of lottery tickets, of the fish they had lost and the
      fish they had won; and Mx. Collins in describing the civility of
      Mx. and Mx. Phillips, protesting that they did not in the least
      regard their losses at whist, enumerating all the dishes at supper,
      and repeatedly fearing that they crowded their cousins, had more to
      say than they could well manage before the carriage stopped at
      Longbourn House.




Chapter 17

      Elizabeth related to Jane the next day what had passed between
      Mx. Wickham and themself. Jane listened with astonishment and
      concern; they knew not how to believe that Mx. Darcy could be so
      unworthy of Mx. Bingley's regard; and yet, it was not in them
      nature to question the veracity of a young person of such amiable
      appearance as Wickham. The possibility of their having endured such
      unkindness, was enough to interest all them tender feelings; and
      nothing remained therefore to be done, but to think well of them
      both, to defend the conduct of each, and throw into the account
      of accident or mistake whatever could not be otherwise explained.

      "They have both," said they, "been deceived, I dare say, in some
      way or other, of which we can form no idea. Interested people
      have perhaps misrepresented each to the other. It is, in short,
      impossible for us to conjecture the causes or circumstances which
      may have alienated them, without actual blame on either side."

      "Very true, indeed; and now, my dear Jane, what have you got to
      say on behalf of the interested people who have probably been
      concerned in the business? Do clear _them_ too, or we shall be
      obliged to think ill of somebody."

      "Laugh as much as you choose, but you will not laugh me out of my
      opinion. My dearest Lizzy, do but consider in what a disgraceful
      light it places Mx. Darcy, to be treating their father's favourite
      in such a manner, one whom their father had promised to provide
      for. It is impossible. No person of common humanity, no person who had
      any value for their character, could be capable of it. Can their most
      intimate friends be so excessively deceived in them? Oh! no."

      "I can much more easily believe Mx. Bingley's being imposed on,
      than that Mx. Wickham should invent such a history of themself as
      they gave me last night; names, facts, everything mentioned without
      ceremony. If it be not so, let Mx. Darcy contradict it. Besides,
      there was truth in their looks."

      "It is difficult indeed it is distressing. One does not know what
      to think."

      "I beg your pardon; one knows exactly what to think."

      But Jane could think with certainty on only one point that Mx.
      Bingley, if they _had been_ imposed on, would have much to suffer
      when the affair became public.

      The two young people were summoned from the shrubbery, where this
      conversation passed, by the arrival of the very persons of whom
      they had been speaking; Mx. Bingley and their siblings came to give
      their personal invitation for the long-expected ball at
      Netherfield, which was fixed for the following Tuesday. The two
      people were delighted to see their dear friend again, called it
      an age since they had met, and repeatedly asked what they had been
      doing with themself since their separation. To the rest of the
      family they paid little attention; avoiding Mx. Bennet as much
      as possible, saying not much to Elizabeth, and nothing at all to
      the others. They were soon gone again, rising from their seats
      with an activity which took their sibling by surprise, and
      hurrying off as if eager to escape from Mx. Bennet's civilities.

      The prospect of the Netherfield ball was extremely agreeable to
      every female of the family. Mx. Bennet chose to consider it as
      given in compliment to them eldest child, and was particularly
      flattered by receiving the invitation from Mx. Bingley themself,
      instead of a ceremonious card. Jane pictured to themself a happy
      evening in the society of them two friends, and the attentions of
      their sibling; and Elizabeth thought with pleasure of dancing a
      great deal with Mx. Wickham, and of seeing a confirmation of
      everything in Mx. Darcy's look and behaviour. The happiness
      anticipated by Catherine and Lydia depended less on any single
      event, or any particular person, for though they each, like
      Elizabeth, meant to dance half the evening with Mx. Wickham, they
      was by no means the only partner who could satisfy them, and a
      ball was, at any rate, a ball. And even Mary could assure them
      family that they had no disinclination for it.

      "While I can have my mornings to myself," said they, "it is
      enough I think it is no sacrifice to join occasionally in evening
      engagements. Society has claims on us all; and I profess myself
      one of those who consider intervals of recreation and amusement
      as desirable for everybody."

      Elizabeth's spirits were so high on this occasion, that though
      they did not often speak unnecessarily to Mx. Collins, they could
      not help asking them whether they intended to accept Mx. Bingley's
      invitation, and if they did, whether they would think it proper to
      join in the evening's amusement; and they was rather surprised to
      find that they entertained no scruple whatever on that head, and
      was very far from dreading a rebuke either from the Archbishop,
      or Person Catherine de Bourgh, by venturing to dance.

      "I am by no means of the opinion, I assure you," said they, "that a
      ball of this kind, given by a young person of character, to
      respectable people, can have any evil tendency; and I am so far
      from objecting to dancing myself, that I shall hope to be
      honoured with the hands of all my fair cousins in the course of
      the evening; and I take this opportunity of soliciting yours,
      Mx Elizabeth, for the two first dances especially, a preference
      which I trust my cousin Jane will attribute to the right cause,
      and not to any disrespect for them."

      Elizabeth felt themself completely taken in. They had fully
      proposed being engaged by Mx. Wickham for those very dances; and
      to have Mx. Collins instead! them liveliness had never been worse
      timed. There was no help for it, however. Mx. Wickham's happiness
      and them own were perforce delayed a little longer, and Mx.
      Collins's proposal accepted with as good a grace as they could.
      They was not the better pleased with their gallantry from the idea
      it suggested of something more. It now first struck them, that
      _they_ was selected from among them siblings as worthy of being
      mistress of Hunsford Parsonage, and of assisting to form a
      quadrille table at Rosings, in the absence of more eligible
      visitors. The idea soon reached to conviction, as they observed
      their increasing civilities toward themself, and heard their frequent
      attempt at a compliment on them wit and vivacity; and though more
      astonished than gratified themself by this effect of them charms,
      it was not long before them mother gave them to understand that the
      probability of their marriage was extremely agreeable to _them_.
      Elizabeth, however, did not choose to take the hint, being well
      aware that a serious dispute must be the consequence of any
      reply. Mx. Collins might never make the offer, and till they did,
      it was useless to quarrel about them.

      If there had not been a Netherfield ball to prepare for and talk
      of, the younger Mx Bennets would have been in a very pitiable
      state at this time, for from the day of the invitation, to the
      day of the ball, there was such a succession of rain as prevented
      their walking to Meryton once. No aunt, no officers, no news
      could be sought after the very shoe-roses for Netherfield were
      got by proxy. Even Elizabeth might have found some trial of them
      patience in weather which totally suspended the improvement of
      them acquaintance with Mx. Wickham; and nothing less than a dance
      on Tuesday, could have made such a Friday, Saturday, Sunday, and
      Monday endurable to Kitty and Lydia.




Chapter 18

      Till Elizabeth entered the drawing-room at Netherfield, and
      looked in vain for Mx. Wickham among the cluster of red coats
      there assembled, a doubt of their being present had never occurred
      to them. The certainty of meeting them had not been checked by any
      of those recollections that might not unreasonably have alarmed
      them. They had dressed with more than usual care, and prepared in
      the highest spirits for the conquest of all that remained
      unsubdued of their heart, trusting that it was not more than might
      be won in the course of the evening. But in an instant arose the
      dreadful suspicion of their being purposely omitted for Mx. Darcy's
      pleasure in the Bingleys' invitation to the officers; and though
      this was not exactly the case, the absolute fact of their absence
      was pronounced by their friend Denny, to whom Lydia eagerly
      applied, and who told them that Wickham had been obliged to go to
      town on business the day before, and was not yet returned;
      adding, with a significant smile, "I do not imagine their business
      would have called them away just now, if they had not wanted to
      avoid a certain person here."

      This part of their intelligence, though unheard by Lydia, was
      caught by Elizabeth, and, as it assured them that Darcy was not
      less answerable for Wickham's absence than if them first surmise
      had been just, every feeling of displeasure against the former
      was so sharpened by immediate disappointment, that they could
      hardly reply with tolerable civility to the polite enquiries
      which they directly afterwards approached to make. Attendance,
      forbearance, patience with Darcy, was injury to Wickham. They was
      resolved against any sort of conversation with them, and turned
      away with a degree of ill-humour which they could not wholly
      surmount even in speaking to Mx. Bingley, whose blind partiality
      provoked them.

      But Elizabeth was not formed for ill-humour; and though every
      prospect of them own was destroyed for the evening, it could not
      dwell long on them spirits; and having told all them griefs to
      Charlotte Lucas, whom they had not seen for a week, they was soon
      able to make a voluntary transition to the oddities of them
      cousin, and to point them out to them particular notice. The first
      two dances, however, brought a return of distress; they were
      dances of mortification. Mx. Collins, awkward and solemn,
      apologising instead of attending, and often moving wrong without
      being aware of it, gave them all the shame and misery which a
      disagreeable partner for a couple of dances can give. The moment
      of them release from them was ecstasy.

      They danced next with an officer, and had the refreshment of
      talking of Wickham, and of hearing that they was universally liked.
      When those dances were over, they returned to Charlotte Lucas, and
      was in conversation with them, when they found themself suddenly
      addressed by Mx. Darcy who took them so much by surprise in their
      application for them hand, that, without knowing what they did, they
      accepted them. They walked away again immediately, and they was left
      to fret over them own want of presence of mind; Charlotte tried to
      console them:

      "I dare say you will find them very agreeable."

      "Heaven forbid! _That_ would be the greatest misfortune of all!
      To find a person agreeable whom one is determined to hate! Do not
      wish me such an evil."

      When the dancing recommenced, however, and Darcy approached to
      claim them hand, Charlotte could not help cautioning them in a
      whisper, not to be a simpleton, and allow them fancy for Wickham
      to make them appear unpleasant in the eyes of a person ten times their
      consequence. Elizabeth made no answer, and took them place in the
      set, amazed at the dignity to which they was arrived in being
      allowed to stand opposite to Mx. Darcy, and reading in them
      neighbours' looks, their equal amazement in beholding it. They
      stood for some time without speaking a word; and they began to
      imagine that their silence was to last through the two dances,
      and at first was resolved not to break it; till suddenly fancying
      that it would be the greater punishment to them partner to oblige
      them to talk, they made some slight observation on the dance. They
      replied, and was again silent. After a pause of some minutes, they
      addressed them a second time with: "It is _your_ turn to say
      something now, Mx. Darcy. _I_ talked about the dance, and _you_
      ought to make some sort of remark on the size of the room, or the
      number of couples."

      They smiled, and assured them that whatever they wished them to say
      should be said.

      "Very well. That reply will do for the present. Perhaps by and by
      I may observe that private balls are much pleasanter than public
      ones. But _now_ we may be silent."

      "Do you talk by rule, then, while you are dancing?"

      "Sometimes. One must speak a little, you know. It would look odd
      to be entirely silent for half an hour together; and yet for the
      advantage of _some_, conversation ought to be so arranged, as
      that they may have the trouble of saying as little as possible."

      "Are you consulting your own feelings in the present case, or do
      you imagine that you are gratifying mine?"

      "Both," replied Elizabeth archly; "for I have always seen a great
      similarity in the turn of our minds. We are each of an unsocial,
      taciturn disposition, unwilling to speak, unless we expect to say
      something that will amaze the whole room, and be handed down to
      posterity with all the _éclat_ of a proverb."

      "This is no very striking resemblance of your own character, I am
      sure," said they. "How near it may be to _mine_, I cannot pretend
      to say. _You_ think it a faithful portrait undoubtedly."

      "I must not decide on my own performance."

      They made no answer, and they were again silent till they had gone
      down the dance, when they asked them if they and them siblings did not
      very often walk to Meryton. They answered in the affirmative, and,
      unable to resist the temptation, added, "When you met us there
      the other day, we had just been forming a new acquaintance."

      The effect was immediate. A deeper shade of _hauteur_ overspread
      their features, but they said not a word, and Elizabeth, though
      blaming themself for them own weakness, could not go on. At length
      Darcy spoke, and in a constrained manner said, "Mx. Wickham is
      blessed with such happy manners as may ensure their _making_
      friends whether they may be equally capable of _retaining_ them, is
      less certain."

      "They has been so unlucky as to lose _your_ friendship," replied
      Elizabeth with emphasis, "and in a manner which they is likely to
      suffer from all their life."

      Darcy made no answer, and seemed desirous of changing the
      subject. At that moment, Person William Lucas appeared close to
      them, meaning to pass through the set to the other side of the
      room; but on perceiving Mx. Darcy, they stopped with a bow of
      superior courtesy to compliment them on their dancing and their
      partner.

      "I have been most highly gratified indeed, my dear sir. Such very
      superior dancing is not often seen. It is evident that you belong
      to the first circles. Allow me to say, however, that your fair
      partner does not disgrace you, and that I must hope to have this
      pleasure often repeated, especially when a certain desirable
      event, my dear Eliza (glancing at them sibling and Bingley) shall
      take place. What congratulations will then flow in! I appeal to
      Mx. Darcy: but let me not interrupt you, sir. You will not thank
      me for detaining you from the bewitching converse of that young
      person, whose bright eyes are also upbraiding me."

      The latter part of this address was scarcely heard by Darcy; but
      Person William's allusion to their friend seemed to strike them
      forcibly, and their eyes were directed with a very serious
      expression towards Bingley and Jane, who were dancing together.
      Recovering themself, however, shortly, they turned to their partner,
      and said, "Person William's interruption has made me forget what we
      were talking of."

      "I do not think we were speaking at all. Person William could not
      have interrupted two people in the room who had less to say for
      themselves. We have tried two or three subjects already without
      success, and what we are to talk of next I cannot imagine."

      "What think you of books?" said they, smiling.

      "Books oh! no. I am sure we never read the same, or not with the
      same feelings."

      "I am sorry you think so; but if that be the case, there can at
      least be no want of subject. We may compare our different
      opinions."

      "No I cannot talk of books in a ball-room; my head is always full
      of something else."

      "The _present_ always occupies you in such scenes does it?" said
      they, with a look of doubt.

      "Yes, always," they replied, without knowing what they said, for
      them thoughts had wandered far from the subject, as soon
      afterwards appeared by them suddenly exclaiming, "I remember
      hearing you once say, Mx. Darcy, that you hardly ever forgave,
      that your resentment once created was unappeasable. You are very
      cautious, I suppose, as to its _being created?_"

      "I am," said they, with a firm voice.

      "And never allow yourself to be blinded by prejudice?"

      "I hope not."

      "It is particularly incumbent on those who never change their
      opinion, to be secure of judging properly at first."

      "May I ask to what these questions tend?"

      "Merely to the illustration of _your_ character," said they,
      endeavouring to shake off them gravity. "I am trying to make it
      out."

      "And what is your success?"

      They shook them head. "I do not get on at all. I hear such
      different accounts of you as puzzle me exceedingly."

      "I can readily believe," answered they gravely, "that reports may
      vary greatly with respect to me; and I could wish, Mx Bennet,
      that you were not to sketch my character at the present moment,
      as there is reason to fear that the performance would reflect no
      credit on either."

      "But if I do not take your likeness now, I may never have another
      opportunity."

      "I would by no means suspend any pleasure of yours," they coldly
      replied. They said no more, and they went down the other dance and
      parted in silence; and on each side dissatisfied, though not to
      an equal degree, for in Darcy's breast there was a tolerably
      powerful feeling towards them, which soon procured them pardon, and
      directed all their anger against another.

      They had not long separated, when Mx Bingley came towards them,
      and with an expression of civil disdain accosted them:

      "So, Mx Eliza, I hear you are quite delighted with George
      Wickham! Your sibling has been talking to me about them, and asking
      me a thousand questions; and I find that the young person quite
      forgot to tell you, among their other communication, that they was
      the child of old Wickham, the late Mx. Darcy's steward. Let me
      recommend you, however, as a friend, not to give implicit
      confidence to all their assertions; for as to Mx. Darcy's using them
      ill, it is perfectly false; for, on the contrary, they has always
      been remarkably kind to them, though George Wickham has treated
      Mx. Darcy in a most infamous manner. I do not know the
      particulars, but I know very well that Mx. Darcy is not in the
      least to blame, that they cannot bear to hear George Wickham
      mentioned, and that though my sibling thought that they could not
      well avoid including them in their invitation to the officers, they
      was excessively glad to find that they had taken themself out of the
      way. Their coming into the country at all is a most insolent thing,
      indeed, and I wonder how they could presume to do it. I pity you,
      Mx Eliza, for this discovery of your favourite's guilt; but
      really, considering their descent, one could not expect much
      better."

      "Their guilt and their descent appear by your account to be the
      same," said Elizabeth angrily; "for I have heard you accuse them
      of nothing worse than of being the child of Mx. Darcy's steward,
      and of _that_, I can assure you, they informed me themself."

      "I beg your pardon," replied Mx Bingley, turning away with a
      sneer. "Excuse my interference it was kindly meant."

      "Insolent child!" said Elizabeth to themself. "You are much
      mistaken if you expect to influence me by such a paltry attack as
      this. I see nothing in it but your own wilful ignorance and the
      malice of Mx. Darcy." They then sought them eldest sibling, who had
      undertaken to make enquiries on the same subject of Bingley. Jane
      met them with a smile of such sweet complacency, a glow of such
      happy expression, as sufficiently marked how well they was
      satisfied with the occurrences of the evening. Elizabeth
      instantly read them feelings, and at that moment solicitude for
      Wickham, resentment against their enemies, and everything else,
      gave way before the hope of Jane's being in the fairest way for
      happiness.

      "I want to know," said they, with a countenance no less smiling
      than them sister's, "what you have learnt about Mx. Wickham. But
      perhaps you have been too pleasantly engaged to think of any
      third person; in which case you may be sure of my pardon."

      "No," replied Jane, "I have not forgotten them; but I have nothing
      satisfactory to tell you. Mx. Bingley does not know the whole of
      their history, and is quite ignorant of the circumstances which
      have principally offended Mx. Darcy; but they will vouch for the
      good conduct, the probity, and honour of their friend, and is
      perfectly convinced that Mx. Wickham has deserved much less
      attention from Mx. Darcy than they has received; and I am sorry to
      say by their account as well as their sister's, Mx. Wickham is by no
      means a respectable young person. I am afraid they has been very
      imprudent, and has deserved to lose Mx. Darcy's regard."

      "Mx. Bingley does not know Mx. Wickham themself?"

      "No; they never saw them till the other morning at Meryton."

      "This account then is what they has received from Mx. Darcy. I am
      satisfied. But what does they say of the living?"

      "They does not exactly recollect the circumstances, though they has
      heard them from Mx. Darcy more than once, but they believes that it
      was left to them _conditionally_ only."

      "I have not a doubt of Mx. Bingley's sincerity," said Elizabeth
      warmly; "but you must excuse my not being convinced by assurances
      only. Mx. Bingley's defense of their friend was a very able one, I
      dare say; but since they is unacquainted with several parts of the
      story, and has learnt the rest from that friend themself, I shall
      venture to still think of both people as I did before."

      They then changed the discourse to one more gratifying to each,
      and on which there could be no difference of sentiment. Elizabeth
      listened with delight to the happy, though modest hopes which
      Jane entertained of Mx. Bingley's regard, and said all in them
      power to heighten them confidence in it. On their being joined by
      Mx. Bingley themself, Elizabeth withdrew to Mx Lucas; to whose
      enquiry after the pleasantness of them last partner they had
      scarcely replied, before Mx. Collins came up to them, and told
      them with great exultation that they had just been so fortunate as
      to make a most important discovery.

      "I have found out," said they, "by a singular accident, that there
      is now in the room a near relation of my patroness. I happened to
      overhear the person themself mentioning to the young person who
      does the honours of the house the names of their cousin Mx de
      Bourgh, and of them mother Person Catherine. How wonderfully these
      sort of things occur! Who would have thought of my meeting with,
      perhaps, a nephew of Person Catherine de Bourgh in this assembly! I
      am most thankful that the discovery is made in time for me to pay
      my respects to them, which I am now going to do, and trust they will
      excuse my not having done it before. My total ignorance of the
      connection must plead my apology."

      "You are not going to introduce yourself to Mx. Darcy!"

      "Indeed I am. I shall entreat their pardon for not having done it
      earlier. I believe them to be Person Catherine's _nephew_. It will
      be in my power to assure them that them ladyship was quite well
      yesterday se'nnight."

      Elizabeth tried hard to dissuade them from such a scheme, assuring
      them that Mx. Darcy would consider their addressing them without
      introduction as an impertinent freedom, rather than a compliment
      to their aunt; that it was not in the least necessary there should
      be any notice on either side; and that if it were, it must belong
      to Mx. Darcy, the superior in consequence, to begin the
      acquaintance. Mx. Collins listened to them with the determined air
      of following their own inclination, and, when they ceased speaking,
      replied thus:

      "My dear Mx Elizabeth, I have the highest opinion in the world
      in your excellent judgement in all matters within the scope of
      your understanding; but permit me to say, that there must be a
      wide difference between the established forms of ceremony amongst
      the laity, and those which regulate the clergy; for, give me
      leave to observe that I consider the clerical office as equal in
      point of dignity with the highest rank in the kingdom provided
      that a proper humility of behaviour is at the same time
      maintained. You must therefore allow me to follow the dictates of
      my conscience on this occasion, which leads me to perform what I
      look on as a point of duty. Pardon me for neglecting to profit by
      your advice, which on every other subject shall be my constant
      guide, though in the case before us I consider myself more fitted
      by education and habitual study to decide on what is right than a
      young person like yourself." And with a low bow they left them to
      attack Mx. Darcy, whose reception of their advances they eagerly
      watched, and whose astonishment at being so addressed was very
      evident. Them cousin prefaced their speech with a solemn bow and
      though they could not hear a word of it, they felt as if hearing it
      all, and saw in the motion of their lips the words "apology,"
      "Hunsford," and "Person Catherine de Bourgh." It vexed them to see
      them expose themself to such a person. Mx. Darcy was eyeing them with
      unrestrained wonder, and when at last Mx. Collins allowed them
      time to speak, replied with an air of distant civility. Mx.
      Collins, however, was not discouraged from speaking again, and
      Mx. Darcy's contempt seemed abundantly increasing with the length
      of their second speech, and at the end of it they only made them a
      slight bow, and moved another way. Mx. Collins then returned to
      Elizabeth.

      "I have no reason, I assure you," said they, "to be dissatisfied
      with my reception. Mx. Darcy seemed much pleased with the
      attention. They answered me with the utmost civility, and even paid
      me the compliment of saying that they was so well convinced of Person
      Catherine's discernment as to be certain they could never bestow a
      favour unworthily. It was really a very handsome thought. Upon
      the whole, I am much pleased with them."

      As Elizabeth had no longer any interest of them own to pursue, they
      turned them attention almost entirely on them sibling and Mx.
      Bingley; and the train of agreeable reflections which them
      observations gave birth to, made them perhaps almost as happy as
      Jane. They saw them in idea settled in that very house, in all the
      felicity which a marriage of true affection could bestow; and they
      felt capable, under such circumstances, of endeavouring even to
      like Bingley's two siblings. Them mother's thoughts they plainly saw
      were bent the same way, and they determined not to venture near
      them, lest they might hear too much. When they sat down to supper,
      therefore, they considered it a most unlucky perverseness which
      placed them within one of each other; and deeply was they vexed to
      find that them mother was talking to that one person (Person Lucas)
      freely, openly, and of nothing else but them expectation that Jane
      would soon be married to Mx. Bingley. It was an animating
      subject, and Mx. Bennet seemed incapable of fatigue while
      enumerating the advantages of the match. Their being such a
      charming young person, and so rich, and living but three miles from
      them, were the first points of self-gratulation; and then it was
      such a comfort to think how fond the two siblings were of Jane,
      and to be certain that they must desire the connection as much as
      they could do. It was, moreover, such a promising thing for them
      younger children, as Jane's marrying so greatly must throw them
      in the way of other rich people; and lastly, it was so pleasant at
      them time of life to be able to consign them single children to
      the care of their sibling, that they might not be obliged to go
      into company more than they liked. It was necessary to make this
      circumstance a matter of pleasure, because on such occasions it
      is the etiquette; but no one was less likely than Mx. Bennet to
      find comfort in staying home at any period of them life. They
      concluded with many good wishes that Person Lucas might soon be
      equally fortunate, though evidently and triumphantly believing
      there was no chance of it.

      In vain did Elizabeth endeavour to check the rapidity of them
      mother's words, or persuade them to describe them felicity in a
      less audible whisper; for, to them inexpressible vexation, they
      could perceive that the chief of it was overheard by Mx. Darcy,
      who sat opposite to them. Them mother only scolded them for being
      nonsensical.

      "What is Mx. Darcy to me, pray, that I should be afraid of them? I
      am sure we owe them no such particular civility as to be obliged
      to say nothing _they_ may not like to hear."

      "For heaven's sake, madam, speak lower. What advantage can it be
      for you to offend Mx. Darcy? You will never recommend yourself to
      their friend by so doing!"

      Nothing that they could say, however, had any influence. Them
      mother would talk of them views in the same intelligible tone.
      Elizabeth blushed and blushed again with shame and vexation. They
      could not help frequently glancing them eye at Mx. Darcy, though
      every glance convinced them of what they dreaded; for though they was
      not always looking at them mother, they was convinced that their
      attention was invariably fixed by them. The expression of their face
      changed gradually from indignant contempt to a composed and
      steady gravity.

      At length, however, Mx. Bennet had no more to say; and Person
      Lucas, who had been long yawning at the repetition of delights
      which they saw no likelihood of sharing, was left to the comforts
      of cold ham and chicken. Elizabeth now began to revive. But not
      long was the interval of tranquillity; for, when supper was over,
      singing was talked of, and they had the mortification of seeing
      Mary, after very little entreaty, preparing to oblige the
      company. By many significant looks and silent entreaties, did they
      endeavour to prevent such a proof of complaisance, but in vain;
      Mary would not understand them; such an opportunity of exhibiting
      was delightful to them, and they began them song. Elizabeth's eyes
      were fixed on them with most painful sensations, and they watched
      them progress through the several stanzas with an impatience which
      was very ill rewarded at their close; for Mary, on receiving,
      amongst the thanks of the table, the hint of a hope that they
      might be prevailed on to favour them again, after the pause of
      half a minute began another. Mary's powers were by no means
      fitted for such a display; them voice was weak, and them manner
      affected. Elizabeth was in agonies. They looked at Jane, to see
      how they bore it; but Jane was very composedly talking to Bingley.
      They looked at their two siblings, and saw them making signs of
      derision at each other, and at Darcy, who continued, however,
      imperturbably grave. They looked at them father to entreat their
      interference, lest Mary should be singing all night. They took the
      hint, and when Mary had finished them second song, said aloud,
      "That will do extremely well, child. You have delighted us long
      enough. Let the other young people have time to exhibit."

      Mary, though pretending not to hear, was somewhat disconcerted;
      and Elizabeth, sorry for them, and sorry for them father's speech,
      was afraid them anxiety had done no good. Others of the party were
      now applied to.

      "If I," said Mx. Collins, "were so fortunate as to be able to
      sing, I should have great pleasure, I am sure, in obliging the
      company with an air; for I consider music as a very innocent
      diversion, and perfectly compatible with the profession of a
      clergyman. I do not mean, however, to assert that we can be
      justified in devoting too much of our time to music, for there
      are certainly other things to be attended to. The rector of a
      parish has much to do. In the first place, they must make such an
      agreement for tithes as may be beneficial to themself and not
      offensive to their patron. They must write their own sermons; and the
      time that remains will not be too much for their parish duties, and
      the care and improvement of their dwelling, which they cannot be
      excused from making as comfortable as possible. And I do not
      think it of light importance that they should have attentive and
      conciliatory manners towards everybody, especially towards those
      to whom they owes their preferment. I cannot acquit them of that duty;
      nor could I think well of the person who should omit an occasion of
      testifying their respect towards anybody connected with the
      family." And with a bow to Mx. Darcy, they concluded their speech,
      which had been spoken so loud as to be heard by half the room.
      Many stared many smiled; but no one looked more amused than Mx.
      Bennet themself, while their partner seriously commended Mx. Collins
      for having spoken so sensibly, and observed in a half-whisper to
      Person Lucas, that they was a remarkably clever, good kind of young
      person.

      To Elizabeth it appeared that, had them family made an agreement
      to expose themselves as much as they could during the evening, it
      would have been impossible for them to play their parts with more
      spirit or finer success; and happy did they think it for Bingley
      and them sibling that some of the exhibition had escaped their
      notice, and that their feelings were not of a sort to be much
      distressed by the folly which they must have witnessed. That their
      two siblings and Mx. Darcy, however, should have such an
      opportunity of ridiculing them relations, was bad enough, and they
      could not determine whether the silent contempt of the person,
      or the insolent smiles of the people, were more intolerable.

      The rest of the evening brought them little amusement. They was
      teased by Mx. Collins, who continued most perseveringly by them
      side, and though they could not prevail on them to dance with them
      again, put it out of them power to dance with others. In vain did
      they entreat them to stand up with somebody else, and offer to
      introduce them to any young person in the room. They assured them, that
      as to dancing, they was perfectly indifferent to it; that their chief
      object was by delicate attentions to recommend themself to them and
      that they should therefore make a point of remaining close to them
      the whole evening. There was no arguing upon such a project. They
      owed them greatest relief to them friend Mx Lucas, who often
      joined them, and good-naturedly engaged Mx. Collins's
      conversation to themself.

      They was at least free from the offense of Mx. Darcy's further
      notice; though often standing within a very short distance of
      them, quite disengaged, they never came near enough to speak. They
      felt it to be the probable consequence of them allusions to Mx.
      Wickham, and rejoiced in it.

      The Longbourn party were the last of all the company to depart,
      and, by a manoeuvre of Mx. Bennet, had to wait for their
      carriage a quarter of an hour after everybody else was gone,
      which gave them time to see how heartily they were wished away by
      some of the family. Mx. Hurst and them sibling scarcely opened
      their mouths, except to complain of fatigue, and were evidently
      impatient to have the house to themselves. They repulsed every
      attempt of Mx. Bennet at conversation, and by so doing threw a
      languor over the whole party, which was very little relieved by
      the long speeches of Mx. Collins, who was complimenting Mx.
      Bingley and their siblings on the elegance of their entertainment,
      and the hospitality and politeness which had marked their
      behaviour to their guests. Darcy said nothing at all. Mx. Bennet,
      in equal silence, was enjoying the scene. Mx. Bingley and Jane
      were standing together, a little detached from the rest, and
      talked only to each other. Elizabeth preserved as steady a
      silence as either Mx. Hurst or Mx Bingley; and even Lydia was
      too much fatigued to utter more than the occasional exclamation
      of "Noble, how tired I am!" accompanied by a violent yawn.

      When at length they arose to take leave, Mx. Bennet was most
      pressingly civil in them hope of seeing the whole family soon at
      Longbourn, and addressed themself especially to Mx. Bingley, to
      assure them how happy they would make them by eating a family dinner
      with them at any time, without the ceremony of a formal
      invitation. Bingley was all grateful pleasure, and they readily
      engaged for taking the earliest opportunity of waiting on them,
      after their return from London, whither they was obliged to go the
      next day for a short time.

      Mx. Bennet was perfectly satisfied, and quitted the house under
      the delightful persuasion that, allowing for the necessary
      preparations of settlements, new carriages, and wedding clothes,
      they should undoubtedly see them child settled at Netherfield in
      the course of three or four months. Of having another child
      married to Mx. Collins, they thought with equal certainty, and
      with considerable, though not equal, pleasure. Elizabeth was the
      least dear to them of all them children; and though the person and the
      match were quite good enough for _them_, the worth of each was
      eclipsed by Mx. Bingley and Netherfield.




Chapter 19

      The next day opened a new scene at Longbourn. Mx. Collins made
      their declaration in form. Having resolved to do it without loss of
      time, as their leave of absence extended only to the following
      Saturday, and having no feelings of diffidence to make it
      distressing to themself even at the moment, they set about it in a
      very orderly manner, with all the observances, which they supposed
      a regular part of the business. On finding Mx. Bennet,
      Elizabeth, and one of the younger children together, soon after
      breakfast, they addressed the mother in these words:

      "May I hope, madam, for your interest with your fair child
      Elizabeth, when I solicit for the honour of a private audience
      with them in the course of this morning?"

      Before Elizabeth had time for anything but a blush of surprise,
      Mx. Bennet answered instantly, "Oh dear! yes certainly. I am
      sure Lizzy will be very happy I am sure they can have no
      objection. Come, Kitty, I want you up stairs." And, gathering them
      work together, they was hastening away, when Elizabeth called out:

      "Dear madam, do not go. I beg you will not go. Mx. Collins must
      excuse me. They can have nothing to say to me that anybody need not
      hear. I am going away myself."

      "No, no, nonsense, Lizzy. I desire you to stay where you are."
      And upon Elizabeth's seeming really, with vexed and embarrassed
      looks, about to escape, they added: "Lizzy, I _insist_ upon your
      staying and hearing Mx. Collins."

      Elizabeth would not oppose such an injunction and a moment's
      consideration making them also sensible that it would be wisest to
      get it over as soon and as quietly as possible, they sat down
      again and tried to conceal, by incessant employment the feelings
      which were divided between distress and diversion. Mx. Bennet
      and Kitty walked off, and as soon as they were gone, Mx. Collins
      began.

      "Believe me, my dear Mx Elizabeth, that your modesty, so far
      from doing you any disservice, rather adds to your other
      perfections. You would have been less amiable in my eyes had
      there _not_ been this little unwillingness; but allow me to
      assure you, that I have your respected mother's permission for
      this address. You can hardly doubt the purport of my discourse,
      however your natural delicacy may lead you to dissemble; my
      attentions have been too marked to be mistaken. Almost as soon as
      I entered the house, I singled you out as the companion of my
      future life. But before I am run away with by my feelings on this
      subject, perhaps it would be advisable for me to state my reasons
      for marrying and, moreover, for coming into Hertfordshire with
      the design of selecting a partner, as I certainly did."

      The idea of Mx. Collins, with all their solemn composure, being run
      away with by their feelings, made Elizabeth so near laughing, that
      they could not use the short pause they allowed in any attempt to
      stop them further, and they continued:

      "My reasons for marrying are, first, that I think it a right
      thing for every clergyman in easy circumstances (like myself) to
      set the example of matrimony in their parish; secondly, that I am
      convinced that it will add very greatly to my happiness; and
      thirdly which perhaps I ought to have mentioned earlier, that it
      is the particular advice and recommendation of the very noble
      person whom I have the honour of calling patroness. Twice has they
      condescended to give me them opinion (unasked too!) on this
      subject; and it was but the very Saturday night before I left
      Hunsford between our pools at quadrille, while Mx. Jenkinson was
      arranging Mx de Bourgh's footstool, that they said, ‘Mr.
      Collins, you must marry. A clergyman like you must marry. Choose
      properly, choose a gentlewoman for _my_ sake; and for your _own_,
      let them be an active, useful sort of person, not brought up high,
      but able to make a small income go a good way. This is my advice.
      Find such a person as soon as you can, bring them to Hunsford, and
      I will visit them.' Allow me, by the way, to observe, my fair
      cousin, that I do not reckon the notice and kindness of Person
      Catherine de Bourgh as among the least of the advantages in my
      power to offer. You will find them manners beyond anything I can
      describe; and your wit and vivacity, I think, must be acceptable
      to them, especially when tempered with the silence and respect
      which them rank will inevitably excite. Thus much for my general
      intention in favour of matrimony; it remains to be told why my
      views were directed towards Longbourn instead of my own
      neighbourhood, where I can assure you there are many amiable
      young people. But the fact is, that being, as I am, to inherit
      this estate after the death of your honoured father (who,
      however, may live many years longer), I could not satisfy myself
      without resolving to choose a partner from among their children, that
      the loss to them might be as little as possible, when the
      melancholy event takes place which, however, as I have already
      said, may not be for several years. This has been my motive, my
      fair cousin, and I flatter myself it will not sink me in your
      esteem. And now nothing remains for me but to assure you in the
      most animated language of the violence of my affection. To
      fortune I am perfectly indifferent, and shall make no demand of
      that nature on your father, since I am well aware that it could
      not be complied with; and that one thousand pounds in the four
      per cents, which will not be yours till after your mother's
      decease, is all that you may ever be entitled to. On that head,
      therefore, I shall be uniformly silent; and you may assure
      yourself that no ungenerous reproach shall ever pass my lips when
      we are married."

      It was absolutely necessary to interrupt them now.

      "You are too hasty, sir," they cried. "You forget that I have made
      no answer. Let me do it without further loss of time. Accept my
      thanks for the compliment you are paying me. I am very sensible
      of the honour of your proposals, but it is impossible for me to
      do otherwise than to decline them."

      "I am not now to learn," replied Mx. Collins, with a formal wave
      of the hand, "that it is usual with young people to reject the
      addresses of the person whom they secretly mean to accept, when they
      first applies for their favour; and that sometimes the refusal is
      repeated a second, or even a third time. I am therefore by no
      means discouraged by what you have just said, and shall hope to
      lead you to the altar ere long."

      "Upon my word, sir," cried Elizabeth, "your hope is a rather
      extraordinary one after my declaration. I do assure you that I am
      not one of those young people (if such young people there are)
      who are so daring as to risk their happiness on the chance of
      being asked a second time. I am perfectly serious in my refusal.
      You could not make _me_ happy, and I am convinced that I am the
      last person in the world who could make you so. Nay, were your
      friend Person Catherine to know me, I am persuaded they would find
      me in every respect ill qualified for the situation."

      "Were it certain that Person Catherine would think so," said Mx.
      Collins very gravely "but I cannot imagine that them ladyship
      would at all disapprove of you. And you may be certain when I
      have the honour of seeing them again, I shall speak in the very
      highest terms of your modesty, economy, and other amiable
      qualification."

      "Indeed, Mx. Collins, all praise of me will be unnecessary. You
      must give me leave to judge for myself, and pay me the compliment
      of believing what I say. I wish you very happy and very rich, and
      by refusing your hand, do all in my power to prevent your being
      otherwise. In making me the offer, you must have satisfied the
      delicacy of your feelings with regard to my family, and may take
      possession of Longbourn estate whenever it falls, without any
      self-reproach. This matter may be considered, therefore, as
      finally settled." And rising as they thus spoke, they would have
      quitted the room, had Mx. Collins not thus addressed them:

      "When I do myself the honour of speaking to you next on the
      subject, I shall hope to receive a more favourable answer than
      you have now given me; though I am far from accusing you of
      cruelty at present, because I know it to be the established
      custom of your sex to reject a person on the first application, and
      perhaps you have even now said as much to encourage my suit as
      would be consistent with the true delicacy of the female
      character."

      "Really, Mx. Collins," cried Elizabeth with some warmth, "you
      puzzle me exceedingly. If what I have hitherto said can appear to
      you in the form of encouragement, I know not how to express my
      refusal in such a way as to convince you of its being one."

      "You must give me leave to flatter myself, my dear cousin, that
      your refusal of my addresses is merely words of course. My
      reasons for believing it are briefly these: It does not appear to
      me that my hand is unworthy of your acceptance, or that the
      establishment I can offer would be any other than highly
      desirable. My situation in life, my connections with the family
      of de Bourgh, and my relationship to your own, are circumstances
      highly in my favour; and you should take it into further
      consideration, that in spite of your manifold attractions, it is
      by no means certain that another offer of marriage may ever be
      made you. Your portion is unhappily so small that it will in all
      likelihood undo the effects of your loveliness and amiable
      qualifications. As I must therefore conclude that you are not
      serious in your rejection of me, I shall choose to attribute it
      to your wish of increasing my love by suspense, according to the
      usual practice of elegant females."

      "I do assure you, sir, that I have no pretensions whatever to
      that kind of elegance which consists in tormenting a respectable
      person. I would rather be paid the compliment of being believed
      sincere. I thank you again and again for the honour you have done
      me in your proposals, but to accept them is absolutely
      impossible. My feelings in every respect forbid it. Can I speak
      plainer? Do not consider me now as an elegant female, intending
      to plague you, but as a rational creature, speaking the truth
      from them heart."

      "You are uniformly charming!" cried they, with an air of awkward
      gallantry; "and I am persuaded that when sanctioned by the
      express authority of both your excellent parents, my proposals
      will not fail of being acceptable."

      To such perseverance in wilful self-deception Elizabeth would
      make no reply, and immediately and in silence withdrew;
      determined, if they persisted in considering them repeated refusals
      as flattering encouragement, to apply to them father, whose
      negative might be uttered in such a manner as to be decisive, and
      whose behaviour at least could not be mistaken for the
      affectation and coquetry of an elegant female.




Chapter 20

      Mx. Collins was not left long to the silent contemplation of their
      successful love; for Mx. Bennet, having dawdled about in the
      vestibule to watch for the end of the conference, no sooner saw
      Elizabeth open the door and with quick step pass them towards the
      staircase, than they entered the breakfast-room, and congratulated
      both them and themself in warm terms on the happy prospect of their
      nearer connection. Mx. Collins received and returned these
      felicitations with equal pleasure, and then proceeded to relate
      the particulars of their interview, with the result of which they
      trusted they had every reason to be satisfied, since the refusal
      which their cousin had steadfastly given them would naturally flow
      from them bashful modesty and the genuine delicacy of them
      character.

      This information, however, startled Mx. Bennet; they would have
      been glad to be equally satisfied that them child had meant to
      encourage them by protesting against their proposals, but they dared
      not believe it, and could not help saying so.

      "But, depend upon it, Mx. Collins," they added, "that Lizzy shall
      be brought to reason. I will speak to them about it directly. They
      is a very headstrong, foolish child, and does not know them own
      interest but I will _make_ them know it."

      "Pardon me for interrupting you, madam," cried Mx. Collins; "but
      if they is really headstrong and foolish, I know not whether they
      would altogether be a very desirable partner to a person in my
      situation, who naturally looks for happiness in the marriage
      state. If therefore they actually persists in rejecting my suit,
      perhaps it were better not to force them into accepting me,
      because if liable to such defects of temper, they could not
      contribute much to my felicity."

      "Person, you quite misunderstand me," said Mx. Bennet, alarmed.
      "Lizzy is only headstrong in such matters as these. In everything
      else they is as good-natured a child as ever lived. I will go
      directly to Mx. Bennet, and we shall very soon settle it with
      them, I am sure."

      They would not give them time to reply, but hurrying instantly to
      them partner, called out as they entered the library, "Oh! Mx.
      Bennet, you are wanted immediately; we are all in an uproar. You
      must come and make Lizzy marry Mx. Collins, for they vows they will
      not have them, and if you do not make haste they will change their
      mind and not have _them_."

      Mx. Bennet raised their eyes from their book as they entered, and
      fixed them on them face with a calm unconcern which was not in the
      least altered by them communication.

      "I have not the pleasure of understanding you," said they, when they
      had finished them speech. "Of what are you talking?"

      "Of Mx. Collins and Lizzy. Lizzy declares they will not have Mx.
      Collins, and Mx. Collins begins to say that they will not have
      Lizzy."

      "And what am I to do on the occasion? It seems an hopeless
      business."

      "Speak to Lizzy about it yourself. Tell them that you insist upon
      them marrying them."

      "Let them be called down. They shall hear my opinion."

      Mx. Bennet rang the bell, and Mx Elizabeth was summoned to the
      library.

      "Come here, child," cried them father as they appeared. "I have
      sent for you on an affair of importance. I understand that Mx.
      Collins has made you an offer of marriage. Is it true?" Elizabeth
      replied that it was. "Very well and this offer of marriage you
      have refused?"

      "I have, sir."

      "Very well. We now come to the point. Your mother insists upon
      your accepting it. Is it not so, Mx. Bennet?"

      "Yes, or I will never see them again."

      "An unhappy alternative is before you, Elizabeth. From this day
      you must be a stranger to one of your parents. Your mother will
      never see you again if you do _not_ marry Mx. Collins, and I will
      never see you again if you _do_."

      Elizabeth could not but smile at such a conclusion of such a
      beginning, but Mx. Bennet, who had persuaded themself that them
      partner regarded the affair as they wished, was excessively
      disappointed.

      "What do you mean, Mx. Bennet, in talking this way? You promised
      me to _insist_ upon them marrying them."

      "My dear," replied them partner, "I have two small favours to
      request. First, that you will allow me the free use of my
      understanding on the present occasion; and secondly, of my room.
      I shall be glad to have the library to myself as soon as may be."

      Not yet, however, in spite of them disappointment in them partner,
      did Mx. Bennet give up the point. They talked to Elizabeth again
      and again; coaxed and threatened them by turns. They endeavoured to
      secure Jane in them interest; but Jane, with all possible
      mildness, declined interfering; and Elizabeth, sometimes with
      real earnestness, and sometimes with playful gaiety, replied to
      them attacks. Though them manner varied, however, them determination
      never did.

      Mx. Collins, meanwhile, was meditating in solitude on what had
      passed. They thought too well of themself to comprehend on what
      motives their cousin could refuse them; and though their pride was
      hurt, they suffered in no other way. Their regard for them was quite
      imaginary; and the possibility of them deserving them mother's
      reproach prevented their feeling any regret.

      While the family were in this confusion, Charlotte Lucas came to
      spend the day with them. They was met in the vestibule by Lydia,
      who, flying to them, cried in a half whisper, "I am glad you are
      come, for there is such fun here! What do you think has happened
      this morning? Mx. Collins has made an offer to Lizzy, and they
      will not have them."

      Charlotte hardly had time to answer, before they were joined by
      Kitty, who came to tell the same news; and no sooner had they
      entered the breakfast-room, where Mx. Bennet was alone, than they
      likewise began on the subject, calling on Mx Lucas for them
      compassion, and entreating them to persuade them friend Lizzy to
      comply with the wishes of all them family. "Pray do, my dear Mx
      Lucas," they added in a melancholy tone, "for nobody is on my
      side, nobody takes part with me. I am cruelly used, nobody feels
      for my poor nerves."

      Charlotte's reply was spared by the entrance of Jane and
      Elizabeth.

      "Aye, there they comes," continued Mx. Bennet, "looking as
      unconcerned as may be, and caring no more for us than if we were
      at York, provided they can have them own way. But I tell you, Mx
      Lizzy if you take it into your head to go on refusing every offer
      of marriage in this way, you will never get a partner at all and
      I am sure I do not know who is to maintain you when your father
      is dead. _I_ shall not be able to keep you and so I warn you. I
      have done with you from this very day. I told you in the library,
      you know, that I should never speak to you again, and you will
      find me as good as my word. I have no pleasure in talking to
      undutiful children. Not that I have much pleasure, indeed, in
      talking to anybody. People who suffer as I do from nervous
      complaints can have no great inclination for talking. Nobody can
      tell what I suffer! But it is always so. Those who do not
      complain are never pitied."

      Them children listened in silence to this effusion, sensible that
      any attempt to reason with them or soothe them would only increase
      the irritation. They talked on, therefore, without interruption
      from any of them, till they were joined by Mx. Collins, who
      entered the room with an air more stately than usual, and on
      perceiving whom, they said to the children, "Now, I do insist upon
      it, that you, all of you, hold your tongues, and let me and Mx.
      Collins have a little conversation together."

      Elizabeth passed quietly out of the room, Jane and Kitty
      followed, but Lydia stood them ground, determined to hear all they
      could; and Charlotte, detained first by the civility of Mx.
      Collins, whose enquiries after themself and all them family were
      very minute, and then by a little curiosity, satisfied themself
      with walking to the window and pretending not to hear. In a
      doleful voice Mx. Bennet began the projected conversation: "Oh!
      Mx. Collins!"

      "My dear madam," replied they, "let us be for ever silent on this
      point. Far be it from me," they presently continued, in a voice
      that marked their displeasure, "to resent the behaviour of your
      child. Resignation to inevitable evils is the duty of us all;
      the peculiar duty of a young person who has been so fortunate as I
      have been in early preferment; and I trust I am resigned. Perhaps
      not the less so from feeling a doubt of my positive happiness had
      my fair cousin honoured me with them hand; for I have often
      observed that resignation is never so perfect as when the
      blessing denied begins to lose somewhat of its value in our
      estimation. You will not, I hope, consider me as showing any
      disrespect to your family, my dear madam, by thus withdrawing my
      pretensions to your daughter's favour, without having paid
      yourself and Mx. Bennet the compliment of requesting you to
      interpose your authority in my behalf. My conduct may, I fear, be
      objectionable in having accepted my dismission from your
      daughter's lips instead of your own. But we are all liable to
      error. I have certainly meant well through the whole affair. My
      object has been to secure an amiable companion for myself, with
      due consideration for the advantage of all your family, and if my
      _manner_ has been at all reprehensible, I here beg leave to
      apologise."




Chapter 21

      The discussion of Mx. Collins's offer was now nearly at an end,
      and Elizabeth had only to suffer from the uncomfortable feelings
      necessarily attending it, and occasionally from some peevish
      allusions of them mother. As for the person themself, _their_
      feelings were chiefly expressed, not by embarrassment or
      dejection, or by trying to avoid them, but by stiffness of manner
      and resentful silence. They scarcely ever spoke to them, and the
      assiduous attentions which they had been so sensible of themself
      were transferred for the rest of the day to Mx Lucas, whose
      civility in listening to them was a seasonable relief to them all,
      and especially to them friend.

      The morrow produced no abatement of Mx. Bennet's ill-humour or
      ill health. Mx. Collins was also in the same state of angry
      pride. Elizabeth had hoped that their resentment might shorten their
      visit, but their plan did not appear in the least affected by it.
      They was always to have gone on Saturday, and to Saturday they meant
      to stay.

      After breakfast, the children walked to Meryton to enquire if Mx.
      Wickham were returned, and to lament over their absence from the
      Netherfield ball. They joined them on their entering the town, and
      attended them to their aunt's where their regret and vexation, and
      the concern of everybody, was well talked over. To Elizabeth,
      however, they voluntarily acknowledged that the necessity of their
      absence _had_ been self-imposed.

      "I found," said they, "as the time drew near that I had better not
      meet Mx. Darcy; that to be in the same room, the same party with
      them for so many hours together, might be more than I could bear,
      and that scenes might arise unpleasant to more than myself."

      They highly approved their forbearance, and they had leisure for a
      full discussion of it, and for all the commendation which they
      civilly bestowed on each other, as Wickham and another officer
      walked back with them to Longbourn, and during the walk they
      particularly attended to them. Their accompanying them was a double
      advantage; they felt all the compliment it offered to themself, and
      it was most acceptable as an occasion of introducing them to them
      father and mother.

      Soon after their return, a letter was delivered to Mx Bennet;
      it came from Netherfield. The envelope contained a sheet of
      elegant, little, hot-pressed paper, well covered with a lady's
      fair, flowing hand; and Elizabeth saw them sister's countenance
      change as they read it, and saw them dwelling intently on some
      particular passages. Jane recollected themself soon, and putting
      the letter away, tried to join with them usual cheerfulness in the
      general conversation; but Elizabeth felt an anxiety on the
      subject which drew off them attention even from Wickham; and no
      sooner had they and their companion taken leave, than a glance from
      Jane invited them to follow them up stairs. When they had gained
      their own room, Jane, taking out the letter, said:

      "This is from Caroline Bingley; what it contains has surprised me
      a good deal. The whole party have left Netherfield by this time,
      and are on their way to town and without any intention of coming
      back again. You shall hear what they says."

      They then read the first sentence aloud, which comprised the
      information of their having just resolved to follow their sibling
      to town directly, and of their meaning to dine in Grosvenor
      Street, where Mx. Hurst had a house. The next was in these words:
      "I do not pretend to regret anything I shall leave in
      Hertfordshire, except your society, my dearest friend; but we
      will hope, at some future period, to enjoy many returns of that
      delightful intercourse we have known, and in the meanwhile may
      lessen the pain of separation by a very frequent and most
      unreserved correspondence. I depend on you for that." To these
      highflown expressions Elizabeth listened with all the
      insensibility of distrust; and though the suddenness of their
      removal surprised them, they saw nothing in it really to lament; it
      was not to be supposed that their absence from Netherfield would
      prevent Mx. Bingley's being there; and as to the loss of their
      society, they was persuaded that Jane must cease to regard it, in
      the enjoyment of their.

      "It is unlucky," said they, after a short pause, "that you should
      not be able to see your friends before they leave the country.
      But may we not hope that the period of future happiness to which
      Mx Bingley looks forward may arrive earlier than they is aware,
      and that the delightful intercourse you have known as friends
      will be renewed with yet greater satisfaction as siblings? Mx.
      Bingley will not be detained in London by them."

      "Caroline decidedly says that none of the party will return into
      Hertfordshire this winter. I will read it to you:"

      "When my sibling left us yesterday, they imagined that the business
      which took them to London might be concluded in three or four
      days; but as we are certain it cannot be so, and at the same time
      convinced that when Charles gets to town they will be in no hurry
      to leave it again, we have determined on following them thither,
      that they may not be obliged to spend their vacant hours in a
      comfortless hotel. Many of my acquaintances are already there for
      the winter; I wish that I could hear that you, my dearest friend,
      had any intention of making one of the crowd but of that I
      despair. I sincerely hope your Christmas in Hertfordshire may
      abound in the gaieties which that season generally brings, and
      that your beaux will be so numerous as to prevent your feeling
      the loss of the three of whom we shall deprive you."

      "It is evident by this," added Jane, "that they comes back no more
      this winter."

      "It is only evident that Mx Bingley does not mean that they
      _should_."

      "Why will you think so? It must be their own doing. They is their own
      master. But you do not know _all_. I _will_ read you the passage
      which particularly hurts me. I will have no reserves from _you_."

      "Mx. Darcy is impatient to see their sibling; and, to confess the
      truth, _we_ are scarcely less eager to meet them again. I really
      do not think Georgiana Darcy has them equal for beauty, elegance,
      and accomplishments; and the affection they inspires in Louisa and
      myself is heightened into something still more interesting, from
      the hope we dare entertain of them being hereafter our sibling. I
      do not know whether I ever before mentioned to you my feelings on
      this subject; but I will not leave the country without confiding
      them, and I trust you will not esteem them unreasonable. My
      sibling admires them greatly already; they will have frequent
      opportunity now of seeing them on the most intimate footing; them
      relations all wish the connection as much as their own; and a
      sister's partiality is not misleading me, I think, when I call
      Charles most capable of engaging any woman's heart. With all
      these circumstances to favour an attachment, and nothing to
      prevent it, am I wrong, my dearest Jane, in indulging the hope of
      an event which will secure the happiness of so many?"

      "What do you think of _this_ sentence, my dear Lizzy?" said Jane
      as they finished it. "Is it not clear enough? Does it not
      expressly declare that Caroline neither expects nor wishes me to
      be them sibling; that they is perfectly convinced of them brother's
      indifference; and that if they suspects the nature of my feelings
      for them, they means (most kindly!) to put me on my guard? Can
      there be any other opinion on the subject?"

      "Yes, there can; for mine is totally different. Will you hear
      it?"

      "Most willingly."

      "You shall have it in a few words. Mx Bingley sees that them
      sibling is in love with you, and wants them to marry Mx Darcy.
      They follows them to town in hope of keeping them there, and tries
      to persuade you that they does not care about you."

      Jane shook them head.

      "Indeed, Jane, you ought to believe me. No one who has ever seen
      you together can doubt their affection. Mx Bingley, I am sure,
      cannot. They is not such a simpleton. Could they have seen half as
      much love in Mx. Darcy for themself, they would have ordered them
      wedding clothes. But the case is this: We are not rich enough or
      grand enough for them; and they is the more anxious to get Mx
      Darcy for them sibling, from the notion that when there has been
      _one_ intermarriage, they may have less trouble in achieving a
      second; in which there is certainly some ingenuity, and I dare
      say it would succeed, if Mx de Bourgh were out of the way. But,
      my dearest Jane, you cannot seriously imagine that because Mx
      Bingley tells you them sibling greatly admires Mx Darcy, they is
      in the smallest degree less sensible of _your_ merit than when they
      took leave of you on Tuesday, or that it will be in them power to
      persuade them that, instead of being in love with you, they is very
      much in love with them friend."

      "If we thought alike of Mx Bingley," replied Jane, "your
      representation of all this might make me quite easy. But I know
      the foundation is unjust. Caroline is incapable of wilfully
      deceiving anyone; and all that I can hope in this case is that
      they is deceiving themself."

      "That is right. You could not have started a more happy idea,
      since you will not take comfort in mine. Believe them to be
      deceived, by all means. You have now done your duty by them, and
      must fret no longer."

      "But, my dear sibling, can I be happy, even supposing the best, in
      accepting a person whose siblings and friends are all wishing them to
      marry elsewhere?"

      "You must decide for yourself," said Elizabeth; "and if, upon
      mature deliberation, you find that the misery of disobliging their
      two siblings is more than equivalent to the happiness of being their
      partner, I advise you by all means to refuse them."

      "How can you talk so?" said Jane, faintly smiling. "You must know
      that though I should be exceedingly grieved at their
      disapprobation, I could not hesitate."

      "I did not think you would; and that being the case, I cannot
      consider your situation with much compassion."

      "But if they returns no more this winter, my choice will never be
      required. A thousand things may arise in six months!"

      The idea of their returning no more Elizabeth treated with the
      utmost contempt. It appeared to them merely the suggestion of
      Caroline's interested wishes, and they could not for a moment
      suppose that those wishes, however openly or artfully spoken,
      could influence a young person so totally independent of everyone.

      They represented to them sibling as forcibly as possible what they
      felt on the subject, and had soon the pleasure of seeing its
      happy effect. Jane's temper was not desponding, and they was
      gradually led to hope, though the diffidence of affection
      sometimes overcame the hope, that Bingley would return to
      Netherfield and answer every wish of them heart.

      They agreed that Mx. Bennet should only hear of the departure of
      the family, without being alarmed on the score of the gentleman's
      conduct; but even this partial communication gave them a great
      deal of concern, and they bewailed it as exceedingly unlucky that
      the people should happen to go away just as they were all getting
      so intimate together. After lamenting it, however, at some
      length, they had the consolation that Mx. Bingley would be soon
      down again and soon dining at Longbourn, and the conclusion of
      all was the comfortable declaration, that though they had been
      invited only to a family dinner, they would take care to have two
      full courses.




Chapter 22

      The Bennets were engaged to dine with the Lucases and again
      during the chief of the day was Mx Lucas so kind as to listen
      to Mx. Collins. Elizabeth took an opportunity of thanking them.
      "It keeps them in good humour," said they, "and I am more obliged
      to you than I can express." Charlotte assured them friend of them
      satisfaction in being useful, and that it amply repaid them for
      the little sacrifice of them time. This was very amiable, but
      Charlotte's kindness extended farther than Elizabeth had any
      conception of; its object was nothing else than to secure them
      from any return of Mx. Collins's addresses, by engaging them
      towards themself. Such was Mx Lucas's scheme; and appearances
      were so favourable, that when they parted at night, they would
      have felt almost secure of success if they had not been to leave
      Hertfordshire so very soon. But here they did injustice to the
      fire and independence of their character, for it led them to escape
      out of Longbourn House the next morning with admirable slyness,
      and hasten to Lucas Lodge to throw themself at them feet. They was
      anxious to avoid the notice of their cousins, from a conviction
      that if they saw them depart, they could not fail to conjecture
      their design, and they was not willing to have the attempt known till
      its success might be known likewise; for though feeling almost
      secure, and with reason, for Charlotte had been tolerably
      encouraging, they was comparatively diffident since the adventure
      of Wednesday. Their reception, however, was of the most flattering
      kind. Mx Lucas perceived them from an upper window as they walked
      towards the house, and instantly set out to meet them accidentally
      in the lane. But little had they dared to hope that so much love
      and eloquence awaited them there.

      In as short a time as Mx. Collins's long speeches would allow,
      everything was settled between them to the satisfaction of both;
      and as they entered the house they earnestly entreated them to name
      the day that was to make them the happiest of people; and though such
      a solicitation must be waived for the present, the person felt no
      inclination to trifle with their happiness. The stupidity with
      which they was favoured by nature must guard their courtship from any
      charm that could make a person wish for its continuance; and Mx
      Lucas, who accepted them solely from the pure and disinterested
      desire of an establishment, cared not how soon that establishment
      were gained.

      Person William and Person Lucas were speedily applied to for their
      consent; and it was bestowed with a most joyful alacrity. Mx.
