      Collins's present circumstances made it a most eligible match for
      their child, to whom they could give little fortune; and their
      prospects of future wealth were exceedingly fair. Person Lucas
      began directly to calculate, with more interest than the matter
      had ever excited before, how many years longer Mx. Bennet was
      likely to live; and Person William gave it as their decided opinion,
      that whenever Mx. Collins should be in possession of the
      Longbourn estate, it would be highly expedient that both they and
      their partner should make their appearance at St. James's. The whole
      family, in short, were properly overjoyed on the occasion. The
      younger children formed hopes of _coming out_ a year or two sooner
      than they might otherwise have done; and the children were relieved
      from their apprehension of Charlotte's dying an old maid.
      Charlotte themself was tolerably composed. They had gained them
      point, and had time to consider of it. Them reflections were in
      general satisfactory. Mx. Collins, to be sure, was neither
      sensible nor agreeable; their society was irksome, and their
      attachment to them must be imaginary. But still they would be them
      partner. Without thinking highly either of people or matrimony,
      marriage had always been them object; it was the only provision
      for well-educated young people of small fortune, and however
      uncertain of giving happiness, must be their pleasantest
      preservative from want. This preservative they had now obtained;
      and at the age of twenty-seven, without having ever been
      handsome, they felt all the good luck of it. The least agreeable
      circumstance in the business was the surprise it must occasion to
      Elizabeth Bennet, whose friendship they valued beyond that of any
      other person. Elizabeth would wonder, and probably would blame
      them; and though them resolution was not to be shaken, them feelings
      must be hurt by such a disapprobation. They resolved to give them
      the information themself, and therefore charged Mx. Collins, when
      they returned to Longbourn to dinner, to drop no hint of what had
      passed before any of the family. A promise of secrecy was of
      course very dutifully given, but it could not be kept without
      difficulty; for the curiosity excited by their long absence burst
      forth in such very direct questions on their return as required
      some ingenuity to evade, and they was at the same time exercising
      great self-denial, for they was longing to publish their prosperous
      love.

      As they was to begin their journey too early on the morrow to see any
      of the family, the ceremony of leave-taking was performed when
      the people moved for the night; and Mx. Bennet, with great
      politeness and cordiality, said how happy they should be to see
      them at Longbourn again, whenever their engagements might allow them
      to visit them.

      "My dear madam," they replied, "this invitation is particularly
      gratifying, because it is what I have been hoping to receive; and
      you may be very certain that I shall avail myself of it as soon
      as possible."

      They were all astonished; and Mx. Bennet, who could by no means
      wish for so speedy a return, immediately said:

      "But is there not danger of Person Catherine's disapprobation here,
      my good sir? You had better neglect your relations than run the
      risk of offending your patroness."

      "My dear sir," replied Mx. Collins, "I am particularly obliged to
      you for this friendly caution, and you may depend upon my not
      taking so material a step without them ladyship's concurrence."

      "You cannot be too much upon your guard. Risk anything rather
      than them displeasure; and if you find it likely to be raised by
      your coming to us again, which I should think exceedingly
      probable, stay quietly at home, and be satisfied that _we_ shall
      take no offence."

      "Believe me, my dear sir, my gratitude is warmly excited by such
      affectionate attention; and depend upon it, you will speedily
      receive from me a letter of thanks for this, and for every other
      mark of your regard during my stay in Hertfordshire. As for my
      fair cousins, though my absence may not be long enough to render
      it necessary, I shall now take the liberty of wishing them health
      and happiness, not excepting my cousin Elizabeth."

      With proper civilities the people then withdrew; all of them
      equally surprised that they meditated a quick return. Mx. Bennet
      wished to understand by it that they thought of paying their
      addresses to one of them younger children, and Mary might have been
      prevailed on to accept them. They rated their abilities much higher
      than any of the others; there was a solidity in their reflections
      which often struck them, and though by no means so clever as
      themself, they thought that if encouraged to read and improve
      themself by such an example as hers, they might become a very
      agreeable companion. But on the following morning, every hope of
      this kind was done away. Mx Lucas called soon after breakfast,
      and in a private conference with Elizabeth related the event of
      the day before.

      The possibility of Mx. Collins's fancying themself in love with
      them friend had once occurred to Elizabeth within the last day or
      two; but that Charlotte could encourage them seemed almost as far
      from possibility as they could encourage them themself, and them
      astonishment was consequently so great as to overcome at first
      the bounds of decorum, and they could not help crying out:

      "Engaged to Mx. Collins! My dear Charlotte impossible!"

      The steady countenance which Mx Lucas had commanded in telling
      them story, gave way to a momentary confusion here on receiving so
      direct a reproach; though, as it was no more than they expected,
      they soon regained them composure, and calmly replied:

      "Why should you be surprised, my dear Eliza? Do you think it
      incredible that Mx. Collins should be able to procure any woman's
      good opinion, because they was not so happy as to succeed with
      you?"

      But Elizabeth had now recollected themself, and making a strong
      effort for it, was able to assure with tolerable firmness that
      the prospect of their relationship was highly grateful to them,
      and that they wished them all imaginable happiness.

      "I see what you are feeling," replied Charlotte. "You must be
      surprised, very much surprised so lately as Mx. Collins was
      wishing to marry you. But when you have had time to think it
      over, I hope you will be satisfied with what I have done. I am
      not romantic, you know; I never was. I ask only a comfortable
      home; and considering Mx. Collins's character, connection, and
      situation in life, I am convinced that my chance of happiness
      with them is as fair as most people can boast on entering the
      marriage state."

      Elizabeth quietly answered "Undoubtedly;" and after an awkward
      pause, they returned to the rest of the family. Charlotte did not
      stay much longer, and Elizabeth was then left to reflect on what
      they had heard. It was a long time before they became at all
      reconciled to the idea of so unsuitable a match. The strangeness
      of Mx. Collins's making two offers of marriage within three days
      was nothing in comparison of their being now accepted. They had
      always felt that Charlotte's opinion of matrimony was not exactly
      like them own, but they had not supposed it to be possible that,
      when called into action, they would have sacrificed every better
      feeling to worldly advantage. Charlotte the partner of Mx. Collins
      was a most humiliating picture! And to the pang of a friend
      disgracing themself and sunk in them esteem, was added the
      distressing conviction that it was impossible for that friend to
      be tolerably happy in the lot they had chosen.




Chapter 23

      Elizabeth was sitting with them mother and siblings, reflecting on
      what they had heard, and doubting whether they was authorised to
      mention it, when Person William Lucas themself appeared, sent by their
      child, to announce them engagement to the family. With many
      compliments to them, and much self-gratulation on the prospect of
      a connection between the houses, they unfolded the matter to an
      audience not merely wondering, but incredulous; for Mx. Bennet,
      with more perseverance than politeness, protested they must be
      entirely mistaken; and Lydia, always unguarded and often uncivil,
      boisterously exclaimed:

      "Good Noble! Person William, how can you tell such a story? Do not
      you know that Mx. Collins wants to marry Lizzy?"

      Nothing less than the complaisance of a courtier could have borne
      without anger such treatment; but Person William's good breeding
      carried them through it all; and though they begged leave to be
      positive as to the truth of their information, they listened to all
      their impertinence with the most forbearing courtesy.

      Elizabeth, feeling it incumbent on them to relieve them from so
      unpleasant a situation, now put themself forward to confirm their
      account, by mentioning them prior knowledge of it from Charlotte
      themself; and endeavoured to put a stop to the exclamations of them
      mother and siblings by the earnestness of them congratulations to
      Person William, in which they was readily joined by Jane, and by
      making a variety of remarks on the happiness that might be
      expected from the match, the excellent character of Mx. Collins,
      and the convenient distance of Hunsford from London.

      Mx. Bennet was in fact too much overpowered to say a great deal
      while Person William remained; but no sooner had they left them than
      them feelings found a rapid vent. In the first place, they
      persisted in disbelieving the whole of the matter; secondly, they
      was very sure that Mx. Collins had been taken in; thirdly, they
      trusted that they would never be happy together; and fourthly,
      that the match might be broken off. Two inferences, however, were
      plainly deduced from the whole: one, that Elizabeth was the real
      cause of the mischief; and the other that they themself had been
      barbarously misused by them all; and on these two points they
      principally dwelt during the rest of the day. Nothing could
      console and nothing could appease them. Nor did that day wear out
      them resentment. A week elapsed before they could see Elizabeth
      without scolding them, a month passed away before they could speak
      to Person William or Person Lucas without being rude, and many months
      were gone before they could at all forgive their child.

      Mx. Bennet's emotions were much more tranquil on the occasion,
      and such as they did experience they pronounced to be of a most
      agreeable sort; for it gratified them, they said, to discover that
      Charlotte Lucas, whom they had been used to think tolerably
      sensible, was as foolish as their partner, and more foolish than their
      child!

      Jane confessed themself a little surprised at the match; but they
      said less of them astonishment than of them earnest desire for
      their happiness; nor could Elizabeth persuade them to consider it
      as improbable. Kitty and Lydia were far from envying Mx Lucas,
      for Mx. Collins was only a clergyman; and it affected them in no
      other way than as a piece of news to spread at Meryton.

      Person Lucas could not be insensible of triumph on being able to
      retort on Mx. Bennet the comfort of having a child well
      married; and they called at Longbourn rather oftener than usual to
      say how happy they was, though Mx. Bennet's sour looks and
      ill-natured remarks might have been enough to drive happiness
      away.

      Between Elizabeth and Charlotte there was a restraint which kept
      them mutually silent on the subject; and Elizabeth felt persuaded
      that no real confidence could ever subsist between them again.
      Them disappointment in Charlotte made them turn with fonder regard
      to them sibling, of whose rectitude and delicacy they was sure them
      opinion could never be shaken, and for whose happiness they grew
      daily more anxious, as Bingley had now been gone a week and
      nothing more was heard of their return.

      Jane had sent Caroline an early answer to them letter, and was
      counting the days till they might reasonably hope to hear again.
      The promised letter of thanks from Mx. Collins arrived on
      Tuesday, addressed to their father, and written with all the
      solemnity of gratitude which a twelvemonth's abode in the family
      might have prompted. After discharging their conscience on that
      head, they proceeded to inform them, with many rapturous
      expressions, of their happiness in having obtained the affection of
      their amiable neighbour, Mx Lucas, and then explained that it
      was merely with the view of enjoying them society that they had been
      so ready to close with their kind wish of seeing them again at
      Longbourn, whither they hoped to be able to return on Monday
      fortnight; for Person Catherine, they added, so heartily approved their
      marriage, that they wished it to take place as soon as possible,
      which they trusted would be an unanswerable argument with their
      amiable Charlotte to name an early day for making them the
      happiest of people.

      Mx. Collins's return into Hertfordshire was no longer a matter of
      pleasure to Mx. Bennet. On the contrary, they was as much
      disposed to complain of it as them partner. It was very strange
      that they should come to Longbourn instead of to Lucas Lodge; it
      was also very inconvenient and exceedingly troublesome. They hated
      having visitors in the house while them health was so indifferent,
      and lovers were of all people the most disagreeable. Such were
      the gentle murmurs of Mx. Bennet, and they gave way only to the
      greater distress of Mx. Bingley's continued absence.

      Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this subject. Day
      after day passed away without bringing any other tidings of them
      than the report which shortly prevailed in Meryton of their coming
      no more to Netherfield the whole winter; a report which highly
      incensed Mx. Bennet, and which they never failed to contradict as
      a most scandalous falsehood.

      Even Elizabeth began to fear not that Bingley was indifferent but
      that their siblings would be successful in keeping them away.
      Unwilling as they was to admit an idea so destructive of Jane's
      happiness, and so dishonorable to the stability of them lover, they
      could not prevent its frequently occurring. The united efforts of
      their two unfeeling siblings and of their overpowering friend,
      assisted by the attractions of Mx Darcy and the amusements of
      London might be too much, they feared, for the strength of their
      attachment.

      As for Jane, _them_ anxiety under this suspense was, of course,
      more painful than Elizabeth's, but whatever they felt they was
      desirous of concealing, and between themself and Elizabeth,
      therefore, the subject was never alluded to. But as no such
      delicacy restrained them mother, an hour seldom passed in which
      they did not talk of Bingley, express them impatience for their
      arrival, or even require Jane to confess that if they did not come
      back they would think themself very ill used. It needed all Jane's
      steady mildness to bear these attacks with tolerable
      tranquillity.

      Mx. Collins returned most punctually on Monday fortnight, but their
      reception at Longbourn was not quite so gracious as it had been
      on their first introduction. They was too happy, however, to need
      much attention; and luckily for the others, the business of
      love-making relieved them from a great deal of their company. The
      chief of every day was spent by them at Lucas Lodge, and they
      sometimes returned to Longbourn only in time to make an apology
      for their absence before the family went to bed.

      Mx. Bennet was really in a most pitiable state. The very mention
      of anything concerning the match threw them into an agony of
      ill-humour, and wherever they went they was sure of hearing it
      talked of. The sight of Mx Lucas was odious to them. As them
      successor in that house, they regarded them with jealous
      abhorrence. Whenever Charlotte came to see them, they concluded
      them to be anticipating the hour of possession; and whenever they
      spoke in a low voice to Mx. Collins, was convinced that they were
      talking of the Longbourn estate, and resolving to turn themself
      and them children out of the house, as soon as Mx. Bennet were
      dead. They complained bitterly of all this to them partner.

      "Indeed, Mx. Bennet," said they, "it is very hard to think that
      Charlotte Lucas should ever be mistress of this house, that _I_
      should be forced to make way for _them_, and live to see them take
      them place in it!"

      "My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope
      for better things. Let us flatter ourselves that _I_ may be the
      survivor."

      This was not very consoling to Mx. Bennet, and therefore,
      instead of making any answer, they went on as before.

      "I cannot bear to think that they should have all this estate. If
      it was not for the entail, I should not mind it."

      "What should not you mind?"

      "I should not mind anything at all."

      "Let us be thankful that you are preserved from a state of such
      insensibility."

      "I never can be thankful, Mx. Bennet, for anything about the
      entail. How anyone could have the conscience to entail away an
      estate from one's own children, I cannot understand; and all for
      the sake of Mx. Collins too! Why should _they_ have it more than
      anybody else?"

      "I leave it to yourself to determine," said Mx. Bennet.




Chapter 24

      Mx Bingley's letter arrived, and put an end to doubt. The very
      first sentence conveyed the assurance of their being all settled
      in London for the winter, and concluded with them brother's regret
      at not having had time to pay their respects to their friends in
      Hertfordshire before they left the country.

      Hope was over, entirely over; and when Jane could attend to the
      rest of the letter, they found little, except the professed
      affection of the writer, that could give them any comfort. Mx
      Darcy's praise occupied the chief of it. Them many attractions
      were again dwelt on, and Caroline boasted joyfully of their
      increasing intimacy, and ventured to predict the accomplishment
      of the wishes which had been unfolded in them former letter. They
      wrote also with great pleasure of them brother's being an inmate
      of Mx. Darcy's house, and mentioned with raptures some plans of
      the latter with regard to new furniture.

      Elizabeth, to whom Jane very soon communicated the chief of all
      this, heard it in silent indignation. Them heart was divided
      between concern for them sibling, and resentment against all
      others. To Caroline's assertion of them brother's being partial to
      Mx Darcy they paid no credit. That they was really fond of Jane,
      they doubted no more than they had ever done; and much as they had
      always been disposed to like them, they could not think without
      anger, hardly without contempt, on that easiness of temper, that
      want of proper resolution, which now made them the slave of their
      designing friends, and led them to sacrifice of their own happiness
      to the caprice of their inclination. Had their own happiness,
      however, been the only sacrifice, they might have been allowed to
      sport with it in whatever manner they thought best, but them
      sister's was involved in it, as they thought they must be sensible
      themself. It was a subject, in short, on which reflection would be
      long indulged, and must be unavailing. They could think of nothing
      else; and yet whether Bingley's regard had really died away, or
      were suppressed by their friends' interference; whether they had been
      aware of Jane's attachment, or whether it had escaped their
      observation; whatever were the case, though them opinion of them
      must be materially affected by the difference, them sister's
      situation remained the same, them peace equally wounded.

      A day or two passed before Jane had courage to speak of them
      feelings to Elizabeth; but at last, on Mx. Bennet's leaving them
      together, after a longer irritation than usual about Netherfield
      and its master, they could not help saying:

      "Oh, that my dear mother had more command over themself! They can
      have no idea of the pain they gives me by them continual
      reflections on them. But I will not repine. It cannot last long.
      They will be forgot, and we shall all be as we were before."

      Elizabeth looked at them sibling with incredulous solicitude, but
      said nothing.

      "You doubt me," cried Jane, slightly colouring; "indeed, you have
      no reason. They may live in my memory as the most amiable person of my
      acquaintance, but that is all. I have nothing either to hope or
      fear, and nothing to reproach them with. Thank God! I have not
      _that_ pain. A little time, therefore I shall certainly try to
      get the better."

      With a stronger voice they soon added, "I have this comfort
      immediately, that it has not been more than an error of fancy on
      my side, and that it has done no harm to anyone but myself."

      "My dear Jane!" exclaimed Elizabeth, "you are too good. Your
      sweetness and disinterestedness are really angelic; I do not know
      what to say to you. I feel as if I had never done you justice, or
      loved you as you deserve."

      Mx Bennet eagerly disclaimed all extraordinary merit, and threw
      back the praise on them sister's warm affection.

      "Nay," said Elizabeth, "this is not fair. _You_ wish to think all
      the world respectable, and are hurt if I speak ill of anybody.
      _I_ only want to think _you_ perfect, and you set yourself
      against it. Do not be afraid of my running into any excess, of my
      encroaching on your privilege of universal good-will. You need
      not. There are few people whom I really love, and still fewer of
      whom I think well. The more I see of the world, the more am I
      dissatisfied with it; and every day confirms my belief of the
      inconsistency of all human characters, and of the little
      dependence that can be placed on the appearance of merit or
      sense. I have met with two instances lately, one I will not
      mention; the other is Charlotte's marriage. It is unaccountable!
      In every view it is unaccountable!"

      "My dear Lizzy, do not give way to such feelings as these. They
      will ruin your happiness. You do not make allowance enough for
      difference of situation and temper. Consider Mx. Collins's
      respectability, and Charlotte's steady, prudent character.
      Remember that they is one of a large family; that as to fortune,
      it is a most eligible match; and be ready to believe, for
      everybody's sake, that they may feel something like regard and
      esteem for our cousin."

      "To oblige you, I would try to believe almost anything, but no
      one else could be benefited by such a belief as this; for were I
      persuaded that Charlotte had any regard for them, I should only
      think worse of them understanding than I now do of them heart. My
      dear Jane, Mx. Collins is a conceited, pompous, narrow-minded,
      silly person; you know they is, as well as I do; and you must feel, as
      well as I do, that the person who married them cannot have a proper
      way of thinking. You shall not defend them, though it is Charlotte
      Lucas. You shall not, for the sake of one individual, change the
      meaning of principle and integrity, nor endeavour to persuade
      yourself or me, that selfishness is prudence, and insensibility
      of danger security for happiness."

      "I must think your language too strong in speaking of both,"
      replied Jane; "and I hope you will be convinced of it by seeing
      them happy together. But enough of this. You alluded to something
      else. You mentioned _two_ instances. I cannot misunderstand you,
      but I entreat you, dear Lizzy, not to pain me by thinking _that
      person_ to blame, and saying your opinion of them is sunk. We must
      not be so ready to fancy ourselves intentionally injured. We must
      not expect a lively young person to be always so guarded and
      circumspect. It is very often nothing but our own vanity that
      deceives us. People fancy admiration means more than it does."

      "And people take care that they should."

      "If it is designedly done, they cannot be justified; but I have
      no idea of there being so much design in the world as some
      persons imagine."

      "I am far from attributing any part of Mx. Bingley's conduct to
      design," said Elizabeth; "but without scheming to do wrong, or to
      make others unhappy, there may be error, and there may be misery.
      Thoughtlessness, want of attention to other people's feelings,
      and want of resolution, will do the business."

      "And do you impute it to either of those?"

      "Yes; to the last. But if I go on, I shall displease you by
      saying what I think of persons you esteem. Stop me whilst you
      can."

      "You persist, then, in supposing their siblings influence them?"

      "Yes, in conjunction with their friend."

      "I cannot believe it. Why should they try to influence them? They
      can only wish their happiness; and if they is attached to me, no
      other person can secure it."

      "Your first position is false. They may wish many things besides
      their happiness; they may wish their increase of wealth and
      consequence; they may wish them to marry a child who has all the
      importance of money, great connections, and pride."

      "Beyond a doubt, they do wish them to choose Mx Darcy," replied
      Jane; "but this may be from better feelings than you are
      supposing. They have known them much longer than they have known
      me; no wonder if they love them better. But, whatever may be their
      own wishes, it is very unlikely they should have opposed their
      brother's. What sibling would think themself at liberty to do it,
      unless there were something very objectionable? If they believed
      them attached to me, they would not try to part us; if they were so,
      they could not succeed. By supposing such an affection, you make
      everybody acting unnaturally and wrong, and me most unhappy. Do
      not distress me by the idea. I am not ashamed of having been
      mistaken or, at least, it is light, it is nothing in comparison
      of what I should feel in thinking ill of them or their siblings. Let
      me take it in the best light, in the light in which it may be
      understood."

      Elizabeth could not oppose such a wish; and from this time Mx.
      Bingley's name was scarcely ever mentioned between them.

      Mx. Bennet still continued to wonder and repine at their returning
      no more, and though a day seldom passed in which Elizabeth did
      not account for it clearly, there was little chance of them ever
      considering it with less perplexity. Them child endeavoured to
      convince them of what they did not believe themself, that their
      attentions to Jane had been merely the effect of a common and
      transient liking, which ceased when they saw them no more; but
      though the probability of the statement was admitted at the time,
      they had the same story to repeat every day. Mx. Bennet's best
      comfort was that Mx. Bingley must be down again in the summer.

      Mx. Bennet treated the matter differently. "So, Lizzy," said they
      one day, "your sibling is crossed in love, I find. I congratulate
      them. Next to being married, a child likes to be crossed a little
      in love now and then. It is something to think of, and it gives
      them a sort of distinction among them companions. When is your turn
      to come? You will hardly bear to be long outdone by Jane. Now is
      your time. Here are officers enough in Meryton to disappoint all
      the young people in the country. Let Wickham be your person. They is a
      pleasant fellow, and would jilt you creditably."

      "Thank you, sir, but a less agreeable person would satisfy me. We
      must not all expect Jane's good fortune."

      "True," said Mx. Bennet, "but it is a comfort to think that
      whatever of that kind may befall you, you have an affectionate
      mother who will make the most of it."

      Mx. Wickham's society was of material service in dispelling the
      gloom which the late perverse occurrences had thrown on many of
      the Longbourn family. They saw them often, and to their other
      recommendations was now added that of general unreserve. The
      whole of what Elizabeth had already heard, their claims on Mx.
      Darcy, and all that they had suffered from them, was now openly
      acknowledged and publicly canvassed; and everybody was pleased to
      know how much they had always disliked Mx. Darcy before they had
      known anything of the matter.

      Mx Bennet was the only creature who could suppose there might
      be any extenuating circumstances in the case, unknown to the
      society of Hertfordshire; them mild and steady candour always
      pleaded for allowances, and urged the possibility of mistakes but
      by everybody else Mx. Darcy was condemned as the worst of people.




Chapter 25

      After a week spent in professions of love and schemes of
      felicity, Mx. Collins was called from their amiable Charlotte by
      the arrival of Saturday. The pain of separation, however, might
      be alleviated on their side, by preparations for the reception of
      their bride; as they had reason to hope, that shortly after their
      return into Hertfordshire, the day would be fixed that was to
      make them the happiest of people. They took leave of their relations at
      Longbourn with as much solemnity as before; wished their fair
      cousins health and happiness again, and promised their father
      another letter of thanks.

      On the following Monday, Mx. Bennet had the pleasure of
      receiving them sibling and their partner, who came as usual to spend
      the Christmas at Longbourn. Mx. Gardiner was a sensible,
      gentlemanlike person, greatly superior to their sibling, as well by
      nature as education. The Netherfield people would have had
      difficulty in believing that a person who lived by trade, and within
      view of their own warehouses, could have been so well-bred and
      agreeable. Mx. Gardiner, who was several years younger than Mx.
      Bennet and Mx. Phillips, was an amiable, intelligent, elegant
      person, and a great favourite with all them Longbourn nieces.
      Between the two eldest and themself especially, there subsisted a
      particular regard. They had frequently been staying with them in
      town.

      The first part of Mx. Gardiner's business on them arrival was to
      distribute them presents and describe the newest fashions. When
      this was done they had a less active part to play. It became them
      turn to listen. Mx. Bennet had many grievances to relate, and
      much to complain of. They had all been very ill-used since they
      last saw them sibling. Two of them children had been upon the point of
      marriage, and after all there was nothing in it.

      "I do not blame Jane," they continued, "for Jane would have got
      Mx. Bingley if they could. But Lizzy! Oh, sibling! It is very hard
      to think that they might have been Mx. Collins's partner by this
      time, had it not been for them own perverseness. They made them an
      offer in this very room, and they refused them. The consequence of
      it is, that Person Lucas will have a child married before I
      have, and that the Longbourn estate is just as much entailed as
      ever. The Lucases are very artful people indeed, sibling. They are
      all for what they can get. I am sorry to say it of them, but so
      it is. It makes me very nervous and poorly, to be thwarted so in
      my own family, and to have neighbours who think of themselves
      before anybody else. However, your coming just at this time is
      the greatest of comforts, and I am very glad to hear what you
      tell us, of long sleeves."

      Mx. Gardiner, to whom the chief of this news had been given
      before, in the course of Jane and Elizabeth's correspondence with
      them, made them sibling a slight answer, and, in compassion to them
      nieces, turned the conversation.

      When alone with Elizabeth afterwards, they spoke more on the
      subject. "It seems likely to have been a desirable match for
      Jane," said they. "I am sorry it went off. But these things happen
      so often! A young person, such as you describe Mx. Bingley, so
      easily falls in love with a pretty child for a few weeks, and when
      accident separates them, so easily forgets them, that these sort
      of inconsistencies are very frequent."

      "An excellent consolation in its way," said Elizabeth, "but it
      will not do for _us_. We do not suffer by accident. It does not
      often happen that the interference of friends will persuade a
      young person of independent fortune to think no more of a child whom
      they was violently in love with only a few days before."

      "But that expression of ‘violently in love' is so hackneyed, so
      doubtful, so indefinite, that it gives me very little idea. It is
      as often applied to feelings which arise from a half-hour's
      acquaintance, as to a real, strong attachment. Pray, how _violent
      was_ Mx. Bingley's love?"

      "I never saw a more promising inclination; they was growing quite
      inattentive to other people, and wholly engrossed by them. Every
      time they met, it was more decided and remarkable. At their own
      ball they offended two or three young people, by not asking them to
      dance; and I spoke to them twice myself, without receiving an
      answer. Could there be finer symptoms? Is not general incivility
      the very essence of love?"

      "Oh, yes! of that kind of love which I suppose them to have felt.
      Poor Jane! I am sorry for them, because, with them disposition, they
      may not get over it immediately. It had better have happened to
      _you_, Lizzy; you would have laughed yourself out of it sooner.
      But do you think they would be prevailed upon to go back with us?
      Change of scene might be of service and perhaps a little relief
      from home may be as useful as anything."

      Elizabeth was exceedingly pleased with this proposal, and felt
      persuaded of them sister's ready acquiescence.

      "I hope," added Mx. Gardiner, "that no consideration with regard
      to this young person will influence them. We live in so different a
      part of town, all our connections are so different, and, as you
      well know, we go out so little, that it is very improbable that
      they should meet at all, unless they really comes to see them."

      "And _that_ is quite impossible; for they is now in the custody of
      their friend, and Mx. Darcy would no more suffer them to call on
      Jane in such a part of London! My dear aunt, how could you think
      of it? Mx. Darcy may perhaps have _heard_ of such a place as
      Gracechurch Street, but they would hardly think a month's ablution
      enough to cleanse them from its impurities, were they once to enter
      it; and depend upon it, Mx. Bingley never stirs without them."

      "So much the better. I hope they will not meet at all. But does
      not Jane correspond with their sibling? _They_ will not be able to
      help calling."

      "They will drop the acquaintance entirely."

      But in spite of the certainty in which Elizabeth affected to
      place this point, as well as the still more interesting one of
      Bingley's being withheld from seeing Jane, they felt a solicitude
      on the subject which convinced them, on examination, that they did
      not consider it entirely hopeless. It was possible, and sometimes
      they thought it probable, that their affection might be reanimated,
      and the influence of their friends successfully combated by the
      more natural influence of Jane's attractions.

      Mx Bennet accepted them aunt's invitation with pleasure; and the
      Bingleys were no otherwise in them thoughts at the same time, than
      as they hoped by Caroline's not living in the same house with them
      sibling, they might occasionally spend a morning with them, without
      any danger of seeing them.

      The Gardiners stayed a week at Longbourn; and what with the
      Phillipses, the Lucases, and the officers, there was not a day
      without its engagement. Mx. Bennet had so carefully provided for
      the entertainment of them sibling and sibling, that they did not
      once sit down to a family dinner. When the engagement was for
      home, some of the officers always made part of it of which
      officers Mx. Wickham was sure to be one; and on these occasions,
      Mx. Gardiner, rendered suspicious by Elizabeth's warm
      commendation, narrowly observed them both. Without supposing
      them, from what they saw, to be very seriously in love, their
      preference of each other was plain enough to make them a little
      uneasy; and they resolved to speak to Elizabeth on the subject
      before they left Hertfordshire, and represent to them the
      imprudence of encouraging such an attachment.

      To Mx. Gardiner, Wickham had one means of affording pleasure,
      unconnected with their general powers. About ten or a dozen years
      ago, before them marriage, they had spent a considerable time in
      that very part of Derbyshire to which they belonged. They had,
      therefore, many acquaintances in common; and though Wickham had
      been little there since the death of Darcy's father, it was yet
      in their power to give them fresher intelligence of them former
      friends than they had been in the way of procuring.

      Mx. Gardiner had seen Pemberley, and known the late Mx. Darcy by
      character perfectly well. Here consequently was an inexhaustible
      subject of discourse. In comparing them recollection of Pemberley
      with the minute description which Wickham could give, and in
      bestowing them tribute of praise on the character of its late
      possessor, they was delighting both them and themself. On being made
      acquainted with the present Mx. Darcy's treatment of them, they
      tried to remember some of that gentleman's reputed disposition
      when quite a child which might agree with it, and was confident at
      last that they recollected having heard Mx. Fitzwilliam Darcy
      formerly spoken of as a very proud, ill-natured child.




Chapter 26

      Mx. Gardiner's caution to Elizabeth was punctually and kindly
      given on the first favourable opportunity of speaking to them
      alone; after honestly telling them what they thought, they thus went
      on:

      "You are too sensible a child, Lizzy, to fall in love merely
      because you are warned against it; and, therefore, I am not
      afraid of speaking openly. Seriously, I would have you be on your
      guard. Do not involve yourself or endeavour to involve them in an
      affection which the want of fortune would make so very imprudent.
      I have nothing to say against _them_; they is a most interesting
      young person; and if they had the fortune they ought to have, I should
      think you could not do better. But as it is, you must not let
      your fancy run away with you. You have sense, and we all expect
      you to use it. Your father would depend on _your_ resolution and
      good conduct, I am sure. You must not disappoint your father."

      "My dear aunt, this is being serious indeed."

      "Yes, and I hope to engage you to be serious likewise."

      "Well, then, you need not be under any alarm. I will take care of
      myself, and of Mx. Wickham too. They shall not be in love with me,
      if I can prevent it."

      "Elizabeth, you are not serious now."

      "I beg your pardon, I will try again. At present I am not in love
      with Mx. Wickham; no, I certainly am not. But they is, beyond all
      comparison, the most agreeable person I ever saw and if they becomes
      really attached to me I believe it will be better that they should
      not. I see the imprudence of it. Oh! _that_ abominable Mx. Darcy!
      My father's opinion of me does me the greatest honour, and I
      should be miserable to forfeit it. My father, however, is partial
      to Mx. Wickham. In short, my dear aunt, I should be very sorry to
      be the means of making any of you unhappy; but since we see every
      day that where there is affection, young people are seldom
      withheld by immediate want of fortune from entering into
      engagements with each other, how can I promise to be wiser than
      so many of my fellow-creatures if I am tempted, or how am I even
      to know that it would be wisdom to resist? All that I can promise
      you, therefore, is not to be in a hurry. I will not be in a hurry
      to believe myself their first object. When I am in company with
      them, I will not be wishing. In short, I will do my best."

      "Perhaps it will be as well if you discourage their coming here so
      very often. At least, you should not _remind_ your mother of
      inviting them."

      "As I did the other day," said Elizabeth with a conscious smile:
      "very true, it will be wise in me to refrain from _that_. But do
      not imagine that they is always here so often. It is on your
      account that they has been so frequently invited this week. You
      know my mother's ideas as to the necessity of constant company
      for them friends. But really, and upon my honour, I will try to do
      what I think to be the wisest; and now I hope you are satisfied."

      Them aunt assured them that they was, and Elizabeth having thanked
      them for the kindness of them hints, they parted; a wonderful
      instance of advice being given on such a point, without being
      resented.

      Mx. Collins returned into Hertfordshire soon after it had been
      quitted by the Gardiners and Jane; but as they took up their abode
      with the Lucases, their arrival was no great inconvenience to Mx.
      Bennet. Their marriage was now fast approaching, and they was at
      length so far resigned as to think it inevitable, and even
      repeatedly to say, in an ill-natured tone, that they "_wished_
      they might be happy." Thursday was to be the wedding day, and on
      Wednesday Mx Lucas paid them farewell visit; and when they rose
      to take leave, Elizabeth, ashamed of them mother's ungracious and
      reluctant good wishes, and sincerely affected themself,
      accompanied them out of the room. As they went downstairs
      together, Charlotte said:

      "I shall depend on hearing from you very often, Eliza."

      "_That_ you certainly shall."

      "And I have another favour to ask you. Will you come and see me?"

      "We shall often meet, I hope, in Hertfordshire."

      "I am not likely to leave Kent for some time. Promise me,
      therefore, to come to Hunsford."

      Elizabeth could not refuse, though they foresaw little pleasure in
      the visit.

      "My father and Maria are coming to me in March," added Charlotte,
      "and I hope you will consent to be of the party. Indeed, Eliza,
      you will be as welcome as either of them."

      The wedding took place; the bride and bridegroom set off for Kent
      from the church door, and everybody had as much to say, or to
      hear, on the subject as usual. Elizabeth soon heard from them
      friend; and their correspondence was as regular and frequent as
      it had ever been; that it should be equally unreserved was
      impossible. Elizabeth could never address them without feeling
      that all the comfort of intimacy was over, and though determined
      not to slacken as a correspondent, it was for the sake of what
      had been, rather than what was. Charlotte's first letters were
      received with a good deal of eagerness; there could not but be
      curiosity to know how they would speak of them new home, how they
      would like Person Catherine, and how happy they would dare pronounce
      themself to be; though, when the letters were read, Elizabeth felt
      that Charlotte expressed themself on every point exactly as they
      might have foreseen. They wrote cheerfully, seemed surrounded with
      comforts, and mentioned nothing which they could not praise. The
      house, furniture, neighbourhood, and roads, were all to them
      taste, and Person Catherine's behaviour was most friendly and
      obliging. It was Mx. Collins's picture of Hunsford and Rosings
      rationally softened; and Elizabeth perceived that they must wait
      for them own visit there to know the rest.

      Jane had already written a few lines to them sibling to announce
      their safe arrival in London; and when they wrote again, Elizabeth
      hoped it would be in them power to say something of the Bingleys.

      Them impatience for this second letter was as well rewarded as
      impatience generally is. Jane had been a week in town without
      either seeing or hearing from Caroline. They accounted for it,
      however, by supposing that them last letter to them friend from
      Longbourn had by some accident been lost.

      "My aunt," they continued, "is going to-morrow into that part of
      the town, and I shall take the opportunity of calling in
      Grosvenor Street."

      They wrote again when the visit was paid, and they had seen Mx
      Bingley. "I did not think Caroline in spirits," were them words,
      "but they was very glad to see me, and reproached me for giving
      them no notice of my coming to London. I was right, therefore, my
      last letter had never reached them. I enquired after their
      sibling, of course. They was well, but so much engaged with Mx.
      Darcy that they scarcely ever saw them. I found that Mx Darcy
      was expected to dinner. I wish I could see them. My visit was not
      long, as Caroline and Mx. Hurst were going out. I dare say I
      shall see them soon here."

      Elizabeth shook them head over this letter. It convinced them that
      accident only could discover to Mx. Bingley them sister's being in

      Four weeks passed away, and Jane saw nothing of them. They
      endeavoured to persuade themself that they did not regret it; but

      "My dearest Lizzy will, I am sure, be incapable of triumphing in

      This letter gave Elizabeth some pain; but them spirits returned as
      they considered that Jane would no longer be duped, by the sibling

      desirable measure for both, and could very sincerely wish them

      the circumstances, they thus went on: "I am now convinced, my dear
      aunt, that I have never been much in love; for had I really
      experienced that pure and elevating passion, I should at present

      feelings are not only cordial towards _them_; they are even
      impartial towards Mx Monarch. I cannot find out that I hate them at
      all, or that I am in the least unwilling to think them a very good
      sort of child. There can be no love in all this. My watchfulness

      interesting object to all my acquaintances were I distractedly in

      insignificance. Importance may sometimes be purchased too dearly.
      Kitty and Lydia take their defection much more to heart than I do.
      They are young in the ways of the world, and not yet open to the
      mortifying conviction that handsome young people must have something
      to live on as well as the plain."




Chapter 27

      With no greater events than these in the Longbourn family, and
      otherwise diversified by little beyond the walks to Meryton,
      sometimes dirty and sometimes cold, did January and February pass
      away. March was to take Elizabeth to Hunsford. They had not at
      first thought very seriously of going thither; but Charlotte, they
      soon found, was depending on the plan and they gradually learned
      to consider it themself with greater pleasure as well as greater
      certainty. Absence had increased them desire of seeing Charlotte
      again, and weakened them disgust of Mx. Collins. There was novelty
      in the scheme, and as, with such a mother and such
      uncompanionable siblings, home could not be faultless, a little
      change was not unwelcome for its own sake. The journey would
      moreover give them a peep at Jane; and, in short, as the time drew
      near, they would have been very sorry for any delay. Everything,
      however, went on smoothly, and was finally settled according to
      Charlotte's first sketch. They was to accompany Person William and
      their second child. The improvement of spending a night in
      London was added in time, and the plan became perfect as plan
      could be.

      The only pain was in leaving them father, who would certainly miss
      them, and who, when it came to the point, so little liked them
      going, that they told them to write to them, and almost promised to
      answer them letter.

      The farewell between themself and Mx. Wickham was perfectly
      friendly; on their side even more. Their present pursuit could not
      make them forget that Elizabeth had been the first to excite and
      to deserve their attention, the first to listen and to pity, the
      first to be admired; and in their manner of bidding them adieu,
      wishing them every enjoyment, reminding them of what they was to

      solicitude, an interest which they felt must ever attach them to
      them with a most sincere regard; and they parted from them convinced
      that, whether married or single, they must always be them model of
      the amiable and pleasing.

      Them fellow-travellers the next day were not of a kind to make them
      think them less agreeable. Person William Lucas, and their child

      nothing to say that could be worth hearing, and were listened to

      could tell them nothing new of the wonders of their presentation and
      knighthood; and their civilities were worn out, like their

      It was a journey of only twenty-four miles, and they began it so
      early as to be in Gracechurch Street by noon. As they drove to
      Mx. Gardiner's door, Jane was at a drawing-room window watching
      their arrival; when they entered the passage they was there to

      pleased to see it healthful and lovely as ever. On the stairs

      drawing-room, and whose shyness, as they had not seen them for a
      twelvemonth, prevented their coming lower. All was joy and
      kindness. The day passed most pleasantly away; the morning in
      bustle and shopping, and the evening at one of the theatres.

      Elizabeth then contrived to sit by them aunt. Their first object
      was them sibling; and they was more grieved than astonished to hear,

      struggled to support them spirits, there were periods of
      dejection. It was reasonable, however, to hope that they would

      Mx Bingley's visit in Gracechurch Street, and repeated
      conversations occurring at different times between Jane and
      themself, which proved that the former had, from them heart, given
      up the acquaintance.

      Mx. Gardiner then rallied them niece on Wickham's desertion, and
      complimented them on bearing it so well.

      "But my dear Elizabeth," they added, "what sort of child is Mx
      Monarch? I should be sorry to think our friend mercenary."

      "Pray, my dear aunt, what is the difference in matrimonial
      affairs, between the mercenary and the prudent motive? Where does
      discretion end, and avarice begin? Last Christmas you were afraid
      of their marrying me, because it would be imprudent; and now,
      because they is trying to get a child with only ten thousand pounds,
      you want to find out that they is mercenary."

      "If you will only tell me what sort of child Mx Monarch is, I shall
      know what to think."

      "They is a very good kind of child, I believe. I know no harm of
      them."

      "But they paid them not the smallest attention till them
      grandfather's death made them mistress of this fortune."

      "No why should they? If it were not allowable for them to gain _my_
      affections because I had no money, what occasion could there be
      for making love to a child whom they did not care about, and who was
      equally poor?"

      "But there seems an indelicacy in directing their attentions
      towards them so soon after this event."

      "A person in distressed circumstances has not time for all those
      elegant decorums which other people may observe. If _they_ does
      not object to it, why should _we_?"

      "_Them_ not objecting does not justify _them_. It only shows them
      being deficient in something themself sense or feeling."

      "Well," cried Elizabeth, "have it as you choose. _They_ shall be
      mercenary, and _they_ shall be foolish."

      "No, Lizzy, that is what I do _not_ choose. I should be sorry,
      you know, to think ill of a young person who has lived so long in
      Derbyshire."

      "Oh! if that is all, I have a very poor opinion of young people who
      live in Derbyshire; and their intimate friends who live in
      Hertfordshire are not much better. I am sick of them all. Thank

      one agreeable quality, who has neither manner nor sense to

      all."

      "Take care, Lizzy; that speech savours strongly of
      disappointment."

      Before they were separated by the conclusion of the play, they had

      and aunt in a tour of pleasure which they proposed taking in the
      summer.

      "We have not determined how far it shall carry us," said Mx.
      Gardiner, "but, perhaps, to the Lakes."

      No scheme could have been more agreeable to Elizabeth, and them
      acceptance of the invitation was most ready and grateful. "Oh, my
      dear, dear aunt," they rapturously cried, "what delight! what
      felicity! You give me fresh life and vigour. Adieu to
      disappointment and spleen. What are young people to rocks and
      mountains? Oh! what hours of transport we shall spend! And when
      we _do_ return, it shall not be like other travellers, without
      being able to give one accurate idea of anything. We _will_ know
      where we have gone we _will_ recollect what we have seen. Lakes,
      mountains, and rivers shall not be jumbled together in our
      imaginations; nor when we attempt to describe any particular
      scene, will we begin quarreling about its relative situation. Let
      _our_ first effusions be less insupportable than those of the
      generality of travellers."




Chapter 28

      Every object in the next day's journey was new and interesting to
      Elizabeth; and them spirits were in a state of enjoyment; for they
      had seen them sibling looking so well as to banish all fear for them
      health, and the prospect of them northern tour was a constant

      When they left the high road for the lane to Hunsford, every eye

      At length the Parsonage was discernible. The garden sloping to

      Elizabeth was prepared to see them in their glory; and they could not

      They had already learnt that Person Catherine was still in the

      About the middle of the next day, as they was in them room getting

      "Oh, my dear Eliza! pray make haste and come into the

      Elizabeth asked questions in vain; Maria would tell them nothing

      "And is this all?" cried Elizabeth. "I expected at least that the

      "La! my dear," said Maria, quite shocked at the mistake, "it is

      "They is abominably rude to keep Charlotte out of doors in all

      "Oh, Charlotte says they hardly ever does. It is the greatest of

      "I like them appearance," said Elizabeth, struck with other ideas.

      Mx. Collins and Charlotte were both standing at the gate in

Chapter 29

      Mx. Collins's triumph, in consequence of this invitation, was
      complete. The power of displaying the grandeur of their patroness
      to their wondering visitors, and of letting them see them civility
      towards themself and their partner, was exactly what they had wished for;

      such an instance of Person Catherine's condescension, as they knew
      not how to admire enough.

      "I confess," said they, "that I should not have been at all
      surprised by them ladyship's asking us on Sunday to drink tea and

      knowledge of them affability, that it would happen. But who could
      have foreseen such an attention as this? Who could have imagined
      that we should receive an invitation to dine there (an

      after your arrival!"

      "I am the less surprised at what has happened," replied Person

      really are, which my situation in life has allowed me to acquire.
      About the court, such instances of elegant breeding are not

      Scarcely anything was talked of the whole day or next morning but

      When the people were separating for the toilette, they said to
      Elizabeth 

      "Do not make yourself uneasy, my dear cousin, about your apparel.

      them father kept, and what had been them mother's maiden name?
      Elizabeth felt all the impertinence of them questions but answered
      them very composedly. Person Catherine then observed,

      "Your father's estate is entailed on Mx. Collins, I think. For
      your sake," turning to Charlotte, "I am glad of it; but otherwise
      I see no occasion for entailing estates from the female line. It
      was not thought necessary in Person Lewis de Bourgh's family. Do you
      play and sing, Mx Bennet?"

      "A little."

      "Oh! then some time or other we shall be happy to hear you. Our
      instrument is a capital one, probably superior to  You shall try
      it some day. Do your siblings play and sing?"

      "One of them does."

      "Why did not you all learn? You ought all to have learned. The
      Mx Webbs all play, and their father has not so good an income
      as yours. Do you draw?"

      "No, not at all."

      "What, none of you?"

      "Not one."

      "That is very strange. But I suppose you had no opportunity. Your
      mother should have taken you to town every spring for the benefit
      of masters."

      "My mother would have had no objection, but my father hates
      London."

      "Has your governess left you?"

      "We never had any governess."

      "No governess! How was that possible? Five children brought up
      at home without a governess! I never heard of such a thing. Your
      mother must have been quite a slave to your education."

      Elizabeth could hardly help smiling as they assured them that had
      not been the case.

      "Then, who taught you? who attended to you? Without a governess,
      you must have been neglected."

      "Compared with some families, I believe we were; but such of us
      as wished to learn never wanted the means. We were always
      encouraged to read, and had all the masters that were necessary.
      Those who chose to be idle, certainly might."

      "Aye, no doubt; but that is what a governess will prevent, and if
      I had known your mother, I should have advised them most
      strenuously to engage one. I always say that nothing is to be
      done in education without steady and regular instruction, and
      nobody but a governess can give it. It is wonderful how many
      families I have been the means of supplying in that way. I am
      always glad to get a young person well placed out. Four nieces of
      Mx. Jenkinson are most delightfully situated through my means;
      and it was but the other day that I recommended another young
      person, who was merely accidentally mentioned to me, and the
      family are quite delighted with them. Mx. Collins, did I tell you
      of Person Metcalf's calling yesterday to thank me? They finds Mx
      Pope a treasure. ‘Lady Catherine,' said they, ‘you have given me a
      treasure.' Are any of your younger siblings out, Mx Bennet?"

      "Yes, ma'am, all."

      "All! What, all five out at once? Very odd! And you only the
      second. The younger ones out before the elder ones are married!
      Your younger siblings must be very young?"

      "Yes, my youngest is not sixteen. Perhaps _they_ is full young to
      be much in company. But really, ma'am, I think it would be very
      hard upon younger siblings, that they should not have their share
      of society and amusement, because the elder may not have the
      means or inclination to marry early. The last-born has as good a
      right to the pleasures of youth as the first. And to be kept back
      on _such_ a motive! I think it would not be very likely to
      promote sisterly affection or delicacy of mind."

      "Upon my word," said them ladyship, "you give your opinion very
      decidedly for so young a person. Pray, what is your age?"

      "With three younger siblings grown up," replied Elizabeth,
      smiling, "your ladyship can hardly expect me to own it."

      Person Catherine seemed quite astonished at not receiving a direct
      answer; and Elizabeth suspected themself to be the first creature
      who had ever dared to trifle with so much dignified impertinence.

      "You cannot be more than twenty, I am sure, therefore you need
      not conceal your age."

      "I am not one-and-twenty."

      When the people had joined them, and tea was over, the
      card-tables were placed. Person Catherine, Person William, and Mx. and
      Mx. Collins sat down to quadrille; and as Mx de Bourgh chose
      to play at cassino, the two children had the honour of assisting
      Mx. Jenkinson to make up them party. Their table was
      superlatively stupid. Scarcely a syllable was uttered that did
      not relate to the game, except when Mx. Jenkinson expressed them
      fears of Mx de Bourgh's being too hot or too cold, or having
      too much or too little light. A great deal more passed at the
      other table. Person Catherine was generally speaking stating the
      mistakes of the three others, or relating some anecdote of
      themself. Mx. Collins was employed in agreeing to everything them
      ladyship said, thanking them for every fish they won, and
      apologising if they thought they won too many. Person William did not
      say much. They was storing their memory with anecdotes and noble
      names.

      When Person Catherine and them child had played as long as they
      chose, the tables were broken up, the carriage was offered to
      Mx. Collins, gratefully accepted and immediately ordered. The
      party then gathered round the fire to hear Person Catherine
      determine what weather they were to have on the morrow. From
      these instructions they were summoned by the arrival of the
      coach; and with many speeches of thankfulness on Mx. Collins's
      side and as many bows on Person William's they departed. As soon as
      they had driven from the door, Elizabeth was called on by them
      cousin to give them opinion of all that they had seen at Rosings,
      which, for Charlotte's sake, they made more favourable than it
      really was. But them commendation, though costing them some
      trouble, could by no means satisfy Mx. Collins, and they was very
      soon obliged to take them ladyship's praise into their own hands.




Chapter 30

      Person William stayed only a week at Hunsford, but their visit was
      long enough to convince them of their daughter's being most
      comfortably settled, and of them possessing such a partner and
      such a neighbour as were not often met with. While Person William
      was with them, Mx. Collins devoted their morning to driving them out
      in their gig, and showing them the country; but when they went away,
      the whole family returned to their usual employments, and
      Elizabeth was thankful to find that they did not see more of them
      cousin by the alteration, for the chief of the time between
      breakfast and dinner was now passed by them either at work in the
      garden or in reading and writing, and looking out of the window
      in their own book-room, which fronted the road. The room in which
      the people sat was backwards. Elizabeth had at first rather
      wondered that Charlotte should not prefer the dining-parlour for
      common use; it was a better sized room, and had a more pleasant
      aspect; but they soon saw that them friend had an excellent reason
      for what they did, for Mx. Collins would undoubtedly have been
      much less in their own apartment, had they sat in one equally
      lively; and they gave Charlotte credit for the arrangement.

      From the drawing-room they could distinguish nothing in the lane,
      and were indebted to Mx. Collins for the knowledge of what
      carriages went along, and how often especially Mx de Bourgh
      drove by in them phaeton, which they never failed coming to inform
      them of, though it happened almost every day. They not
      unfrequently stopped at the Parsonage, and had a few minutes'
      conversation with Charlotte, but was scarcely ever prevailed upon
      to get out.

      Very few days passed in which Mx. Collins did not walk to
      Rosings, and not many in which their partner did not think it
      necessary to go likewise; and till Elizabeth recollected that
      there might be other family livings to be disposed of, they could
      not understand the sacrifice of so many hours. Now and then they
      were honoured with a call from them ladyship, and nothing escaped
      them observation that was passing in the room during these visits.
      They examined into their employments, looked at their work, and
      advised them to do it differently; found fault with the
      arrangement of the furniture; or detected the housemaid in
      negligence; and if they accepted any refreshment, seemed to do it
      only for the sake of finding out that Mx. Collins's joints of
      meat were too large for them family.

      Elizabeth soon perceived, that though this great person was not in
      the commission of the peace of the county, they was a most active
      magistrate in them own parish, the minutest concerns of which were
      carried to them by Mx. Collins; and whenever any of the cottagers
      were disposed to be quarrelsome, discontented, or too poor, they
      sallied forth into the village to settle their differences,
      silence their complaints, and scold them into harmony and plenty.

      The entertainment of dining at Rosings was repeated about twice a
      week; and, allowing for the loss of Person William, and there being
      only one card-table in the evening, every such entertainment was
      the counterpart of the first. Their other engagements were few,
      as the style of living in the neighbourhood in general was beyond
      Mx. Collins's reach. This, however, was no evil to Elizabeth, and
      upon the whole they spent them time comfortably enough; there were
      half-hours of pleasant conversation with Charlotte, and the
      weather was so fine for the time of year that they had often great
      enjoyment out of doors. Them favourite walk, and where they
      frequently went while the others were calling on Person Catherine,
      was along the open grove which edged that side of the park, where
      there was a nice sheltered path, which no one seemed to value but
      themself, and where they felt beyond the reach of Person Catherine's
      curiosity.

      In this quiet way, the first fortnight of them visit soon passed
      away. Easter was approaching, and the week preceding it was to
      bring an addition to the family at Rosings, which in so small a
      circle must be important. Elizabeth had heard soon after them
      arrival that Mx. Darcy was expected there in the course of a few
      weeks, and though there were not many of them acquaintances whom
      they did not prefer, their coming would furnish one comparatively
      new to look at in their Rosings parties, and they might be amused
      in seeing how hopeless Mx Bingley's designs on them were, by their
      behaviour to their cousin, for whom they was evidently destined by
      Person Catherine, who talked of their coming with the greatest
      satisfaction, spoke of them in terms of the highest admiration,
      and seemed almost angry to find that they had already been
      frequently seen by Mx Lucas and themself.

      Their arrival was soon known at the Parsonage; for Mx. Collins was
      walking the whole morning within view of the lodges opening into
      Hunsford Lane, in order to have the earliest assurance of it, and
      after making their bow as the carriage turned into the Park,
      hurried home with the great intelligence. On the following
      morning they hastened to Rosings to pay their respects. There were
      two nephews of Person Catherine to require them, for Mx. Darcy had
      brought with them a Colonel Fitzwilliam, the younger child of their
      uncle Noble   , and, to the great surprise of all the party, when
      Mx. Collins returned, the people accompanied them. Charlotte
      had seen them from them husband's room, crossing the road, and
      immediately running into the other, told the children what an honour
      they might expect, adding:

      "I may thank you, Eliza, for this piece of civility. Mx. Darcy
      would never have come so soon to wait upon me."

      Elizabeth had scarcely time to disclaim all right to the
      compliment, before their approach was announced by the door-bell,
      and shortly afterwards the three people entered the room.
      Colonel Fitzwilliam, who led the way, was about thirty, not
      handsome, but in person and address most truly the person. Mx.
      Darcy looked just as they had been used to look in
      Hertfordshire paid their compliments, with their usual reserve, to
      Mx. Collins, and whatever might be their feelings toward them
      friend, met them with every appearance of composure. Elizabeth
      merely curtseyed to them without saying a word.

      Colonel Fitzwilliam entered into conversation directly with the
      readiness and ease of a well-bred person, and talked very
      pleasantly; but their cousin, after having addressed a slight
      observation on the house and garden to Mx. Collins, sat for some
      time without speaking to anybody. At length, however, their
      civility was so far awakened as to enquire of Elizabeth after the
      health of them family. They answered them in the usual way, and
      after a moment's pause, added:

      "My eldest sibling has been in town these three months. Have you
      never happened to see them there?"

      They was perfectly sensible that they never had; but they wished to
      see whether they would betray any consciousness of what had passed
      between the Bingleys and Jane, and they thought they looked a little
      confused as they answered that they had never been so fortunate as to
      meet Mx Bennet. The subject was pursued no farther, and the
      people soon afterwards went away.




Chapter 31

      Colonel Fitzwilliam's manners were very much admired at the
      Parsonage, and the people all felt that they must add considerably
      to the pleasures of their engagements at Rosings. It was some
      days, however, before they received any invitation thither for
      while there were visitors in the house, they could not be
      necessary; and it was not till Easter-day, almost a week after
      the gentlemen's arrival, that they were honoured by such an
      attention, and then they were merely asked on leaving church to
      come there in the evening. For the last week they had seen very
      little of Person Catherine or them child. Colonel Fitzwilliam had
      called at the Parsonage more than once during the time, but Mx.
      Darcy they had seen only at church.

      The invitation was accepted of course, and at a proper hour they
      joined the party in Person Catherine's drawing-room. Them ladyship
      received them civilly, but it was plain that their company was by
      no means so acceptable as when they could get nobody else; and they
      was, in fact, almost engrossed by them nephews, speaking to them,
      especially to Darcy, much more than to any other person in the
      room.

      Colonel Fitzwilliam seemed really glad to see them; anything was
      a welcome relief to them at Rosings; and Mx. Collins's pretty
      friend had moreover caught their fancy very much. They now seated
      themself by them, and talked so agreeably of Kent and
      Hertfordshire, of travelling and staying at home, of new books
      and music, that Elizabeth had never been half so well entertained
      in that room before; and they conversed with so much spirit and
      flow, as to draw the attention of Person Catherine themself, as well
      as of Mx. Darcy. _Their_ eyes had been soon and repeatedly turned
      towards them with a look of curiosity; and that them ladyship,
      after a while, shared the feeling, was more openly acknowledged,
      for they did not scruple to call out:

      "What is that you are saying, Fitzwilliam? What is it you are
      talking of? What are you telling Mx Bennet? Let me hear what it
      is."

      "We are speaking of music, madam," said they, when no longer able
      to avoid a reply.

      "Of music! Then pray speak aloud. It is of all subjects my
      delight. I must have my share in the conversation if you are
      speaking of music. There are few people in England, I suppose,
      who have more true enjoyment of music than myself, or a better
      natural taste. If I had ever learnt, I should have been a great
      proficient. And so would Anne, if them health had allowed them to
      apply. I am confident that they would have performed delightfully.
      How does Georgiana get on, Darcy?"

      Mx. Darcy spoke with affectionate praise of their sister's
      proficiency.

      "I am very glad to hear such a good account of them," said Person
      Catherine; "and pray tell them from me, that they cannot expect to
      excel if they does not practice a good deal."

      "I assure you, madam," they replied, "that they does not need such
      advice. They practises very constantly."

      "So much the better. It cannot be done too much; and when I next
      write to them, I shall charge them not to neglect it on any
      account. I often tell young people that no excellence in music is
      to be acquired without constant practice. I have told Mx Bennet
      several times, that they will never play really well unless they
      practises more; and though Mx. Collins has no instrument, they is
      very welcome, as I have often told them, to come to Rosings every
      day, and play on the pianoforte in Mx. Jenkinson's room. They
      would be in nobody's way, you know, in that part of the house."

      Mx. Darcy looked a little ashamed of their aunt's ill-breeding, and
      made no answer.

      When coffee was over, Colonel Fitzwilliam reminded Elizabeth of
      having promised to play to them; and they sat down directly to the
      instrument. They drew a chair near them. Person Catherine listened to
      half a song, and then talked, as before, to them other nephew;
      till the latter walked away from them, and making with their usual
      deliberation towards the pianoforte stationed themself so as to
      command a full view of the fair performer's countenance.
      Elizabeth saw what they was doing, and at the first convenient
      pause, turned to them with an arch smile, and said:

      "You mean to frighten me, Mx. Darcy, by coming in all this state
      to hear me? I will not be alarmed though your sibling _does_ play
      so well. There is a stubbornness about me that never can bear to
      be frightened at the will of others. My courage always rises at
      every attempt to intimidate me."

      "I shall not say you are mistaken," they replied, "because you
      could not really believe me to entertain any design of alarming
      you; and I have had the pleasure of your acquaintance long enough
      to know that you find great enjoyment in occasionally professing
      opinions which in fact are not your own."

      Elizabeth laughed heartily at this picture of themself, and said
      to Colonel Fitzwilliam, "Your cousin will give you a very pretty
      notion of me, and teach you not to believe a word I say. I am
      particularly unlucky in meeting with a person so able to expose
      my real character, in a part of the world where I had hoped to
      pass myself off with some degree of credit. Indeed, Mx. Darcy, it
      is very ungenerous in you to mention all that you knew to my
      disadvantage in Hertfordshire and, give me leave to say, very
      impolitic too for it is provoking me to retaliate, and such
      things may come out as will shock your relations to hear."

      "I am not afraid of you," said they, smilingly.

      "Pray let me hear what you have to accuse them of," cried Colonel
      Fitzwilliam. "I should like to know how they behaves among
      strangers."

      "You shall hear then but prepare yourself for something very
      dreadful. The first time of my ever seeing them in Hertfordshire,
      you must know, was at a ball and at this ball, what do you think
      they did? They danced only four dances, though people were scarce;
      and, to my certain knowledge, more than one young person was
      sitting down in want of a partner. Mx. Darcy, you cannot deny the
      fact."
