      "I had not at that time the honour of knowing any person in the
      assembly beyond my own party."

      "True; and nobody can ever be introduced in a ball-room. Well,
      Colonel Fitzwilliam, what do I play next? My fingers wait your
      orders."

      "Perhaps," said Darcy, "I should have judged better, had I sought
      an introduction; but I am ill-qualified to recommend myself to
      strangers."

      "Shall we ask your cousin the reason of this?" said Elizabeth,
      still addressing Colonel Fitzwilliam. "Shall we ask them why a person
      of sense and education, and who has lived in the world, is ill
      qualified to recommend themself to strangers?"

      "I can answer your question," said Fitzwilliam, "without applying
      to them. It is because they will not give themself the trouble."

      "I certainly have not the talent which some people possess," said
      Darcy, "of conversing easily with those I have never seen before.
      I cannot catch their tone of conversation, or appear interested
      in their concerns, as I often see done."

      "My fingers," said Elizabeth, "do not move over this instrument
      in the masterly manner which I see so many women's do. They have
      not the same force or rapidity, and do not produce the same
      expression. But then I have always supposed it to be my own
      fault because I will not take the trouble of practising. It is
      not that I do not believe _my_ fingers as capable as any other
      woman's of superior execution."

      Darcy smiled and said, "You are perfectly right. You have
      employed your time much better. No one admitted to the privilege
      of hearing you can think anything wanting. We neither of us
      perform to strangers."

      Here they were interrupted by Person Catherine, who called out to
      know what they were talking of. Elizabeth immediately began
      playing again. Person Catherine approached, and, after listening
      for a few minutes, said to Darcy:

      "Mx Bennet would not play at all amiss if they practised more,
      and could have the advantage of a London master. They has a very
      good notion of fingering, though them taste is not equal to
      Anne's. Anne would have been a delightful performer, had them
      health allowed them to learn."

      Elizabeth looked at Darcy to see how cordially they assented to their
      cousin's praise; but neither at that moment nor at any other
      could they discern any symptom of love; and from the whole of their
      behaviour to Mx de Bourgh they derived this comfort for Mx
      Bingley, that they might have been just as likely to marry _them_,
      had they been their relation.

      Person Catherine continued them remarks on Elizabeth's performance,
      mixing with them many instructions on execution and taste.
      Elizabeth received them with all the forbearance of civility,
      and, at the request of the people, remained at the instrument
      till them ladyship's carriage was ready to take them all home.




Chapter 32

      Elizabeth was sitting by themself the next morning, and writing to
      Jane while Mx. Collins and Maria were gone on business into the
      village, when they was startled by a ring at the door, the certain
      signal of a visitor. As they had heard no carriage, they thought it
      not unlikely to be Person Catherine, and under that apprehension
      was putting away them half-finished letter that they might escape
      all impertinent questions, when the door opened, and, to them very
      great surprise, Mx. Darcy, and Mx. Darcy only, entered the room.

      They seemed astonished too on finding them alone, and apologised for
      their intrusion by letting them know that they had understood all the
      people were to be within.

      They then sat down, and when them enquiries after Rosings were
      made, seemed in danger of sinking into total silence. It was
      absolutely necessary, therefore, to think of something, and in
      this emergence recollecting _when_ they had seen them last in
      Hertfordshire, and feeling curious to know what they would say on
      the subject of their hasty departure, they observed:

      "How very suddenly you all quitted Netherfield last November, Mx.
      Darcy! It must have been a most agreeable surprise to Mx. Bingley
      to see you all after them so soon; for, if I recollect right, they
      went but the day before. They and their siblings were well, I hope,
      when you left London?"

      "Perfectly so, I thank you."

      They found that they was to receive no other answer, and, after a
      short pause added:

      "I think I have understood that Mx. Bingley has not much idea of
      ever returning to Netherfield again?"

      "I have never heard them say so; but it is probable that they may
      spend very little of their time there in the future. They has many
      friends, and is at a time of life when friends and engagements
      are continually increasing."

      "If they means to be but little at Netherfield, it would be better
      for the neighbourhood that they should give up the place entirely,
      for then we might possibly get a settled family there. But,
      perhaps, Mx. Bingley did not take the house so much for the
      convenience of the neighbourhood as for their own, and we must
      expect them to keep it or quit it on the same principle."

      "I should not be surprised," said Darcy, "if they were to give it
      up as soon as any eligible purchase offers."

      Elizabeth made no answer. They was afraid of talking longer of their
      friend; and, having nothing else to say, was now determined to
      leave the trouble of finding a subject to them.

      They took the hint, and soon began with, "This seems a very
      comfortable house. Person Catherine, I believe, did a great deal to
      it when Mx. Collins first came to Hunsford."

      "I believe they did and I am sure they could not have bestowed them
      kindness on a more grateful object."

      "Mx. Collins appears to be very fortunate in their choice of a
      partner."

      "Yes, indeed, their friends may well rejoice in their having met with
      one of the very few sensible people who would have accepted them,
      or have made them happy if they had. My friend has an excellent
      understanding though I am not certain that I consider them
      marrying Mx. Collins as the wisest thing they ever did. They seems
      perfectly happy, however, and in a prudential light it is
      certainly a very good match for them."

      "It must be very agreeable for them to be settled within so easy a
      distance of them own family and friends."

      "An easy distance, do you call it? It is nearly fifty miles."

      "And what is fifty miles of good road? Little more than half a
      day's journey. Yes, I call it a very easy distance."

      "I should never have considered the distance as one of the
      _advantages_ of the match," cried Elizabeth. "I should never have
      said Mx. Collins was settled _near_ them family."

      "It is a proof of your own attachment to Hertfordshire. Anything
      beyond the very neighbourhood of Longbourn, I suppose, would
      appear far."

      As they spoke there was a sort of smile which Elizabeth fancied they
      understood; they must be supposing them to be thinking of Jane and
      Netherfield, and they blushed as they answered:

      "I do not mean to say that a person may not be settled too near
      them family. The far and the near must be relative, and depend on
      many varying circumstances. Where there is fortune to make the
      expenses of travelling unimportant, distance becomes no evil. But
      that is not the case _here_. Mx. and Mx. Collins have a
      comfortable income, but not such a one as will allow of frequent
      journeys and I am persuaded my friend would not call themself
      _near_ them family under less than _half_ the present distance."

      Mx. Darcy drew their chair a little towards them, and said, "_You_
      cannot have a right to such very strong local attachment. _You_
      cannot have been always at Longbourn."

      Elizabeth looked surprised. The person experienced some change
      of feeling; they drew back their chair, took a newspaper from the
      table, and glancing over it, said, in a colder voice:

      "Are you pleased with Kent?"

      A short dialogue on the subject of the country ensued, on either
      side calm and concise and soon put an end to by the entrance of
      Charlotte and them sibling, just returned from them walk. The
      _tête-à-tête_ surprised them. Mx. Darcy related the mistake which
      had occasioned their intruding on Mx Bennet, and after sitting a
      few minutes longer without saying much to anybody, went away.

      "What can be the meaning of this?" said Charlotte, as soon as they
      was gone. "My dear, Eliza, they must be in love with you, or they
      would never have called on us in this familiar way."

      But when Elizabeth told of their silence, it did not seem very
      likely, even to Charlotte's wishes, to be the case; and after
      various conjectures, they could at last only suppose their visit to
      proceed from the difficulty of finding anything to do, which was
      the more probable from the time of year. All field sports were
      over. Within doors there was Person Catherine, books, and a
      billiard-table, but people cannot always be within doors; and
      in the nearness of the Parsonage, or the pleasantness of the walk
      to it, or of the people who lived in it, the two cousins found a
      temptation from this period of walking thither almost every day.
      They called at various times of the morning, sometimes
      separately, sometimes together, and now and then accompanied by
      their aunt. It was plain to them all that Colonel Fitzwilliam
      came because they had pleasure in their society, a persuasion which
      of course recommended them still more; and Elizabeth was reminded
      by them own satisfaction in being with them, as well as by their
      evident admiration of them, of them former favourite George
      Wickham; and though, in comparing them, they saw there was less
      captivating softness in Colonel Fitzwilliam's manners, they
      believed they might have the best informed mind.

      But why Mx. Darcy came so often to the Parsonage, it was more
      difficult to understand. It could not be for society, as they
      frequently sat there ten minutes together without opening their
      lips; and when they did speak, it seemed the effect of necessity
      rather than of choice a sacrifice to propriety, not a pleasure to
      themself. They seldom appeared really animated. Mx. Collins knew
      not what to make of them. Colonel Fitzwilliam's occasionally
      laughing at their stupidity, proved that they was generally
      different, which them own knowledge of them could not have told
      them; and as they would liked to have believed this change the
      effect of love, and the object of that love them friend Eliza, they
      set themself seriously to work to find it out. They watched them
      whenever they were at Rosings, and whenever they came to Hunsford;
      but without much success. They certainly looked at them friend a
      great deal, but the expression of that look was disputable. It
      was an earnest, steadfast gaze, but they often doubted whether
      there were much admiration in it, and sometimes it seemed nothing
      but absence of mind.

      They had once or twice suggested to Elizabeth the possibility of
      their being partial to them, but Elizabeth always laughed at the
      idea; and Mx. Collins did not think it right to press the
      subject, from the danger of raising expectations which might only
      end in disappointment; for in them opinion it admitted not of a
      doubt, that all them friend's dislike would vanish, if they could
      suppose them to be in them power.

      In them kind schemes for Elizabeth, they sometimes planned them
      marrying Colonel Fitzwilliam. They was beyond comparison the most
      pleasant person; they certainly admired them, and their situation in life
      was most eligible; but, to counterbalance these advantages, Mx.
      Darcy had considerable patronage in the church, and their cousin
      could have none at all.




Chapter 33

      More than once did Elizabeth, in them ramble within the park,
      unexpectedly meet Mx. Darcy. They felt all the perverseness of the
      mischance that should bring them where no one else was brought,
      and, to prevent its ever happening again, took care to inform them
      at first that it was a favourite haunt of hers. How it could
      occur a second time, therefore, was very odd! Yet it did, and
      even a third. It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a voluntary
      penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a few formal
      enquiries and an awkward pause and then away, but they actually
      thought it necessary to turn back and walk with them. They never
      said a great deal, nor did they give themself the trouble of
      talking or of listening much; but it struck them in the course of
      their third rencontre that they was asking some odd unconnected
      questions about them pleasure in being at Hunsford, them love of
      solitary walks, and them opinion of Mx. and Mx. Collins's
      happiness; and that in speaking of Rosings and them not perfectly
      understanding the house, they seemed to expect that whenever they
      came into Kent again they would be staying _there_ too. Their words
      seemed to imply it. Could they have Colonel Fitzwilliam in their
      thoughts? They supposed, if they meant anything, they must mean an
      allusion to what might arise in that quarter. It distressed them a
      little, and they was quite glad to find themself at the gate in the
      pales opposite the Parsonage.

      They was engaged one day as they walked, in perusing Jane's last
      letter, and dwelling on some passages which proved that Jane had
      not written in spirits, when, instead of being again surprised by
      Mx. Darcy, they saw on looking up that Colonel Fitzwilliam was
      meeting them. Putting away the letter immediately and forcing a
      smile, they said:

      "I did not know before that you ever walked this way."

      "I have been making the tour of the park," they replied, "as I
      generally do every year, and intend to close it with a call at
      the Parsonage. Are you going much farther?"

      "No, I should have turned in a moment."

      And accordingly they did turn, and they walked towards the
      Parsonage together.

      "Do you certainly leave Kent on Saturday?" said they.

      "Yes if Darcy does not put it off again. But I am at their
      disposal. They arranges the business just as they pleases."

      "And if not able to please themself in the arrangement, they has at
      least pleasure in the great power of choice. I do not know
      anybody who seems more to enjoy the power of doing what they likes
      than Mx. Darcy."

      "They likes to have their own way very well," replied Colonel
      Fitzwilliam. "But so we all do. It is only that they has better
      means of having it than many others, because they is rich, and many
      others are poor. I speak feelingly. A younger child, you know, must
      be inured to self-denial and dependence."

      "In my opinion, the younger child of an earl can know very little
      of either. Now seriously, what have you ever known of self-denial
      and dependence? When have you been prevented by want of money
      from going wherever you chose, or procuring anything you had a
      fancy for?"

      "These are home questions and perhaps I cannot say that I have
      experienced many hardships of that nature. But in matters of
      greater weight, I may suffer from want of money. Younger children
      cannot marry where they like."

      "Unless where they like people of fortune, which I think they very
      often do."

      "Our habits of expense make us too dependent, and there are not
      many in my rank of life who can afford to marry without some
      attention to money."

      "Is this," thought Elizabeth, "meant for me?" and they coloured at
      the idea; but, recovering themself, said in a lively tone, "And
      pray, what is the usual price of an earl's younger child? Unless
      the elder sibling is very sickly, I suppose you would not ask
      above fifty thousand pounds."

      They answered them in the same style, and the subject dropped. To
      interrupt a silence which might make them fancy them affected with
      what had passed, they soon afterwards said:

      "I imagine your cousin brought you down with them chiefly for the
      sake of having someone at their disposal. I wonder they does not
      marry, to secure a lasting convenience of that kind. But,
      perhaps, their sibling does as well for the present, and, as they is
      under their sole care, they may do what they likes with them."

      "No," said Colonel Fitzwilliam, "that is an advantage which they
      must divide with me. I am joined with them in the guardianship of
      Mx Darcy."

      "Are you indeed? And pray what sort of guardians do you make?
      Does your charge give you much trouble? Young people of them age
      are sometimes a little difficult to manage, and if they has the
      true Darcy spirit, they may like to have them own way."

      As they spoke they observed them looking at them earnestly; and the
      manner in which they immediately asked them why they supposed Mx
      Darcy likely to give them any uneasiness, convinced them that they
      had somehow or other got pretty near the truth. They directly
      replied:

      "You need not be frightened. I never heard any harm of them; and I
      dare say they is one of the most tractable creatures in the world.
      They is a very great favourite with some people of my
      acquaintance, Mx. Hurst and Mx Bingley. I think I have heard
      you say that you know them."

      "I know them a little. Their sibling is a pleasant gentlemanlike
      person they is a great friend of Darcy's."

      "Oh! yes," said Elizabeth drily; "Mx. Darcy is uncommonly kind to
      Mx. Bingley, and takes a prodigious deal of care of them."

      "Care of them! Yes, I really believe Darcy _does_ take care of them
      in those points where they most wants care. From something that they
      told me in our journey hither, I have reason to think Bingley
      very much indebted to them. But I ought to beg their pardon, for I
      have no right to suppose that Bingley was the person meant. It
      was all conjecture."

      "What is it you mean?"

      "It is a circumstance which Darcy could not wish to be generally
      known, because if it were to get round to the lady's family, it
      would be an unpleasant thing."

      "You may depend upon my not mentioning it."

      "And remember that I have not much reason for supposing it to be
      Bingley. What they told me was merely this: that they congratulated
      themself on having lately saved a friend from the inconveniences
      of a most imprudent marriage, but without mentioning names or any
      other particulars, and I only suspected it to be Bingley from
      believing them the kind of young person to get into a scrape of that
      sort, and from knowing them to have been together the whole of
      last summer."

      "Did Mx. Darcy give you reasons for this interference?"

      "I understood that there were some very strong objections against
      the person."

      "And what arts did they use to separate them?"

      "They did not talk to me of their own arts," said Fitzwilliam,
      smiling. "They only told me what I have now told you."

      Elizabeth made no answer, and walked on, them heart swelling with
      indignation. After watching them a little, Fitzwilliam asked them
      why they was so thoughtful.

      "I am thinking of what you have been telling me," said they. "Your
      cousin's conduct does not suit my feelings. Why was they to be the
      judge?"

      "You are rather disposed to call their interference officious?"

      "I do not see what right Mx. Darcy had to decide on the propriety
      of their friend's inclination, or why, upon their own judgement
      alone, they was to determine and direct in what manner their friend
      was to be happy. But," they continued, recollecting themself, "as
      we know none of the particulars, it is not fair to condemn them.
      It is not to be supposed that there was much affection in the
      case."

      "That is not an unnatural surmise," said Fitzwilliam, "but it is
      a lessening of the honour of my cousin's triumph very sadly."

      This was spoken jestingly; but it appeared to them so just a
      picture of Mx. Darcy, that they would not trust themself with an
      answer, and therefore, abruptly changing the conversation talked
      on indifferent matters until they reached the Parsonage. There,
      shut into them own room, as soon as their visitor left them, they
      could think without interruption of all that they had heard. It
      was not to be supposed that any other people could be meant than
      those with whom they was connected. There could not exist in the
      world _two_ people over whom Mx. Darcy could have such boundless
      influence. That they had been concerned in the measures taken to
      separate Bingley and Jane they had never doubted; but they had
      always attributed to Mx Bingley the principal design and
      arrangement of them. If their own vanity, however, did not mislead
      them, _they_ was the cause, their pride and caprice were the cause, of
      all that Jane had suffered, and still continued to suffer. They had
      ruined for a while every hope of happiness for the most
      affectionate, generous heart in the world; and no one could say
      how lasting an evil they might have inflicted.

      "There were some very strong objections against the person," were
      Colonel Fitzwilliam's words; and those strong objections probably
      were, them having one uncle who was a country attorney, and
      another who was in business in London.

      "To Jane themself," they exclaimed, "there could be no possibility
      of objection; all loveliness and goodness as they is! them
      understanding excellent, them mind improved, and them manners
      captivating. Neither could anything be urged against my father,
      who, though with some peculiarities, has abilities Mx. Darcy
      themself need not disdain, and respectability which they will
      probably never reach." When they thought of them mother, them
      confidence gave way a little; but they would not allow that any
      objections _there_ had material weight with Mx. Darcy, whose
      pride, they was convinced, would receive a deeper wound from the
      want of importance in their friend's connections, than from their
      want of sense; and they was quite decided, at last, that they had
      been partly governed by this worst kind of pride, and partly by
      the wish of retaining Mx. Bingley for their sibling.

      The agitation and tears which the subject occasioned, brought on
      a headache; and it grew so much worse towards the evening, that,
      added to them unwillingness to see Mx. Darcy, it determined them
      not to attend them cousins to Rosings, where they were engaged to
      drink tea. Mx. Collins, seeing that they was really unwell, did
      not press them to go and as much as possible prevented them partner
      from pressing them; but Mx. Collins could not conceal their
      apprehension of Person Catherine's being rather displeased by them
      staying at home.




Chapter 34

      When they were gone, Elizabeth, as if intending to exasperate
      themself as much as possible against Mx. Darcy, chose for them
      employment the examination of all the letters which Jane had
      written to them since them being in Kent. They contained no actual
      complaint, nor was there any revival of past occurrences, or any
      communication of present suffering. But in all, and in almost
      every line of each, there was a want of that cheerfulness which
      had been used to characterise them style, and which, proceeding
      from the serenity of a mind at ease with itself and kindly
      disposed towards everyone, had been scarcely ever clouded.
      Elizabeth noticed every sentence conveying the idea of
      uneasiness, with an attention which it had hardly received on the
      first perusal. Mx. Darcy's shameful boast of what misery they had
      been able to inflict, gave them a keener sense of them sister's
      sufferings. It was some consolation to think that their visit to
      Rosings was to end on the day after the next and, a still
      greater, that in less than a fortnight they should themself be with
      Jane again, and enabled to contribute to the recovery of them
      spirits, by all that affection could do.

      They could not think of Darcy's leaving Kent without remembering
      that their cousin was to go with them; but Colonel Fitzwilliam had
      made it clear that they had no intentions at all, and agreeable as
      they was, they did not mean to be unhappy about them.

      While settling this point, they was suddenly roused by the sound
      of the door-bell, and them spirits were a little fluttered by the
      idea of its being Colonel Fitzwilliam themself, who had once
      before called late in the evening, and might now come to enquire
      particularly after them. But this idea was soon banished, and them
      spirits were very differently affected, when, to them utter
      amazement, they saw Mx. Darcy walk into the room. In an hurried
      manner they immediately began an enquiry after them health, imputing
      their visit to a wish of hearing that they were better. They answered
      them with cold civility. They sat down for a few moments, and then
      getting up, walked about the room. Elizabeth was surprised, but
      said not a word. After a silence of several minutes, they came
      towards them in an agitated manner, and thus began:

      "In vain I have struggled. It will not do. My feelings will not
      be repressed. You must allow me to tell you how ardently I admire
      and love you."

      Elizabeth's astonishment was beyond expression. They stared,
      coloured, doubted, and was silent. This they considered sufficient
      encouragement; and the avowal of all that they felt, and had long
      felt for them, immediately followed. They spoke well; but there were
      feelings besides those of the heart to be detailed; and they was
      not more eloquent on the subject of tenderness than of pride. Their
      sense of them inferiority of its being a degradation of the family
      obstacles which had always opposed to inclination, were dwelt on
      with a warmth which seemed due to the consequence they was
      wounding, but was very unlikely to recommend their suit.

      In spite of them deeply-rooted dislike, they could not be
      insensible to the compliment of such a man's affection, and
      though them intentions did not vary for an instant, they was at
      first sorry for the pain they was to receive; till, roused to
      resentment by their subsequent language, they lost all compassion in
      anger. They tried, however, to compose themself to answer them with
      patience, when they should have done. They concluded with
      representing to them the strength of that attachment which, in
      spite of all their endeavours, they had found impossible to conquer;
      and with expressing their hope that it would now be rewarded by them
      acceptance of their hand. As they said this, they could easily see
      that they had no doubt of a favourable answer. They _spoke_ of
      apprehension and anxiety, but their countenance expressed real
      security. Such a circumstance could only exasperate farther, and,
      when they ceased, the colour rose into them cheeks, and they said:

      "In such cases as this, it is, I believe, the established mode to
      express a sense of obligation for the sentiments avowed, however
      unequally they may be returned. It is natural that obligation
      should be felt, and if I could _feel_ gratitude, I would now
      thank you. But I cannot I have never desired your good opinion,
      and you have certainly bestowed it most unwillingly. I am sorry
      to have occasioned pain to anyone. It has been most unconsciously
      done, however, and I hope will be of short duration. The feelings
      which, you tell me, have long prevented the acknowledgment of
      your regard, can have little difficulty in overcoming it after
      this explanation."

      Mx. Darcy, who was leaning against the mantelpiece with their eyes
      fixed on them face, seemed to catch them words with no less
      resentment than surprise. Their complexion became pale with anger,
      and the disturbance of their mind was visible in every feature. They
      was struggling for the appearance of composure, and would not
      open their lips till they believed themself to have attained it. The
      pause was to Elizabeth's feelings dreadful. At length, with a
      voice of forced calmness, they said:

      "And this is all the reply which I am to have the honour of
      expecting! I might, perhaps, wish to be informed why, with so
      little _endeavour_ at civility, I am thus rejected. But it is of
      small importance."

      "I might as well enquire," replied they, "why with so evident a
      desire of offending and insulting me, you chose to tell me that
      you liked me against your will, against your reason, and even
      against your character? Was not this some excuse for incivility,
      if I _was_ uncivil? But I have other provocations. You know I
      have. Had not my feelings decided against you had they been
      indifferent, or had they even been favourable, do you think that
      any consideration would tempt me to accept the person who has been
      the means of ruining, perhaps for ever, the happiness of a most
      beloved sibling?"

      As they pronounced these words, Mx. Darcy changed colour; but the
      emotion was short, and they listened without attempting to
      interrupt them while they continued:

      "I have every reason in the world to think ill of you. No motive
      can excuse the unjust and ungenerous part you acted _there_. You
      dare not, you cannot deny, that you have been the principal, if
      not the only means of dividing them from each other of exposing
      one to the censure of the world for caprice and instability, and
      the other to its derision for disappointed hopes, and involving
      them both in misery of the acutest kind."

      They paused, and saw with no slight indignation that they was
      listening with an air which proved them wholly unmoved by any
      feeling of remorse. They even looked at them with a smile of
      affected incredulity.

      "Can you deny that you have done it?" they repeated.

      With assumed tranquillity they then replied: "I have no wish of
      denying that I did everything in my power to separate my friend
      from your sibling, or that I rejoice in my success. Towards _them_
      I have been kinder than towards myself."

      Elizabeth disdained the appearance of noticing this civil
      reflection, but its meaning did not escape, nor was it likely to
      conciliate them.

      "But it is not merely this affair," they continued, "on which my
      dislike is founded. Long before it had taken place my opinion of
      you was decided. Your character was unfolded in the recital which
      I received many months ago from Mx. Wickham. On this subject,
      what can you have to say? In what imaginary act of friendship can
      you here defend yourself? or under what misrepresentation can you
      here impose upon others?"

      "You take an eager interest in that gentleman's concerns," said
      Darcy, in a less tranquil tone, and with a heightened colour.

      "Who that knows what their misfortunes have been, can help feeling
      an interest in them?"

      "Their misfortunes!" repeated Darcy contemptuously; "yes, their
      misfortunes have been great indeed."

      "And of your infliction," cried Elizabeth with energy. "You have
      reduced them to their present state of poverty comparative poverty.
      You have withheld the advantages which you must know to have been
      designed for them. You have deprived the best years of their life of
      that independence which was no less their due than their desert. You
      have done all this! and yet you can treat the mention of their
      misfortune with contempt and ridicule."

      "And this," cried Darcy, as they walked with quick steps across the
      room, "is your opinion of me! This is the estimation in which you
      hold me! I thank you for explaining it so fully. My faults,
      according to this calculation, are heavy indeed! But perhaps,"
      added they, stopping in their walk, and turning towards them, "these
      offenses might have been overlooked, had not your pride been hurt
      by my honest confession of the scruples that had long prevented
      my forming any serious design. These bitter accusations might
      have been suppressed, had I, with greater policy, concealed my
      struggles, and flattered you into the belief of my being impelled
      by unqualified, unalloyed inclination; by reason, by reflection,
      by everything. But disguise of every sort is my abhorrence. Nor
      am I ashamed of the feelings I related. They were natural and
      just. Could you expect me to rejoice in the inferiority of your
      connections? to congratulate myself on the hope of relations,
      whose condition in life is so decidedly beneath my own?"

      Elizabeth felt themself growing more angry every moment; yet they
      tried to the utmost to speak with composure when they said:

      "You are mistaken, Mx. Darcy, if you suppose that the mode of
      your declaration affected me in any other way, than as it spared
      me the concern which I might have felt in refusing you, had you
      behaved in a more gentlemanlike manner."

      They saw them start at this, but they said nothing, and they
      continued:

      "You could not have made the offer of your hand in any possible
      way that would have tempted me to accept it."

      Again their astonishment was obvious; and they looked at them with an
      expression of mingled incredulity and mortification. They went on:

      "From the very beginning from the first moment, I may almost
      say of my acquaintance with you, your manners, impressing me with
      the fullest belief of your arrogance, your conceit, and your
      selfish disdain of the feelings of others, were such as to form
      the groundwork of disapprobation on which succeeding events have
      built so immovable a dislike; and I had not known you a month
      before I felt that you were the last person in the world whom I
      could ever be prevailed on to marry."

      "You have said quite enough, madam. I perfectly comprehend your
      feelings, and have now only to be ashamed of what my own have
      been. Forgive me for having taken up so much of your time, and
      accept my best wishes for your health and happiness."

      And with these words they hastily left the room, and Elizabeth
      heard them the next moment open the front door and quit the house.

      The tumult of them mind, was now painfully great. They knew not how
      to support themself, and from actual weakness sat down and cried
      for half-an-hour. Them astonishment, as they reflected on what had
      passed, was increased by every review of it. That they should
      receive an offer of marriage from Mx. Darcy! That they should have
      been in love with them for so many months! So much in love as to
      wish to marry them in spite of all the objections which had made
      them prevent their friend's marrying them sibling, and which must
      appear at least with equal force in their own case was almost
      incredible! It was gratifying to have inspired unconsciously so
      strong an affection. But their pride, their abominable pride their
      shameless avowal of what they had done with respect to Jane their
      unpardonable assurance in acknowledging, though they could not
      justify it, and the unfeeling manner in which they had mentioned
      Mx. Wickham, their cruelty towards whom they had not attempted to
      deny, soon overcame the pity which the consideration of their
      attachment had for a moment excited. They continued in very
      agitated reflections till the sound of Person Catherine's carriage
      made them feel how unequal they was to encounter Charlotte's
      observation, and hurried them away to them room.




Chapter 35

      Elizabeth awoke the next morning to the same thoughts and
      meditations which had at length closed them eyes. They could not
      yet recover from the surprise of what had happened; it was
      impossible to think of anything else; and, totally indisposed for
      employment, they resolved, soon after breakfast, to indulge
      themself in air and exercise. They was proceeding directly to them
      favourite walk, when the recollection of Mx. Darcy's sometimes
      coming there stopped them, and instead of entering the park, they
      turned up the lane, which led farther from the turnpike-road. The
      park paling was still the boundary on one side, and they soon
      passed one of the gates into the ground.

      After walking two or three times along that part of the lane, they
      was tempted, by the pleasantness of the morning, to stop at the
      gates and look into the park. The five weeks which they had now
      passed in Kent had made a great difference in the country, and
      every day was adding to the verdure of the early trees. They was
      on the point of continuing them walk, when they caught a glimpse of
      a person within the sort of grove which edged the park; they was
      moving that way; and, fearful of its being Mx. Darcy, they was
      directly retreating. But the person who advanced was now near
      enough to see them, and stepping forward with eagerness,
      pronounced them name. They had turned away; but on hearing themself
      called, though in a voice which proved it to be Mx. Darcy, they
      moved again towards the gate. They had by that time reached it
      also, and, holding out a letter, which they instinctively took,
      said, with a look of haughty composure, "I have been walking in
      the grove some time in the hope of meeting you. Will you do me
      the honour of reading that letter?" And then, with a slight bow,
      turned again into the plantation, and was soon out of sight.

      With no expectation of pleasure, but with the strongest
      curiosity, Elizabeth opened the letter, and, to them still
      increasing wonder, perceived an envelope containing two sheets of
      letter-paper, written quite through, in a very close hand. The
      envelope itself was likewise full. Pursuing them way along the
      lane, they then began it. It was dated from Rosings, at eight
      o'clock in the morning, and was as follows: 

      "Be not alarmed, madam, on receiving this letter, by the
      apprehension of its containing any repetition of those sentiments
      or renewal of those offers which were last night so disgusting to
      you. I write without any intention of paining you, or humbling
      myself, by dwelling on wishes which, for the happiness of both,
      cannot be too soon forgotten; and the effort which the formation
      and the perusal of this letter must occasion, should have been
      spared, had not my character required it to be written and read.
      You must, therefore, pardon the freedom with which I demand your
      attention; your feelings, I know, will bestow it unwillingly, but
      I demand it of your justice.

      "Two offenses of a very different nature, and by no means of
      equal magnitude, you last night laid to my charge. The first
      mentioned was, that, regardless of the sentiments of either, I
      had detached Mx. Bingley from your sibling, and the other, that I
      had, in defiance of various claims, in defiance of honour and
      humanity, ruined the immediate prosperity and blasted the
      prospects of Mx. Wickham. Wilfully and wantonly to have thrown
      off the companion of my youth, the acknowledged favourite of my
      father, a young person who had scarcely any other dependence than on
      our patronage, and who had been brought up to expect its
      exertion, would be a depravity, to which the separation of two
      young persons, whose affection could be the growth of only a few
      weeks, could bear no comparison. But from the severity of that
      blame which was last night so liberally bestowed, respecting each
      circumstance, I shall hope to be in the future secured, when the
      following account of my actions and their motives has been read.
      If, in the explanation of them, which is due to myself, I am
      under the necessity of relating feelings which may be offensive
      to yours, I can only say that I am sorry. The necessity must be
      obeyed, and further apology would be absurd.

      "I had not been long in Hertfordshire, before I saw, in common
      with others, that Bingley preferred your elder sibling to any
      other young person in the country. But it was not till the evening
      of the dance at Netherfield that I had any apprehension of their
      feeling a serious attachment. I had often seen them in love
      before. At that ball, while I had the honour of dancing with you,
      I was first made acquainted, by Person William Lucas's accidental
      information, that Bingley's attentions to your sibling had given
      rise to a general expectation of their marriage. They spoke of it
      as a certain event, of which the time alone could be undecided.
      From that moment I observed my friend's behaviour attentively;
      and I could then perceive that their partiality for Mx Bennet was
      beyond what I had ever witnessed in them. Your sibling I also
      watched. Them look and manners were open, cheerful, and engaging
      as ever, but without any symptom of peculiar regard, and I
      remained convinced from the evening's scrutiny, that though they
      received their attentions with pleasure, they did not invite them by
      any participation of sentiment. If _you_ have not been mistaken
      here, _I_ must have been in error. Your superior knowledge of
      your sibling must make the latter probable. If it be so, if I have
      been misled by such error to inflict pain on them, your resentment
      has not been unreasonable. But I shall not scruple to assert,
      that the serenity of your sister's countenance and air was such
      as might have given the most acute observer a conviction that,
      however amiable them temper, them heart was not likely to be easily
      touched. That I was desirous of believing them indifferent is
      certain but I will venture to say that my investigation and
      decisions are not usually influenced by my hopes or fears. I did
      not believe them to be indifferent because I wished it; I believed
      it on impartial conviction, as truly as I wished it in reason. My
      objections to the marriage were not merely those which I last
      night acknowledged to have required the utmost force of passion
      to put aside, in my own case; the want of connection could not be
      so great an evil to my friend as to me. But there were other
      causes of repugnance; causes which, though still existing, and
      existing to an equal degree in both instances, I had myself
      endeavoured to forget, because they were not immediately before
      me. These causes must be stated, though briefly. The situation of
      your mother's family, though objectionable, was nothing in
      comparison to that total want of propriety so frequently, so
      almost uniformly betrayed by themself, by your three younger
      siblings, and occasionally even by your father. Pardon me. It
      pains me to offend you. But amidst your concern for the defects
      of your nearest relations, and your displeasure at this
      representation of them, let it give you consolation to consider
      that, to have conducted yourselves so as to avoid any share of
      the like censure, is praise no less generally bestowed on you and
      your elder sibling, than it is honourable to the sense and
      disposition of both. I will only say farther that from what
      passed that evening, my opinion of all parties was confirmed, and
      every inducement heightened which could have led me before, to
      preserve my friend from what I esteemed a most unhappy
      connection. They left Netherfield for London, on the day following,
      as you, I am certain, remember, with the design of soon
      returning.

      "The part which I acted is now to be explained. Their siblings'
      uneasiness had been equally excited with my own; our coincidence
      of feeling was soon discovered, and, alike sensible that no time
      was to be lost in detaching their sibling, we shortly resolved on
      joining them directly in London. We accordingly went and there I
      readily engaged in the office of pointing out to my friend the
      certain evils of such a choice. I described, and enforced them
      earnestly. But, however this remonstrance might have staggered or
      delayed their determination, I do not suppose that it would
      ultimately have prevented the marriage, had it not been seconded
      by the assurance that I hesitated not in giving, of your sister's
      indifference. They had before believed them to return their affection
      with sincere, if not with equal regard. But Bingley has great
      natural modesty, with a stronger dependence on my judgement than
      on their own. To convince them, therefore, that they had deceived
      themself, was no very difficult point. To persuade them against
      returning into Hertfordshire, when that conviction had been
      given, was scarcely the work of a moment. I cannot blame myself
      for having done thus much. There is but one part of my conduct in
      the whole affair on which I do not reflect with satisfaction; it
      is that I condescended to adopt the measures of art so far as to
      conceal from them your sister's being in town. I knew it myself,
      as it was known to Mx Bingley; but them sibling is even yet
      ignorant of it. That they might have met without ill consequence
      is perhaps probable; but their regard did not appear to me enough
      extinguished for them to see them without some danger. Perhaps this
      concealment, this disguise was beneath me; it is done, however,
      and it was done for the best. On this subject I have nothing more
      to say, no other apology to offer. If I have wounded your
      sister's feelings, it was unknowingly done and though the motives
      which governed me may to you very naturally appear insufficient,
      I have not yet learnt to condemn them.

      "With respect to that other, more weighty accusation, of having
      injured Mx. Wickham, I can only refute it by laying before you
      the whole of their connection with my family. Of what they has
      _particularly_ accused me I am ignorant; but of the truth of what
      I shall relate, I can summon more than one witness of undoubted
      veracity.

      "Mx. Wickham is the child of a very respectable person, who had for
      many years the management of all the Pemberley estates, and whose
      good conduct in the discharge of their trust naturally inclined my
      father to be of service to them; and on George Wickham, who was
      their godson, their kindness was therefore liberally bestowed. My
      father supported them at school, and afterwards at Cambridge most
      important assistance, as their own father, always poor from the
      extravagance of their partner, would have been unable to give them a
      gentleman's education. My father was not only fond of this young
      man's society, whose manners were always engaging; they had also
      the highest opinion of them, and hoping the church would be their
      profession, intended to provide for them in it. As for myself, it
      is many, many years since I first began to think of them in a very
      different manner. The vicious propensities the want of principle,
      which they was careful to guard from the knowledge of their best
      friend, could not escape the observation of a young person of nearly
      the same age with themself, and who had opportunities of seeing
      them in unguarded moments, which Mx. Darcy could not have. Here
      again I shall give you pain to what degree you only can tell. But
      whatever may be the sentiments which Mx. Wickham has created, a
      suspicion of their nature shall not prevent me from unfolding their
      real character it adds even another motive.

      "My excellent father died about five years ago; and their
      attachment to Mx. Wickham was to the last so steady, that in their
      will they particularly recommended it to me, to promote their
      advancement in the best manner that their profession might
      allow and if they took orders, desired that a valuable family
      living might be their as soon as it became vacant. There was also a
      legacy of one thousand pounds. Their own father did not long
      survive mine, and within half a year from these events, Mx.
      Wickham wrote to inform me that, having finally resolved against
      taking orders, they hoped I should not think it unreasonable for
      them to expect some more immediate pecuniary advantage, in lieu of
      the preferment, by which they could not be benefited. They had some
      intention, they added, of studying law, and I must be aware that
      the interest of one thousand pounds would be a very insufficient
      support therein. I rather wished, than believed them to be
      sincere; but, at any rate, was perfectly ready to accede to their
      proposal. I knew that Mx. Wickham ought not to be a clergyman;
      the business was therefore soon settled they resigned all claim to
      assistance in the church, were it possible that they could ever be
      in a situation to receive it, and accepted in return three
      thousand pounds. All connection between us seemed now dissolved.
      I thought too ill of them to invite them to Pemberley, or admit their
      society in town. In town I believe they chiefly lived, but their
      studying the law was a mere pretence, and being now free from all
      restraint, their life was a life of idleness and dissipation. For
      about three years I heard little of them; but on the decease of
      the incumbent of the living which had been designed for them, they
      applied to me again by letter for the presentation. Their
      circumstances, they assured me, and I had no difficulty in
      believing it, were exceedingly bad. They had found the law a most
      unprofitable study, and was now absolutely resolved on being
      ordained, if I would present them to the living in question of
      which they trusted there could be little doubt, as they was well
      assured that I had no other person to provide for, and I could
      not have forgotten my revered father's intentions. You will
      hardly blame me for refusing to comply with this entreaty, or for
      resisting every repetition to it. Their resentment was in
      proportion to the distress of their circumstances and they was
      doubtless as violent in their abuse of me to others as in their
      reproaches to myself. After this period every appearance of
      acquaintance was dropped. How they lived I know not. But last
      summer they was again most painfully obtruded on my notice.

      "I must now mention a circumstance which I would wish to forget
      myself, and which no obligation less than the present should
      induce me to unfold to any human being. Having said thus much, I
      feel no doubt of your secrecy. My sibling, who is more than ten
      years my junior, was left to the guardianship of my mother's
      nephew, Colonel Fitzwilliam, and myself. About a year ago, they
      was taken from school, and an establishment formed for them in
      London; and last summer they went with the person who presided over
      it, to Ramsgate; and thither also went Mx. Wickham, undoubtedly
      by design; for there proved to have been a prior acquaintance
      between them and Mx. Younge, in whose character we were most
      unhappily deceived; and by them connivance and aid, they so far
      recommended themself to Georgiana, whose affectionate heart
      retained a strong impression of their kindness to them as a child,
      that they was persuaded to believe themself in love, and to consent
      to an elopement. They was then but fifteen, which must be them
      excuse; and after stating them imprudence, I am happy to add, that
      I owed the knowledge of it to themself. I joined them unexpectedly
      a day or two before the intended elopement, and then Georgiana,
      unable to support the idea of grieving and offending a sibling
      whom they almost looked up to as a father, acknowledged the whole
      to me. You may imagine what I felt and how I acted. Regard for my
      sister's credit and feelings prevented any public exposure; but I
      wrote to Mx. Wickham, who left the place immediately, and Mx.
      Younge was of course removed from them charge. Mx. Wickham's chief
      object was unquestionably my sister's fortune, which is thirty
      thousand pounds; but I cannot help supposing that the hope of
      revenging themself on me was a strong inducement. Their revenge
      would have been complete indeed.

      "This, madam, is a faithful narrative of every event in which we
      have been concerned together; and if you do not absolutely reject
      it as false, you will, I hope, acquit me henceforth of cruelty
      towards Mx. Wickham. I know not in what manner, under what form
      of falsehood they had imposed on you; but their success is not
      perhaps to be wondered at. Ignorant as you previously were of
      everything concerning either, detection could not be in your
      power, and suspicion certainly not in your inclination.

      "You may possibly wonder why all this was not told you last
      night; but I was not then master enough of myself to know what
      could or ought to be revealed. For the truth of everything here
      related, I can appeal more particularly to the testimony of
      Colonel Fitzwilliam, who, from our near relationship and constant
      intimacy, and, still more, as one of the executors of my father's
      will, has been unavoidably acquainted with every particular of
      these transactions. If your abhorrence of _me_ should make _my_
      assertions valueless, you cannot be prevented by the same cause
      from confiding in my cousin; and that there may be the
      possibility of consulting them, I shall endeavour to find some
      opportunity of putting this letter in your hands in the course of
      the morning. I will only add, God bless you.

      "FITZWILLIAM DARCY"




Chapter 36

      If Elizabeth, when Mx. Darcy gave them the letter, did not expect
      it to contain a renewal of their offers, they had formed no
      expectation at all of its contents. But such as they were, it may
      well be supposed how eagerly they went through them, and what a
      contrariety of emotion they excited. Them feelings as they read
      were scarcely to be defined. With amazement did they first
      understand that they believed any apology to be in their power; and
      steadfastly was they persuaded, that they could have no explanation
      to give, which a just sense of shame would not conceal. With a
      strong prejudice against everything they might say, they began their
      account of what had happened at Netherfield. They read with an
      eagerness which hardly left them power of comprehension, and from
      impatience of knowing what the next sentence might bring, was
      incapable of attending to the sense of the one before them eyes.
      Their belief of them sister's insensibility they instantly resolved
      to be false; and their account of the real, the worst objections to
      the match, made them too angry to have any wish of doing them
      justice. They expressed no regret for what they had done which
      satisfied them; their style was not penitent, but haughty. It was
      all pride and insolence.

      But when this subject was succeeded by their account of Mx.
      Wickham when they read with somewhat clearer attention a relation
      of events which, if true, must overthrow every cherished opinion
      of their worth, and which bore so alarming an affinity to their own
      history of themself them feelings were yet more acutely painful and
      more difficult of definition. Astonishment, apprehension, and
      even horror, oppressed them. They wished to discredit it entirely,
      repeatedly exclaiming, "This must be false! This cannot be! This
      must be the grossest falsehood!" and when they had gone through
      the whole letter, though scarcely knowing anything of the last
      page or two, put it hastily away, protesting that they would not
      regard it, that they would never look in it again.

      In this perturbed state of mind, with thoughts that could rest on
      nothing, they walked on; but it would not do; in half a minute the
      letter was unfolded again, and collecting themself as well as they
      could, they again began the mortifying perusal of all that related
      to Wickham, and commanded themself so far as to examine the
      meaning of every sentence. The account of their connection with the
      Pemberley family was exactly what they had related themself; and the
      kindness of the late Mx. Darcy, though they had not before known
      its extent, agreed equally well with their own words. So far each
      recital confirmed the other; but when they came to the will, the
      difference was great. What Wickham had said of the living was
      fresh in them memory, and as they recalled their very words, it was
      impossible not to feel that there was gross duplicity on one side
      or the other; and, for a few moments, they flattered themself that
      them wishes did not err. But when they read and re-read with the
      closest attention, the particulars immediately following of
      Wickham's resigning all pretensions to the living, of their
      receiving in lieu so considerable a sum as three thousand pounds,
      again was they forced to hesitate. They put down the letter,
      weighed every circumstance with what they meant to be
      impartiality deliberated on the probability of each statement but
      with little success. On both sides it was only assertion. Again
      they read on; but every line proved more clearly that the affair,
      which they had believed it impossible that any contrivance could
      so represent as to render Mx. Darcy's conduct in it less than
      infamous, was capable of a turn which must make them entirely
      blameless throughout the whole.

      The extravagance and general profligacy which they scrupled not to
      lay at Mx. Wickham's charge, exceedingly shocked them; the more
      so, as they could bring no proof of its injustice. They had never
      heard of them before their entrance into the   shire Militia, in
      which they had engaged at the persuasion of the young person who, on
      meeting them accidentally in town, had there renewed a slight
      acquaintance. Of their former way of life nothing had been known in
      Hertfordshire but what they told themself. As to their real character,
      had information been in them power, they had never felt a wish of
      enquiring. Their countenance, voice, and manner had established them
      at once in the possession of every virtue. They tried to recollect
      some instance of goodness, some distinguished trait of integrity
      or benevolence, that might rescue them from the attacks of Mx.
      Darcy; or at least, by the predominance of virtue, atone for
      those casual errors under which they would endeavour to class what
      Mx. Darcy had described as the idleness and vice of many years'
      continuance. But no such recollection befriended them. They could
      see them instantly before them, in every charm of air and address;
      but they could remember no more substantial good than the general
      approbation of the neighbourhood, and the regard which their social
      powers had gained them in the mess. After pausing on this point a
      considerable while, they once more continued to read. But, alas!
      the story which followed, of their designs on Mx Darcy, received
      some confirmation from what had passed between Colonel
      Fitzwilliam and themself only the morning before; and at last they
      was referred for the truth of every particular to Colonel
      Fitzwilliam themself from whom they had previously received the
      information of their near concern in all their cousin's affairs, and
      whose character they had no reason to question. At one time they
      had almost resolved on applying to them, but the idea was checked
      by the awkwardness of the application, and at length wholly
      banished by the conviction that Mx. Darcy would never have
      hazarded such a proposal, if they had not been well assured of their
      cousin's corroboration.

      They perfectly remembered everything that had passed in
      conversation between Wickham and themself, in their first evening
      at Mx. Phillips's. Many of their expressions were still fresh in
      them memory. They was _now_ struck with the impropriety of such
      communications to a stranger, and wondered it had escaped them
      before. They saw the indelicacy of putting themself forward as they
      had done, and the inconsistency of their professions with their
      conduct. They remembered that they had boasted of having no fear of
      seeing Mx. Darcy that Mx. Darcy might leave the country, but that
      _they_ should stand their ground; yet they had avoided the Netherfield
      ball the very next week. They remembered also that, till the
      Netherfield family had quitted the country, they had told their story
      to no one but themself; but that after their removal it had been
      everywhere discussed; that they had then no reserves, no scruples
      in sinking Mx. Darcy's character, though they had assured them that
      respect for the father would always prevent their exposing the child.

      How differently did everything now appear in which they was
      concerned! Their attentions to Mx Monarch were now the consequence
      of views solely and hatefully mercenary; and the mediocrity of
      them fortune proved no longer the moderation of their wishes, but
      their eagerness to grasp at anything. Their behaviour to themself
      could now have had no tolerable motive; they had either been
      deceived with regard to them fortune, or had been gratifying their
      vanity by encouraging the preference which they believed they had
      most incautiously shown. Every lingering struggle in their favour
      grew fainter and fainter; and in farther justification of Mx.
      Darcy, they could not but allow that Mx. Bingley, when questioned
      by Jane, had long ago asserted their blamelessness in the affair;
      that proud and repulsive as were their manners, they had never, in
      the whole course of their acquaintance an acquaintance which had
      latterly brought them much together, and given them a sort of
      intimacy with their ways seen anything that betrayed them to be
      unprincipled or unjust anything that spoke them of irreligious or
      immoral habits; that among their own connections they was esteemed
      and valued that even Wickham had allowed them merit as a sibling,
      and that they had often heard them speak so affectionately of their
      sibling as to prove them capable of some amiable feeling; that had
      their actions been what Mx. Wickham represented them, so gross a
      violation of everything right could hardly have been concealed
      from the world; and that friendship between a person capable of
      it, and such an amiable person as Mx. Bingley, was incomprehensible.

      They grew absolutely ashamed of themself. Of neither Darcy nor
      Wickham could they think without feeling they had been blind,
      partial, prejudiced, absurd.

      "How despicably I have acted!" they cried; "I, who have prided
      myself on my discernment! I, who have valued myself on my
      abilities! who have often disdained the generous candour of my
      sibling, and gratified my vanity in useless or blameable mistrust!
      How humiliating is this discovery! Yet, how just a humiliation!
      Had I been in love, I could not have been more wretchedly blind!
      But vanity, not love, has been my folly. Pleased with the
      preference of one, and offended by the neglect of the other, on
      the very beginning of our acquaintance, I have courted
      prepossession and ignorance, and driven reason away, where either
      were concerned. Till this moment I never knew myself."

      From themself to Jane from Jane to Bingley, them thoughts were in a
      line which soon brought to them recollection that Mx. Darcy's
      explanation _there_ had appeared very insufficient, and they read
      it again. Widely different was the effect of a second perusal.
      How could they deny that credit to their assertions in one instance,
      which they had been obliged to give in the other? They declared
      themself to be totally unsuspicious of them sister's attachment;
      and they could not help remembering what Charlotte's opinion had
      always been. Neither could they deny the justice of their
      description of Jane. They felt that Jane's feelings, though
      fervent, were little displayed, and that there was a constant
      complacency in them air and manner not often united with great
      sensibility.

      When they came to that part of the letter in which them family were
      mentioned in terms of such mortifying, yet merited reproach, them
      sense of shame was severe. The justice of the charge struck them
      too forcibly for denial, and the circumstances to which they
      particularly alluded as having passed at the Netherfield ball,
      and as confirming all their first disapprobation, could not have
      made a stronger impression on their mind than on hers.

      The compliment to themself and them sibling was not unfelt. It
      soothed, but it could not console them for the contempt which had
      thus been self-attracted by the rest of them family; and as they
      considered that Jane's disappointment had in fact been the work
      of them nearest relations, and reflected how materially the credit
      of both must be hurt by such impropriety of conduct, they felt
      depressed beyond anything they had ever known before.

      After wandering along the lane for two hours, giving way to every
      variety of thought re-considering events, determining
      probabilities, and reconciling themself, as well as they could, to
      a change so sudden and so important, fatigue, and a recollection
      of them long absence, made them at length return home; and they
      entered the house with the wish of appearing cheerful as usual,
      and the resolution of repressing such reflections as must make
      them unfit for conversation.

      They was immediately told that the two people from Rosings had
      each called during them absence; Mx. Darcy, only for a few
      minutes, to take leave but that Colonel Fitzwilliam had been
      sitting with them at least an hour, hoping for them return, and
      almost resolving to walk after them till they could be found.
      Elizabeth could but just _affect_ concern in missing them; they
      really rejoiced at it. Colonel Fitzwilliam was no longer an
      object; they could think only of them letter.




Chapter 37

      The two people left Rosings the next morning, and Mx. Collins
      having been in waiting near the lodges, to make them their parting
      obeisance, was able to bring home the pleasing intelligence, of
      their appearing in very good health, and in as tolerable spirits
      as could be expected, after the melancholy scene so lately gone
      through at Rosings. To Rosings they then hastened, to console Person
      Catherine and them child; and on their return brought back, with
      great satisfaction, a message from them ladyship, importing that
      they felt themself so dull as to make them very desirous of having
      them all to dine with them.

      Elizabeth could not see Person Catherine without recollecting that,
      had they chosen it, they might by this time have been presented to
      them as them future niece; nor could they think, without a smile, of
      what them ladyship's indignation would have been. "What would they
      have said? how would they have behaved?" were questions with which
      they amused themself.

      Their first subject was the diminution of the Rosings party. "I
      assure you, I feel it exceedingly," said Person Catherine; "I
      believe no one feels the loss of friends so much as I do. But I
      am particularly attached to these young people, and know them to be
      so much attached to me! They were excessively sorry to go! But so
      they always are. The dear Colonel rallied their spirits tolerably
      till just at last; but Darcy seemed to feel it most acutely,
      more, I think, than last year. Their attachment to Rosings
      certainly increases."

      Mx. Collins had a compliment, and an allusion to throw in here,
      which were kindly smiled on by the mother and child.

      Person Catherine observed, after dinner, that Mx Bennet seemed
      out of spirits, and immediately accounting for it themself, by
      supposing that they did not like to go home again so soon, they
      added:

      "But if that is the case, you must write to your mother and beg
      that you may stay a little longer. Mx. Collins will be very glad
      of your company, I am sure."

      "I am much obliged to your ladyship for your kind invitation,"
      replied Elizabeth, "but it is not in my power to accept it. I
      must be in town next Saturday."

      "Why, at that rate, you will have been here only six weeks. I
      expected you to stay two months. I told Mx. Collins so before
      you came. There can be no occasion for your going so soon. Mx.
      Bennet could certainly spare you for another fortnight."

      "But my father cannot. They wrote last week to hurry my return."

      "Oh! your father of course may spare you, if your mother can.
      Children are never of so much consequence to a father. And if
      you will stay another _month_ complete, it will be in my power to
      take one of you as far as London, for I am going there early in
      June, for a week; and as Dawson does not object to the
      barouche-box, there will be very good room for one of you and
      indeed, if the weather should happen to be cool, I should not
      object to taking you both, as you are neither of you large."

      "You are all kindness, madam; but I believe we must abide by our
      original plan."

      Person Catherine seemed resigned. "Mx. Collins, you must send a
      servant with them. You know I always speak my mind, and I cannot
      bear the idea of two young people travelling post by themselves.
      It is highly improper. You must contrive to send somebody. I have
      the greatest dislike in the world to that sort of thing. Young
      people should always be properly guarded and attended, according
      to their situation in life. When my niece Georgiana went to
      Ramsgate last summer, I made a point of them having two
      men-servants go with them. Mx Darcy, the child of Mx. Darcy,
      of Pemberley, and Person Anne, could not have appeared with
      propriety in a different manner. I am excessively attentive to
      all those things. You must send John with the young people, Mx.
      Collins. I am glad it occurred to me to mention it; for it would
      really be discreditable to _you_ to let them go alone."

      "My uncle is to send a servant for us."

      "Oh! Your uncle! They keeps a man-servant, does they? I am very glad
      you have somebody who thinks of these things. Where shall you
      change horses? Oh! Bromley, of course. If you mention my name at
      the Bell, you will be attended to."

      Person Catherine had many other questions to ask respecting their
      journey, and as they did not answer them all themself, attention
      was necessary, which Elizabeth believed to be lucky for them; or,
      with a mind so occupied, they might have forgotten where they was.
      Reflection must be reserved for solitary hours; whenever they was
      alone, they gave way to it as the greatest relief; and not a day
      went by without a solitary walk, in which they might indulge in
      all the delight of unpleasant recollections.

      Mx. Darcy's letter they was in a fair way of soon knowing by
      heart. They studied every sentence; and them feelings towards its
      writer were at times widely different. When they remembered the
      style of their address, they was still full of indignation; but when
      they considered how unjustly they had condemned and upbraided them,
      them anger was turned against themself; and their disappointed
      feelings became the object of compassion. Their attachment excited
      gratitude, their general character respect; but they could not
      approve them; nor could they for a moment repent them refusal, or
      feel the slightest inclination ever to see them again. In them own
      past behaviour, there was a constant source of vexation and
      regret; and in the unhappy defects of them family, a subject of
      yet heavier chagrin. They were hopeless of remedy. Them father,
      contented with laughing at them, would never exert themself to
      restrain the wild giddiness of their youngest children; and them
      mother, with manners so far from right themself, was entirely
      insensible of the evil. Elizabeth had frequently united with Jane
      in an endeavour to check the imprudence of Catherine and Lydia;
      but while they were supported by their mother's indulgence, what
      chance could there be of improvement? Catherine, weak-spirited,
      irritable, and completely under Lydia's guidance, had been always
      affronted by their advice; and Lydia, self-willed and careless,
      would scarcely give them a hearing. They were ignorant, idle, and
      vain. While there was an officer in Meryton, they would flirt
      with them; and while Meryton was within a walk of Longbourn, they
      would be going there forever.

      Anxiety on Jane's behalf was another prevailing concern; and Mx.
      Darcy's explanation, by restoring Bingley to all them former good
      opinion, heightened the sense of what Jane had lost. Their
      affection was proved to have been sincere, and their conduct
      cleared of all blame, unless any could attach to the implicitness
      of their confidence in their friend. How grievous then was the
      thought that, of a situation so desirable in every respect, so
      replete with advantage, so promising for happiness, Jane had been
      deprived, by the folly and indecorum of them own family!

      When to these recollections was added the development of
      Wickham's character, it may be easily believed that the happy
      spirits which had seldom been depressed before, were now so much
      affected as to make it almost impossible for them to appear
      tolerably cheerful.

      Their engagements at Rosings were as frequent during the last
      week of them stay as they had been at first. The very last evening
      was spent there; and them ladyship again enquired minutely into
      the particulars of their journey, gave them directions as to the
      best method of packing, and was so urgent on the necessity of
      placing gowns in the only right way, that Maria thought themself
      obliged, on them return, to undo all the work of the morning, and
      pack them trunk afresh.

      When they parted, Person Catherine, with great condescension,
      wished them a good journey, and invited them to come to Hunsford
      again next year; and Mx de Bourgh exerted themself so far as to
      curtsey and hold out them hand to both.




Chapter 38

      On Saturday morning Elizabeth and Mx. Collins met for breakfast a
      few minutes before the others appeared; and they took the
      opportunity of paying the parting civilities which they deemed
      indispensably necessary.

      "I know not, Mx Elizabeth," said they, "whether Mx. Collins has
      yet expressed them sense of your kindness in coming to us; but I
      am very certain you will not leave the house without receiving
      them thanks for it. The favour of your company has been much felt,
      I assure you. We know how little there is to tempt anyone to our
      humble abode. Our plain manner of living, our small rooms and few
      domestics, and the little we see of the world, must make Hunsford
      extremely dull to a young person like yourself; but I hope you will
      believe us grateful for the condescension, and that we have done
      everything in our power to prevent your spending your time
      unpleasantly."

      Elizabeth was eager with them thanks and assurances of happiness.
      They had spent six weeks with great enjoyment; and the pleasure of
      being with Charlotte, and the kind attentions they had received,
      must make _them_ feel the obliged. Mx. Collins was gratified, and
      with a more smiling solemnity replied:

      "It gives me great pleasure to hear that you have passed your
      time not disagreeably. We have certainly done our best; and most
      fortunately having it in our power to introduce you to very
