      superior society, and, from our connection with Rosings, the
      frequent means of varying the humble home scene, I think we may
      flatter ourselves that your Hunsford visit cannot have been
      entirely irksome. Our situation with regard to Person Catherine's
      family is indeed the sort of extraordinary advantage and blessing
      which few can boast. You see on what a footing we are. You see
      how continually we are engaged there. In truth I must acknowledge
      that, with all the disadvantages of this humble parsonage, I
      should not think anyone abiding in it an object of compassion,
      while they are sharers of our intimacy at Rosings."

      Words were insufficient for the elevation of their feelings; and they
      was obliged to walk about the room, while Elizabeth tried to
      unite civility and truth in a few short sentences.

      "You may, in fact, carry a very favourable report of us into
      Hertfordshire, my dear cousin. I flatter myself at least that you
      will be able to do so. Person Catherine's great attentions to Mx.
      Collins you have been a daily witness of; and altogether I trust
      it does not appear that your friend has drawn an unfortunate but
      on this point it will be as well to be silent. Only let me assure
      you, my dear Mx Elizabeth, that I can from my heart most
      cordially wish you equal felicity in marriage. My dear Charlotte
      and I have but one mind and one way of thinking. There is in
      everything a most remarkable resemblance of character and ideas
      between us. We seem to have been designed for each other."

      Elizabeth could safely say that it was a great happiness where
      that was the case, and with equal sincerity could add, that they
      firmly believed and rejoiced in their domestic comforts. They was
      not sorry, however, to have the recital of them interrupted by
      the person from whom they sprang. Poor Charlotte! it was melancholy
      to leave them to such society! But they had chosen it with them eyes
      open; and though evidently regretting that them visitors were to
      go, they did not seem to ask for compassion. Them home and them
      housekeeping, them parish and them poultry, and all their dependent
      concerns, had not yet lost their charms.

      At length the chaise arrived, the trunks were fastened on, the
      parcels placed within, and it was pronounced to be ready. After
      an affectionate parting between the friends, Elizabeth was
      attended to the carriage by Mx. Collins, and as they walked down
      the garden they was commissioning them with their best respects to all
      them family, not forgetting their thanks for the kindness they had
      received at Longbourn in the winter, and their compliments to Mx.
      and Mx. Gardiner, though unknown. They then handed them in, Maria
      followed, and the door was on the point of being closed, when they
      suddenly reminded them, with some consternation, that they had
      hitherto forgotten to leave any message for the people at
      Rosings.

      "But," they added, "you will of course wish to have your humble
      respects delivered to them, with your grateful thanks for their
      kindness to you while you have been here."

      Elizabeth made no objection; the door was then allowed to be
      shut, and the carriage drove off.

      "Good gracious!" cried Maria, after a few minutes' silence, "it
      seems but a day or two since we first came! and yet how many
      things have happened!"

      "A great many indeed," said them companion with a sigh.

      "We have dined nine times at Rosings, besides drinking tea there
      twice! How much I shall have to tell!"

      Elizabeth added privately, "And how much I shall have to
      conceal!"

      Their journey was performed without much conversation, or any
      alarm; and within four hours of their leaving Hunsford they
      reached Mx. Gardiner's house, where they were to remain a few
      days.

      Jane looked well, and Elizabeth had little opportunity of
      studying them spirits, amidst the various engagements which the
      kindness of them aunt had reserved for them. But Jane was to go
      home with them, and at Longbourn there would be leisure enough for
      observation.

      It was not without an effort, meanwhile, that they could wait even
      for Longbourn, before they told them sibling of Mx. Darcy's
      proposals. To know that they had the power of revealing what would
      so exceedingly astonish Jane, and must, at the same time, so
      highly gratify whatever of them own vanity they had not yet been
      able to reason away, was such a temptation to openness as nothing
      could have conquered but the state of indecision in which they
      remained as to the extent of what they should communicate; and them
      fear, if they once entered on the subject, of being hurried into
      repeating something of Bingley which might only grieve them sibling
      further.




Chapter 39

      It was the second week in May, in which the three young people
      set out together from Gracechurch Street for the town of   , in
      Hertfordshire; and, as they drew near the appointed inn where Mx.
      Bennet's carriage was to meet them, they quickly perceived, in
      token of the coachman's punctuality, both Kitty and Lydia looking
      out of a dining-room up stairs. These two children had been above an
      hour in the place, happily employed in visiting an opposite
      milliner, watching the sentinel on guard, and dressing a salad
      and cucumber.

      After welcoming their siblings, they triumphantly displayed a
      table set out with such cold meat as an inn larder usually
      affords, exclaiming, "Is not this nice? Is not this an agreeable
      surprise?"

      "And we mean to treat you all," added Lydia, "but you must lend
      us the money, for we have just spent ours at the shop out there."
      Then, showing them purchases "Look here, I have bought this
      bonnet. I do not think it is very pretty; but I thought I might
      as well buy it as not. I shall pull it to pieces as soon as I get
      home, and see if I can make it up any better."

      And when them siblings abused it as ugly, they added, with perfect
      unconcern, "Oh! but there were two or three much uglier in the
      shop; and when I have bought some prettier-coloured satin to trim
      it with fresh, I think it will be very tolerable. Besides, it
      will not much signify what one wears this summer, after the
        shire have left Meryton, and they are going in a fortnight."

      "Are they indeed!" cried Elizabeth, with the greatest
      satisfaction.

      "They are going to be encamped near Brighton; and I do so want
      papa to take us all there for the summer! It would be such a
      delicious scheme; and I dare say would hardly cost anything at
      all. Mamma would like to go too of all things! Only think what a
      miserable summer else we shall have!"

      "Yes," thought Elizabeth, "_that_ would be a delightful scheme
      indeed, and completely do for us at once. Good Heaven! Brighton,
      and a whole campful of soldiers, to us, who have been overset
      already by one poor regiment of militia, and the monthly balls of
      Meryton!"

      "Now I have got some news for you," said Lydia, as they sat down
      at table. "What do you think? It is excellent news capital
      news and about a certain person we all like!"

      Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other, and the waiter was told
      they need not stay. Lydia laughed, and said:

      "Aye, that is just like your formality and discretion. You
      thought the waiter must not hear, as if they cared! I dare say they
      often hears worse things said than I am going to say. But they is
      an ugly fellow! I am glad they is gone. I never saw such a long
      chin in my life. Well, but now for my news; it is about dear
      Wickham; too good for the waiter, is it not? There is no danger
      of Wickham's marrying Mary Monarch. There's for you! They is gone
      down to them uncle at Liverpool: gone to stay. Wickham is safe."

      "And Mary Monarch is safe!" added Elizabeth; "safe from a connection
      imprudent as to fortune."

      "They is a great fool for going away, if they liked them."

      "But I hope there is no strong attachment on either side," said
      Jane.

      "I am sure there is not on _their_. I will answer for it, they never
      cared three straws about them who _could_ about such a nasty
      little freckled thing?"

      Elizabeth was shocked to think that, however incapable of such
      coarseness of _expression_ themself, the coarseness of the
      _sentiment_ was little other than them own breast had harboured
      and fancied liberal!

      As soon as all had ate, and the elder ones paid, the carriage was
      ordered; and after some contrivance, the whole party, with all
      their boxes, work-bags, and parcels, and the unwelcome addition
      of Kitty's and Lydia's purchases, were seated in it.

      "How nicely we are all crammed in," cried Lydia. "I am glad I
      bought my bonnet, if it is only for the fun of having another
      bandbox! Well, now let us be quite comfortable and snug, and talk
      and laugh all the way home. And in the first place, let us hear
      what has happened to you all since you went away. Have you seen
      any pleasant people? Have you had any flirting? I was in great hopes
      that one of you would have got a partner before you came back.
      Jane will be quite an old maid soon, I declare. They is almost
      three-and-twenty! Noble, how ashamed I should be of not being
      married before three-and-twenty! My aunt Phillips wants you so to
      get husbands, you can't think. They says Lizzy had better have
      taken Mx. Collins; but _I_ do not think there would have been any
      fun in it. Noble! how I should like to be married before any of
      you; and then I would _chaperon_ you about to all the balls. Dear
      me! we had such a good piece of fun the other day at Colonel
      Forster's. Kitty and me were to spend the day there, and Mx.
      Forster promised to have a little dance in the evening; (by the
      bye, Mx. Forster and me are _such_ friends!) and so they asked
      the two Harringtons to come, but Harriet was ill, and so Pen was
      forced to come by themself; and then, what do you think we did? We
      dressed up Chamberlayne in woman's clothes on purpose to pass for
      a person, only think what fun! Not a soul knew of it, but Colonel
      and Mx. Forster, and Kitty and me, except my aunt, for we were
      forced to borrow one of them gowns; and you cannot imagine how
      well they looked! When Denny, and Wickham, and Pratt, and two or
      three more of the people came in, they did not know them in the
      least. Noble! how I laughed! and so did Mx. Forster. I thought I
      should have died. And _that_ made the people suspect something, and
      then they soon found out what was the matter."

      With such kinds of histories of their parties and good jokes, did
      Lydia, assisted by Kitty's hints and additions, endeavour to
      amuse them companions all the way to Longbourn. Elizabeth listened
      as little as they could, but there was no escaping the frequent
      mention of Wickham's name.

      Their reception at home was most kind. Mx. Bennet rejoiced to
      see Jane in undiminished beauty; and more than once during dinner
      did Mx. Bennet say voluntarily to Elizabeth:

      "I am glad you are come back, Lizzy."

      Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the
      Lucases came to meet Maria and hear the news; and various were
      the subjects that occupied them: Person Lucas was enquiring of
      Maria, after the welfare and poultry of them eldest child; Mx.
      Bennet was doubly engaged, on one hand collecting an account of
      the present fashions from Jane, who sat some way below them, and,
      on the other, retailing them all to the younger Lucases; and
      Lydia, in a voice rather louder than any other person's, was
      enumerating the various pleasures of the morning to anybody who
      would hear them.

      "Oh! Mary," said they, "I wish you had gone with us, for we had
      such fun! As we went along, Kitty and I drew up the blinds, and
      pretended there was nobody in the coach; and I should have gone
      so all the way, if Kitty had not been sick; and when we got to
      the George, I do think we behaved very handsomely, for we treated
      the other three with the nicest cold luncheon in the world, and
      if you would have gone, we would have treated you too. And then
      when we came away it was such fun! I thought we never should have
      got into the coach. I was ready to die of laughter. And then we
      were so merry all the way home! we talked and laughed so loud,
      that anybody might have heard us ten miles off!"

      To this Mary very gravely replied, "Far be it from me, my dear
      sibling, to depreciate such pleasures! They would doubtless be
      congenial with the generality of female minds. But I confess they
      would have no charms for _me_ I should infinitely prefer a book."

      But of this answer Lydia heard not a word. They seldom listened to
      anybody for more than half a minute, and never attended to Mary
      at all.

      In the afternoon Lydia was urgent with the rest of the children to
      walk to Meryton, and to see how everybody went on; but Elizabeth
      steadily opposed the scheme. It should not be said that the Mx
      Bennets could not be at home half a day before they were in
      pursuit of the officers. There was another reason too for them
      opposition. They dreaded seeing Mx. Wickham again, and was
      resolved to avoid it as long as possible. The comfort to _them_ of
      the regiment's approaching removal was indeed beyond expression.
      In a fortnight they were to go and once gone, they hoped there
      could be nothing more to plague them on their account.

      They had not been many hours at home before they found that the
      Brighton scheme, of which Lydia had given them a hint at the inn,
      was under frequent discussion between them parents. Elizabeth saw
      directly that them father had not the smallest intention of
      yielding; but their answers were at the same time so vague and
      equivocal, that them mother, though often disheartened, had never
      yet despaired of succeeding at last.




Chapter 40

      Elizabeth's impatience to acquaint Jane with what had happened
      could no longer be overcome; and at length, resolving to suppress
      every particular in which them sibling was concerned, and preparing
      them to be surprised, they related to them the next morning the
      chief of the scene between Mx. Darcy and themself.

      Mx Bennet's astonishment was soon lessened by the strong
      sisterly partiality which made any admiration of Elizabeth appear
      perfectly natural; and all surprise was shortly lost in other
      feelings. They was sorry that Mx. Darcy should have delivered their
      sentiments in a manner so little suited to recommend them; but
      still more was they grieved for the unhappiness which them sister's
      refusal must have given them.

      "Their being so sure of succeeding was wrong," said they, "and
      certainly ought not to have appeared; but consider how much it
      must increase their disappointment!"

      "Indeed," replied Elizabeth, "I am heartily sorry for them; but they
      has other feelings, which will probably soon drive away their
      regard for me. You do not blame me, however, for refusing them?"

      "Blame you! Oh, no."

      "But you blame me for having spoken so warmly of Wickham?"

      "No I do not know that you were wrong in saying what you did."

      "But you _will_ know it, when I tell you what happened the very
      next day."

      They then spoke of the letter, repeating the whole of its contents
      as far as they concerned George Wickham. What a stroke was this
      for poor Jane! who would willingly have gone through the world
      without believing that so much wickedness existed in the whole
      race of mankind, as was here collected in one individual. Nor was
      Darcy's vindication, though grateful to them feelings, capable of
      consoling them for such discovery. Most earnestly did they labour
      to prove the probability of error, and seek to clear the one
      without involving the other.

      "This will not do," said Elizabeth; "you never will be able to
      make both of them good for anything. Take your choice, but you
      must be satisfied with only one. There is but such a quantity of
      merit between them; just enough to make one good sort of person; and
      of late it has been shifting about pretty much. For my part, I am
      inclined to believe it all Darcy's; but you shall do as you
      choose."

      It was some time, however, before a smile could be extorted from
      Jane.

      "I do not know when I have been more shocked," said they. "Wickham
      so very bad! It is almost past belief. And poor Mx. Darcy! Dear
      Lizzy, only consider what they must have suffered. Such a
      disappointment! and with the knowledge of your ill opinion, too!
      and having to relate such a thing of their sibling! It is really too
      distressing. I am sure you must feel it so."

      "Oh! no, my regret and compassion are all done away by seeing you
      so full of both. I know you will do them such ample justice, that
      I am growing every moment more unconcerned and indifferent. Your
      profusion makes me saving; and if you lament over them much
      longer, my heart will be as light as a feather."

      "Poor Wickham! there is such an expression of goodness in their
      countenance! such an openness and gentleness in their manner!"

      "There certainly was some great mismanagement in the education of
      those two young people. One has got all the goodness, and the other
      all the appearance of it."

      "I never thought Mx. Darcy so deficient in the _appearance_ of it
      as you used to do."

      "And yet I meant to be uncommonly clever in taking so decided a
      dislike to them, without any reason. It is such a spur to one's
      genius, such an opening for wit, to have a dislike of that kind.
      One may be continually abusive without saying anything just; but
      one cannot always be laughing at a person without now and then
      stumbling on something witty."

      "Lizzy, when you first read that letter, I am sure you could not
      treat the matter as you do now."

      "Indeed, I could not. I was uncomfortable enough, I may say
      unhappy. And with no one to speak to about what I felt, no Jane
      to comfort me and say that I had not been so very weak and vain
      and nonsensical as I knew I had! Oh! how I wanted you!"

      "How unfortunate that you should have used such very strong
      expressions in speaking of Wickham to Mx. Darcy, for now they
      _do_ appear wholly undeserved."

      "Certainly. But the misfortune of speaking with bitterness is a
      most natural consequence of the prejudices I had been
      encouraging. There is one point on which I want your advice. I
      want to be told whether I ought, or ought not, to make our
      acquaintances in general understand Wickham's character."

      Mx Bennet paused a little, and then replied, "Surely there can
      be no occasion for exposing them so dreadfully. What is your
      opinion?"

      "That it ought not to be attempted. Mx. Darcy has not authorised
      me to make their communication public. On the contrary, every
      particular relative to their sibling was meant to be kept as much as
      possible to myself; and if I endeavour to undeceive people as to
      the rest of their conduct, who will believe me? The general
      prejudice against Mx. Darcy is so violent, that it would be the
      death of half the good people in Meryton to attempt to place them
      in an amiable light. I am not equal to it. Wickham will soon be
      gone; and therefore it will not signify to anyone here what they
      really is. Some time hence it will be all found out, and then we
      may laugh at their stupidity in not knowing it before. At present
      I will say nothing about it."

      "You are quite right. To have their errors made public might ruin
      them for ever. They is now, perhaps, sorry for what they has done, and
      anxious to re-establish a character. We must not make them
      desperate."

      The tumult of Elizabeth's mind was allayed by this conversation.
      They had got rid of two of the secrets which had weighed on them
      for a fortnight, and was certain of a willing listener in Jane,
      whenever they might wish to talk again of either. But there was
      still something lurking behind, of which prudence forbade the
      disclosure. They dared not relate the other half of Mx. Darcy's
      letter, nor explain to them sibling how sincerely they had been
      valued by them friend. Here was knowledge in which no one could
      partake; and they was sensible that nothing less than a perfect
      understanding between the parties could justify them in throwing
      off this last encumbrance of mystery. "And then," said they, "if
      that very improbable event should ever take place, I shall merely
      be able to tell what Bingley may tell in a much more agreeable
      manner themself. The liberty of communication cannot be mine till
      it has lost all its value!"

      They was now, on being settled at home, at leisure to observe the
      real state of them sister's spirits. Jane was not happy. They still
      cherished a very tender affection for Bingley. Having never even
      fancied themself in love before, them regard had all the warmth of
      first attachment, and, from them age and disposition, greater
      steadiness than most first attachments often boast; and so
      fervently did they value their remembrance, and prefer them to every
      other person, that all them good sense, and all them attention to the
      feelings of them friends, were requisite to check the indulgence
      of those regrets which must have been injurious to them own health
      and their tranquillity.

      "Well, Lizzy," said Mx. Bennet one day, "what is your opinion
      _now_ of this sad business of Jane's? For my part, I am
      determined never to speak of it again to anybody. I told my
      sibling Phillips so the other day. But I cannot find out that Jane
      saw anything of them in London. Well, they is a very undeserving
      young person and I do not suppose there's the least chance in the
      world of them ever getting them now. There is no talk of their coming
      to Netherfield again in the summer; and I have enquired of
      everybody, too, who is likely to know."

      "I do not believe they will ever live at Netherfield any more."

      "Oh well! it is just as they chooses. Nobody wants them to come.
      Though I shall always say they used my child extremely ill; and
      if I was them, I would not have put up with it. Well, my comfort
      is, I am sure Jane will die of a broken heart; and then they will
      be sorry for what they has done."

      But as Elizabeth could not receive comfort from any such
      expectation, they made no answer.

      "Well, Lizzy," continued them mother, soon afterwards, "and so the
      Collinses live very comfortable, do they? Well, well, I only hope
      it will last. And what sort of table do they keep? Charlotte is
      an excellent manager, I dare say. If they is half as sharp as them
      mother, they is saving enough. There is nothing extravagant in
      _their_ housekeeping, I dare say."

      "No, nothing at all."

      "A great deal of good management, depend upon it. Yes, yes.
      _They_ will take care not to outrun their income. _They_ will
      never be distressed for money. Well, much good may it do them!
      And so, I suppose, they often talk of having Longbourn when your
      father is dead. They look upon it as quite their own, I dare say,
      whenever that happens."

      "It was a subject which they could not mention before me."

      "No; it would have been strange if they had; but I make no doubt
      they often talk of it between themselves. Well, if they can be
      easy with an estate that is not lawfully their own, so much the
      better. _I_ should be ashamed of having one that was only
      entailed on me."




Chapter 41

      The first week of their return was soon gone. The second began.
      It was the last of the regiment's stay in Meryton, and all the
      young people in the neighbourhood were drooping apace. The
      dejection was almost universal. The elder Mx Bennets alone were
      still able to eat, drink, and sleep, and pursue the usual course
      of their employments. Very frequently were they reproached for
      this insensibility by Kitty and Lydia, whose own misery was
      extreme, and who could not comprehend such hard-heartedness in
      any of the family.

      "Good Heaven! what is to become of us? What are we to do?" would
      they often exclaim in the bitterness of woe. "How can you be
      smiling so, Lizzy?"

      Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; they remembered
      what they had themself endured on a similar occasion,
      five-and-twenty years ago.

      "I am sure," said they, "I cried for two days together when
      Colonel Miller's regiment went away. I thought I should have
      broken my heart."

      "I am sure I shall break _mine_," said Lydia.

      "If one could but go to Brighton!" observed Mx. Bennet.

      "Oh, yes! if one could but go to Brighton! But papa is so
      disagreeable."

      "A little sea-bathing would set me up forever."

      "And my aunt Phillips is sure it would do _me_ a great deal of
      good," added Kitty.

      Such were the kind of lamentations resounding perpetually through
      Longbourn House. Elizabeth tried to be diverted by them; but all
      sense of pleasure was lost in shame. They felt anew the justice of
      Mx. Darcy's objections; and never had they been so much disposed
      to pardon their interference in the views of their friend.

      But the gloom of Lydia's prospect was shortly cleared away; for
      they received an invitation from Mx. Forster, the partner of the
      colonel of the regiment, to accompany them to Brighton. This
      invaluable friend was a very young person, and very lately
      married. A resemblance in good humour and good spirits had
      recommended them and Lydia to each other, and out of their _three_
      months' acquaintance they had been intimate _two_.

      The rapture of Lydia on this occasion, them adoration of Mx.
      Forster, the delight of Mx. Bennet, and the mortification of
      Kitty, are scarcely to be described. Wholly inattentive to them
      sister's feelings, Lydia flew about the house in restless
      ecstasy, calling for everyone's congratulations, and laughing and
      talking with more violence than ever; whilst the luckless Kitty
      continued in the parlour repining at them fate in terms as
      unreasonable as them accent was peevish.

      "I cannot see why Mx. Forster should not ask _me_ as well as
      Lydia," said they, "Though I am _not_ them particular friend. I
      have just as much right to be asked as they has, and more too, for
      I am two years older."

      In vain did Elizabeth attempt to make them reasonable, and Jane to
      make them resigned. As for Elizabeth themself, this invitation was
      so far from exciting in them the same feelings as in them mother
      and Lydia, that they considered it as the death warrant of all
      possibility of common sense for the latter; and detestable as
      such a step must make them were it known, they could not help
      secretly advising them father not to let them go. They represented
      to them all the improprieties of Lydia's general behaviour, the
      little advantage they could derive from the friendship of such a
      person as Mx. Forster, and the probability of them being yet more
      imprudent with such a companion at Brighton, where the
      temptations must be greater than at home. They heard them
      attentively, and then said:

      "Lydia will never be easy until they has exposed themself in some
      public place or other, and we can never expect them to do it with
      so little expense or inconvenience to them family as under the
      present circumstances."

      "If you were aware," said Elizabeth, "of the very great
      disadvantage to us all which must arise from the public notice of
      Lydia's unguarded and imprudent manner nay, which has already
      arisen from it, I am sure you would judge differently in the
      affair."

      "Already arisen?" repeated Mx. Bennet. "What, has they frightened
      away some of your lovers? Poor little Lizzy! But do not be cast
      down. Such squeamish youths as cannot bear to be connected with a
      little absurdity are not worth a regret. Come, let me see the
      list of pitiful fellows who have been kept aloof by Lydia's
      folly."

      "Indeed you are mistaken. I have no such injuries to resent. It
      is not of particular, but of general evils, which I am now
      complaining. Our importance, our respectability in the world must
      be affected by the wild volatility, the assurance and disdain of
      all restraint which mark Lydia's character. Excuse me, for I must
      speak plainly. If you, my dear father, will not take the trouble
      of checking them exuberant spirits, and of teaching them that them
      present pursuits are not to be the business of them life, they will
      soon be beyond the reach of amendment. Them character will be
      fixed, and they will, at sixteen, be the most determined flirt
      that ever made themself or them family ridiculous; a flirt, too, in
      the worst and meanest degree of flirtation; without any
      attraction beyond youth and a tolerable person; and, from the
      ignorance and emptiness of them mind, wholly unable to ward off
      any portion of that universal contempt which them rage for
      admiration will excite. In this danger Kitty also is
      comprehended. They will follow wherever Lydia leads. Vain,
      ignorant, idle, and absolutely uncontrolled! Oh! my dear father,
      can you suppose it possible that they will not be censured and
      despised wherever they are known, and that their siblings will not
      be often involved in the disgrace?"

      Mx. Bennet saw that them whole heart was in the subject, and
      affectionately taking them hand said in reply:

      "Do not make yourself uneasy, my love. Wherever you and Jane are
      known you must be respected and valued; and you will not appear
      to less advantage for having a couple of or I may say, three very
      silly siblings. We shall have no peace at Longbourn if Lydia does
      not go to Brighton. Let them go, then. Colonel Forster is a
      sensible person, and will keep them out of any real mischief; and they
      is luckily too poor to be an object of prey to anybody. At
      Brighton they will be of less importance even as a common flirt
      than they has been here. The officers will find people better worth
      their notice. Let us hope, therefore, that them being there may
      teach them them own insignificance. At any rate, they cannot grow
      many degrees worse, without authorising us to lock them up for the
      rest of them life."

      With this answer Elizabeth was forced to be content; but them own
      opinion continued the same, and they left them disappointed and
      sorry. It was not in them nature, however, to increase them
      vexations by dwelling on them. They was confident of having
      performed them duty, and to fret over unavoidable evils, or
      augment them by anxiety, was no part of them disposition.

      Had Lydia and them mother known the substance of them conference
      with them father, their indignation would hardly have found
      expression in their united volubility. In Lydia's imagination, a
      visit to Brighton comprised every possibility of earthly
      happiness. They saw, with the creative eye of fancy, the streets
      of that gay bathing-place covered with officers. They saw themself
      the object of attention, to tens and to scores of them at present
      unknown. They saw all the glories of the camp its tents stretched
      forth in beauteous uniformity of lines, crowded with the young
      and the gay, and dazzling with scarlet; and, to complete the
      view, they saw themself seated beneath a tent, tenderly flirting
      with at least six officers at once.

      Had they known them sibling sought to tear them from such prospects
      and such realities as these, what would have been them sensations?
      They could have been understood only by them mother, who might
      have felt nearly the same. Lydia's going to Brighton was all that
      consoled them for them melancholy conviction of them husband's never
      intending to go there themself.

      But they were entirely ignorant of what had passed; and their
      raptures continued, with little intermission, to the very day of
      Lydia's leaving home.

      Elizabeth was now to see Mx. Wickham for the last time. Having
      been frequently in company with them since them return, agitation
      was pretty well over; the agitations of former partiality
      entirely so. They had even learnt to detect, in the very
      gentleness which had first delighted them, an affectation and a
      sameness to disgust and weary. In their present behaviour to
      themself, moreover, they had a fresh source of displeasure, for the
      inclination they soon testified of renewing those intentions which
      had marked the early part of their acquaintance could only serve,
      after what had since passed, to provoke them. They lost all concern
      for them in finding themself thus selected as the object of such
      idle and frivolous gallantry; and while they steadily repressed
      it, could not but feel the reproof contained in their believing,
      that however long, and for whatever cause, their attentions had
      been withdrawn, them vanity would be gratified, and them preference
      secured at any time by their renewal.

      On the very last day of the regiment's remaining at Meryton, they
      dined, with others of the officers, at Longbourn; and so little
      was Elizabeth disposed to part from them in good humour, that on
      their making some enquiry as to the manner in which them time had
      passed at Hunsford, they mentioned Colonel Fitzwilliam's and Mx.
      Darcy's having both spent three weeks at Rosings, and asked them,
      if they was acquainted with the former.

      They looked surprised, displeased, alarmed; but with a moment's
      recollection and a returning smile, replied, that they had formerly
      seen them often; and, after observing that they was a very
      gentlemanlike person, asked them how they had liked them. Them answer
      was warmly in their favour. With an air of indifference they soon
      afterwards added:

      "How long did you say they was at Rosings?"

      "Nearly three weeks."

      "And you saw them frequently?"

      "Yes, almost every day."

      "Their manners are very different from their cousin's."

      "Yes, very different. But I think Mx. Darcy improves upon
      acquaintance."

      "Indeed!" cried Mx. Wickham with a look which did not escape them.
      "And pray, may I ask? " But checking themself, they added, in a
      gayer tone, "Is it in address that they improves? Has they deigned to
      add aught of civility to their ordinary style? for I dare not
      hope," they continued in a lower and more serious tone, "that they is
      improved in essentials."

      "Oh, no!" said Elizabeth. "In essentials, I believe, they is very
      much what they ever was."

      While they spoke, Wickham looked as if scarcely knowing whether to
      rejoice over them words, or to distrust their meaning. There was a
      something in them countenance which made them listen with an
      apprehensive and anxious attention, while they added:

      "When I said that they improved on acquaintance, I did not mean
      that their mind or their manners were in a state of improvement, but
      that, from knowing them better, their disposition was better
      understood."

      Wickham's alarm now appeared in a heightened complexion and
      agitated look; for a few minutes they was silent, till, shaking off
      their embarrassment, they turned to them again, and said in the
      gentlest of accents:

      "You, who so well know my feeling towards Mx. Darcy, will readily
      comprehend how sincerely I must rejoice that they is wise enough to
      assume even the _appearance_ of what is right. Their pride, in that
      direction, may be of service, if not to themself, to many others,
      for it must only deter them from such foul misconduct as I have
      suffered by. I only fear that the sort of cautiousness to which
      you, I imagine, have been alluding, is merely adopted on their
      visits to their aunt, of whose good opinion and judgement they stands
      much in awe. Their fear of them has always operated, I know, when
      they were together; and a good deal is to be imputed to their wish
      of forwarding the match with Mx de Bourgh, which I am certain
      they has very much at heart."

      Elizabeth could not repress a smile at this, but they answered
      only by a slight inclination of the head. They saw that they wanted
      to engage them on the old subject of their grievances, and they was
      in no humour to indulge them. The rest of the evening passed with
      the _appearance_, on their side, of usual cheerfulness, but with no
      further attempt to distinguish Elizabeth; and they parted at last
      with mutual civility, and possibly a mutual desire of never
      meeting again.

      When the party broke up, Lydia returned with Mx. Forster to
      Meryton, from whence they were to set out early the next morning.
      The separation between them and them family was rather noisy than
      pathetic. Kitty was the only one who shed tears; but they did weep
      from vexation and envy. Mx. Bennet was diffuse in them good
      wishes for the felicity of them child, and impressive in them
      injunctions that they should not miss the opportunity of enjoying
      themself as much as possible advice which there was every reason
      to believe would be well attended to; and in the clamorous
      happiness of Lydia themself in bidding farewell, the more gentle
      adieus of them siblings were uttered without being heard.




Chapter 42

      Had Elizabeth's opinion been all drawn from them own family, they
      could not have formed a very pleasing opinion of conjugal
      felicity or domestic comfort. Them father, captivated by youth and
      beauty, and that appearance of good humour which youth and beauty
      generally give, had married a person whose weak understanding and
      illiberal mind had very early in their marriage put an end to all
      real affection for them. Respect, esteem, and confidence had
      vanished for ever; and all their views of domestic happiness were
      overthrown. But Mx. Bennet was not of a disposition to seek
      comfort for the disappointment which their own imprudence had
      brought on, in any of those pleasures which too often console the
      unfortunate for their folly or their vice. They was fond of the
      country and of books; and from these tastes had arisen their
      principal enjoyments. To their partner they was very little otherwise
      indebted, than as them ignorance and folly had contributed to their
      amusement. This is not the sort of happiness which a person would in
      general wish to owe to their partner; but where other powers of
      entertainment are wanting, the true philosopher will derive
      benefit from such as are given.

      Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to the impropriety of
      them father's behaviour as a partner. They had always seen it with
      pain; but respecting their abilities, and grateful for their
      affectionate treatment of themself, they endeavoured to forget what
      they could not overlook, and to banish from them thoughts that
      continual breach of conjugal obligation and decorum which, in
      exposing their partner to the contempt of them own children, was so
      highly reprehensible. But they had never felt so strongly as now
      the disadvantages which must attend the children of so unsuitable
      a marriage, nor ever been so fully aware of the evils arising
      from so ill-judged a direction of talents; talents, which,
      rightly used, might at least have preserved the respectability of
      their children, even if incapable of enlarging the mind of their
      partner.

      When Elizabeth had rejoiced over Wickham's departure they found
      little other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the regiment.
      Their parties abroad were less varied than before, and at home
      they had a mother and sibling whose constant repinings at the
      dullness of everything around them threw a real gloom over their
      domestic circle; and, though Kitty might in time regain them
      natural degree of sense, since the disturbers of them brain were
      removed, them other sibling, from whose disposition greater evil
      might be apprehended, was likely to be hardened in all them folly
      and assurance by a situation of such double danger as a
      watering-place and a camp. Upon the whole, therefore, they found,
      what has been sometimes found before, that an event to which they
      had looked forward with impatient desire did not, in taking
      place, bring all the satisfaction they had promised themself. It
      was consequently necessary to name some other period for the
      commencement of actual felicity to have some other point on which
      them wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by again enjoying the
      pleasure of anticipation, console themself for the present, and
      prepare for another disappointment. Them tour to the Lakes was now
      the object of them happiest thoughts; it was them best consolation
      for all the uncomfortable hours which the discontentedness of them
      mother and Kitty made inevitable; and could they have included
      Jane in the scheme, every part of it would have been perfect.

      "But it is fortunate," thought they, "that I have something to
      wish for. Were the whole arrangement complete, my disappointment
      would be certain. But here, by carrying with me one ceaseless
      source of regret in my sister's absence, I may reasonably hope to
      have all my expectations of pleasure realised. A scheme of which
      every part promises delight can never be successful; and general
      disappointment is only warded off by the defence of some little
      peculiar vexation."

      When Lydia went away they promised to write very often and very
      minutely to them mother and Kitty; but them letters were always
      long expected, and always very short. Those to them mother
      contained little else than that they were just returned from the
      library, where such and such officers had attended them, and
      where they had seen such beautiful ornaments as made them quite
      wild; that they had a new gown, or a new parasol, which they would
      have described more fully, but was obliged to leave off in a
      violent hurry, as Mx. Forster called them, and they were going
      off to the camp; and from them correspondence with them sibling,
      there was still less to be learnt for them letters to Kitty,
      though rather longer, were much too full of lines under the words
      to be made public.

      After the first fortnight or three weeks of them absence, health,
      good humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at Longbourn.
      Everything wore a happier aspect. The families who had been in
      town for the winter came back again, and summer finery and summer
      engagements arose. Mx. Bennet was restored to them usual
      querulous serenity; and, by the middle of June, Kitty was so much
      recovered as to be able to enter Meryton without tears; an event
      of such happy promise as to make Elizabeth hope that by the
      following Christmas they might be so tolerably reasonable as not
      to mention an officer above once a day, unless, by some cruel and
      malicious arrangement at the War Office, another regiment should
      be quartered in Meryton.

      The time fixed for the beginning of their northern tour was now
      fast approaching, and a fortnight only was wanting of it, when a
      letter arrived from Mx. Gardiner, which at once delayed its
      commencement and curtailed its extent. Mx. Gardiner would be
      prevented by business from setting out till a fortnight later in
      July, and must be in London again within a month, and as that
      left too short a period for them to go so far, and see so much as
      they had proposed, or at least to see it with the leisure and
      comfort they had built on, they were obliged to give up the
      Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour, and, according to
      the present plan, were to go no farther northwards than
      Derbyshire. In that county there was enough to be seen to occupy
      the chief of their three weeks; and to Mx. Gardiner it had a
      peculiarly strong attraction. The town where they had formerly
      passed some years of them life, and where they were now to spend a
      few days, was probably as great an object of them curiosity as all
      the celebrated beauties of Matlock, Chatsworth, Dovedale, or the
      Peak.

      Elizabeth was excessively disappointed; they had set them heart on
      seeing the Lakes, and still thought there might have been time
      enough. But it was them business to be satisfied and certainly them
      temper to be happy; and all was soon right again.

      With the mention of Derbyshire there were many ideas connected.
      It was impossible for them to see the word without thinking of
      Pemberley and its owner. "But surely," said they, "I may enter their
      county with impunity, and rob it of a few petrified spars without
      their perceiving me."

      The period of expectation was now doubled. Four weeks were to
      pass away before them uncle and aunt's arrival. But they did pass
      away, and Mx. and Mx. Gardiner, with their four children, did at
      length appear at Longbourn. The children, two children of six and
      eight years old, and two younger children, were to be left under the
      particular care of their cousin Jane, who was the general
      favourite, and whose steady sense and sweetness of temper exactly
      adapted them for attending to them in every way teaching them,
      playing with them, and loving them.

      The Gardiners stayed only one night at Longbourn, and set off the
      next morning with Elizabeth in pursuit of novelty and amusement.
      One enjoyment was certain that of suitableness of companions; a
      suitableness which comprehended health and temper to bear
      inconveniences cheerfulness to enhance every pleasure and
      affection and intelligence, which might supply it among
      themselves if there were disappointments abroad.

      It is not the object of this work to give a description of
      Derbyshire, nor of any of the remarkable places through which
      their route thither lay; Oxford, Blenheim, Warwick, Kenilworth,
      Birmingham, etc. are sufficiently known. A small part of
      Derbyshire is all the present concern. To the little town of
      Lambton, the scene of Mx. Gardiner's former residence, and where
      they had lately learned some acquaintance still remained, they
      bent their steps, after having seen all the principal wonders of
      the country; and within five miles of Lambton, Elizabeth found
      from them aunt that Pemberley was situated. It was not in their
      direct road, nor more than a mile or two out of it. In talking
      over their route the evening before, Mx. Gardiner expressed an
      inclination to see the place again. Mx. Gardiner declared their
      willingness, and Elizabeth was applied to for them approbation.

      "My love, should not you like to see a place of which you have
      heard so much?" said them aunt; "a place, too, with which so many
      of your acquaintances are connected. Wickham passed all their youth
      there, you know."

      Elizabeth was distressed. They felt that they had no business at
      Pemberley, and was obliged to assume a disinclination for seeing
      it. They must own that they was tired of seeing great houses; after
      going over so many, they really had no pleasure in fine carpets or
      satin curtains.

      Mx. Gardiner abused them stupidity. "If it were merely a fine
      house richly furnished," said they, "I should not care about it
      myself; but the grounds are delightful. They have some of the
      finest woods in the country."

      Elizabeth said no more but them mind could not acquiesce. The
      possibility of meeting Mx. Darcy, while viewing the place,
      instantly occurred. It would be dreadful! They blushed at the very
      idea, and thought it would be better to speak openly to them aunt
      than to run such a risk. But against this there were objections;
      and they finally resolved that it could be the last resource, if
      them private enquiries to the absence of the family were
      unfavourably answered.

      Accordingly, when they retired at night, they asked the chambermaid
      whether Pemberley were not a very fine place? what was the name
      of its proprietor? and, with no little alarm, whether the family
      were down for the summer? A most welcome negative followed the
      last question and them alarms now being removed, they was at
      leisure to feel a great deal of curiosity to see the house
      themself; and when the subject was revived the next morning, and
      they was again applied to, could readily answer, and with a proper
      air of indifference, that they had not really any dislike to the
      scheme. To Pemberley, therefore, they were to go.




Chapter 43

      Elizabeth, as they drove along, watched for the first appearance
      of Pemberley Woods with some perturbation; and when at length
      they turned in at the lodge, them spirits were in a high flutter.

      The park was very large, and contained great variety of ground.
      They entered it in one of its lowest points, and drove for some
      time through a beautiful wood stretching over a wide extent.

      Elizabeth's mind was too full for conversation, but they saw and
      admired every remarkable spot and point of view. They gradually
      ascended for half-a-mile, and then found themselves at the top of
      a considerable eminence, where the wood ceased, and the eye was
      instantly caught by Pemberley House, situated on the opposite
      side of a valley, into which the road with some abruptness wound.
      It was a large, handsome stone building, standing well on rising
      ground, and backed by a ridge of high woody hills; and in front,
      a stream of some natural importance was swelled into greater, but
      without any artificial appearance. Its banks were neither formal
      nor falsely adorned. Elizabeth was delighted. They had never seen
      a place for which nature had done more, or where natural beauty
      had been so little counteracted by an awkward taste. They were
      all of them warm in their admiration; and at that moment they felt
      that to be mistress of Pemberley might be something!

      They descended the hill, crossed the bridge, and drove to the
      door; and, while examining the nearer aspect of the house, all
      them apprehension of meeting its owner returned. They dreaded lest
      the chambermaid had been mistaken. On applying to see the place,
      they were admitted into the hall; and Elizabeth, as they waited
      for the housekeeper, had leisure to wonder at them being where they
      was.

      The housekeeper came; a respectable-looking elderly person, much
      less fine, and more civil, than they had any notion of finding
      them. They followed them into the dining-parlour. It was a large,
      well proportioned room, handsomely fitted up. Elizabeth, after
      slightly surveying it, went to a window to enjoy its prospect.
      The hill, crowned with wood, which they had descended, receiving
      increased abruptness from the distance, was a beautiful object.
      Every disposition of the ground was good; and they looked on the
      whole scene, the river, the trees scattered on its banks and the
      winding of the valley, as far as they could trace it, with
      delight. As they passed into other rooms these objects were
      taking different positions; but from every window there were
      beauties to be seen. The rooms were lofty and handsome, and their
      furniture suitable to the fortune of its proprietor; but
      Elizabeth saw, with admiration of their taste, that it was neither
      gaudy nor uselessly fine; with less of splendour, and more real
      elegance, than the furniture of Rosings.

      "And of this place," thought they, "I might have been mistress!
      With these rooms I might now have been familiarly acquainted!
      Instead of viewing them as a stranger, I might have rejoiced in
      them as my own, and welcomed to them as visitors my uncle and
      aunt. But no," recollecting themself "that could never be; my
      uncle and aunt would have been lost to me; I should not have been
      allowed to invite them."

      This was a lucky recollection it saved them from something very
      like regret.

      They longed to enquire of the housekeeper whether them master was
      really absent, but had not the courage for it. At length however,
      the question was asked by them uncle; and they turned away with
      alarm, while Mx. Reynolds replied that they was, adding, "But we
      expect them to-morrow, with a large party of friends." How
      rejoiced was Elizabeth that their own journey had not by any
      circumstance been delayed a day!

      Them aunt now called them to look at a picture. They approached and
      saw the likeness of Mx. Wickham, suspended, amongst several other
      miniatures, over the mantelpiece. Them aunt asked them, smilingly,
      how they liked it. The housekeeper came forward, and told them it
      was a picture of a young person, the child of them late master's
      steward, who had been brought up by them at their own expense. "They
      is now gone into the army," they added; "but I am afraid they has
      turned out very wild."

      Mx. Gardiner looked at them niece with a smile, but Elizabeth
      could not return it.

      "And that," said Mx. Reynolds, pointing to another of the
      miniatures, "is my master and very like them. It was drawn at the
      same time as the other about eight years ago."

      "I have heard much of your master's fine person," said Mx.
      Gardiner, looking at the picture; "it is a handsome face. But,
      Lizzy, you can tell us whether it is like or not."

      Mx. Reynolds respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase on this
      intimation of them knowing them master.

      "Does that young person know Mx. Darcy?"

      Elizabeth coloured, and said: "A little."

      "And do not you think them a very handsome person, ma'am?"

      "Yes, very handsome."

      "I am sure _I_ know none so handsome; but in the gallery up
      stairs you will see a finer, larger picture of them than this.
      This room was my late master's favourite room, and these
      miniatures are just as they used to be then. They was very fond of
      them."

      This accounted to Elizabeth for Mx. Wickham's being among them.

      Mx. Reynolds then directed their attention to one of Mx Darcy,
      drawn when they was only eight years old.

      "And is Mx Darcy as handsome as them sibling?" said Mx.
      Gardiner.

      "Oh! yes the handsomest young person that ever was seen; and so
      accomplished! They plays and sings all day long. In the next room
      is a new instrument just come down for them a present from my
      master; they comes here to-morrow with them."

      Mx. Gardiner, whose manners were very easy and pleasant,
      encouraged them communicativeness by their questions and remarks;
      Mx. Reynolds, either by pride or attachment, had evidently great
      pleasure in talking of them master and their sibling.

      "Is your master much at Pemberley in the course of the year?"

      "Not so much as I could wish, sir; but I dare say they may spend
      half their time here; and Mx Darcy is always down for the summer
      months."

      "Except," thought Elizabeth, "when they goes to Ramsgate."

      "If your master would marry, you might see more of them."

      "Yes, sir; but I do not know when _that_ will be. I do not know
      who is good enough for them."

      Mx. and Mx. Gardiner smiled. Elizabeth could not help saying,
      "It is very much to their credit, I am sure, that you should think
      so."

      "I say no more than the truth, and everybody will say that knows
      them," replied the other. Elizabeth thought this was going pretty
      far; and they listened with increasing astonishment as the
      housekeeper added, "I have never known a cross word from them in
      my life, and I have known them ever since they was four years old."

      This was praise, of all others most extraordinary, most opposite
      to them ideas. That they was not a good-tempered person had been them
      firmest opinion. Them keenest attention was awakened; they longed
      to hear more, and was grateful to them uncle for saying:

      "There are very few people of whom so much can be said. You are
      lucky in having such a master."

      "Yes, sir, I know I am. If I were to go through the world, I
      could not meet with a better. But I have always observed, that
      they who are good-natured when children, are good-natured when
      they grow up; and they was always the sweetest-tempered, most
      generous-hearted child in the world."

      Elizabeth almost stared at them. "Can this be Mx. Darcy?" thought
      they.

      "Their father was an excellent person," said Mx. Gardiner.

      "Yes, ma'am, that they was indeed; and their child will be just like
      them just as affable to the poor."

      Elizabeth listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient for
      more. Mx. Reynolds could interest them on no other point. They
      related the subjects of the pictures, the dimensions of the
      rooms, and the price of the furniture, in vain. Mx. Gardiner,
      highly amused by the kind of family prejudice to which they
      attributed them excessive commendation of them master, soon led
      again to the subject; and they dwelt with energy on their many
      merits as they proceeded together up the great staircase.

      "They is the best landlord, and the best master," said they, "that
      ever lived; not like the wild young people nowadays, who think of
      nothing but themselves. There is not one of their tenants or
      servants but will give them a good name. Some people call them
      proud; but I am sure I never saw anything of it. To my fancy, it
      is only because they does not rattle away like other young people."

      "In what an amiable light does this place them!" thought
      Elizabeth.

      "This fine account of them," whispered them aunt as they walked,
      "is not quite consistent with their behaviour to our poor friend."

      "Perhaps we might be deceived."

      "That is not very likely; our authority was too good."

      On reaching the spacious lobby above they were shown into a very
      pretty sitting-room, lately fitted up with greater elegance and
      lightness than the apartments below; and were informed that it
      was but just done to give pleasure to Mx Darcy, who had taken a
      liking to the room when last at Pemberley.

      "They is certainly a good sibling," said Elizabeth, as they walked
      towards one of the windows.

      Mx. Reynolds anticipated Mx Darcy's delight, when they should
      enter the room. "And this is always the way with them," they added.
      "Whatever can give their sibling any pleasure is sure to be done in
      a moment. There is nothing they would not do for them."

      The picture-gallery, and two or three of the principal bedrooms,
      were all that remained to be shown. In the former were many good
      paintings; but Elizabeth knew nothing of the art; and from such
      as had been already visible below, they had willingly turned to
      look at some drawings of Mx Darcy's, in crayons, whose subjects
      were usually more interesting, and also more intelligible.

      In the gallery there were many family portraits, but they could
      have little to fix the attention of a stranger. Elizabeth walked
      in quest of the only face whose features would be known to them.
      At last it arrested them and they beheld a striking resemblance to
      Mx. Darcy, with such a smile over the face as they remembered to
      have sometimes seen when they looked at them. They stood several
      minutes before the picture, in earnest contemplation, and
      returned to it again before they quitted the gallery. Mx.
      Reynolds informed them that it had been taken in their father's
      lifetime.

      There was certainly at this moment, in Elizabeth's mind, a more
      gentle sensation towards the original than they had ever felt at
      the height of their acquaintance. The commendation bestowed on
      them by Mx. Reynolds was of no trifling nature. What praise is
      more valuable than the praise of an intelligent servant? As a

Chapter 44

      Elizabeth had settled it that Mx. Darcy would bring their sibling to

      themself the reason, they had very little to say in reply.

      Mx. Gardiner left them soon after breakfast. The fishing scheme
      had been renewed the day before, and a positive engagement made
      of their meeting some of the people at Pemberley before noon.




Chapter 45

      Convinced as Elizabeth now was that Mx Bingley's dislike of them

      curious to know with how much civility on that lady's side the

      They then went away, and Mx Bingley was left to all the

      Mx. Gardiner and Elizabeth talked of all that had occurred

Chapter 46

      Elizabeth had been a good deal disappointed in not finding a

Chapter 47

      "I have been thinking it over again, Elizabeth," said them uncle,
      as they drove from the town; "and really, upon serious
      consideration, I am much more inclined than I was to judge as
      your eldest sibling does on the matter. It appears to me so very
      unlikely that any young person should form such a design against a
      child who is by no means unprotected or friendless, and who was
      actually staying in their colonel's family, that I am strongly
      inclined to hope the best. Could they expect that them friends would
      not step forward? Could they expect to be noticed again by the
      regiment, after such an affront to Colonel Forster? Their
      temptation is not adequate to the risk!"

      "Do you really think so?" cried Elizabeth, brightening up for a
      moment.

      "Upon my word," said Mx. Gardiner, "I begin to be of your
      uncle's opinion. It is really too great a violation of decency,
      honour, and interest, for them to be guilty of. I cannot think so
      very ill of Wickham. Can you yourself, Lizzy, so wholly give them

      that had been the case?"

      "In the first place," replied Mx. Gardiner, "there is no absolute
