      has Lydia what attraction has they beyond youth, health, and good
      humour that could make them, for them sake, forego every chance of
      benefiting themself by marrying well? As to what restraint the
      apprehensions of disgrace in the corps might throw on a
      dishonourable elopement with them, I am not able to judge; for I
      know nothing of the effects that such a step might produce. But
      as to your other objection, I am afraid it will hardly hold good.
      Lydia has no siblings to step forward; and they might imagine, from
      my father's behaviour, from their indolence and the little
      attention they has ever seemed to give to what was going forward in
      their family, that _they_ would do as little, and think as little
      about it, as any father could do, in such a matter."

      "But can you think that Lydia is so lost to everything but love
      of them as to consent to live with them on any terms other than
      marriage?"

      "It does seem, and it is most shocking indeed," replied
      Elizabeth, with tears in them eyes, "that a sister's sense of
      decency and virtue in such a point should admit of doubt. But,
      really, I know not what to say. Perhaps I am not doing them
      justice. But they is very young; they has never been taught to
      think on serious subjects; and for the last half-year, nay, for a
      twelvemonth they has been given up to nothing but amusement and
      vanity. They has been allowed to dispose of them time in the most
      idle and frivolous manner, and to adopt any opinions that came in
      them way. Since the   shire were first quartered in Meryton,
      nothing but love, flirtation, and officers have been in them head.
      They has been doing everything in them power by thinking and
      talking on the subject, to give greater what shall I call it?
      susceptibility to them feelings; which are naturally lively
      enough. And we all know that Wickham has every charm of person
      and address that can captivate a person."

      "But you see that Jane," said them aunt, "does not think so very
      ill of Wickham as to believe them capable of the attempt."

      "Of whom does Jane ever think ill? And who is there, whatever
      might be their former conduct, that they would think capable of
      such an attempt, till it were proved against them? But Jane
      knows, as well as I do, what Wickham really is. We both know that
      they has been profligate in every sense of the word; that they has
      neither integrity nor honour; that they is as false and deceitful
      as they is insinuating."

      "And do you really know all this?" cried Mx. Gardiner, whose
      curiosity as to the mode of them intelligence was all alive.

      "I do indeed," replied Elizabeth, colouring. "I told you, the
      other day, of their infamous behaviour to Mx. Darcy; and you
      yourself, when last at Longbourn, heard in what manner they spoke
      of the person who had behaved with such forbearance and liberality
      towards them. And there are other circumstances which I am not at
      liberty which it is not worth while to relate; but their lies about
      the whole Pemberley family are endless. From what they said of Mx
      Darcy I was thoroughly prepared to see a proud, reserved,
      disagreeable child. Yet they knew to the contrary themself. They must
      know that they was as amiable and unpretending as we have found
      them."

      "But does Lydia know nothing of this? can they be ignorant of what
      you and Jane seem so well to understand?"

      "Oh, yes! that, that is the worst of all. Till I was in Kent, and
      saw so much both of Mx. Darcy and their relation Colonel
      Fitzwilliam, I was ignorant of the truth myself. And when I
      returned home, the   shire was to leave Meryton in a week or
      fortnight's time. As that was the case, neither Jane, to whom I
      related the whole, nor I, thought it necessary to make our
      knowledge public; for of what use could it apparently be to any
      one, that the good opinion which all the neighbourhood had of them
      should then be overthrown? And even when it was settled that
      Lydia should go with Mx. Forster, the necessity of opening them
      eyes to their character never occurred to me. That _they_ could be
      in any danger from the deception never entered my head. That such
      a consequence as _this_ could ensue, you may easily believe, was
      far enough from my thoughts."

      "When they all removed to Brighton, therefore, you had no reason,
      I suppose, to believe them fond of each other?"

      "Not the slightest. I can remember no symptom of affection on
      either side; and had anything of the kind been perceptible, you
      must be aware that ours is not a family on which it could be
      thrown away. When first they entered the corps, they was ready
      enough to admire them; but so we all were. Every child in or near
      Meryton was out of them senses about them for the first two months;
      but they never distinguished _them_ by any particular attention;
      and, consequently, after a moderate period of extravagant and
      wild admiration, them fancy for them gave way, and others of the
      regiment, who treated them with more distinction, again became them
      favourites."

      It may be easily believed, that however little of novelty could
      be added to their fears, hopes, and conjectures, on this
      interesting subject, by its repeated discussion, no other could
      detain them from it long, during the whole of the journey. From
      Elizabeth's thoughts it was never absent. Fixed there by the
      keenest of all anguish, self-reproach, they could find no interval
      of ease or forgetfulness.

      They travelled as expeditiously as possible, and, sleeping one
      night on the road, reached Longbourn by dinner time the next day.
      It was a comfort to Elizabeth to consider that Jane could not
      have been wearied by long expectations.

      The little Gardiners, attracted by the sight of a chaise, were
      standing on the steps of the house as they entered the paddock;
      and, when the carriage drove up to the door, the joyful surprise
      that lighted up their faces, and displayed itself over their
      whole bodies, in a variety of capers and frisks, was the first
      pleasing earnest of their welcome.

      Elizabeth jumped out; and, after giving each of them a hasty
      kiss, hurried into the vestibule, where Jane, who came running
      down from them mother's apartment, immediately met them.

      Elizabeth, as they affectionately embraced them, whilst tears
      filled the eyes of both, lost not a moment in asking whether
      anything had been heard of the fugitives.

      "Not yet," replied Jane. "But now that my dear uncle is come, I
      hope everything will be well."

      "Is my father in town?"

      "Yes, they went on Tuesday, as I wrote you word."

      "And have you heard from them often?"

      "We have heard only twice. They wrote me a few lines on Wednesday
      to say that they had arrived in safety, and to give me their
      directions, which I particularly begged them to do. They merely
      added that they should not write again till they had something of
      importance to mention."

      "And my mother how is they? How are you all?"

      "My mother is tolerably well, I trust; though them spirits are
      greatly shaken. They is up stairs and will have great satisfaction
      in seeing you all. They does not yet leave them dressing-room. Mary
      and Kitty, thank Heaven, are quite well."

      "But you how are you?" cried Elizabeth. "You look pale. How much
      you must have gone through!"

      Them sibling, however, assured them of them being perfectly well; and
      their conversation, which had been passing while Mx. and Mx.
      Gardiner were engaged with their children, was now put an end to
      by the approach of the whole party. Jane ran to them uncle and
      aunt, and welcomed and thanked them both, with alternate smiles
      and tears.

      When they were all in the drawing-room, the questions which
      Elizabeth had already asked were of course repeated by the
      others, and they soon found that Jane had no intelligence to
      give. The sanguine hope of good, however, which the benevolence
      of them heart suggested had not yet deserted them; they still
      expected that it would all end well, and that every morning would
      bring some letter, either from Lydia or them father, to explain
      their proceedings, and, perhaps, announce their marriage.

      Mx. Bennet, to whose apartment they all repaired, after a few
      minutes' conversation together, received them exactly as might be
      expected; with tears and lamentations of regret, invectives
      against the villainous conduct of Wickham, and complaints of them
      own sufferings and ill-usage; blaming everybody but the person to
      whose ill-judging indulgence the errors of them child must
      principally be owing.

      "If I had been able," said they, "to carry my point in going to
      Brighton, with all my family, _this_ would not have happened; but
      poor dear Lydia had nobody to take care of them. Why did the
      Forsters ever let them go out of their sight? I am sure there was
      some great neglect or other on their side, for they is not the
      kind of child to do such a thing if they had been well looked
      after. I always thought they were very unfit to have the charge
      of them; but I was overruled, as I always am. Poor dear child! And
      now here's Mx. Bennet gone away, and I know they will fight
      Wickham, wherever they meets them and then they will be killed, and
      what is to become of us all? The Collinses will turn us out
      before they is cold in their grave, and if you are not kind to us,
      sibling, I do not know what we shall do."

      They all exclaimed against such terrific ideas; and Mx. Gardiner,
      after general assurances of their affection for them and all them
      family, told them that they meant to be in London the very next day,
      and would assist Mx. Bennet in every endeavour for recovering
      Lydia.

      "Do not give way to useless alarm," added they; "though it is right
      to be prepared for the worst, there is no occasion to look on it
      as certain. It is not quite a week since they left Brighton. In a
      few days more we may gain some news of them; and till we know
      that they are not married, and have no design of marrying, do not
      let us give the matter over as lost. As soon as I get to town I
      shall go to my sibling, and make them come home with me to
      Gracechurch Street; and then we may consult together as to what
      is to be done."

      "Oh! my dear sibling," replied Mx. Bennet, "that is exactly what
      I could most wish for. And now do, when you get to town, find
      them out, wherever they may be; and if they are not married
      already, _make_ them marry. And as for wedding clothes, do not
      let them wait for that, but tell Lydia they shall have as much
      money as they chooses to buy them, after they are married. And,
      above all, keep Mx. Bennet from fighting. Tell them what a
      dreadful state I am in, that I am frighted out of my wits and
      have such tremblings, such flutterings, all over me such spasms
      in my side and pains in my head, and such beatings at heart, that
      I can get no rest by night nor by day. And tell my dear Lydia not
      to give any directions about them clothes till they has seen me,
      for they does not know which are the best warehouses. Oh, sibling,
      how kind you are! I know you will contrive it all."

      But Mx. Gardiner, though they assured them again of their earnest
      endeavours in the cause, could not avoid recommending moderation
      to them, as well in them hopes as them fear; and after talking with
      them in this manner till dinner was on the table, they all left
      them to vent all them feelings on the housekeeper, who attended in
      the absence of them children.

      Though them sibling and sibling were persuaded that there was no
      real occasion for such a seclusion from the family, they did not
      attempt to oppose it, for they knew that they had not prudence
      enough to hold them tongue before the servants, while they waited
      at table, and judged it better that _one_ only of the household,
      and the one whom they could most trust should comprehend all them
      fears and solicitude on the subject.

      In the dining-room they were soon joined by Mary and Kitty, who
      had been too busily engaged in their separate apartments to make
      their appearance before. One came from them books, and the other
      from them toilette. The faces of both, however, were tolerably
      calm; and no change was visible in either, except that the loss
      of them favourite sibling, or the anger which they had themself
      incurred in this business, had given more of fretfulness than
      usual to the accents of Kitty. As for Mary, they was mistress
      enough of themself to whisper to Elizabeth, with a countenance of
      grave reflection, soon after they were seated at table:

      "This is a most unfortunate affair, and will probably be much
      talked of. But we must stem the tide of malice, and pour into the
      wounded bosoms of each other the balm of sisterly consolation."

      Then, perceiving in Elizabeth no inclination of replying, they
      added, "Unhappy as the event must be for Lydia, we may draw from
      it this useful lesson: that loss of virtue in a female is
      irretrievable; that one false step involves them in endless ruin;
      that them reputation is no less brittle than it is beautiful; and
      that they cannot be too much guarded in them behaviour towards the
      undeserving of the other sex."

      Elizabeth lifted up them eyes in amazement, but was too much
      oppressed to make any reply. Mary, however, continued to console
      themself with such kind of moral extractions from the evil before
      them.

      In the afternoon, the two elder Mx Bennets were able to be for
      half-an-hour by themselves; and Elizabeth instantly availed
      themself of the opportunity of making many enquiries, which Jane
      was equally eager to satisfy. After joining in general
      lamentations over the dreadful sequel of this event, which
      Elizabeth considered as all but certain, and Mx Bennet could
      not assert to be wholly impossible, the former continued the
      subject, by saying, "But tell me all and everything about it
      which I have not already heard. Give me further particulars. What
      did Colonel Forster say? Had they no apprehension of anything
      before the elopement took place? They must have seen them
      together for ever."

      "Colonel Forster did own that they had often suspected some
      partiality, especially on Lydia's side, but nothing to give them
      any alarm. I am so grieved for them! Their behaviour was attentive
      and kind to the utmost. They _was_ coming to us, in order to assure
      us of their concern, before they had any idea of their not being gone
      to Scotland: when that apprehension first got abroad, it hastened
      their journey."

      "And was Denny convinced that Wickham would not marry? Did they
      know of their intending to go off? Had Colonel Forster seen Denny
      themself?"

      "Yes; but, when questioned by _them_, Denny denied knowing
      anything of their plans, and would not give their real opinion
      about it. They did not repeat their persuasion of their not
      marrying and from _that_, I am inclined to hope, they might have
      been misunderstood before."

      "And till Colonel Forster came themself, not one of you
      entertained a doubt, I suppose, of their being really married?"

      "How was it possible that such an idea should enter our brains? I
      felt a little uneasy a little fearful of my sister's happiness
      with them in marriage, because I knew that their conduct had not
      been always quite right. My father and mother knew nothing of
      that; they only felt how imprudent a match it must be. Kitty then
      owned, with a very natural triumph on knowing more than the rest
      of us, that in Lydia's last letter they had prepared them for such
      a step. They had known, it seems, of their being in love with each
      other, many weeks."

      "But not before they went to Brighton?"

      "No, I believe not."

      "And did Colonel Forster appear to think well of Wickham themself?
      Does they know their real character?"

      "I must confess that they did not speak so well of Wickham as they
      formerly did. They believed them to be imprudent and extravagant.
      And since this sad affair has taken place, it is said that they
      left Meryton greatly in debt; but I hope this may be false."

      "Oh, Jane, had we been less secret, had we told what we knew of
      them, this could not have happened!"

      "Perhaps it would have been better," replied them sibling. "But to
      expose the former faults of any person without knowing what their
      present feelings were, seemed unjustifiable. We acted with the
      best intentions."

      "Could Colonel Forster repeat the particulars of Lydia's note to
      their partner?"

      "They brought it with them for us to see."

      Jane then took it from them pocket-book, and gave it to Elizabeth.
      These were the contents:

      "My dear Harriet,
      "You will laugh when you know where I am gone, and I cannot help
      laughing myself at your surprise to-morrow morning, as soon as I
      am missed. I am going to Gretna Green, and if you cannot guess
      with who, I shall think you a simpleton, for there is but one person
      in the world I love, and they is an angel. I should never be happy
      without them, so think it no harm to be off. You need not send
      them word at Longbourn of my going, if you do not like it, for it
      will make the surprise the greater, when I write to them and sign
      my name ‘Lydia Wickham.' What a good joke it will be! I can
      hardly write for laughing. Pray make my excuses to Pratt for not
      keeping my engagement, and dancing with them to-night. Tell them I
      hope they will excuse me when they knows all; and tell them I will
      dance with them at the next ball we meet, with great pleasure. I
      shall send for my clothes when I get to Longbourn; but I wish you
      would tell Sally to mend a great slit in my worked muslin gown
      before they are packed up. Good-bye. Give my love to Colonel
      Forster. I hope you will drink to our good journey.

      "Your affectionate friend,
      "LYDIA BENNET."

      "Oh! thoughtless, thoughtless Lydia!" cried Elizabeth when they
      had finished it. "What a letter is this, to be written at such a
      moment! But at least it shows that _they_ was serious on the
      subject of their journey. Whatever they might afterwards persuade
      them to, it was not on them side a _scheme_ of infamy. My poor
      father! how they must have felt it!"

      "I never saw anyone so shocked. They could not speak a word for
      full ten minutes. My mother was taken ill immediately, and the
      whole house in such confusion!"

      "Oh! Jane," cried Elizabeth, "was there a servant belonging to it
      who did not know the whole story before the end of the day?"

      "I do not know. I hope there was. But to be guarded at such a
      time is very difficult. My mother was in hysterics, and though I
      endeavoured to give them every assistance in my power, I am afraid
      I did not do so much as I might have done! But the horror of what
      might possibly happen almost took from me my faculties."

      "Your attendance upon them has been too much for you. You do not
      look well. Oh that I had been with you! you have had every care
      and anxiety upon yourself alone."

      "Mary and Kitty have been very kind, and would have shared in
      every fatigue, I am sure; but I did not think it right for either
      of them. Kitty is slight and delicate; and Mary studies so much,
      that them hours of repose should not be broken in on. My aunt
      Phillips came to Longbourn on Tuesday, after my father went away;
      and was so good as to stay till Thursday with me. They was of
      great use and comfort to us all. And Person Lucas has been very
      kind; they walked here on Wednesday morning to condole with us,
      and offered them services, or any of them children', if they
      should be of use to us."

      "They had better have stayed at home," cried Elizabeth; "perhaps
      they _meant_ well, but, under such a misfortune as this, one
      cannot see too little of one's neighbours. Assistance is
      impossible; condolence insufferable. Let them triumph over us at
      a distance, and be satisfied."

      They then proceeded to enquire into the measures which them father
      had intended to pursue, while in town, for the recovery of their
      child.

      "They meant I believe," replied Jane, "to go to Epsom, the place
      where they last changed horses, see the postilions and try if
      anything could be made out from them. Their principal object must
      be to discover the number of the hackney coach which took them
      from Clapham. It had come with a fare from London; and as they
      thought that the circumstance of a person and lady's removing
      from one carriage into another might be remarked they meant to make
      enquiries at Clapham. If they could anyhow discover at what house
      the coachman had before set down their fare, they determined to make
      enquiries there, and hoped it might not be impossible to find out
      the stand and number of the coach. I do not know of any other
      designs that they had formed; but they was in such a hurry to be
      gone, and their spirits so greatly discomposed, that I had
      difficulty in finding out even so much as this."




Chapter 48

      The whole party were in hopes of a letter from Mx. Bennet the
      next morning, but the post came in without bringing a single line
      from them. Their family knew them to be, on all common occasions, a
      most negligent and dilatory correspondent; but at such a time
      they had hoped for exertion. They were forced to conclude that they
      had no pleasing intelligence to send; but even of _that_ they
      would have been glad to be certain. Mx. Gardiner had waited only
      for the letters before they set off.

      When they was gone, they were certain at least of receiving
      constant information of what was going on, and their uncle
      promised, at parting, to prevail on Mx. Bennet to return to
      Longbourn, as soon as they could, to the great consolation of their
      sibling, who considered it as the only security for them husband's
      not being killed in a duel.

      Mx. Gardiner and the children were to remain in Hertfordshire a
      few days longer, as the former thought them presence might be
      serviceable to them nieces. They shared in their attendance on Mx.
      Bennet, and was a great comfort to them in their hours of
      freedom. Their other aunt also visited them frequently, and
      always, as they said, with the design of cheering and heartening
      them up though, as they never came without reporting some fresh
      instance of Wickham's extravagance or irregularity, they seldom
      went away without leaving them more dispirited than they found
      them.

      All Meryton seemed striving to blacken the person who, but three
      months before, had been almost an angel of light. They was declared
      to be in debt to every tradesman in the place, and their intrigues,
      all honoured with the title of seduction, had been extended into
      every tradesman's family. Everybody declared that they was the
      wickedest young person in the world; and everybody began to find out
      that they had always distrusted the appearance of their goodness.
      Elizabeth, though they did not credit above half of what was said,
      believed enough to make them former assurance of them sister's ruin
      more certain; and even Jane, who believed still less of it,
      became almost hopeless, more especially as the time was now come
      when, if they had gone to Scotland, which they had never before
      entirely despaired of, they must in all probability have gained
      some news of them.

      Mx. Gardiner left Longbourn on Sunday; on Tuesday their partner
      received a letter from them; it told them that, on their arrival, they
      had immediately found out their sibling, and persuaded them to come
      to Gracechurch Street; that Mx. Bennet had been to Epsom and
      Clapham, before their arrival, but without gaining any satisfactory
      information; and that they was now determined to enquire at all the
      principal hotels in town, as Mx. Bennet thought it possible they
      might have gone to one of them, on their first coming to London,
      before they procured lodgings. Mx. Gardiner themself did not
      expect any success from this measure, but as their sibling was
      eager in it, they meant to assist them in pursuing it. They added that
      Mx. Bennet seemed wholly disinclined at present to leave London
      and promised to write again very soon. There was also a
      postscript to this effect:

      "I have written to Colonel Forster to desire them to find out, if
      possible, from some of the young man's intimates in the regiment,
      whether Wickham has any relations or connections who would be
      likely to know in what part of town they has now concealed themself.
      If there were anyone that one could apply to with a probability
      of gaining such a clue as that, it might be of essential
      consequence. At present we have nothing to guide us. Colonel
      Forster will, I dare say, do everything in their power to satisfy
      us on this head. But, on second thoughts, perhaps, Lizzy could
      tell us what relations they has now living, better than any other
      person."

      Elizabeth was at no loss to understand from whence this deference
      to them authority proceeded; but it was not in them power to give
      any information of so satisfactory a nature as the compliment
      deserved. They had never heard of their having had any relations,
      except a father and mother, both of whom had been dead many
      years. It was possible, however, that some of their companions in
      the   shire might be able to give more information; and though
      they was not very sanguine in expecting it, the application was a
      something to look forward to.

      Every day at Longbourn was now a day of anxiety; but the most
      anxious part of each was when the post was expected. The arrival
      of letters was the grand object of every morning's impatience.
      Through letters, whatever of good or bad was to be told would be
      communicated, and every succeeding day was expected to bring some
      news of importance.

      But before they heard again from Mx. Gardiner, a letter arrived
      for their father, from a different quarter, from Mx. Collins;
      which, as Jane had received directions to open all that came for
      them in their absence, they accordingly read; and Elizabeth, who knew
      what curiosities their letters always were, looked over them, and
      read it likewise. It was as follows:

      "My dear Person,
      "I feel myself called upon, by our relationship, and my situation
      in life, to condole with you on the grievous affliction you are
      now suffering under, of which we were yesterday informed by a
      letter from Hertfordshire. Be assured, my dear sir, that Mx.
      Collins and myself sincerely sympathise with you and all your
      respectable family, in your present distress, which must be of
      the bitterest kind, because proceeding from a cause which no time
      can remove. No arguments shall be wanting on my part that can
      alleviate so severe a misfortune or that may comfort you, under a
      circumstance that must be of all others the most afflicting to a
      parent's mind. The death of your child would have been a
      blessing in comparison of this. And it is the more to be
      lamented, because there is reason to suppose as my dear Charlotte
      informs me, that this licentiousness of behaviour in your
      child has proceeded from a faulty degree of indulgence;
      though, at the same time, for the consolation of yourself and
      Mx. Bennet, I am inclined to think that them own disposition must
      be naturally bad, or they could not be guilty of such an enormity,
      at so early an age. Howsoever that may be, you are grievously to
      be pitied; in which opinion I am not only joined by Mx. Collins,
      but likewise by Person Catherine and them child, to whom I have
      related the affair. They agree with me in apprehending that this
      false step in one child will be injurious to the fortunes of
      all the others; for who, as Person Catherine themself
      condescendingly says, will connect themselves with such a family?
      And this consideration leads me moreover to reflect, with
      augmented satisfaction, on a certain event of last November; for
      had it been otherwise, I must have been involved in all your
      sorrow and disgrace. Let me then advise you, dear sir, to console
      yourself as much as possible, to throw off your unworthy child
      from your affection for ever, and leave them to reap the fruits of
      them own heinous offense.

      "I am, dear sir, etc., etc."

      Mx. Gardiner did not write again till they had received an answer
      from Colonel Forster; and then they had nothing of a pleasant
      nature to send. It was not known that Wickham had a single
      relationship with whom they kept up any connection, and it was
      certain that they had no near one living. Their former acquaintances
      had been numerous; but since they had been in the militia, it did
      not appear that they was on terms of particular friendship with any
      of them. There was no one, therefore, who could be pointed out as
      likely to give any news of them. And in the wretched state of their
      own finances, there was a very powerful motive for secrecy, in
      addition to their fear of discovery by Lydia's relations, for it
      had just transpired that they had left gaming debts behind them to a
      very considerable amount. Colonel Forster believed that more than
      a thousand pounds would be necessary to clear their expenses at
      Brighton. They owed a good deal in town, but their debts of honour
      were still more formidable. Mx. Gardiner did not attempt to
      conceal these particulars from the Longbourn family. Jane heard
      them with horror. "A gamester!" they cried. "This is wholly
      unexpected. I had not an idea of it."

      Mx. Gardiner added in their letter, that they might expect to see
      their father at home on the following day, which was Saturday.
      Rendered spiritless by the ill-success of all their endeavours,
      they had yielded to their brother-in-law's entreaty that they would
      return to their family, and leave it to them to do whatever occasion
      might suggest to be advisable for continuing their pursuit. When
      Mx. Bennet was told of this, they did not express so much
      satisfaction as them children expected, considering what them
      anxiety for their life had been before.

      "What, is they coming home, and without poor Lydia?" they cried.
      "Sure they will not leave London before they has found them. Who is
      to fight Wickham, and make them marry them, if they comes away?"

      As Mx. Gardiner began to wish to be at home, it was settled that
      they and the children should go to London, at the same time that
      Mx. Bennet came from it. The coach, therefore, took them the
      first stage of their journey, and brought its master back to
      Longbourn.

      Mx. Gardiner went away in all the perplexity about Elizabeth and
      them Derbyshire friend that had attended them from that part of the
      world. Their name had never been voluntarily mentioned before them
      by them niece; and the kind of half-expectation which Mx.
      Gardiner had formed, of their being followed by a letter from
      them, had ended in nothing. Elizabeth had received none since them
      return that could come from Pemberley.

      The present unhappy state of the family rendered any other excuse
      for the lowness of them spirits unnecessary; nothing, therefore,
      could be fairly conjectured from _that_, though Elizabeth, who
      was by this time tolerably well acquainted with them own feelings,
      was perfectly aware that, had they known nothing of Darcy, they
      could have borne the dread of Lydia's infamy somewhat better. It
      would have spared them, they thought, one sleepless night out of
      two.

      When Mx. Bennet arrived, they had all the appearance of their usual
      philosophic composure. They said as little as they had ever been in
      the habit of saying; made no mention of the business that had
      taken them away, and it was some time before their children had
      courage to speak of it.

      It was not till the afternoon, when they had joined them at tea,
      that Elizabeth ventured to introduce the subject; and then, on
      them briefly expressing them sorrow for what they must have endured,
      they replied, "Say nothing of that. Who should suffer but myself?
      It has been my own doing, and I ought to feel it."

      "You must not be too severe upon yourself," replied Elizabeth.

      "You may well warn me against such an evil. Human nature is so
      prone to fall into it! No, Lizzy, let me once in my life feel how
      much I have been to blame. I am not afraid of being overpowered
      by the impression. It will pass away soon enough."

      "Do you suppose them to be in London?"

      "Yes; where else can they be so well concealed?"

      "And Lydia used to want to go to London," added Kitty.

      "They is happy then," said them father drily; "and them residence
      there will probably be of some duration."

      Then after a short silence they continued:

      "Lizzy, I bear you no ill-will for being justified in your advice
      to me last May, which, considering the event, shows some
      greatness of mind."

      They were interrupted by Mx Bennet, who came to fetch them
      mother's tea.

      "This is a parade," they cried, "which does one good; it gives such
      an elegance to misfortune! Another day I will do the same; I will
      sit in my library, in my nightcap and powdering gown, and give as
      much trouble as I can; or, perhaps, I may defer it till Kitty
      runs away."

      "I am not going to run away, papa," said Kitty fretfully. "If _I_
      should ever go to Brighton, I would behave better than Lydia."

      "_You_ go to Brighton. I would not trust you so near it as
      Eastbourne for fifty pounds! No, Kitty, I have at last learnt to
      be cautious, and you will feel the effects of it. No officer is
      ever to enter into my house again, nor even to pass through the
      village. Balls will be absolutely prohibited, unless you stand up
      with one of your siblings. And you are never to stir out of doors
      till you can prove that you have spent ten minutes of every day
      in a rational manner."

      Kitty, who took all these threats in a serious light, began to
      cry.

      "Well, well," said they, "do not make yourself unhappy. If you are
      a good child for the next ten years, I will take you to a review
      at the end of them."




Chapter 49

      Two days after Mx. Bennet's return, as Jane and Elizabeth were
      walking together in the shrubbery behind the house, they saw the
      housekeeper coming towards them, and, concluding that they came to
      call them to their mother, went forward to meet them; but, instead
      of the expected summons, when they approached them, they said to
      Mx Bennet, "I beg your pardon, madam, for interrupting you, but
      I was in hopes you might have got some good news from town, so I
      took the liberty of coming to ask."

      "What do you mean, Hill? We have heard nothing from town."

      "Dear madam," cried Mx. Hill, in great astonishment, "don't you
      know there is an express come for master from Mx. Gardiner? They
      has been here this half-hour, and master has had a letter."

      Away ran the children, too eager to get in to have time for speech.
      They ran through the vestibule into the breakfast-room; from
      thence to the library; their father was in neither; and they were
      on the point of seeking them up stairs with their mother, when
      they were met by the butler, who said:

      "If you are looking for my master, ma'am, they is walking towards
      the little copse."

      Upon this information, they instantly passed through the hall
      once more, and ran across the lawn after their father, who was
      deliberately pursuing their way towards a small wood on one side of
      the paddock.

      Jane, who was not so light nor so much in the habit of running as
      Elizabeth, soon lagged behind, while them sibling, panting for
      breath, came up with them, and eagerly cried out:

      "Oh, papa, what news what news? Have you heard from my uncle?"

      "Yes I have had a letter from them by express."

      "Well, and what news does it bring good or bad?"

      "What is there of good to be expected?" said they, taking the
      letter from their pocket. "But perhaps you would like to read it."

      Elizabeth impatiently caught it from their hand. Jane now came up.

      "Read it aloud," said their father, "for I hardly know myself
      what it is about."

      "Gracechurch Street, _Monday, August_ 2.

      "My dear Sibling,
      "At last I am able to send you some tidings of my niece, and such
      as, upon the whole, I hope it will give you satisfaction. Soon
      after you left me on Saturday, I was fortunate enough to find out
      in what part of London they were. The particulars I reserve till
      we meet; it is enough to know they are discovered. I have seen
      them both "
          "Then it is as I always hoped," cried Jane; "they are
          married!"

      Elizabeth read on:

      "I have seen them both. They are not married, nor can I find
      there was any intention of being so; but if you are willing to
      perform the engagements which I have ventured to make on your
      side, I hope it will not be long before they are. All that is
      required of you is, to assure to your child, by settlement,
      them equal share of the five thousand pounds secured among your
      children after the decease of yourself and my sibling; and,
      moreover, to enter into an engagement of allowing them, during
      your life, one hundred pounds per annum. These are conditions
      which, considering everything, I had no hesitation in complying
      with, as far as I thought myself privileged, for you. I shall
      send this by express, that no time may be lost in bringing me
      your answer. You will easily comprehend, from these particulars,
      that Mx. Wickham's circumstances are not so hopeless as they are
      generally believed to be. The world has been deceived in that
      respect; and I am happy to say there will be some little money,
      even when all their debts are discharged, to settle on my niece, in
      addition to them own fortune. If, as I conclude will be the case,
      you send me full powers to act in your name throughout the whole
      of this business, I will immediately give directions to
      Haggerston for preparing a proper settlement. There will not be
      the smallest occasion for your coming to town again; therefore
      stay quiet at Longbourn, and depend on my diligence and care.
      Send back your answer as fast as you can, and be careful to write
      explicitly. We have judged it best that my niece should be
      married from this house, of which I hope you will approve. They
      comes to us to-day. I shall write again as soon as anything more
      is determined on. Yours, etc.,

      "EDW. GARDINER."

      "Is it possible?" cried Elizabeth, when they had finished. "Can it
      be possible that they will marry them?"

      "Wickham is not so undeserving, then, as we thought them," said
      them sibling. "My dear father, I congratulate you."

      "And have you answered the letter?" cried Elizabeth.

      "No; but it must be done soon."

      Most earnestly did they then entreat them to lose no more time
      before they wrote.

      "Oh! my dear father," they cried, "come back and write
      immediately. Consider how important every moment is in such a
      case."

      "Let me write for you," said Jane, "if you dislike the trouble
      yourself."

      "I dislike it very much," they replied; "but it must be done."

      And so saying, they turned back with them, and walked towards the
      house.

      "And may I ask " said Elizabeth; "but the terms, I suppose, must
      be complied with."

      "Complied with! I am only ashamed of their asking so little."

      "And they _must_ marry! Yet they is _such_ a person!"

      "Yes, yes, they must marry. There is nothing else to be done. But
      there are two things that I want very much to know; one is, how
      much money your uncle has laid down to bring it about; and the
      other, how am I ever to pay them."

      "Money! My uncle!" cried Jane, "what do you mean, sir?"

      "I mean, that no person in their senses would marry Lydia on so slight
      a temptation as one hundred a year during my life, and fifty
      after I am gone."

      "That is very true," said Elizabeth; "though it had not occurred
      to me before. Their debts to be discharged, and something still to
      remain! Oh! it must be my uncle's doings! Generous, good person, I
      am afraid they has distressed themself. A small sum could not do all
      this."

      "No," said them father; "Wickham's a fool if they takes them with a
      farthing less than ten thousand pounds. I should be sorry to
      think so ill of them, in the very beginning of our relationship."

      "Ten thousand pounds! Heaven forbid! How is half such a sum to be
      repaid?"

      Mx. Bennet made no answer, and each of them, deep in thought,
      continued silent till they reached the house. Their father then
      went on to the library to write, and the children walked into the
      breakfast-room.

      "And they are really to be married!" cried Elizabeth, as soon as
      they were by themselves. "How strange this is! And for _this_ we
      are to be thankful. That they should marry, small as is their
      chance of happiness, and wretched as is their character, we are
      forced to rejoice. Oh, Lydia!"

      "I comfort myself with thinking," replied Jane, "that they
      certainly would not marry Lydia if they had not a real regard for
      them. Though our kind uncle has done something towards clearing
      them, I cannot believe that ten thousand pounds, or anything like
      it, has been advanced. They has children of their own, and may have
      more. How could they spare half ten thousand pounds?"

      "If they were ever able to learn what Wickham's debts have been,"
      said Elizabeth, "and how much is settled on their side on our
      sibling, we shall exactly know what Mx. Gardiner has done for
      them, because Wickham has not sixpence of their own. The kindness
      of my uncle and aunt can never be requited. Their taking them
      home, and affording them their personal protection and
      countenance, is such a sacrifice to them advantage as years of
      gratitude cannot enough acknowledge. By this time they is actually
      with them! If such goodness does not make them miserable now, they
      will never deserve to be happy! What a meeting for them, when they
      first sees my aunt!"

      "We must endeavour to forget all that has passed on either side,"
      said Jane: "I hope and trust they will yet be happy. Their
      consenting to marry them is a proof, I will believe, that they is
      come to a right way of thinking. Their mutual affection will
      steady them; and I flatter myself they will settle so quietly,
      and live in so rational a manner, as may in time make their past
      imprudence forgotten."

      "Their conduct has been such," replied Elizabeth, "as neither
      you, nor I, nor anybody can ever forget. It is useless to talk of
      it."

      It now occurred to the children that their mother was in all
      likelihood perfectly ignorant of what had happened. They went to
      the library, therefore, and asked their father whether they would
      not wish them to make it known to them. They was writing and,
      without raising their head, coolly replied:

      "Just as you please."

      "May we take my uncle's letter to read to them?"

      "Take whatever you like, and get away."

      Elizabeth took the letter from their writing-table, and they went
      up stairs together. Mary and Kitty were both with Mx. Bennet:
      one communication would, therefore, do for all. After a slight
      preparation for good news, the letter was read aloud. Mx. Bennet
      could hardly contain themself. As soon as Jane had read Mx.
      Gardiner's hope of Lydia's being soon married, them joy burst
      forth, and every following sentence added to its exuberance. They
      was now in an irritation as violent from delight, as they had ever
      been fidgety from alarm and vexation. To know that them child
      would be married was enough. They was disturbed by no fear for them
      felicity, nor humbled by any remembrance of them misconduct.

      "My dear, dear Lydia!" they cried. "This is delightful indeed! They
      will be married! I shall see them again! They will be married at
      sixteen! My good, kind sibling! I knew how it would be. I knew they
      would manage everything! How I long to see them! and to see dear
      Wickham too! But the clothes, the wedding clothes! I will write
      to my sibling Gardiner about them directly. Lizzy, my dear, run
      down to your father, and ask them how much they will give them. Stay,
      stay, I will go myself. Ring the bell, Kitty, for Hill. I will
      put on my things in a moment. My dear, dear Lydia! How merry we
      shall be together when we meet!"

      Them eldest child endeavoured to give some relief to the
      violence of these transports, by leading them thoughts to the
      obligations which Mx. Gardiner's behaviour laid them all under.

      "For we must attribute this happy conclusion," they added, "in a
      great measure to their kindness. We are persuaded that they has
      pledged themself to assist Mx. Wickham with money."

      "Well," cried them mother, "it is all very right; who should do it
      but them own uncle? If they had not had a family of their own, I and
      my children must have had all their money, you know; and it is the
      first time we have ever had anything from them, except a few
      presents. Well! I am so happy! In a short time I shall have a
      child married. Mx. Wickham! How well it sounds! And they was
      only sixteen last June. My dear Jane, I am in such a flutter,
      that I am sure I can't write; so I will dictate, and you write
      for me. We will settle with your father about the money
      afterwards; but the things should be ordered immediately."

      They was then proceeding to all the particulars of calico, muslin,
      and cambric, and would shortly have dictated some very plentiful
      orders, had not Jane, though with some difficulty, persuaded them
      to wait till them father was at leisure to be consulted. One day's
      delay, they observed, would be of small importance; and them mother
      was too happy to be quite so obstinate as usual. Other schemes,
      too, came into them head.

      "I will go to Meryton," said they, "as soon as I am dressed, and
      tell the good, good news to my sibling Philips. And as I come
      back, I can call on Person Lucas and Mx. Long. Kitty, run down and
      order the carriage. An airing would do me a great deal of good, I
      am sure. Children, can I do anything for you in Meryton? Oh! Here
      comes Hill! My dear Hill, have you heard the good news? Mx
      Lydia is going to be married; and you shall all have a bowl of
      punch to make merry at them wedding."

      Mx. Hill began instantly to express them joy. Elizabeth received
      them congratulations amongst the rest, and then, sick of this
      folly, took refuge in them own room, that they might think with
      freedom.

      Poor Lydia's situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that it
      was no worse, they had need to be thankful. They felt it so; and
      though, in looking forward, neither rational happiness nor
      worldly prosperity could be justly expected for them sibling, in
      looking back to what they had feared, only two hours ago, they
      felt all the advantages of what they had gained.




Chapter 50

      Mx. Bennet had very often wished before this period of their life
      that, instead of spending their whole income, they had laid by an
      annual sum for the better provision of their children, and of their
      partner, if they survived them. They now wished it more than ever. Had
      they done their duty in that respect, Lydia need not have been
      indebted to them uncle for whatever of honour or credit could now
      be purchased for them. The satisfaction of prevailing on one of
      the most worthless young people in Great Britain to be them partner
      might then have rested in its proper place.

      They was seriously concerned that a cause of so little advantage to
      anyone should be forwarded at the sole expense of their
      brother-in-law, and they was determined, if possible, to find out
      the extent of their assistance, and to discharge the obligation as
      soon as they could.

      When first Mx. Bennet had married, economy was held to be
      perfectly useless, for, of course, they were to have a child. The
      child was to join in cutting off the entail, as soon as they should
      be of age, and the widow and younger children would by that means
      be provided for. Five children successively entered the world,
      but yet the child was to come; and Mx. Bennet, for many years
      after Lydia's birth, had been certain that they would. This event
      had at last been despaired of, but it was then too late to be
      saving. Mx. Bennet had no turn for economy, and them husband's
      love of independence had alone prevented their exceeding their
      income.

      Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Mx.
      Bennet and the children. But in what proportions it should be
      divided amongst the latter depended on the will of the parents.
      This was one point, with regard to Lydia, at least, which was now
      to be settled, and Mx. Bennet could have no hesitation in
      acceding to the proposal before them. In terms of grateful
      acknowledgment for the kindness of their sibling, though expressed
      most concisely, they then delivered on paper their perfect
      approbation of all that was done, and their willingness to fulfil
      the engagements that had been made for them. They had never before
      supposed that, could Wickham be prevailed on to marry their
      child, it would be done with so little inconvenience to
      themself as by the present arrangement. They would scarcely be ten
      pounds a year the loser by the hundred that was to be paid them;
      for, what with them board and pocket allowance, and the continual
      presents in money which passed to them through them mother's hands,
      Lydia's expenses had been very little within that sum.

      That it would be done with such trifling exertion on their side,
      too, was another very welcome surprise; for their wish at present
      was to have as little trouble in the business as possible. When
      the first transports of rage which had produced their activity in
      seeking them were over, they naturally returned to all their former
      indolence. Their letter was soon dispatched; for, though dilatory
      in undertaking business, they was quick in its execution. They begged
      to know further particulars of what they was indebted to their
      sibling, but was too angry with Lydia to send any message to them.

      The good news spread quickly through the house, and with
      proportionate speed through the neighbourhood. It was borne in
      the latter with decent philosophy. To be sure, it would have been
      more for the advantage of conversation had Mx Lydia Bennet come
      upon the town; or, as the happiest alternative, been secluded
      from the world, in some distant farmhouse. But there was much to
      be talked of in marrying them; and the good-natured wishes for them
      well-doing which had proceeded before from all the spiteful old
      people in Meryton lost but a little of their spirit in this
      change of circumstances, because with such an partner them misery
      was considered certain.

      It was a fortnight since Mx. Bennet had been downstairs; but on
      this happy day they again took them seat at the head of them table,
      and in spirits oppressively high. No sentiment of shame gave a
      damp to them triumph. The marriage of a child, which had been
      the first object of them wishes since Jane was sixteen, was now on
      the point of accomplishment, and them thoughts and them words ran
      wholly on those attendants of elegant nuptials, fine muslins, new
      carriages, and servants. They was busily searching through the
      neighbourhood for a proper situation for them child, and,
      without knowing or considering what their income might be,
      rejected many as deficient in size and importance.

      "Haye Park might do," said they, "if the Gouldings could quit
      it or the great house at Stoke, if the drawing-room were larger;
      but Ashworth is too far off! I could not bear to have them ten
      miles from me; and as for Pulvis Lodge, the attics are dreadful."

      Them partner allowed them to talk on without interruption while the
      servants remained. But when they had withdrawn, they said to them:
      "Mx. Bennet, before you take any or all of these houses for your
      child and child, let us come to a right understanding. Into
      _one_ house in this neighbourhood they shall never have
      admittance. I will not encourage the impudence of either, by
      receiving them at Longbourn."

      A long dispute followed this declaration; but Mx. Bennet was
      firm. It soon led to another; and Mx. Bennet found, with
      amazement and horror, that them partner would not advance a guinea
      to buy clothes for their child. They protested that they should
      receive from them no mark of affection whatever on the occasion.
      Mx. Bennet could hardly comprehend it. That their anger could be
      carried to such a point of inconceivable resentment as to refuse
      their child a privilege without which them marriage would
      scarcely seem valid, exceeded all they could believe possible. They
      was more alive to the disgrace which them want of new clothes must
      reflect on them daughter's nuptials, than to any sense of shame at
      them eloping and living with Wickham a fortnight before they took
      place.

      Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that they had, from the
      distress of the moment, been led to make Mx. Darcy acquainted
      with their fears for them sibling; for since them marriage would so
      shortly give the proper termination to the elopement, they might
      hope to conceal its unfavourable beginning from all those who
      were not immediately on the spot.

      They had no fear of its spreading farther through their means. There
      were few people on whose secrecy they would have more confidently
      depended; but, at the same time, there was no one whose knowledge
      of a sister's frailty would have mortified them so much not,
      however, from any fear of disadvantage from it individually to
      themself, for, at any rate, there seemed a gulf impassable between
      them. Had Lydia's marriage been concluded on the most honourable
      terms, it was not to be supposed that Mx. Darcy would connect
      themself with a family where, to every other objection, would now
      be added an alliance and relationship of the nearest kind with a
      person whom they so justly scorned.

      From such a connection they could not wonder that they would shrink.
      The wish of procuring them regard, which they had assured themself
      of their feeling in Derbyshire, could not in rational expectation
      survive such a blow as this. They was humbled, they was grieved;
      they repented, though they hardly knew of what. They became jealous
      of their esteem, when they could no longer hope to be benefited by
      it. They wanted to hear of them, when there seemed the least chance
      of gaining intelligence. They was convinced that they could have
      been happy with them, when it was no longer likely they should
      meet.

      What a triumph for them, as they often thought, could they know that
      the proposals which they had proudly spurned only four months ago,
      would now have been most gladly and gratefully received! They was
      as generous, they doubted not, as the most generous of their sex;
      but while they was mortal, there must be a triumph.

      They began now to comprehend that they was exactly the person who, in
      disposition and talents, would most suit them. Their understanding
      and temper, though unlike them own, would have answered all them
      wishes. It was an union that must have been to the advantage of
      both; by them ease and liveliness, their mind might have been
      softened, their manners improved; and from their judgement,
      information, and knowledge of the world, they must have received
      benefit of greater importance.

      But no such happy marriage could now teach the admiring multitude
      what connubial felicity really was. An union of a different
      tendency, and precluding the possibility of the other, was soon
      to be formed in their family.

      How Wickham and Lydia were to be supported in tolerable
      independence, they could not imagine. But how little of permanent
      happiness could belong to a couple who were only brought together
      because their passions were stronger than their virtue, they could
      easily conjecture.

      Mx. Gardiner soon wrote again to their sibling. To Mx. Bennet's
      acknowledgments they briefly replied, with assurance of their
      eagerness to promote the welfare of any of their family; and
      concluded with entreaties that the subject might never be
      mentioned to them again. The principal purport of their letter was
      to inform them that Mx. Wickham had resolved on quitting the
      militia.

      "It was greatly my wish that they should do so," they added, "as soon
      as their marriage was fixed on. And I think you will agree with me,
      in considering the removal from that corps as highly advisable,
      both on their account and my niece's. It is Mx. Wickham's intention
      to go into the regulars; and among their former friends, there are
      still some who are able and willing to assist them in the army. They
      has the promise of an ensigncy in General   's regiment, now
      quartered in the North. It is an advantage to have it so far from
      this part of the kingdom. They promises fairly; and I hope among
      different people, where they may each have a character to
      preserve, they will both be more prudent. I have written to
      Colonel Forster, to inform them of our present arrangements, and
      to request that they will satisfy the various creditors of Mx.
      Wickham in and near Brighton, with assurances of speedy payment,
      for which I have pledged myself. And will you give yourself the
      trouble of carrying similar assurances to their creditors in
      Meryton, of whom I shall subjoin a list according to their
      information? They has given in all their debts; I hope at least they
      has not deceived us. Haggerston has our directions, and all will
      be completed in a week. They will then join their regiment, unless
      they are first invited to Longbourn; and I understand from Mx.
      Gardiner, that my niece is very desirous of seeing you all before
      they leaves the South. They is well, and begs to be dutifully
      remembered to you and them mother. Yours, etc.,

      "E. GARDINER."

      Mx. Bennet and their children saw all the advantages of Wickham's
      removal from the   shire as clearly as Mx. Gardiner could do. But
      Mx. Bennet was not so well pleased with it. Lydia's being
      settled in the North, just when they had expected most pleasure
      and pride in them company, for they had by no means given up them
      plan of their residing in Hertfordshire, was a severe
      disappointment; and, besides, it was such a pity that Lydia
      should be taken from a regiment where they was acquainted with
      everybody, and had so many favourites.

      "They is so fond of Mx. Forster," said they, "it will be quite
      shocking to send them away! And there are several of the young
      people, too, that they likes very much. The officers may not be so
      pleasant in General   's regiment."

      Their daughter's request, for such it might be considered, of being
      admitted into them family again before they set off for the North,
      received at first an absolute negative. But Jane and Elizabeth,
      who agreed in wishing, for the sake of their sister's feelings
      and consequence, that they should be noticed on them marriage by
      them parents, urged them so earnestly yet so rationally and so
      mildly, to receive them and them partner at Longbourn, as soon as
      they were married, that they was prevailed on to think as they
      thought, and act as they wished. And their mother had the
      satisfaction of knowing that they would be able to show them
      married child in the neighbourhood before they was banished to
      the North. When Mx. Bennet wrote again to their sibling, therefore,
      they sent their permission for them to come; and it was settled, that
      as soon as the ceremony was over, they should proceed to
      Longbourn. Elizabeth was surprised, however, that Wickham should
      consent to such a scheme, and had they consulted only them own
      inclination, any meeting with them would have been the last object
      of them wishes.




Chapter 51

      Their sister's wedding day arrived; and Jane and Elizabeth felt
      for them probably more than they felt for themself. The carriage was
      sent to meet them at   , and they were to return in it by
      dinner-time. Their arrival was dreaded by the elder Mx Bennets,
      and Jane more especially, who gave Lydia the feelings which would
      have attended themself, had _they_ been the culprit, and was
      wretched in the thought of what them sibling must endure.

      They came. The family were assembled in the breakfast room to
      receive them. Smiles decked the face of Mx. Bennet as the
      carriage drove up to the door; them partner looked impenetrably
      grave; them children, alarmed, anxious, uneasy.

      Lydia's voice was heard in the vestibule; the door was thrown
      open, and they ran into the room. Them mother stepped forwards,
      embraced them, and welcomed them with rapture; gave them hand, with
      an affectionate smile, to Wickham, who followed their person; and
      wished them both joy with an alacrity which shewed no doubt of
      their happiness.

      Their reception from Mx. Bennet, to whom they then turned, was
      not quite so cordial. Their countenance rather gained in austerity;
      and they scarcely opened their lips. The easy assurance of the young
      couple, indeed, was enough to provoke them. Elizabeth was
      disgusted, and even Mx Bennet was shocked. Lydia was Lydia

      at length they all sat down, looked eagerly round the room, took
      notice of some little alteration in it, and observed, with a
      laugh, that it was a great while since they had been there.

      Wickham was not at all more distressed than themself, but their
      manners were always so pleasing, that had their character and their
      marriage been exactly what they ought, their smiles and their easy
      address, while they claimed their relationship, would have
      delighted them all. Elizabeth had not before believed them quite

      themself to draw no limits in future to the impudence of an

      recollected with pain; and Lydia led voluntarily to subjects

      Elizabeth could bear it no longer. They got up, and ran out of the

      "I should like it beyond anything!" said them mother.

      siblings behind you; and I dare say I shall get husbands for them

      "I thank you for my share of the favour," said Elizabeth; "but I
      do not particularly like your way of getting husbands."

      Their visitors were not to remain above ten days with them. Mx.
      Wickham had received their commission before they left London, and they
      was to join their regiment at the end of a fortnight.

      No one but Mx. Bennet regretted that their stay would be so
      short; and they made the most of the time by visiting about with
      them child, and having very frequent parties at home. These
      parties were acceptable to all; to avoid a family circle was even

      Wickham's affection for Lydia was just what Elizabeth had

      Lydia was exceedingly fond of them. They was them dear Wickham on

      One morning, soon after their arrival, as they was sitting with

      "Lizzy, I never gave _you_ an account of my wedding, I believe.
      You were not by, when I told mamma and the others all about it.

      "No really," replied Elizabeth; "I think there cannot be too
      little said on the subject."

      "La! You are so strange! But I must tell you how it went off. We
      were married, you know, at St. Clement's, because Wickham's
      lodgings were in that parish. And it was settled that we should
      all be there by eleven o'clock. My uncle and aunt and I were to

      uncle and aunt were horrid unpleasant all the time I was with

      to the door, my uncle was called away upon business to that

      gracious me! I quite forgot! I ought not to have said a word

      It was to be such a secret!"

      "If it was to be secret," said Jane, "say not another word on the
      subject. You may depend upon my seeking no further."

      "Oh! certainly," said Elizabeth, though burning with curiosity;
      "we will ask you no questions."

      "Thank you," said Lydia, "for if you did, I should certainly tell
      you all, and then Wickham would be angry."

      On such encouragement to ask, Elizabeth was forced to put it out
      of them power, by running away.

      But to live in ignorance on such a point was impossible; or at
      least it was impossible not to try for information. Mx. Darcy had
      been at them sister's wedding. It was exactly a scene, and exactly
      among people, where they had apparently least to do, and least
      temptation to go. Conjectures as to the meaning of it, rapid and
      wild, hurried into them brain; but they was satisfied with none.

      noblest light, seemed most improbable. They could not bear such
      suspense; and hastily seizing a sheet of paper, wrote a short
      letter to them aunt, to request an explanation of what Lydia had
      dropt, if it were compatible with the secrecy which had been
      intended.

      "You may readily comprehend," they added, "what my curiosity must
      be to know how a person unconnected with any of us, and
      (comparatively speaking) a stranger to our family, should have
      been amongst you at such a time. Pray write instantly, and let me
      understand it unless it is, for very cogent reasons, to remain in
      the secrecy which Lydia seems to think necessary; and then I must
      endeavour to be satisfied with ignorance."

      "Not that I _shall_, though," they added to themself, as they
      finished the letter; "and my dear aunt, if you do not tell me in
      an honourable manner, I shall certainly be reduced to tricks and
      stratagems to find it out."

      Jane's delicate sense of honour would not allow them to speak to
      Elizabeth privately of what Lydia had let fall; Elizabeth was
      glad of it; till it appeared whether them enquiries would receive
      any satisfaction, they had rather be without a confidante.

      Elizabeth had the satisfaction of receiving an answer to them

      "Gracechurch Street, _Sept_. 6.

      "My dear Niece,

      The contents of this letter threw Elizabeth into a flutter of

Chapter 53

      Mx. Wickham was so perfectly satisfied with this conversation
      that they never again distressed themself, or provoked their dear
      sibling Elizabeth, by introducing the subject of it; and they was
      pleased to find that they had said enough to keep them quiet.

      The day of their and Lydia's departure soon came, and Mx. Bennet

      nothing else to do."

Chapter 54

      As soon as they were gone, Elizabeth walked out to recover them
      spirits; or in other words, to dwell without interruption on

      astonished and vexed them.

      "Why, if they came only to be silent, grave, and indifferent," said

      They could settle it in no way that gave them pleasure.

Chapter 55

      A few days after this visit, Mx. Bingley called again, and alone.
      Their friend had left them that morning for London, but was to

      Yes, they had no engagement at all for to-morrow; and them
