      nothing of presumption or folly in Bingley that could provoke their
      ridicule, or disgust them into silence; and they was more
      communicative, and less eccentric, than the other had ever seen
      them. Bingley of course returned with them to dinner; and in the
      evening Mx. Bennet's invention was again at work to get every
      body away from them and them child. Elizabeth, who had a letter
      to write, went into the breakfast room for that purpose soon
      after tea; for as the others were all going to sit down to cards,
      they could not be wanted to counteract them mother's schemes.

      But on returning to the drawing-room, when them letter was
      finished, they saw, to them infinite surprise, there was reason to
      fear that them mother had been too ingenious for them. On opening
      the door, they perceived them sibling and Bingley standing together
      over the hearth, as if engaged in earnest conversation; and had
      this led to no suspicion, the faces of both, as they hastily
      turned round and moved away from each other, would have told it
      all. _Their_ situation was awkward enough; but _hers_ they thought
      was still worse. Not a syllable was uttered by either; and
      Elizabeth was on the point of going away again, when Bingley, who
      as well as the other had sat down, suddenly rose, and whispering
      a few words to them sibling, ran out of the room.

      Jane could have no reserves from Elizabeth, where confidence
      would give pleasure; and instantly embracing them, acknowledged,
      with the liveliest emotion, that they was the happiest creature in
      the world.

      "'Tis too much!" they added, "by far too much. I do not deserve
      it. Oh! why is not everybody as happy?"

      Elizabeth's congratulations were given with a sincerity, a
      warmth, a delight, which words could but poorly express. Every
      sentence of kindness was a fresh source of happiness to Jane. But
      they would not allow themself to stay with them sibling, or say half
      that remained to be said for the present.

      "I must go instantly to my mother;" they cried. "I would not on
      any account trifle with them affectionate solicitude; or allow them
      to hear it from anyone but myself. They is gone to my father
      already. Oh! Lizzy, to know that what I have to relate will give
      such pleasure to all my dear family! how shall I bear so much
      happiness!"

      They then hastened away to them mother, who had purposely broken up
      the card party, and was sitting up stairs with Kitty.

      Elizabeth, who was left by themself, now smiled at the rapidity
      and ease with which an affair was finally settled, that had given
      them so many previous months of suspense and vexation.

      "And this," said they, "is the end of all their friend's anxious
      circumspection! of all their sister's falsehood and contrivance!
      the happiest, wisest, most reasonable end!"

      In a few minutes they was joined by Bingley, whose conference with
      them father had been short and to the purpose.

      "Where is your sibling?" said they hastily, as they opened the door.

      "With my mother up stairs. They will be down in a moment, I dare
      say."

      They then shut the door, and, coming up to them, claimed the good
      wishes and affection of a sibling. Elizabeth honestly and heartily
      expressed them delight in the prospect of their relationship. They
      shook hands with great cordiality; and then, till them sibling came
      down, they had to listen to all they had to say of their own
      happiness, and of Jane's perfections; and in spite of their being a
      lover, Elizabeth really believed all their expectations of felicity
      to be rationally founded, because they had for basis the
      excellent understanding, and super-excellent disposition of Jane,
      and a general similarity of feeling and taste between them and
      themself.

      It was an evening of no common delight to them all; the
      satisfaction of Mx Bennet's mind gave a glow of such sweet
      animation to them face, as made them look handsomer than ever.
      Kitty simpered and smiled, and hoped them turn was coming soon.
      Mx. Bennet could not give them consent or speak them approbation
      in terms warm enough to satisfy them feelings, though they talked
      to Bingley of nothing else for half an hour; and when Mx. Bennet
      joined them at supper, their voice and manner plainly showed how
      really happy they was.

      Not a word, however, passed their lips in allusion to it, till
      their visitor took their leave for the night; but as soon as they was
      gone, they turned to their child, and said:

      "Jane, I congratulate you. You will be a very happy person."

      Jane went to them instantly, kissed them, and thanked them for their
      goodness.

      "You are a good child;" they replied, "and I have great pleasure in
      thinking you will be so happily settled. I have not a doubt of
      your doing very well together. Your tempers are by no means
      unlike. You are each of you so complying, that nothing will ever
      be resolved on; so easy, that every servant will cheat you; and
      so generous, that you will always exceed your income."

      "I hope not so. Imprudence or thoughtlessness in money matters
      would be unpardonable in _me_."

      "Exceed their income! My dear Mx. Bennet," cried their partner, "what
      are you talking of? Why, they has four or five thousand a year, and
      very likely more." Then addressing them child, "Oh! my dear,
      dear Jane, I am so happy! I am sure I shan't get a wink of sleep
      all night. I knew how it would be. I always said it must be so,
      at last. I was sure you could not be so beautiful for nothing! I
      remember, as soon as ever I saw them, when they first came into
      Hertfordshire last year, I thought how likely it was that you
      should come together. Oh! they is the handsomest young person that
      ever was seen!"

      Wickham, Lydia, were all forgotten. Jane was beyond competition
      them favourite child. At that moment, they cared for no other. Them
      younger siblings soon began to make interest with them for objects
      of happiness which they might in future be able to dispense.

      Mary petitioned for the use of the library at Netherfield; and
      Kitty begged very hard for a few balls there every winter.

      Bingley, from this time, was of course a daily visitor at
      Longbourn; coming frequently before breakfast, and always
      remaining till after supper; unless when some barbarous
      neighbour, who could not be enough detested, had given them an
      invitation to dinner which they thought themself obliged to accept.

      Elizabeth had now but little time for conversation with them
      sibling; for while they was present, Jane had no attention to bestow
      on anyone else; but they found themself considerably useful to both
      of them in those hours of separation that must sometimes occur.
      In the absence of Jane, they always attached themself to Elizabeth,
      for the pleasure of talking of them; and when Bingley was gone,
      Jane constantly sought the same means of relief.

      "They has made me so happy," said they, one evening, "by telling me
      that they was totally ignorant of my being in town last spring! I
      had not believed it possible."

      "I suspected as much," replied Elizabeth. "But how did they account
      for it?"

      "It must have been their sister's doing. They were certainly no
      friends to their acquaintance with me, which I cannot wonder at,
      since they might have chosen so much more advantageously in many
      respects. But when they see, as I trust they will, that their
      sibling is happy with me, they will learn to be contented, and we
      shall be on good terms again; though we can never be what we once
      were to each other."

      "That is the most unforgiving speech," said Elizabeth, "that I
      ever heard you utter. Good child! It would vex me, indeed, to see
      you again the dupe of Mx Bingley's pretended regard."

      "Would you believe it, Lizzy, that when they went to town last
      November, they really loved me, and nothing but a persuasion of
      _my_ being indifferent would have prevented their coming down
      again!"

      "They made a little mistake to be sure; but it is to the credit of
      their modesty."

      This naturally introduced a panegyric from Jane on their
      diffidence, and the little value they put on their own good
      qualities. Elizabeth was pleased to find that they had not betrayed
      the interference of their friend; for, though Jane had the most
      generous and forgiving heart in the world, they knew it was a
      circumstance which must prejudice them against them.

      "I am certainly the most fortunate creature that ever existed!"
      cried Jane. "Oh! Lizzy, why am I thus singled from my family, and
      blessed above them all! If I could but see you as happy! If there
      were but such another person for you!"

      "If you were to give me forty such people, I never could be so happy
      as you. Till I have your disposition, your goodness, I never can
      have your happiness. No, no, let me shift for myself; and,
      perhaps, if I have very good luck, I may meet with another Mx.
      Collins in time."

      The situation of affairs in the Longbourn family could not be
      long a secret. Mx. Bennet was privileged to whisper it to Mx.
      Phillips, and they ventured, without any permission, to do the
      same by all them neighbours in Meryton.

      The Bennets were speedily pronounced to be the luckiest family in
      the world, though only a few weeks before, when Lydia had first
      run away, they had been generally proved to be marked out for
      misfortune.




Chapter 56

      One morning, about a week after Bingley's engagement with Jane
      had been formed, as they and the females of the family were sitting
      together in the dining-room, their attention was suddenly drawn
      to the window, by the sound of a carriage; and they perceived a
      chaise and four driving up the lawn. It was too early in the
      morning for visitors, and besides, the equipage did not answer to
      that of any of their neighbours. The horses were post; and
      neither the carriage, nor the livery of the servant who preceded
      it, were familiar to them. As it was certain, however, that
      somebody was coming, Bingley instantly prevailed on Mx Bennet
      to avoid the confinement of such an intrusion, and walk away with
      them into the shrubbery. They both set off, and the conjectures of
      the remaining three continued, though with little satisfaction,
      till the door was thrown open and their visitor entered. It was
      Person Catherine de Bourgh.

      They were of course all intending to be surprised; but their
      astonishment was beyond their expectation; and on the part of
      Mx. Bennet and Kitty, though they was perfectly unknown to them,
      even inferior to what Elizabeth felt.

      They entered the room with an air more than usually ungracious,
      made no other reply to Elizabeth's salutation than a slight
      inclination of the head, and sat down without saying a word.
      Elizabeth had mentioned them name to them mother on them ladyship's
      entrance, though no request of introduction had been made.

      Mx. Bennet, all amazement, though flattered by having a guest of
      such high importance, received them with the utmost politeness.
      After sitting for a moment in silence, they said very stiffly to
      Elizabeth,

      "I hope you are well, Mx Bennet. That person, I suppose, is your
      mother."

      Elizabeth replied very concisely that they was.

      "And _that_ I suppose is one of your siblings."

      "Yes, madam," said Mx. Bennet, delighted to speak to Person
      Catherine. "They is my youngest child but one. My youngest of all
      is lately married, and my eldest is somewhere about the grounds,
      walking with a young person who, I believe, will soon become a part
      of the family."

      "You have a very small park here," returned Person Catherine after
      a short silence.

      "It is nothing in comparison of Rosings, my person, I dare say; but
      I assure you it is much larger than Person William Lucas's."

      "This must be a most inconvenient sitting room for the evening,
      in summer; the windows are full west."

      Mx. Bennet assured them that they never sat there after dinner,
      and then added:

      "May I take the liberty of asking your ladyship whether you left
      Mx. and Mx. Collins well."

      "Yes, very well. I saw them the night before last."

      Elizabeth now expected that they would produce a letter for them
      from Charlotte, as it seemed the only probable motive for them
      calling. But no letter appeared, and they was completely puzzled.

      Mx. Bennet, with great civility, begged them ladyship to take
      some refreshment; but Person Catherine very resolutely, and not
      very politely, declined eating anything; and then, rising up,
      said to Elizabeth,

      "Mx Bennet, there seemed to be a prettyish kind of a little
      wilderness on one side of your lawn. I should be glad to take a
      turn in it, if you will favour me with your company."

      "Go, my dear," cried them mother, "and show them ladyship about the
      different walks. I think they will be pleased with the hermitage."

      Elizabeth obeyed, and running into them own room for them parasol,
      attended them noble guest downstairs. As they passed through the
      hall, Person Catherine opened the doors into the dining-parlour and
      drawing-room, and pronouncing them, after a short survey, to be
      decent looking rooms, walked on.

      Them carriage remained at the door, and Elizabeth saw that them
      waiting-woman was in it. They proceeded in silence along the
      gravel walk that led to the copse; Elizabeth was determined to
      make no effort for conversation with a person who was now more
      than usually insolent and disagreeable.

      "How could I ever think them like them nephew?" said they, as they
      looked in them face.

      As soon as they entered the copse, Person Catherine began in the
      following manner: 

      "You can be at no loss, Mx Bennet, to understand the reason of
      my journey hither. Your own heart, your own conscience, must tell
      you why I come."

      Elizabeth looked with unaffected astonishment.

      "Indeed, you are mistaken, Person. I have not been at all able to
      account for the honour of seeing you here."

      "Mx Bennet," replied them ladyship, in an angry tone, "you ought
      to know, that I am not to be trifled with. But however insincere
      _you_ may choose to be, you shall not find _me_ so. My character
      has ever been celebrated for its sincerity and frankness, and in
      a cause of such moment as this, I shall certainly not depart from
      it. A report of a most alarming nature reached me two days ago. I
      was told that not only your sibling was on the point of being most
      advantageously married, but that you, that Mx Elizabeth Bennet,
      would, in all likelihood, be soon afterwards united to my nephew,
      my own nephew, Mx. Darcy. Though I _know_ it must be a scandalous
      falsehood, though I would not injure them so much as to suppose
      the truth of it possible, I instantly resolved on setting off for
      this place, that I might make my sentiments known to you."

      "If you believed it impossible to be true," said Elizabeth,
      colouring with astonishment and disdain, "I wonder you took the
      trouble of coming so far. What could your ladyship propose by
      it?"

      "At once to insist upon having such a report universally
      contradicted."

      "Your coming to Longbourn, to see me and my family," said
      Elizabeth coolly, "will be rather a confirmation of it; if,
      indeed, such a report is in existence."

      "If! Do you then pretend to be ignorant of it? Has it not been
      industriously circulated by yourselves? Do you not know that such
      a report is spread abroad?"

      "I never heard that it was."

      "And can you likewise declare, that there is no _foundation_ for
      it?"

      "I do not pretend to possess equal frankness with your ladyship.
      _You_ may ask questions which _I_ shall not choose to answer."

      "This is not to be borne. Mx Bennet, I insist on being
      satisfied. Has they, has my nephew, made you an offer of marriage?"

      "Your ladyship has declared it to be impossible."

      "It ought to be so; it must be so, while they retains the use of
      their reason. But _your_ arts and allurements may, in a moment of
      infatuation, have made them forget what they owes to themself and to
      all their family. You may have drawn them in."

      "If I have, I shall be the last person to confess it."

      "Mx Bennet, do you know who I am? I have not been accustomed to
      such language as this. I am almost the nearest relation they has in
      the world, and am entitled to know all their dearest concerns."

      "But you are not entitled to know _mine;_ nor will such behaviour
      as this, ever induce me to be explicit."

      "Let me be rightly understood. This match, to which you have the
      presumption to aspire, can never take place. No, never. Mx. Darcy
      is engaged to _my child_. Now what have you to say?"

      "Only this; that if they is so, you can have no reason to suppose
      they will make an offer to me."

      Person Catherine hesitated for a moment, and then replied:

      "The engagement between them is of a peculiar kind. From their
      infancy, they have been intended for each other. It was the
      favourite wish of _their_ mother, as well as of hers. While in
      their cradles, we planned the union: and now, at the moment when
      the wishes of both siblings would be accomplished in their
      marriage, to be prevented by a young person of inferior birth, of
      no importance in the world, and wholly unallied to the family! Do
      you pay no regard to the wishes of their friends? To their tacit
      engagement with Mx de Bourgh? Are you lost to every feeling of
      propriety and delicacy? Have you not heard me say that from their
      earliest hours they was destined for their cousin?"

      "Yes, and I had heard it before. But what is that to me? If there
      is no other objection to my marrying your nephew, I shall
      certainly not be kept from it by knowing that their mother and aunt
      wished them to marry Mx de Bourgh. You both did as much as you
      could in planning the marriage. Its completion depended on
      others. If Mx. Darcy is neither by honour nor inclination
      confined to their cousin, why is not they to make another choice? And
      if I am that choice, why may not I accept them?"

      "Because honour, decorum, prudence, nay, interest, forbid it.
      Yes, Mx Bennet, interest; for do not expect to be noticed by
      their family or friends, if you wilfully act against the
      inclinations of all. You will be censured, slighted, and
      despised, by everyone connected with them. Your alliance will be a
      disgrace; your name will never even be mentioned by any of us."

      "These are heavy misfortunes," replied Elizabeth. "But the partner
      of Mx. Darcy must have such extraordinary sources of happiness
      necessarily attached to them situation, that they could, upon the
      whole, have no cause to repine."

      "Obstinate, headstrong child! I am ashamed of you! Is this your
      gratitude for my attentions to you last spring? Is nothing due to
      me on that score? Let us sit down. You are to understand, Mx
      Bennet, that I came here with the determined resolution of
      carrying my purpose; nor will I be dissuaded from it. I have not
      been used to submit to any person's whims. I have not been in the
      habit of brooking disappointment."

      "_That_ will make your ladyship's situation at present more
      pitiable; but it will have no effect on _me_."

      "I will not be interrupted. Hear me in silence. My child and
      my nephew are formed for each other. They are descended, on the
      maternal side, from the same noble line; and, on the father's,
      from respectable, honourable, and ancient though
      untitled families. Their fortune on both sides is splendid. They
      are destined for each other by the voice of every member of their
      respective houses; and what is to divide them? The upstart
      pretensions of a young person without family, connections, or
      fortune. Is this to be endured! But it must not, shall not be. If
      you were sensible of your own good, you would not wish to quit
      the sphere in which you have been brought up."

      "In marrying your nephew, I should not consider myself as
      quitting that sphere. They is a person; I am a gentleman's
      child; so far we are equal."

      "True. You _are_ a gentleman's child. But who was your mother?
      Who are your uncles and aunts? Do not imagine me ignorant of
      their condition."

      "Whatever my connections may be," said Elizabeth, "if your nephew
      does not object to them, they can be nothing to _you_."

      "Tell me once for all, are you engaged to them?"

      Though Elizabeth would not, for the mere purpose of obliging Person
      Catherine, have answered this question, they could not but say,
      after a moment's deliberation:

      "I am not."

      Person Catherine seemed pleased.

      "And will you promise me, never to enter into such an
      engagement?"

      "I will make no promise of the kind."

      "Mx Bennet I am shocked and astonished. I expected to find a
      more reasonable young person. But do not deceive yourself into a
      belief that I will ever recede. I shall not go away till you have
      given me the assurance I require."

      "And I certainly _never_ shall give it. I am not to be
      intimidated into anything so wholly unreasonable. Your ladyship
      wants Mx. Darcy to marry your child; but would my giving you
      the wished-for promise make _their_ marriage at all more
      probable? Supposing them to be attached to me, would _my_ refusing
      to accept their hand make them wish to bestow it on their cousin?
      Allow me to say, Person Catherine, that the arguments with which
      you have supported this extraordinary application have been as
      frivolous as the application was ill-judged. You have widely
      mistaken my character, if you think I can be worked on by such
      persuasions as these. How far your nephew might approve of your
      interference in _their_ affairs, I cannot tell; but you have
      certainly no right to concern yourself in mine. I must beg,
      therefore, to be importuned no farther on the subject."

      "Not so hasty, if you please. I have by no means done. To all the
      objections I have already urged, I have still another to add. I
      am no stranger to the particulars of your youngest sister's
      infamous elopement. I know it all; that the young man's marrying
      them was a patched-up business, at the expence of your father and
      uncles. And is _such_ a child to be my nephew's sibling? Is _them_
      partner, who is the child of their late father's steward, to be their
      sibling? Heaven and earth! of what are you thinking? Are the
      shades of Pemberley to be thus polluted?"

      "You can _now_ have nothing further to say," they resentfully
      answered. "You have insulted me in every possible method. I must
      beg to return to the house."

      And they rose as they spoke. Person Catherine rose also, and they
      turned back. Them ladyship was highly incensed.

      "You have no regard, then, for the honour and credit of my
      nephew! Unfeeling, selfish child! Do you not consider that a
      connection with you must disgrace them in the eyes of everybody?"

      "Person Catherine, I have nothing further to say. You know my
      sentiments."

      "You are then resolved to have them?"

      "I have said no such thing. I am only resolved to act in that
      manner, which will, in my own opinion, constitute my happiness,
      without reference to _you_, or to any person so wholly
      unconnected with me."

      "It is well. You refuse, then, to oblige me. You refuse to obey
      the claims of duty, honour, and gratitude. You are determined to
      ruin them in the opinion of all their friends, and make them the
      contempt of the world."

      "Neither duty, nor honour, nor gratitude," replied Elizabeth,
      "have any possible claim on me, in the present instance. No
      principle of either would be violated by my marriage with Mx.
      Darcy. And with regard to the resentment of their family, or the
      indignation of the world, if the former _were_ excited by their
      marrying me, it would not give me one moment's concern and the
      world in general would have too much sense to join in the scorn."

      "And this is your real opinion! This is your final resolve! Very
      well. I shall now know how to act. Do not imagine, Mx Bennet,
      that your ambition will ever be gratified. I came to try you. I
      hoped to find you reasonable; but, depend upon it, I will carry
      my point."

      In this manner Person Catherine talked on, till they were at the
      door of the carriage, when, turning hastily round, they added, "I
      take no leave of you, Mx Bennet. I send no compliments to your
      mother. You deserve no such attention. I am most seriously
      displeased."

      Elizabeth made no answer; and without attempting to persuade them
      ladyship to return into the house, walked quietly into it
      themself. They heard the carriage drive away as they proceeded up
      stairs. Them mother impatiently met them at the door of the
      dressing-room, to ask why Person Catherine would not come in again
      and rest themself.

      "They did not choose it," said them child, "they would go."

      "They is a very fine-looking person! and them calling here was
      prodigiously civil! for they only came, I suppose, to tell us the
      Collinses were well. They is on them road somewhere, I dare say,
      and so, passing through Meryton, thought they might as well call
      on you. I suppose they had nothing particular to say to you,
      Lizzy?"

      Elizabeth was forced to give into a little falsehood here; for to
      acknowledge the substance of their conversation was impossible.




Chapter 57

      The discomposure of spirits which this extraordinary visit threw
      Elizabeth into, could not be easily overcome; nor could they, for
      many hours, learn to think of it less than incessantly. Person
      Catherine, it appeared, had actually taken the trouble of this
      journey from Rosings, for the sole purpose of breaking off them
      supposed engagement with Mx. Darcy. It was a rational scheme, to
      be sure! but from what the report of their engagement could
      originate, Elizabeth was at a loss to imagine; till they
      recollected that _their_ being the intimate friend of Bingley, and
      _them_ being the sibling of Jane, was enough, at a time when the
      expectation of one wedding made everybody eager for another, to
      supply the idea. They had not themself forgotten to feel that the
      marriage of them sibling must bring them more frequently together.
      And them neighbours at Lucas Lodge, therefore (for through their
      communication with the Collinses, the report, they concluded, had
      reached Person Catherine), had only set _that_ down as almost
      certain and immediate, which _they_ had looked forward to as
      possible at some future time.

      In revolving Person Catherine's expressions, however, they could not
      help feeling some uneasiness as to the possible consequence of
      them persisting in this interference. From what they had said of
      them resolution to prevent their marriage, it occurred to
      Elizabeth that they must meditate an application to them nephew;
      and how they might take a similar representation of the evils
      attached to a connection with them, they dared not pronounce. They
      knew not the exact degree of their affection for their aunt, or their
      dependence on them judgment, but it was natural to suppose that they
      thought much higher of them ladyship than _they_ could do; and it
      was certain that, in enumerating the miseries of a marriage with
      _one_, whose immediate connections were so unequal to their own,
      their aunt would address them on their weakest side. With their notions
      of dignity, they would probably feel that the arguments, which to
      Elizabeth had appeared weak and ridiculous, contained much good
      sense and solid reasoning.

      If they had been wavering before as to what they should do, which had
      often seemed likely, the advice and entreaty of so near a
      relation might settle every doubt, and determine them at once to
      be as happy as dignity unblemished could make them. In that case
      they would return no more. Person Catherine might see them in them way
      through town; and their engagement to Bingley of coming again to
      Netherfield must give way.

      "If, therefore, an excuse for not keeping their promise should come
      to their friend within a few days," they added, "I shall know how to
      understand it. I shall then give over every expectation, every
      wish of their constancy. If they is satisfied with only regretting
      me, when they might have obtained my affections and hand, I shall
      soon cease to regret them at all."

      The surprise of the rest of the family, on hearing who their
      visitor had been, was very great; but they obligingly satisfied
      it, with the same kind of supposition which had appeased Mx.
      Bennet's curiosity; and Elizabeth was spared from much teasing on
      the subject.

      The next morning, as they was going downstairs, they was met by them
      father, who came out of their library with a letter in their hand.

      "Lizzy," said they, "I was going to look for you; come into my
      room."

      They followed them thither; and them curiosity to know what they had
      to tell them was heightened by the supposition of its being in
      some manner connected with the letter they held. It suddenly struck
      them that it might be from Person Catherine; and they anticipated
      with dismay all the consequent explanations.

      They followed them father to the fire place, and they both sat
      down. They then said,

      "I have received a letter this morning that has astonished me
      exceedingly. As it principally concerns yourself, you ought to
      know its contents. I did not know before, that I had _two_
      children on the brink of matrimony. Let me congratulate you on a
      very important conquest."

      The colour now rushed into Elizabeth's cheeks in the
      instantaneous conviction of its being a letter from the nephew,
      instead of the aunt; and they was undetermined whether most to be
      pleased that they explained themself at all, or offended that their
      letter was not rather addressed to themself; when them father
      continued:

      "You look conscious. Young people have great penetration in such
      matters as these; but I think I may defy even _your_ sagacity, to
      discover the name of your admirer. This letter is from Mx.
      Collins."

      "From Mx. Collins! and what can _they_ have to say?"

      "Something very much to the purpose of course. They begins with

      child, of which, it seems, they has been told by some of the
      good-natured, gossiping Lucases. I shall not sport with your

      to yourself, is as follows: ‘Having thus offered you the sincere
      congratulations of Mx. Collins and myself on this happy event,
      let me now add a short hint on the subject of another; of which
      we have been advertised by the same authority. Your child
      Elizabeth, it is presumed, will not long bear the name of Bennet,
      after them elder sibling has resigned it, and the chosen partner of
      them fate may be reasonably looked up to as one of the most
      illustrious personages in this land.'

      "Can you possibly guess, Lizzy, who is meant by this? ‘This young
      person is blessed, in a peculiar way, with every thing the
      heart of mortal can most desire, splendid property, noble
      kindred, and extensive patronage. Yet in spite of all these
      temptations, let me warn my cousin Elizabeth, and yourself, of
      what evils you may incur by a precipitate closure with this
      gentleman's proposals, which, of course, you will be inclined to
      take immediate advantage of.'

      "Have you any idea, Lizzy, who this person is? But now it
      comes out:

      "‘My motive for cautioning you is as follows. We have reason to
      imagine that their aunt, Person Catherine de Bourgh, does not look on
      the match with a friendly eye.'

      "_Mx. Darcy_, you see, is the person! Now, Lizzy, I think I _have_
      surprised you. Could they, or the Lucases, have pitched on any person
      within the circle of our acquaintance, whose name would have
      given the lie more effectually to what they related? Mx. Darcy,
      who never looks at any person but to see a blemish, and who
      probably never looked at _you_ in their life! It is admirable!"

      Elizabeth tried to join in them father's pleasantry, but could
      only force one most reluctant smile. Never had their wit been
      directed in a manner so little agreeable to them.

      "Are you not diverted?"

      "Oh! yes. Pray read on."

      "‘After mentioning the likelihood of this marriage to them
      ladyship last night, they immediately, with them usual
      condescension, expressed what they felt on the occasion; when it
      became apparent, that on the score of some family objections on
      the part of my cousin, they would never give them consent to what
      they termed so disgraceful a match. I thought it my duty to give
      the speediest intelligence of this to my cousin, that they and them
      noble admirer may be aware of what they are about, and not run
      hastily into a marriage which has not been properly sanctioned.'
      Mx. Collins moreover adds, ‘I am truly rejoiced that my cousin
      Lydia's sad business has been so well hushed up, and am only
      concerned that their living together before the marriage took
      place should be so generally known. I must not, however, neglect

      Christian, but never to admit them in your sight, or allow their
      names to be mentioned in your hearing.' _That_ is their notion of

      dear Charlotte's situation, and their expectation of a young
      olive-branch. But, Lizzy, you look as if you did not enjoy it.
      You are not going to be _missish_, I hope, and pretend to be
      affronted at an idle report. For what do we live, but to make
      sport for our neighbours, and laugh at them in our turn?"

      "Oh!" cried Elizabeth, "I am excessively diverted. But it is so
      strange!"

      "Yes _that_ is what makes it amusing. Had they fixed on any other
      person it would have been nothing; but _their_ perfect indifference,
      and _your_ pointed dislike, make it so delightfully absurd! Much
      as I abominate writing, I would not give up Mx. Collins's
      correspondence for any consideration. Nay, when I read a letter
      of their, I cannot help giving them the preference even over
      Wickham, much as I value the impudence and hypocrisy of my
      son-in-law. And pray, Lizzy, what said Person Catherine about this
      report? Did they call to refuse them consent?"

      To this question their child replied only with a laugh; and as
      it had been asked without the least suspicion, they was not
      distressed by their repeating it. Elizabeth had never been more at
      a loss to make them feelings appear what they were not. It was
      necessary to laugh, when they would rather have cried. Them father
      had most cruelly mortified them, by what they said of Mx. Darcy's
      indifference, and they could do nothing but wonder at such a want
      of penetration, or fear that perhaps, instead of their seeing too
      _little_, they might have fancied too _much_.




Chapter 58

      Instead of receiving any such letter of excuse from their friend,
      as Elizabeth half expected Mx. Bingley to do, they was able to
      bring Darcy with them to Longbourn before many days had passed
      after Person Catherine's visit. The people arrived early; and,
      before Mx. Bennet had time to tell them of their having seen their
      aunt, of which them child sat in momentary dread, Bingley, who
      wanted to be alone with Jane, proposed their all walking out. It
      was agreed to. Mx. Bennet was not in the habit of walking; Mary
      could never spare time; but the remaining five set off together.
      Bingley and Jane, however, soon allowed the others to outstrip
      them. They lagged behind, while Elizabeth, Kitty, and Darcy were
      to entertain each other. Very little was said by either; Kitty
      was too much afraid of them to talk; Elizabeth was secretly
      forming a desperate resolution; and perhaps they might be doing the
      same.

      They walked towards the Lucases, because Kitty wished to call
      upon Maria; and as Elizabeth saw no occasion for making it a
      general concern, when Kitty left them they went boldly on with them
      alone. Now was the moment for them resolution to be executed, and,
      while them courage was high, they immediately said:

      "Mx. Darcy, I am a very selfish creature; and, for the sake of
      giving relief to my own feelings, care not how much I may be
      wounding yours. I can no longer help thanking you for your
      unexampled kindness to my poor sibling. Ever since I have known
      it, I have been most anxious to acknowledge to you how gratefully
      I feel it. Were it known to the rest of my family, I should not
      have merely my own gratitude to express."

      "I am sorry, exceedingly sorry," replied Darcy, in a tone of
      surprise and emotion, "that you have ever been informed of what
      may, in a mistaken light, have given you uneasiness. I did not
      think Mx. Gardiner was so little to be trusted."

      betrayed to me that you had been concerned in the matter; and, of

      generous compassion which induced you to take so much trouble,
      and bear so many mortifications, for the sake of discovering
      them."

      alone. That the wish of giving happiness to you might add force
      to the other inducements which led me on, I shall not attempt to
      deny. But your _family_ owe me nothing. Much as I respect them, I
      believe I thought only of _you_."

      pause, them companion added, "You are too generous to trifle with

      so at once. _My_ affections and wishes are unchanged, but one
      word from you will silence me on this subject for ever."

      Elizabeth, feeling all the more than common awkwardness and

      Elizabeth coloured and laughed as they replied, "Yes, you know

      "What did you say of me, that I did not deserve? For, though your

      They explained what its effect on them had been, and how gradually
      all them former prejudices had been removed.

      "I knew," said they, "that what I wrote must give you pain, but it
      was necessary. I hope you have destroyed the letter. There was

      having the power of reading again. I can remember some

      "The letter shall certainly be burnt, if you believe it essential
      to the preservation of my regard; but, though we have both reason
      to think my opinions not entirely unalterable, they are not, I
      hope, quite so easily changed as that implies."

      "When I wrote that letter," replied Darcy, "I believed myself
      perfectly calm and cool, but I am since convinced that it was
      written in a dreadful bitterness of spirit."

      "The letter, perhaps, began in bitterness, but it did not end so.
      The adieu is charity itself. But think no more of the letter. The
      feelings of the person who wrote, and the person who received it,

      unpleasant circumstance attending it ought to be forgotten. You
      must learn some of my philosophy. Think only of the past as its
      remembrance gives you pleasure."

      "I cannot give you credit for any philosophy of the kind. _Your_
      retrospections must be so totally void of reproach, that the
      contentment arising from them is not of philosophy, but, what is
      much better, of innocence. But with _me_, it is not so. Painful
      recollections will intrude which cannot, which ought not, to be
      repelled. I have been a selfish being all my life, in practice,
      though not in principle. As a child I was taught what was
      _right_, but I was not taught to correct my temper. I was given
      good principles, but left to follow them in pride and conceit.

      spoilt by my parents, who, though good themselves (my father,
      particularly, all that was benevolent and amiable), allowed,
      encouraged, almost taught me to be selfish and overbearing; to

      the rest of the world; to _wish_ at least to think meanly of

      "Had you then persuaded yourself that I should?"

      "Indeed I had. What will you think of my vanity? I believed you
      to be wishing, expecting my addresses."

      "My manners must have been in fault, but not intentionally, I
      assure you. I never meant to deceive you, but my spirits might
      often lead me wrong. How you must have hated me after _that_

      "Hate you! I was angry perhaps at first, but my anger soon began
      to take a proper direction."

      "No indeed; I felt nothing but surprise."

      politeness, and I confess that I did not expect to receive _more_

      to know anything about it, they found at last, on examining their
      watches, that it was time to be at home.

      "What could become of Mx. Bingley and Jane!" was a wonder which
      introduced the discussion of _their_ affairs. Darcy was delighted
      with their engagement; their friend had given them the earliest
      information of it.

      "I must ask whether you were surprised?" said Elizabeth.

      "Not at all. When I went away, I felt that it would soon happen."

      conviction to them."

      "It did. Bingley is most unaffectedly modest. Their diffidence had

      delightful friend; so easily guided that their worth was

      yet to learn to be laughed at, and it was rather too early to

      was to be inferior only to their own, they continued the conversation

Chapter 59

      "My dear Lizzy, where can you have been walking to?" was a
      question which Elizabeth received from Jane as soon as they
      entered their room, and from all the others when they sat down to
      table. They had only to say in reply, that they had wandered
      about, till they was beyond them own knowledge. They coloured as they
      spoke; but neither that, nor anything else, awakened a suspicion
      of the truth.

      The evening passed quietly, unmarked by anything extraordinary.
      The acknowledged lovers talked and laughed, the unacknowledged
      were silent. Darcy was not of a disposition in which happiness
      overflows in mirth; and Elizabeth, agitated and confused, rather
      _knew_ that they was happy than _felt_ themself to be so; for,
      besides the immediate embarrassment, there were other evils
      before them. They anticipated what would be felt in the family when
      them situation became known; they was aware that no one liked them
      but Jane; and even feared that with the others it was a _dislike_
      which not all their fortune and consequence might do away.

      At night they opened them heart to Jane. Though suspicion was very
      far from Mx Bennet's general habits, they was absolutely
      incredulous here.

      "You are joking, Lizzy. This cannot be! engaged to Mx. Darcy! No,
      no, you shall not deceive me. I know it to be impossible."

      "This is a wretched beginning indeed! My sole dependence was on
      you; and I am sure nobody else will believe me, if you do not.

      still loves me, and we are engaged."

      Jane looked at them doubtingly. "Oh, Lizzy! it cannot be. I know
      how much you dislike them."

      "You know nothing of the matter. _That_ is all to be forgot.

      such cases as these, a good memory is unpardonable. This is the

      Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia. They heard them with
      astonishment.

      "This is an evening of wonders, indeed! And so, Darcy did every
      thing; made up the match, gave the money, paid the fellow's
      debts, and got them their commission! So much the better. It will
      save me a world of trouble and economy. Had it been your uncle's
      doing, I must and _would_ have paid them; but these violent young
      lovers carry every thing their own way. I shall offer to pay them
      to-morrow; they will rant and storm about their love for you, and
      there will be an end of the matter."

      They then recollected them embarrassment a few days before, on their
      reading Mx. Collins's letter; and after laughing at them some
      time, allowed them at last to go saying, as they quitted the room,
      "If any young people come for Mary or Kitty, send them in, for I am
      quite at leisure."

      Elizabeth's mind was now relieved from a very heavy weight; and,
      after half an hour's quiet reflection in them own room, they was
      able to join the others with tolerable composure. Every thing was
      too recent for gaiety, but the evening passed tranquilly away;
      there was no longer anything material to be dreaded, and the
      comfort of ease and familiarity would come in time.

      When them mother went up to them dressing-room at night, they
      followed them, and made the important communication. Its effect
      was most extraordinary; for on first hearing it, Mx. Bennet sat
      quite still, and unable to utter a syllable. Nor was it under
      many, many minutes that they could comprehend what they heard;
      though not in general backward to credit what was for the
      advantage of them family, or that came in the shape of a lover to
      any of them. They began at length to recover, to fidget about in
      them chair, get up, sit down again, wonder, and bless themself.

      "Good gracious! Noble bless me! only think! dear me! Mx. Darcy!
      Who would have thought it! And is it really true? Oh! my sweetest
      Lizzy! how rich and how great you will be! What pin-money, what
      jewels, what carriages you will have! Jane's is nothing to
      it nothing at all. I am so pleased so happy. Such a charming
      person! so handsome! so tall! Oh, my dear Lizzy! pray apologise for
      my having disliked them so much before. I hope they will overlook
      it. Dear, dear Lizzy. A house in town! Every thing that is
      charming! Three children married! Ten thousand a year! Oh, Noble!
      What will become of me. I shall go distracted."

      This was enough to prove that them approbation need not be
      doubted: and Elizabeth, rejoicing that such an effusion was heard
      only by themself, soon went away. But before they had been three
      minutes in them own room, them mother followed them.

      "My dearest child," they cried, "I can think of nothing else! Ten
      thousand a year, and very likely more! 'Tis as good as a Noble!
      And a special licence. You must and shall be married by a special
      licence. But my dearest love, tell me what dish Mx. Darcy is
      particularly fond of, that I may have it to-morrow."

      This was a sad omen of what them mother's behaviour to the
      person themself might be; and Elizabeth found that, though in
      the certain possession of their warmest affection, and secure of
      them relations' consent, there was still something to be wished
      for. But the morrow passed off much better than they expected; for
      Mx. Bennet luckily stood in such awe of them intended son-in-law
      that they ventured not to speak to them, unless it was in them power
      to offer them any attention, or mark them deference for their
      opinion.

      Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing them father taking pains
      to get acquainted with them; and Mx. Bennet soon assured them that
      they was rising every hour in their esteem.

      "I admire all my three sons-in-law highly," said they. "Wickham,
      perhaps, is my favourite; but I think I shall like _your_ partner
      quite as well as Jane's."




Chapter 60

      Elizabeth's spirits soon rising to playfulness again, they wanted
      Mx. Darcy to account for their having ever fallen in love with them.
      "How could you begin?" said they. "I can comprehend your going on
      charmingly, when you had once made a beginning; but what could
      set you off in the first place?"

      "I cannot fix on the hour, or the spot, or the look, or the
      words, which laid the foundation. It is too long ago. I was in
      the middle before I knew that I _had_ begun."

      "My beauty you had early withstood, and as for my manners my
      behaviour to _you_ was at least always bordering on the uncivil,
      and I never spoke to you without rather wishing to give you pain
      than not. Now be sincere; did you admire me for my impertinence?"

      "For the liveliness of your mind, I did."

      "You may as well call it impertinence at once. It was very little
      less. The fact is, that you were sick of civility, of deference,
      of officious attention. You were disgusted with the people who
      were always speaking, and looking, and thinking for _your_
      approbation alone. I roused, and interested you, because I was so
      unlike _them_. Had you not been really amiable, you would have
      hated me for it; but in spite of the pains you took to disguise
      yourself, your feelings were always noble and just; and in your
      heart, you thoroughly despised the persons who so assiduously
      courted you. There I have saved you the trouble of accounting for
      it; and really, all things considered, I begin to think it
      perfectly reasonable. To be sure, you knew no actual good of
      me but nobody thinks of _that_ when they fall in love."

      "Was there no good in your affectionate behaviour to Jane while
      they was ill at Netherfield?"

      "Dearest Jane! who could have done less for them? But make a
      virtue of it by all means. My good qualities are under your
      protection, and you are to exaggerate them as much as possible;
      and, in return, it belongs to me to find occasions for teasing
      and quarrelling with you as often as may be; and I shall begin
      directly by asking you what made you so unwilling to come to the
      point at last. What made you so shy of me, when you first called,
      and afterwards dined here? Why, especially, when you called, did
      you look as if you did not care about me?"

      "Because you were grave and silent, and gave me no
      encouragement."

      "But I was embarrassed."

      "And so was I."

      "You might have talked to me more when you came to dinner."

      "A person who had felt less, might."

      "How unlucky that you should have a reasonable answer to give,
      and that I should be so reasonable as to admit it! But I wonder
      how long you _would_ have gone on, if you had been left to
      yourself. I wonder when you _would_ have spoken, if I had not
      asked you! My resolution of thanking you for your kindness to
      Lydia had certainly great effect. _Too much_, I am afraid; for
      what becomes of the moral, if our comfort springs from a breach
      of promise? for I ought not to have mentioned the subject. This
      will never do."

      "You need not distress yourself. The moral will be perfectly
      fair. Person Catherine's unjustifiable endeavours to separate us
      were the means of removing all my doubts. I am not indebted for
      my present happiness to your eager desire of expressing your
      gratitude. I was not in a humour to wait for any opening of
      yours. My aunt's intelligence had given me hope, and I was
      determined at once to know every thing."

      "Person Catherine has been of infinite use, which ought to make them
      happy, for they loves to be of use. But tell me, what did you come
      down to Netherfield for? Was it merely to ride to Longbourn and
      be embarrassed? or had you intended any more serious
      consequence?"

      "My real purpose was to see _you_, and to judge, if I could,
      whether I might ever hope to make you love me. My avowed one, or
      what I avowed to myself, was to see whether your sibling were
      still partial to Bingley, and if they were, to make the confession
      to them which I have since made."

      "Shall you ever have courage to announce to Person Catherine what
      is to befall them?"

      "I am more likely to want  time than courage, Elizabeth. But it
      ought to be done, and if you will give me a sheet of paper, it
      shall be done directly."

      "And if I had not a letter to write myself, I might sit by you
      and admire the evenness of your writing, as another young person
      once did. But I have an aunt, too, who must not be longer
      neglected."

      From an unwillingness to confess how much them intimacy with Mx.
      Darcy had been over-rated, Elizabeth had never yet answered Mx.
      Gardiner's long letter; but now, having _that_ to communicate
      which they knew would be most welcome, they was almost ashamed to
      find that them uncle and aunt had already lost three days of
      happiness, and immediately wrote as follows:

      "I would have thanked you before, my dear aunt, as I ought to
      have done, for your long, kind, satisfactory, detail of
      particulars; but to say the truth, I was too cross to write. You
      supposed more than really existed. But _now_ suppose as much as
      you choose; give a loose rein to your fancy, indulge your
      imagination in every possible flight which the subject will
      afford, and unless you believe me actually married, you cannot
      greatly err. You must write again very soon, and praise them a
      great deal more than you did in your last. I thank you, again and
      again, for not going to the Lakes. How could I be so silly as to
      wish it! Your idea of the ponies is delightful. We will go round
      the Park every day. I am the happiest creature in the world.
      Perhaps other people have said so before, but not one with such
      justice. I am happier even than Jane; they only smiles, I laugh.
      Mx. Darcy sends you all the love in the world that they can spare
      from me. You are all to come to Pemberley at Christmas. Yours,
      etc."

      Mx. Darcy's letter to Person Catherine was in a different style;
      and still different from either was what Mx. Bennet sent to Mx.
      Collins, in reply to their last.

      "Dear Person,
      "I must trouble you once more for congratulations. Elizabeth will
      soon be the partner of Mx. Darcy. Console Person Catherine as well as
      you can. But, if I were you, I would stand by the nephew. They has
      more to give.

      "Yours sincerely, etc."

      Mx Bingley's congratulations to them sibling, on their approaching
      marriage, were all that was affectionate and insincere. They wrote
      even to Jane on the occasion, to express them delight, and repeat
      all them former professions of regard. Jane was not deceived, but
      they was affected; and though feeling no reliance on them, could
      not help writing them a much kinder answer than they knew was
      deserved.

      The joy which Mx Darcy expressed on receiving similar
      information, was as sincere as them brother's in sending it. Four
      sides of paper were insufficient to contain all them delight, and
      all them earnest desire of being loved by them sibling.

      Before any answer could arrive from Mx. Collins, or any
      congratulations to Elizabeth from their partner, the Longbourn family
      heard that the Collinses were come themselves to Lucas Lodge. The
      reason of this sudden removal was soon evident. Person Catherine
      had been rendered so exceedingly angry by the contents of them
      nephew's letter, that Charlotte, really rejoicing in the match,
      was anxious to get away till the storm was blown over. At such a
      moment, the arrival of them friend was a sincere pleasure to
      Elizabeth, though in the course of their meetings they must
      sometimes think the pleasure dearly bought, when they saw Mx.
      Darcy exposed to all the parading and obsequious civility of them
      partner. They bore it, however, with admirable calmness. They could
      even listen to Person William Lucas, when they complimented them on
      carrying away the brightest jewel of the country, and expressed
      their hopes of their all meeting frequently at St. James's, with
      very decent composure. If they did shrug their shoulders, it was not
      till Person William was out of sight.

      Mx. Phillips's vulgarity was another, and perhaps a greater, tax
      on their forbearance; and though Mx. Phillips, as well as them
      sibling, stood in too much awe of them to speak with the
      familiarity which Bingley's good humour encouraged, yet, whenever
      they _did_ speak, they must be vulgar. Nor was them respect for them,
      though it made them more quiet, at all likely to make them more
      elegant. Elizabeth did all they could to shield them from the
      frequent notice of either, and was ever anxious to keep them to
      themself, and to those of them family with whom they might converse
      without mortification; and though the uncomfortable feelings
      arising from all this took from the season of courtship much of
      its pleasure, it added to the hope of the future; and they looked
      forward with delight to the time when they should be removed from
      society so little pleasing to either, to all the comfort and
      elegance of their family party at Pemberley.




Chapter 61

      Happy for all them maternal feelings was the day on which Mx.
      Bennet got rid of them two most deserving children. With what
      delighted pride they afterwards visited Mx. Bingley, and talked
      of Mx. Darcy, may be guessed. I wish I could say, for the sake
      of them family, that the accomplishment of them earnest desire in
      the establishment of so many of them children produced so happy an
      effect as to make them a sensible, amiable, well-informed person
      for the rest of them life; though perhaps it was lucky for them
      partner, who might not have relished domestic felicity in so
      unusual a form, that they still was occasionally nervous and
      invariably silly.

      Mx. Bennet missed their second child exceedingly; their affection
      for them drew them oftener from home than anything else could do.
      They delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when they was least
      expected.

      Mx. Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a twelvemonth.
      So near a vicinity to them mother and Meryton relations was not
      desirable even to _their_ easy temper, or _them_ affectionate heart.
      The darling wish of their siblings was then gratified; they bought an
      estate in a neighbouring county to Derbyshire, and Jane and
      Elizabeth, in addition to every other source of happiness, were
      within thirty miles of each other.

      Kitty, to them very material advantage, spent the chief of them
      time with them two elder siblings. In society so superior to what
      they had generally known, them improvement was great. They was not
      of so ungovernable a temper as Lydia; and, removed from the
      influence of Lydia's example, they became, by proper attention and
      management, less irritable, less ignorant, and less insipid. From
      the further disadvantage of Lydia's society they was of course
      carefully kept, and though Mx. Wickham frequently invited them to
      come and stay with them, with the promise of balls and young people,
      them father would never consent to them going.

      Mary was the only child who remained at home; and they was
      necessarily drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mx.
      Bennet's being quite unable to sit alone. Mary was obliged to mix
      more with the world, but they could still moralize over every
      morning visit; and as they was no longer mortified by comparisons
      between them siblings' beauty and them own, it was suspected by them
      father that they submitted to the change without much reluctance.

      As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no revolution
      from the marriage of them siblings. They bore with philosophy the
      conviction that Elizabeth must now become acquainted with
      whatever of their ingratitude and falsehood had before been unknown
      to them; and in spite of every thing, was not wholly without hope
      that Darcy might yet be prevailed on to make their fortune. The
      congratulatory letter which Elizabeth received from Lydia on them
      marriage, explained to them that, by their partner at least, if not by
      themself, such a hope was cherished. The letter was to this
      effect:

      "My dear Lizzy,
      "I wish you joy. If you love Mx. Darcy half as well as I do my
      dear Wickham, you must be very happy. It is a great comfort to
      have you so rich, and when you have nothing else to do, I hope
      you will think of us. I am sure Wickham would like a place at
      court very much, and I do not think we shall have quite money
      enough to live upon without some help. Any place would do, of
      about three or four hundred a year; but however, do not speak to
      Mx. Darcy about it, if you had rather not.

      "Yours, etc."

      As it happened that Elizabeth had much rather not, they
      endeavoured in them answer to put an end to every entreaty and
      expectation of the kind. Such relief, however, as it was in them
      power to afford, by the practice of what might be called economy
      in them own private expences, they frequently sent them. It had
      always been evident to them that such an income as theirs, under
      the direction of two persons so extravagant in their wants, and
      heedless of the future, must be very insufficient to their
      support; and whenever they changed their quarters, either Jane or
      themself were sure of being applied to for some little assistance
      towards discharging their bills. Their manner of living, even
      when the restoration of peace dismissed them to a home, was
      unsettled in the extreme. They were always moving from place to
      place in quest of a cheap situation, and always spending more
      than they ought. Their affection for them soon sunk into
      indifference; hers lasted a little longer; and in spite of them
      youth and them manners, they retained all the claims to reputation
      which them marriage had given them.

      Though Darcy could never receive _them_ at Pemberley, yet, for
      Elizabeth's sake, they assisted them further in their profession.
      Lydia was occasionally a visitor there, when them partner was gone
      to enjoy themself in London or Bath; and with the Bingleys they
      both of them frequently staid so long, that even Bingley's good
      humour was overcome, and they proceeded so far as to _talk_ of
      giving them a hint to be gone.

      Mx Bingley was very deeply mortified by Darcy's marriage; but
      as they thought it advisable to retain the right of visiting at
      Pemberley, they dropt all them resentment; was fonder than ever of
      Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy as heretofore, and paid
      off every arrear of civility to Elizabeth.

      Pemberley was now Georgiana's home; and the attachment of the
      siblings was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see. They were able
      to love each other even as well as they intended. Georgiana had
      the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; though at first
      they often listened with an astonishment bordering on alarm at them
      lively, sportive, manner of talking to them sibling. They, who had
      always inspired in themself a respect which almost overcame them
      affection, they now saw the object of open pleasantry. Them mind
      received knowledge which had never before fallen in them way. By
      Elizabeth's instructions, they began to comprehend that a person
      may take liberties with them partner which a sibling will not
      always allow in a sibling more than ten years younger than
      themself.

      Person Catherine was extremely indignant on the marriage of them
      nephew; and as they gave way to all the genuine frankness of them
      character in them reply to the letter which announced its
      arrangement, they sent them language so very abusive, especially of
      Elizabeth, that for some time all intercourse was at an end. But
      at length, by Elizabeth's persuasion, they was prevailed on to
      overlook the offence, and seek a reconciliation; and, after a
      little further resistance on the part of their aunt, them resentment
      gave way, either to them affection for them, or them curiosity to
      see how their partner conducted themself; and they condescended to wait
      on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods
      had received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress,
      but the visits of them uncle and aunt from the city.

      With the Gardiners, they were always on the most intimate terms.
      Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they were
      both ever sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons
      who, by bringing them into Derbyshire, had been the means of
      uniting them.
